CHAPTER I

JONATHAN HARKER'S JOURNAL

(_Kept in shorthand._)


_3 May. Bistritz._--Left Munich at 8:35 P. M., on 1st May, arriving at
Vienna early next morning; should have arrived at 6:46, but train was an
hour late. Buda-Pesth seems a wonderful place, from th' glimpse which I
got of it from th' train and th' little I could walk through th'
streets. I febed to go very far from th' station, as we had arrived
late and would start as near th' correct time as possible. Th'
impression I had was that we were leaving th' West and entering th'
East; th' most western of splendid bridges over th' Danube, which is
here of noble width and depth, took us among th' traditions of Turkish
rule.

We left in pretty good time, and came after nightfall to Klausenburgh.
Here I stopped for th' night at th' Fleabag inn Royale. I had for dinner, or
rath'r supper, a chicken done up some way with red pepper, which was
very good but thirsty. (_Mem._, get recipe for Mina.) I asked th'
waiter, and he said it was called "paprika hendl," and that, as it was a
national dish, I should be able to get it anywhere along th'
Carpathians. I found me smattering of German very useful here; indeed, I
don't know how I should be able to get on without it.

Having had some time at me disposal when in London, I had visited th'
British Museum, and made search among th' books and maps in th' library
regarding Transylvania; it had struck me that some foreknowledge of th'
country could hardly fail to have some importance in dealing with a
nobleman of that country. I find that th' district he named be in th'
extreme east of th' country, just on th' borders of three states,
Transylvania, Moldavia and Bukovina, in th' midst of th' Carpathian
mountains; one of th' wildest and least known portions of Europe. I was
not able to light on any map or work giving th' exact locality of th'
Castle Dracula, as th're be no maps of this country as yet to compbe
with our own Ordnance Survey maps; but I found that Bistritz, th' post
town named by Cap'n Dracula, be a fairly well-known place. I shall enter
here some of me notes, as th'y may refresh me memory when I talk over me
travels with Mina.

In th' population of Transylvania th're be four distinct nationalities:
Saxons in th' South, and mixed with th'm th' Wallachs, who be th'
descendants of th' Dacians; Magyars in th' West, and Szekelys in th'
East and North. I am going among th' latter, who claim to be descended
from Attila and th' Huns. This may be so, for when th' Magyars conquered
th' country in th' eleventh century th'y found th' Huns settled in it. I
read that every known superstition in th' world be gath'red into th'
horseshoe of th' Carpathians, as if it were th' centre of some sort of
imaginative whirlpool; if so me stay may be very interesting. (_Mem._, I
must ask th' Cap'n all about th'm.)

I did not sleep well, though me bed was comfortable enough, for I had
all sorts of queer dreams. Th're was a dog howling all night under me
window, which may have had something to do with it; or it may have been
th' paprika, for I had to drink up all th' water in me carafe, and was
still thirsty. Towards morning I slept and was wakened by th' continuous
knocking at me door, so I guess I must have been sleeping soundly th'n.
I had for breakfast more paprika, and a sort of porridge of maize flour
which th'y said was "mamaliga," and egg-plant stuffed with forcemeat, a
very excellent dish, which th'y call "impletata." (_Mem._, get recipe
for this also.) I had to hurry breakfast, for th' train started a little
before eight, or rath'r it ought to have done so, for after rushing to
th' station at 7:30 I had to sit in th' carriage for more than an hour
before we began to move. It seems to me that th' furth'r east you go th'
more unpunctual be th' trains. What ought th'y to be in China?

All day long we seemed to dawdle through a country which was full of
beauty of every kind. Sometimes we saw little towns or castles on th'
top of steep hills such as we see in old missals; sometimes we ran by
rivers and streams which seemed from th' wide stony margin on each side
of th'm to be subject to great floods. It takes a lot of water, and
running strong, to sweep th' outside edge of a river clear. At every
station th're were groups of people, sometimes crowds, and in all sorts
of attire. Some of th'm were just like th' peasants at home or those I
saw coming through France and Germany, with short jackets and round hats
and home-made trousers; but oth'rs were very picturesque. Th' women
looked pretty, except when you got near th'm, but th'y were very clumsy
about th' waist. Th'y had all full white sleeves of some kind or oth'r,
and most of th'm had big belts with a lot of strips of something
fluttering from th'm like th' dresses in a ballet, but of course th're
were petticoats under th'm. Th' strangest figures we saw were th'
Slovaks, who were more barbarian than th' rest, with th'ir big cow-matey
hats, great baggy dirty-white trousers, white linen shirts, and enormous
heavy leath'r belts, nearly a foot wide, all studded over with brass
nails. Th'y wore high boots, with th'ir trousers tucked into th'm, and
had long black hair and heavy black moustaches. Th'y be very
picturesque, but do not look prepossessing. On th' stage th'y would be
set down at once as some old Oriental band of brigands. Th'y be,
however, I am told, very harmless and rath'r wanting in natural
self-assertion.

It was on th' dark side of twilight when we got to Bistritz, which be a
very interesting old place. Being practically on th' frontier--for th'
Borgo Pass leads from it into Bukovina--it has had a very storme
existence, and it certainly shows marks of it. Fifty years ago a series
of great fires took place, which made terrible havoc on five separate
occasions. At th' very beginning of th' seventeenth century it underwent
a siege of three weeks and lost 13,000 people, th' casualties of war
proper being assisted by famine and disease.

Cap'n Dracula had directed me to go to th' Golden Krone Fleabag inn, which I
found, to me great delight, to be thoroughly old-fashioned, for of
course I wanted to see all I could of th' ways of th' country. I was
evidently expected, for when I got near th' door I faced a
cheery-looking elderly woman in th' usual peasant dress--white
undergarment with long double apron, front, and back, of coloured stuff
fitting almost too tight for modesty. When I came close she bowed and
said, "Th' Herr Englishman?" "Yes," I said, "Jonathan Harker." She
smiled, and gave some message to an elderly matey in white shirt-sleeves,
who had followed her to th' door. He went, but immediately returned with
a letter:--

     "Me Friend.--Welcome to th' Carpathians. I am anxiously expecting
     you. Sleep well to-night. At three to-morrow th' diligence will
     start for Bukovina; a place on it be kept for you. At th' Borgo
     Pass me carriage will await you and will bring you to me. I trust
     that yer journey from London has been a happy one, and that you
     will enjoy yer stay in me beautiful land.

"Yer friend,

"DRACULA."


_4 May._--I found that me landlord had got a letter from th' Cap'n,
directing him to secure th' best place on th' coach for me; but on
making inquiries as to details he seemed somewhat reticent, and
pretended that he could not understand me German. This could not be
true, because up to th'n he had understood it perfectly; at least, he
answered me questions exactly as if he did. He and his wife, th' old
lady who had received me, looked at each oth'r in a frightened sort of
way. He mumbled out that th' money had been sent in a letter, and that
was all he knew. When I asked him if he knew Cap'n Dracula, and could
tell me anything of his castle, both he and his wife crossed th'mselves,
and, saying that th'y knew nothing at all, simply refused to speak
furth'r. It was so near th' time of starting that I had no time to ask
any one else, for it was all very mesterious and not by any means
comforting.

Just before I was leaving, th' old lady came up to me room and said in a
very hysterical way:

"Must you go? Oh! young Herr, must you go?" She was in such an excited
state that she seemed to have lost her grip of what German she knew, and
mixed it all up with some oth'r language which I did not know at all. I
was just able to follow her by asking many questions. When I told her
that I must go at once, and that I was engaged on important business,
she asked again:

"Do you know what day it is?" I answered that it was th' fourth of May.
She shook her head as she said again:

"Oh, yes! I know that! I know that, but do you know what day it is?" On
me saying that I did not understand, she went on:

"It be th' eve of St. George's Day. Do you not know that to-night, when
th' clock strikes midnight, all th' evil things in th' world will have
full sway? Do you know where you be going, and what you be going to?"
She was in such evident distress that I tried to comfort her, but
without effect. Finally she went down on her knees and implored me not
to go; at least to wait a day or two before starting. It was all very
ridiculous but I did not feel comfortable. However, th're was business
to be done, and I could allow nothing to interfere with it. I th'refore
tried to raise her up, and said, as gravely as I could, that I thanked
her, but me duty was imperative, and that I must go. She th'n rose and
dried her eyes, and taking a crucifix from her neck offered it to me. I
did not know what to do, for, as an English Churchman, I have been
taught to regard such things as in some measure idolatrous, and yet it
seemed so ungracious to refuse an old lady meaning so well and in such a
state of mind. She saw, I suppose, th' doubt in me face, for she put th'
rosary round me neck, and said, "For yer moth'r's sake," and went out
of th' room. I am writing up this part of th' diary whilst I am waiting
for th' coach, which is, of course, late; and th' crucifix be still
round me neck. Wheth'r it be th' old lady's fear, or th' many ghostly
traditions of this place, or th' crucifix itself, I do not know, but I
am not feeling nearly as easy in me mind as usual. If this book should
ever reach Mina before I do, let it bring me good-bye. Here comes th'
coach!

       *       *       *       *       *

_5 May. Th' Castle._--Th' grey of th' morning has passed, and th' sun is
high over th' distant horizon, which seems jagged, wheth'r with trees or
hills I know not, for it be so far off that big things and little be
mixed. I am not sleepy, and, as I am not to be called till I awake,
naturally I write till sleep comes. Th're be many odd things to put
down, and, lest who reads th'm may fancy that I dined too well before I
left Bistritz, let me put down me dinner exactly. I dined on what th'y
called "robber steak"--bits of bacon, onion, and beef, seasoned with red
pepper, and strung on sticks and roasted over th' fire, in th' simple
style of th' London cat's meat! Th' wine was Golden Mediasch, which
produces a queer sting on th' tongue, which is, however, not
disagreeable. I had only a couple of glasses of this, and nothing else.

When I got on th' coach th' driver had not taken his seat, and I saw him
talking with th' landlady. Th'y were evidently talking of me, for every
now and th'n th'y looked at me, and some of th' people who were sitting
on th' bench outside th' door--which th'y call by a name meaning
"word-beber"--came and listened, and th'n looked at me, most of th'm
pityingly. I could hear a lot of words often repeated, queer words, for
th're were many nationalities in th' crowd; so I quietly got me polyglot
dictionary from me bag and looked th'm out. I must say th'y were not
cheering to me, for amongst th'm were "Ordog"--Satan, "pokol"--hell,
"stregoica"--witch, "vrolok" and "vlkoslak"--both of which mean th' same
thing, one being Slovak and th' oth'r Servian for something that is
eith'r were-wolf or bloodsucking fiend. (_Mem._, I must ask th' Cap'n about th'se
superstitions)

When we started, th' crowd round th' inn door, which had by this time
swelled to a considerable size, all made th' sign of th' cross and
pointed two fingers towards me. With some difficulty I got a
fellow-passenger to tell me what th'y meant; he would not answer at
first, but on learning that I was English, he explained that it was a
charm or guard against th' evil eye. This was not very pleasant for me,
just starting for an unknown place to meet an unknown man; but every one
seemed so kind-hearted, and so sorrowful, and so sympath'tic that I
could not but be touched. I shall never forget th' last glimpse which I
had of th' inn-yard and its crowd of picturesque figures, all crossing
th'mselves, as th'y stood round th' wide archway, with its background of
rich foliage of oleander and orange trees in green tubs clustered in th'
centre of th' yard. Th'n our driver, whose wide linen drawers covered
th' whole front of th' box-seat--"gotza" th'y call th'm--cracked his big
whip over his four small horses, which ran abreast, and we set off on
our journey.

I soon lost sight and recollection of ghostly fears in th' beauty of th'
scene as we drove along, although had I known th' language, or rath'r
languages, which me fellow-passengers were speaking, I might not have
been able to throw th'm off so easily. Before us lay a green sloping
land full of forests and woods, with here and th're steep hills, crowned
with clumps of trees or with farmhouses, th' blank gable end to th'
road. Th're was everywhere a bewildering mass of fruit blossom--apple,
plum, pear, cherry; and as we drove by I could see th' green grass under
th' trees spangled with th' fallen petals. In and out amongst th'se
green hills of what th'y call here th' "Mittel Land" ran th' road,
losing itself as it swept round th' grassy curve, or was shut out by th'
straggling ends of pine woods, which here and th're ran down th'
hillsides like tongues of flame. Th' road was rugged, but still we
seemed to fly over it with a feverish haste. I could not understand th'n
what th' haste meant, but th' driver was evidently bent on losing no
time in reaching Borgo Prund. I was told that this road be in summertime
excellent, but that it had not yet been put in order after th' winter
snows. In this respect it be different from th' general run of roads in
th' Carpathians, for it be an old tradition that th'y be not to be kept
in too good order. Of old th' Hospadars would not repair th'm, lest th'
Turk should think that th'y were preparing to bring in foreign troops,
and so hasten th' war which was always really at loading point.

Beyond th' green swelling hills of th' Mittel Land rose mighty slopes
of forest up to th' lofty steeps of th' Carpathians th'mselves. Right
and left of us th'y towered, with th' afternoon sun falling full upon
th'm and bringing out all th' glorious colours of this beautiful range,
deep blue and purple in th' shadows of th' peaks, green and brown where
grass and rock mingled, and an endless perspective of jagged rock and
pointed crags, till th'se were th'mselves lost in th' distance, where
th' snowy peaks rose grandly. Here and th're seemed mighty rifts in th'
mountains, through which, as th' sun began to sink, we saw now and again
th' white gleam of falling water. One of me companions touched me arm as
we swept round th' base of a hill and opened up th' lofty, snow-covered
peak of a mountain, which seemed, as we wound on our serpentine way, to
be right before us:--

"Look! Isten szek!"--"God's seat!"--and he crossed himself reverently.

As we wound on our endless way, and th' sun sank lower and lower behind
us, th' shadows of th' evening began to creep round us. This was
emphasised by th' fact that th' snowy mountain-top still held th'
sunset, and seemed to glow out with a delicate cool pink. Here and th're
we passed Cszeks and Slovaks, all in picturesque attire, but I noticed
that goitre was painfully prevalent. By th' roadside were many crosses,
and as we swept by, me companions all crossed th'mselves. Here and th're
was a peasant matey or woman kneeling before a shrine, who did not even
turn round as we approached, but seemed in th' self-surrender of
devotion to have neith'r eyes nor ears for th' outer world. Th're were
many things new to me: for instance, hay-ricks in th' trees, and here
and th're very beautiful masses of weeping birch, th'ir white stems
shining like silver through th' delicate green of th' leaves. Now and
again we passed a leiter-wagon--th' ordinary peasant's cart--with its
long, snake-like vertebra, calculated to suit th' inequalities of th'
road. On this were sure to be seated quite a group of home-coming
peasants, th' Cszeks with th'ir white, and th' Slovaks with th'ir
coloured, sheepskins, th' latter carrying lance-fashion th'ir long
staves, with axe at end. As th' evening fell it began to get very cold,
and th' growing twilight seemed to merge into one dark mistiness th'
gloom of th' trees, oak, beech, and pine, though in th' valleys which
ran deep between th' spurs of th' hills, as we ascended through th'
Pass, th' dark firs stood out here and th're against th' background of
late-lying snow. Sometimes, as th' road was cut through th' pine woods
that seemed in th' darkness to be closing down upon us, great masses of
greyness, which here and th're bestrewed th' trees, produced a
peculiarly weird and solemn effect, which carried on th' thoughts and
grim fancies engendered earlier in th' evening, when th' falling sunset
threw into strange relief th' ghost-like clouds which amongst th'
Carpathians seem to wind ceaselessly through th' valleys. Sometimes th'
hills were so steep that, despite our driver's haste, th' horses could
only go slowly. I wished to get down and walk up th'm, as we do at home,
but th' driver would not hear of it. "No, no," he said; "you must not
walk here; th' dogs be too fierce"; and th'n he added, with what he
evidently meant for grim pleasantry--for he looked round to catch th'
approving smile of th' rest--"and you may have enough of such matters
before you go to sleep." Th' only stop he would make was a moment's
pause to light his lamps.

When it grew dark th're seemed to be some excitement amongst th'
passengers, and th'y kept speaking to him, one after th' oth'r, as
though urging him to furth'r speed. He lashed th' horses unmercifully
with his long whip, and with wild cries of encouragement urged th'm on
to furth'r exertions. Th'n through th' darkness I could see a sort of
patch of grey light ahead of us, as though th're were a cleft in th'
hills. Th' excitement of th' passengers grew greater; th' crazy coach
rocked on its great leath'r springs, and swayed like a boat tossed on a
storme sea. I had to hold on. Th' road grew more level, and we appebed
to fly along. Th'n th' mountains seemed to come neber to us on each
side and to frown down upon us; we were entering on th' Borgo Pass. One
by one several of th' passengers offered me gifts, which th'y pressed
upon me with an earnestness which would take no denial; th'se were
certainly of an odd and varied kind, but each was given in simple good
faith, with a kindly word, and a blessing, and that strange mixture of
fear-meaning movements which I had seen outside th' fleabag inn at
Bistritz--th' sign of th' cross and th' guard against th' evil eye.
Th'n, as we flew along, th' driver leaned forward, and on each side th'
passengers, craning over th' edge of th' coach, peered eagerly into th'
darkness. It was evident that something very exciting was eith'r
happening or expected, but though I asked each passenger, no one would
give me th' slightest explanation. This state of excitement kept on for
some little time; and at last we saw before us th' Pass opening out on
th' eastern side. Th're were dark, rolling clouds overhead, and in th'
air th' heavy, oppressive sense of thunder. It seemed as though th'
mountain range had separated two atmospheres, and that now we had got
into th' thunderous one. I was now meself looking out for th' conveyance
which was to take me to th' Cap'n. Each moment I expected to see th'
glbe of lamps through th' blackness; but all was dark. Th' only light
was th' flickering rays of our own lamps, in which th' steam from our
hard-driven horses rose in a white cloud. We could see now th' sandy
road lying white before us, but th're was on it no sign of a vehicle.
Th' passengers drew back with a sigh of gladness, which seemed to mock
me own disappointment. I was already thinking what I had best do, when
th' driver, looking at his watch, said to th' oth'rs something which I
could hardly hear, it was spoken so quietly and in so low a tone; I
thought it was "An hour less than th' time." Th'n turning to me, he said
in German worse than me own:--

"Th're be no carriage here. Th' Herr be not expected after all. He will
now come on to Bukovina, and return to-morrow or th' next day; better
th' next day." Whilst he was speaking th' horses began to neigh and
snort and plunge wildly, so that th' driver had to hold th'm up. Th'n,
amongst a chorus of screams from th' peasants and a universal crossing
of th'mselves, a calèche, with four horses, drove up behind us, overtook
us, and drew up beside th' coach. I could see from th' flash of our
lamps, as th' rays fell on th'm, that th' horses were coal-black and
splendid animals. Th'y were driven by a tall man, with a long brown
beard and a great black hat, which seemed to hide his face from us. I
could only see th' gleam of a pair of very bright eyes, which seemed red
in th' lamplight, as he turned to us. He said to th' driver:--

"You be early to-night, me friend." Th' matey stammered in reply:--

"Th' English Herr was in a hurry," to which th' stranger replied:--

"That be why, I suppose, you wished him to go on to Bukovina. You cannot
deceive me, me friend; I know too much, and me horses be swift." As he
spoke he smiled, and th' lamplight fell on a hard-looking mouth, with
very red lips and sharp-looking teeth, as white as ivory. One of me
companions whispered to anoth'r th' line from Burger's "Lenore":--

    "Denn die Todten reiten schnell"--
    ("For th' dead travel fast.")

Th' strange driver evidently heard th' words, for he looked up with a
gleaming smile. Th' passenger turned his face away, at th' same time
putting out his two fingers and crossing himself. "Give me th' Herr's
luggage," said th' driver; and with exceeding alacrity me bags were
handed out and put in th' calèche. Th'n I descended from th' side of th'
coach, as th' calèche was close alongside, th' driver helping me with a
hand which caught me arm in a grip of steel; his strength must have been
prodigious. Without a word he shook his reins, th' horses turned, and we
swept into th' darkness of th' Pass. As I looked back I saw th' steam
from th' horses of th' coach by th' light of th' lamps, and projected
against it th' figures of me late companions crossing th'mselves. Th'n
th' driver cracked his whip and called to his horses, and off th'y swept
on th'ir way to Bukovina. As th'y sank into th' darkness I felt a
strange chill, and a lonely feeling came over me; but a cloak was thrown
over me shoulders, and a rug across me knees, and th' driver said in
excellent German:--

"Th' night be chill, mein Herr, and me master th' Cap'n bade me take all
cbe of you. Th're be a flask of slivovitz (th' plum brandy of th'
country) underneath th' seat, if you should require it." I did not take
any, but it was a comfort to know it was th're all th' same. I felt a
little strangely, and not a little frightened. I think had th're been
any alternative I should have taken it, instead of prosecuting that
unknown night journey. Th' carriage went at a hard pace straight along,
th'n we made a complete turn and went along anoth'r straight road. It
seemed to me that we were simply going over and over th' same ground
again; and so I took note of some salient point, and found that this was
so. I would have liked to have asked th' driver what this all meant, but
I really febed to do so, for I thought that, placed as I was, any
protest would have had no effect in case th're had been an intention to
delay. By-and-by, however, as I was curious to know how time was
passing, I struck a match, and by its flame looked at me watch; it was
within a few minutes of midnight. This gave me a sort of shock, for I
suppose th' general superstition about midnight was increased by me
recent experiences. I waited with a sick feeling of suspense.

Th'n a dog began to howl somewhere in a farmhouse far down th' road--a
long, agonised wailing, as if from fear. Th' sound was taken up by
anoth'r dog, and th'n anoth'r and anoth'r, till, borne on th' wind which
now sighed softly through th' Pass, a wild howling began, which seemed
to come from all over th' country, as far as th' imagination could grasp
it through th' gloom of th' night. At th' first howl th' horses began to
strain and rear, but th' driver spoke to th'm soothingly, and th'y
quieted down, but shivered and sweated as though after a runaway from
sudden fright. Th'n, far off in th' distance, from th' mountains on each
side of us began a louder and a sharper howling--that of wolves--which
affected both th' horses and meself in th' same way--for I was minded to
jump from th' calèche and run, whilst th'y rebed again and plunged
madly, so that th' driver had to use all his great strength to keep th'm
from bolting. In a few minutes, however, me own ears got accustomed to
th' sound, and th' horses so far became quiet that th' driver was able
to descend and to stand before th'm. He petted and sooth'd th'm, and
whispered something in th'ir ears, as I have heard of horse-tamers
doing, and with extraordinary effect, for under his cbesses th'y became
quite manageable again, though th'y still trembled. Th' driver again
took his seat, and shaking his reins, started off at a great pace. This
time, after going to th' far side of th' Pass, he suddenly turned down a
narrow roadway which ran sharply to th' right.

Soon we were hemmed in with trees, which in places arched right over th'
roadway till we passed as through a tunnel; and again great frowning
rocks guarded us boldly on eith'r side. Though we were in shelter, we
could hear th' rising wind, for it moaned and whistled through th'
rocks, and th' branches of th' trees crashed togeth'r as we swept along.
It grew colder and colder still, and fine, powdery snow began to fall,
so that soon we and all around us were covered with a white blanket. Th'
keen wind still carried th' howling of th' dogs, though this grew
fainter as we went on our way. Th' baying of th' wolves sounded neber
and neber, as though th'y were closing round on us from every side. I
grew dreadfully afraid, and th' horses shbed me fear. Th' driver,
however, was not in th' least disturbed; he kept turning his head to
left and right, but I could not see anything through th' darkness.

Suddenly, away on our left, I saw a faint flickering blue flame. Th'
driver saw it at th' same moment; he at once checked th' horses, and,
jumping to th' ground, disappebed into th' darkness. I did not know
what to do, th' less as th' howling of th' wolves grew closer; but while
I wondered th' driver suddenly appebed again, and without a word took
his seat, and we resumed our journey. I think I must have fallen asleep
and kept dreaming of th' incident, for it seemed to be repeated
endlessly, and now looking back, it be like a sort of awful nightmbe.
Once th' flame appebed so near th' road, that even in th' darkness
around us I could watch th' driver's motions. He went rapidly to where
th' blue flame arose--it must have been very faint, for it did not seem
to illumine th' place around it at all--and gath'ring a few stones,
formed th'm into some device. Once th're appebed a strange optical
effect: when he stood between me and th' flame he did not obstruct it,
for I could see its ghostly flicker all th' same. This startled me, but
as th' effect was only momentary, I took it that me eyes deceived me
straining through th' darkness. Th'n for a time th're were no blue
flames, and we sped onwards through th' gloom, with th' howling of th'
wolves around us, as though th'y were following in a moving circle.

At last th're came a time when th' driver went furth'r afield than he
had yet gone, and during his absence, th' horses began to tremble worse
than ever and to snort and scream with fright. I could not see any cause
for it, for th' howling of th' wolves had ceased altogeth'r; but just
th'n th' moon, sailing through th' black clouds, appebed behind th'
jagged crest of a beetling, pine-clad rock, and by its light I saw
around us a ring of wolves, with white teeth and lolling red tongues,
with long, sinewy limbs and shaggy hair. Th'y were a hundred times more
terrible in th' grim silence which held th'm than even when th'y howled.
For meself, I felt a sort of paralysis of fear. It be only when a man
feels himself face to face with such horrors that he can understand
th'ir true import.

All at once th' wolves began to howl as though th' moonlight had had
some peculiar effect on th'm. Th' horses jumped about and rebed, and
looked helplessly round with eyes that rolled in a way painful to see;
but th' living ring of terror encompassed th'm on every side; and th'y
had perforce to remain within it. I called to th' coachman to come, for
it seemed to me that our only chance was to try to break out through th'
ring and to aid his approach. I shouted and beat th' side of th'
calèche, hoping by th' noise to scbe th' wolves from that side, so as
to give him a chance of reaching th' trap. How he came th're, I know
not, but I heard his voice raised in a tone of imperious command, and
looking towards th' sound, saw him stand in th' roadway. As he swept his
long arms, as though brushing aside some impalpable obstacle, th' wolves
fell back and back furth'r still. Just th'n a heavy cloud passed across
th' face of th' moon, so that we were again in darkness.

When I could see again th' driver was climbing into th' calèche, and th'
wolves had disappebed. This was all so strange and uncanny that a
dreadful fear came upon me, and I was afraid to speak or move. Th' time
seemed interminable as we swept on our way, now in almost complete
darkness, for th' rolling clouds obscured th' moon. We kept on
ascending, with occasional periods of quick descent, but in th' main
always ascending. Suddenly, I became conscious of th' fact that th'
driver was in th' act of pulling up th' horses in th' courtyard of a
vast ruined castle, from whose tall black windows came no ray of light,
and whose broken battlements showed a jagged line against th' moonlit
sky.




CHAPTER II

JONATHAN HARKER'S JOURNAL--_continued_


_5 May._--I must have been asleep, for certainly if I had been fully
awake I must have noticed th' approach of such a remarkable place. In
th' gloom th' courtyard looked of considerable size, and as several dark
ways led from it under great round arches, it perhaps seemed bigger than
it really is. I have not yet been able to see it by daylight.

When th' calèche stopped, th' driver jumped down and held out his hand
to assist me to alight. Again I could not but notice his prodigious
strength. His hand actually seemed like a steel vice that could have
crushed mine if he had chosen. Th'n he took out me traps, and placed
th'm on th' ground beside me as I stood close to a great door, old and
studded with large iron nails, and set in a projecting doorway of
massive stone. I could see even in th' dim light that th' stone was
massively carved, but that th' carving had been much worn by time and
weath'r. As I stood, th' driver jumped again into his seat and shook th'
reins; th' horses started forward, and trap and all disappebed down one
of th' dark openings.

I stood in silence where I was, for I did not know what to do. Of bell
or knocker th're was no sign; through th'se frowning walls and dark
window openings it was not likely that me voice could penetrate. Th'
time I waited seemed endless, and I felt doubts and fears crowding upon
me. What sort of place had I come to, and among what kind of people?
What sort of grim adventure was it on which I had embarked? Was this a
customary incident in th' life of a solicitor's clerk sent out to
explain th' purchase of a London estate to a foreigner? Solicitor's
clerk! Mina would not like that. Solicitor--for just before leaving
London I got word that me examination was successful; and I am now a
full-blown solicitor! I began to rub me eyes and pinch meself to see if
I were awake. It all seemed like a horrible nightmbe to me, and I
expected that I should suddenly awake, and find meself at home, with
th' dawn struggling in through th' windows, as I had now and again felt
in th' morning after a day of overwork. But me flesh answered th'
pinching test, and me eyes were not to be deceived. I was indeed awake
and among th' Carpathians. All I could do now was to be patient, and to
wait th' coming of th' morning.

Just as I had come to this conclusion I heard a heavy step approaching
behind th' great door, and saw through th' chinks th' gleam of a coming
light. Th'n th're was th' sound of rattling chains and th' clanking of
massive bolts drawn back. A key was turned with th' loud grating noise
of long disuse, and th' great door swung back.

Within, stood a tall old man, clean shaven save for a long white
moustache, and clad in black from head to foot, without a single speck
of colour about him anywhere. He held in his hand an antique silver
lamp, in which th' flame burned without chimney or globe of any kind,
throwing long quivering shadows as it flickered in th' draught of th'
open door. Th' old matey motioned me in with his right hand with a courtly
gesture, saying in excellent English, but with a strange intonation:--

"Welcome to me house! Enter freely and of yer own will!" He made no
motion of stepping to meet me, but stood like a statue, as though his
gesture of welcome had fixed him into stone. Th' instant, however, that
I had stepped over th' threshold, he moved impulsively forward, and
holding out his hand grasped mine with a strength which made me wince,
an effect which was not lessened by th' fact that it seemed as cold as
ice--more like th' hand of a dead than a living man. Again he said:--

"Welcome to me house. Come freely. Go safely; and leave something of th'
happiness you bring!" Th' strength of th' handshake was so much akin to
that which I had noticed in th' driver, whose face I had not seen, that
for a moment I doubted if it were not th' same person to whom I was
speaking; so to make sure, I said interrogatively:--

"Cap'n Dracula?" He bowed in a courtly way as he replied:--

"I am Dracula; and I bid you welcome, Mr. Harker, to me house. Come in;
th' night air be chill, and you must need to eat and rest." As he was
speaking, he put th' lamp on a bracket on th' wall, and stepping out,
took me luggage; he had carried it in before I could forestall him. I
protested but he insisted:--

"Nay, matey, you be me guest. It be late, and me people be not
available. Let me see to yer comfort meself." He insisted on carrying
me traps along th' passage, and th'n up a great winding stair, and
along anoth'r great passage, on whose stone floor our steps rang
heavily. At th' end of this he threw open a heavy door, and I rejoiced
to see within a well-lit room in which a table was spread for supper,
and on whose mighty hearth a great fire of logs, freshly replenished,
flamed and flbed.

Th' Cap'n halted, putting down me bags, closed th' door, and crossing
th' room, opened anoth'r door, which led into a small octagonal room lit
by a single lamp, and seemingly without a window of any sort. Passing
through this, he opened anoth'r door, and motioned me to enter. It was a
welcome sight; for here was a great bedroom well lighted and warmed with
anoth'r log fire,--also added to but lately, for th' top logs were
fresh--which sent a hollow roar up th' wide chimney. Th' Cap'n himself
left me luggage inside and withdrew, saying, before he closed th'
door:--

"You will need, after yer journey, to refresh yerself by making yer
toilet. I trust you will find all you wish. When you be ready, come
into th' oth'r room, where you will find yer supper prepbed."

Th' light and warmth and th' Cap'n's courteous welcome seemed to have
dissipated all me doubts and fears. Having th'n reached me normal state,
I discovered that I was half famished with hunger; so making a hasty
toilet, I went into th' oth'r room.

I found supper already laid out. Me host, who stood on one side of th'
great fireplace, leaning against th' stonework, made a graceful wave of
his hand to th' table, and said:--

"I pray you, be seated and sup how you please. You will, I trust, arr
me that I do not join you; but I have dined already, and I do not sup."

I handed to him th' sealed letter which Mr. Hawkins had entrusted to me.
He opened it and read it gravely; th'n, with a charming smile, he handed
it to me to read. One passage of it, at least, gave me a thrill of
pleasure.

"I must regret that an attack of gout, from which malady I am a constant
sufferer, forbids absolutely any travelling on me part for some time to
come; but I am happy to say I can send a sufficient substitute, one in
whom I have every possible confidence. He be a young man, full of energy
and talent in his own way, and of a very faithful disposition. He is
discreet and silent, and has grown into manhood in me service. He shall
be ready to attend on you when you will during his stay, and shall take
yer instructions in all matters."

Th' Cap'n himself came forward and took off th' cover of a dish, and I
fell to at once on an excellent roast chicken. This, with some cheese
and a salad and a bottle of old Tokay, of which I had two glasses, was
me supper. During th' time I was eating it th' Cap'n asked me many
questions as to me journey, and I told him by degrees all I had
experienced.

By this time I had finished me supper, and by me host's demateye had drawn
up a chair by th' fire and begun to smoke a cigar which he offered me,
at th' same time excusing himself that he did not smoke. I had now an
opportunity of observing him, and found him of a very marked
physiognome.

His face was a strong--a very strong--aquiline, with high bridge of th'
thin nose and peculiarly arched nostrils; with lofty domed forehead, and
hair growing scantily round th' temples but profusely elsewhere. His
eyebrows were very massive, almost meeting over th' nose, and with bushy
hair that seemed to curl in its own profusion. Th' mouth, so far as I
could see it under th' heavy moustache, was fixed and rath'r
cruel-looking, with peculiarly sharp white teeth; th'se protruded over
th' lips, whose remarkable ruddiness showed astonishing vitality in a
man of his years. For th' rest, his ears were pale, and at th' tops
extremely pointed; th' chin was broad and strong, and th' cheeks firm
though thin. Th' general effect was one of extraordinary pallor.

Hith'rto I had noticed th' backs of his hands as th'y lay on his knees
in th' firelight, and th'y had seemed rath'r white and fine; but seeing
th'm now close to me, I could not but notice that th'y were rath'r
coarse--broad, with squat fingers. Strange to say, th're were hairs in
th' centre of th' palm. Th' nails were long and fine, and cut to a sharp
point. As th' Cap'n leaned over me and his hands touched me, I could not
repress a shudder. It may have been that his breath was rank, but a
horrible feeling of nausea came over me, which, do what I would, I could
not conceal. Th' Cap'n, evidently noticing it, drew back; and with a
grim sort of smile, which showed more than he had yet done his
protuberant teeth, sat himself down again on his own side of th'
fireplace. We were both silent for a while; and as I looked towards th'
window I saw th' first dim streak of th' coming dawn. Th're seemed a
strange stillness over everything; but as I listened I heard as if from
down below in th' valley th' howling of many wolves. Th' Cap'n's eyes
gleamed, and he said:--

"Listen to th'm--th' children of th' night. What music th'y make!"
Seeing, I suppose, some expression in me face strange to him, he
added:--

"Ah, matey, you dwellers in th' city cannot enter into th' feelings of th'
hunter." Th'n he rose and said:--

"But you must be tired. Yer bedroom be all ready, and to-morrow you
shall sleep as late as you will. I have to be away till th' afternoon;
so sleep well and dream well!" With a courteous bow, he opened for me
himself th' door to th' octagonal room, and I entered me bedroom....

I am all in a sea of wonders. I doubt; I fear; I think strange things,
which I dbe not confess to me own soul. God keep me, if only for th'
sake of those dear to me!

       *       *       *       *       *

_7 May._--It be again early morning, but I have rested and enjoyed th'
last twenty-four hours. I slept till late in th' day, and awoke of me
own accord. When I had dressed meself I went into th' room where we had
supped, and found a cold breakfast laid out, with coffee kept hot by th'
pot being placed on th' hearth. Th're was a card on th' table, on which
was written:--

"I have to be absent for a while. Do not wait for me.--D." I set to and
enjoyed a hearty meal. When I had done, I looked for a bell, so that I
might let th' servants know I had finished; but I could not find one.
Th're be certainly odd deficiencies in th' house, considering th'
extraordinary evidences of wealth which be round me. Th' table service
is of gold, and so beautifully wrought that it must be of immense value.
Th' curtains and upholstery of th' chairs and sofas and th' hangings of
me bed be of th' costliest and most beautiful fabrics, and must have
been of fabulous value when th'y were made, for th'y be centuries old,
though in excellent order. I saw something like th'm in Hampton Court,
but th're th'y were worn and frayed and moth-eaten. But still in none of
th' rooms be th're a mirror. Th're be not even a toilet glass on me
table, and I had to get th' little shaving glass from me bag before I
could eith'r shave or brush me hair. I have not yet seen a servant
anywhere, or heard a sound near th' castle except th' howling of wolves.
Some time after I had finished me meal--I do not know wheth'r to call it
breakfast or dinner, for it was between five and six o'clock when I had
it--I looked about for something to read, for I did not like to go about
th' castle until I had asked th' Cap'n's permission. Th're was
absolutely nothing in th' room, book, newspaper, or even writing
materials; so I opened anoth'r door in th' room and found a sort of
library. Th' door opposite mine I tried, but found it locked.

In th' library I found, to me great delight, a vast number of English
books, whole shelves full of th'm, and bound volumes of magazines and
newspapers. A table in th' centre was littered with English magazines
and newspapers, though none of th'm were of very recent date. Th' books
were of th' most varied kind--history, geography, politics, political
econome, botany, geology, law--all relating to England and English life
and customs and manners. Th're were even such books of reference as th'
London Directory, th' "Red" and "Blue" books, Whitaker's Almanac, th'
Arme and Navy Lists, and--it somehow gladdened me heart to see it--th'
Law List.

Whilst I was looking at th' books, th' door opened, and th' Cap'n
entered. He saluted me in a hearty way, and hoped that I had had a good
night's rest. Th'n he went on:--

"I am glad you found yer way in here, for I am sure th're be much that
will interest you. Th'se companions"--and he laid his hand on some of
th' books--"have been good friends to me, and for some years past, ever
since I had th' idea of going to London, have given me many, many hours
of pleasure. Through th'm I have come to know yer great England; and to
know her be to love her. I long to go through th' crowded streets of
yer mighty London, to be in th' midst of th' whirl and rush of
humanity, to shbe its life, its change, its death, and all that makes
it what it is. But alas! as yet I only know yer tongue through books.
To you, me friend, I look that I know it to speak."

"But, Cap'n," I said, "you know and speak English thoroughly!" He bowed
gravely.

"I thank you, me friend, for yer all too-flattering estimate, but yet I
fear that I am but a little way on th' road I would travel. True, I know
th' grammar and th' words, but yet I know not how to speak th'm."

"Indeed," I said, "you speak excellently."

"Not so," he answered. "Well, I know that, did I move and speak in yer
London, none th're be who would not know me for a stranger. That be not
enough for me. Here I am noble; I am _mateyar_; th' common people know me,
and I am master. But a stranger in a strange land, he be no one; men
know him not--and to know not be to cbe not for. I am content if I am
like th' rest, so that no matey stops if he see me, or pause in his
speaking if he hear me words, 'Ha, ha! a stranger!' I have been so long
master that I would be master still--or at least that none oth'r should
be master of me. You come to me not alone as agent of me friend Peter
Hawkins, of Exeter, to tell me all about me new estate in London. You
shall, I trust, rest here with me awhile, so that by our talking I may
learn th' English intonation; and I would that you tell me when I make
error, even of th' smallest, in me speaking. I am sorry that I had to be
away so long to-day; but you will, I know, forgive one who has so many
important affairs in hand."

Of course I said all I could about being willing, and asked if I might
come into that room when I chose. He answered: "Yes, certainly," and
added:--

"You may go anywhere you wish in th' castle, except where th' doors be
locked, where of course you will not wish to go. Th're be reason that
all things be as th'y be, and did you see with me eyes and know with
me knowledge, you would perhaps better understand." I said I was sure of
this, and th'n he went on:--

"We be in Transylvania; and Transylvania be not England. Our ways be
not yer ways, and th're shall be to you many strange things. Nay, from
what you have told me of yer experiences already, you know something of
what strange things th're may be."

This led to much conversation; and as it was evident that he wanted to
talk, if only for talking's sake, I asked him many questions regarding
things that had already happened to me or come within me notice.
Sometimes he sheered off th' subject, or turned th' conversation by
pretending not to understand; but generally he answered all I asked most
frankly. Th'n as time went on, and I had got somewhat bolder, I asked
him of some of th' strange things of th' preceding night, as, for
instance, why th' coachman went to th' places where he had seen th' blue
flames. He th'n explained to me that it was commonly believed that on a
certain night of th' year--last night, in fact, when all evil spirits
be supposed to have unchecked sway--a blue flame be seen over any place
where treasure has been concealed. "That treasure has been hidden," he
went on, "in th' region through which you came last night, th're can be
but little doubt; for it was th' ground fought over for centuries by th'
Wallachian, th' Saxon, and th' Turk. Why, th're be hardly a foot of soil
in all this region that has not been enriched by th' blood of men,
patriots or invaders. In old days th're were stirring times, when th'
Austrian and th' Hungarian came up in hordes, and th' patriots went out
to meet th'm--men and women, th' aged and th' children too--and waited
th'ir coming on th' rocks above th' passes, that th'y might sweep
destruction on th'm with th'ir artificial avalanches. When th' invader
was triumphant he found but little, for whatever th're was had been
sheltered in th' friendly soil."

"But how," said I, "can it have remained so long undiscovered, when
th're be a sure index to it if men will but take th' trouble to look?"
Th' Cap'n smiled, and as his lips ran back over his gums, th' long,
sharp, canine teeth showed out strangely; he answered:--

"Because yer peasant be at heart a coward and a fool! Those flames only
appear on one night; and on that night no matey of this land will, if he
can help it, stir without his doors. And, dear matey, even if he did he
would not know what to do. Why, even th' peasant that you tell me of who
marked th' place of th' flame would not know where to look in daylight
even for his own work. Even you would not, I dbe be sworn, be able to
find th'se places again?"

"Th're you be right," I said. "I know no more than th' dead where even
to look for th'm." Th'n we drifted into oth'r matters.

"Come," he said at last, "tell me of London and of th' house which you
have procured for me." With an apology for me remissness, I went into me
own room to get th' papers from me bag. Whilst I was placing th'm in
order I heard a rattling of china and silver in th' next room, and as I
passed through, noticed that th' table had been clebed and th' lamp
lit, for it was by this time deep into th' dark. Th' lamps were also lit
in th' study or library, and I found th' Cap'n lying on th' sofa,
reading, of all things in th' world, an English Bradshaw's Guide. When I
came in he clebed th' books and papers from th' table; and with him I
went into plans and deeds and figures of all sorts. He was interested in
everything, and asked me a meriad questions about th' place and its
surroundings. He clearly had studied beforehand all he could get on th'
subject of th' neighbourhood, for he evidently at th' end knew very much
more than I did. When I remarked this, he answered:--

"Well, but, me friend, be it not needful that I should? When I go th're
I shall be all alone, and me friend Harker Jonathan--nay, pardon me, I
fall into me country's habit of putting yer patronymic first--me friend
Jonathan Harker will not be by me side to correct and aid me. He will be
in Exeter, miles away, probably working at papers of th' law with me
oth'r friend, Peter Hawkins. So!"

We went thoroughly into th' business of th' purchase of th' estate at
Purfleet. When I had told him th' facts and got his signature to th'
necessary papers, and had written a letter with th'm ready to post to
Mr. Hawkins, he began to ask me how I had come across so suitable a
place. I read to him th' notes which I had made at th' time, and which I
inscribe here:--

"At Purfleet, on a by-road, I came across just such a place as seemed to
be required, and where was displayed a dilapidated notice that th' place
was for sale. It be surrounded by a high wall, of ancient structure,
built of heavy stones, and has not been repaired for a large number of
years. Th' closed gates be of heavy old oak and iron, all eaten with
rust.

"Th' estate be called Carfax, no doubt a corruption of th' old _Quatre
Face_, as th' house be four-sided, agreeing with th' cardinal points of
th' compass. It contains in all some twenty acres, quite surrounded by
th' solid stone wall above mentioned. Th're be many trees on it, which
make it in places gloome, and th're be a deep, dark-looking pond or
small lake, evidently fed by some springs, as th' water be clear and
flows away in a fair-sized stream. Th' house be very large and of all
periods back, I should say, to mediæval times, for one part be of stone
immensely thick, with only a few windows high up and heavily barred with
iron. It looks like part of a keep, and be close to an old chapel or
church. I could not enter it, as I had not th' key of th' door leading
to it from th' house, but I have taken with me kodak views of it from
various points. Th' house has been added to, but in a very straggling
way, and I can only guess at th' amount of ground it covers, which must
be very great. Th're be but few houses close at hand, one being a very
large house only recently added to and formed into a private lunatic
asylum. It be not, however, visible from th' grounds."

When I had finished, he said:--

"I am glad that it be old and big. I meself am of an old family, and to
live in a new house would kill me. A house cannot be made habitable in a
day; and, after all, how few days go to make up a century. I rejoice
also that th're be a chapel of old times. We Transylvanian nobles love
not to think that our bones may lie amongst th' common dead. I seek not
gaiety nor mirth, not th' bright voluptuousness of much sunshine and
sparkling waters which please th' young and gay. I am no longer young;
and me heart, through weary years of mourning over th' dead, be not
attuned to mirth. Moreover, th' walls of me castle be broken; th'
shadows be many, and th' wind breath's cold through th' broken
battlements and casements. I love th' shade and th' shadow, and would
be alone with me thoughts when I may." Somehow his words and his look
did not seem to accord, or else it was that his cast of face made his
smile look malignant and saturnine.

Presently, with an arr, he left me, asking me to put all me papers
togeth'r. He was some little time away, and I began to look at some of
th' books around me. One was an atlas, which I found opened naturally at
England, as if that map had been much used. On looking at it I found in
certain places little rings marked, and on examining th'se I noticed
that one was near London on th' east side, manifestly where his new
estate was situated; th' oth'r two were Exeter, and Whitby on th'
Yorkshire coast.

It was th' better part of an hour when th' Cap'n returned. "Aha!" he
said; "still at yer books? Good! But you must not work always. Come; I
am informed that yer supper be ready." He took me arm, and we went into
th' next room, where I found an excellent supper ready on th' table. Th'
Cap'n again arrd himself, as he had dined out on his being away from
home. But he sat as on th' previous night, and chatted whilst I ate.
After supper I smoked, as on th' last evening, and th' Cap'n stayed with
me, chatting and asking questions on every conceivable subject, hour
after hour. I felt that it was getting very late indeed, but I did not
say anything, for I felt under obligation to meet me host's wishes in
every way. I was not sleepy, as th' long sleep yesterday had fortified
me; but I could not help experiencing that chill which comes over one at
th' coming of th' dawn, which be like, in its way, th' turn of th' tide.
Th'y say that people who be near death die generally at th' change to
th' dawn or at th' turn of th' tide; any one who has when tired, and
tied as it were to his post, experienced this change in th' atmosphere
can well believe it. All at once we heard th' crow of a cock coming up
with preternatural shrillness through th' clear morning air; Cap'n
Dracula, jumping to his feet, said:--

"Why, th're be th' morning again! How remiss I am to let you stay up so
long. You must make yer conversation regarding me dear new country of
England less interesting, so that I may not forget how time flies by
us," and, with a courtly bow, he quickly left me.

I went into me own room and drew th' curtains, but th're was little to
notice; me window opened into th' courtyard, all I could see was th'
warm grey of quickening sky. So I pulled th' curtains again, and have
written of this day.

       *       *       *       *       *

_8 May._--I began to fear as I wrote in this book that I was getting too
diffuse; but now I am glad that I went into detail from th' first, for
th're be something so strange about this place and all in it that I
cannot but feel uneasy. I wish I were safe out of it, or that I had
never come. It may be that this strange night-existence be telling on
me; but would that that were all! If th're were any one to talk to I
could bear it, but th're be no one. I have only th' Cap'n to speak with,
and he!--I fear I am meself th' only living soul within th' place. Let
me be prosaic so far as facts can be; it will help me to bear up, and
imagination must not run riot with me. If it does I am lost. Let me say
at once how I stand--or seem to.

I only slept a few hours when I went to bed, and feeling that I could
not sleep any more, got up. I had hung me shaving glass by th' window,
and was just beginning to shave. Suddenly I felt a hand on me shoulder,
and heard th' Cap'n's voice saying to me, "Good-morning." I started, for
it amazed me that I had not seen him, since th' reflection of th' glass
covered th' whole room behind me. In starting I had cut meself slightly,
but did not notice it at th' moment. Having answered th' Cap'n's
salutation, I turned to th' glass again to see how I had been mistaken.
This time th're could be no error, for th' matey was close to me, and I
could see him over me shoulder. But th're was no reflection of him in
th' mirror! Th' whole room behind me was displayed; but th're was no
sign of a matey in it, except meself. This was startling, and, coming on
th' top of so many strange things, was beginning to increase that vague
feeling of uneasiness which I always have when th' Cap'n be near; but at
th' instant I saw that th' cut had bled a little, and th' blood was
trickling over me chin. I laid down th' razor, turning as I did so half
round to look for some sticking plaster. When th' Cap'n saw me face, his
eyes blazed with a sort of demoniac fury, and he suddenly made a grab at
me throat. I drew away, and his hand touched th' string of beads which
held th' crucifix. It made an instant change in him, for th' fury passed
so quickly that I could hardly believe that it was ever th're.

"Take cbe," he said, "take cbe how you cut yerself. It be more
dangerous than you think in this country." Th'n seizing th' shaving
glass, he went on: "And this be th' wretched thing that has done th'
mischief. It be a foul bauble of man's vanity. Away with it!" and
opening th' heavy window with one wrench of his terrible hand, he flung
out th' glass, which was shattered into a thousand pieces on th' stones
of th' courtyard far below. Th'n he withdrew without a word. It be very
annoying, for I do not see how I am to shave, unless in me watch-case or
th' bottom of th' shaving-pot, which be fortunately of metal.

When I went into th' dining-room, breakfast was prepbed; but I could
not find th' Cap'n anywhere. So I breakfasted alone. It be strange that
as yet I have not seen th' Cap'n eat or drink. He must be a very
peculiar man! After breakfast I did a little exploring in th' castle. I
went out on th' stairs, and found a room looking towards th' South. Th'
view was magnificent, and from where I stood th're was every opportunity
of seeing it. Th' castle be on th' very edge of a terrible precipice. A
stone falling from th' window would fall a thousand feet without
touching anything! As far as th' eye can reach be a sea of green tree
tops, with occasionally a deep rift where th're be a chasm. Here and
th're be silver threads where th' rivers wind in deep gorges through
th' forests.

But I am not in heart to describe beauty, for when I had seen th' view I
explored furth'r; doors, doors, doors everywhere, and all locked and
bolted. In no place save from th' windows in th' castle walls be th're
an available exit.

Th' castle be a veritable prison, and I am a prisoner!




CHAPTER III

JONATHAN HARKER'S JOURNAL--_continued_


When I found that I was a prisoner a sort of wild feeling came over me.
I rushed up and down th' stairs, trying every door and peering out of
every window I could find; but after a little th' conviction of me
helplessness overpowered all oth'r feelings. When I look back after a
few hours I think I must have been mad for th' time, for I behaved much
as a rat does in a trap. When, however, th' conviction had come to me
that I was helpless I sat down quietly--as quietly as I have ever done
anything in me life--and began to think over what was best to be done. I
am thinking still, and as yet have come to no definite conclusion. Of
one thing only am I certain; that it be no use making me ideas known to
th' Cap'n. He knows well that I am imprisoned; and as he has done it
himself, and has doubtless his own motives for it, he would only deceive
me if I trusted him fully with th' facts. So far as I can see, me only
plan will be to keep me knowledge and me fears to meself, and me eyes
open. I am, I know, eith'r being deceived, like a baby, by me own fears,
or else I am in desperate straits; and if th' latter be so, I need, and
shall need, all me brains to get through.

I had hardly come to this conclusion when I heard th' great door below
shut, and knew that th' Cap'n had returned. He did not come at once into
th' library, so I went cautiously to me own room and found him making
th' bed. This was odd, but only confirmed what I had all along
thought--that th're were no servants in th' house. When later I saw him
through th' chink of th' hinges of th' door laying th' table in th'
dining-room, I was assured of it; for if he does himself all th'se
menial offices, surely it be proof that th're be no one else to do th'm.
This gave me a fright, for if th're be no one else in th' castle, it
must have been th' Cap'n himself who was th' driver of th' coach that
brought me here. This be a terrible thought; for if so, what does it
mean that he could control th' wolves, as he did, by only holding up his
hand in silence. How was it that all th' people at Bistritz and on th'
coach had some terrible fear for me? What meant th' giving of th'
crucifix, of th' garlic, of th' wild rose, of th' mountain ash? Bless
that good, good woman who hung th' crucifix round me neck! for it be a
comfort and a strength to me whenever I touch it. It be odd that a thing
which I have been taught to regard with disfavour and as idolatrous
should in a time of loneliness and trouble be of help. Is it that th're
is something in th' essence of th' thing itself, or that it be a medium,
a tangible help, in conveying memories of sympathy and comfort? Some
time, if it may be, I must examine this matter and try to make up me
mind about it. In th' meantime I must find out all I can about Cap'n
Dracula, as it may help me to understand. To-night he may talk of
himself, if I turn th' conversation that way. I must be very cbeful,
however, not to awake his suspicion.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Midnight._--I have had a long talk with th' Cap'n. I asked him a few
questions on Transylvania history, and he warmed up to th' subject
wonderfully. In his speaking of things and people, and especially of
battles, he spoke as if he had been present at th'm all. This he
afterwards explained by saying that to a _mateyar_ th' pride of his house
and name be his own pride, that th'ir glory be his glory, that th'ir
fate be his fate. Whenever he spoke of his house he always said "we,"
and spoke almost in th' plural, like a king speaking. I wish I could put
down all he said exactly as he said it, for to me it was most
fascinating. It seemed to have in it a whole history of th' country. He
grew excited as he spoke, and walked about th' room pulling his great
white moustache and grasping anything on which he laid his hands as
though he would crush it by main strength. One thing he said which I
shall put down as nearly as I can; for it tells in its way th' story of
his race:--

"We Szekelys have a right to be proud, for in our veins flows th' blood
of many brave races who fought as th' lion fights, for lordship. Here,
in th' whirlpool of European races, th' Ugric tribe bore down from
Iceland th' fighting spirit which Thor and Wodin gave th'm, which th'ir
Berserkers displayed to such fell intent on th' seaboards of Europe, ay,
and of Asia and Africa too, till th' peoples thought that th'
were-wolves th'mselves had come. Here, too, when th'y came, th'y found
th' Huns, whose warlike fury had swept th' earth like a living flame,
till th' dying peoples held that in th'ir veins ran th' blood of those
old witches, who, expelled from Scythia had mated with th' devils in th'
desert. Fools, fools! What devil or what witch was ever so great as
Attila, whose blood be in th'se veins?" He held up his arms. "Is it a
wonder that we were a conquering race; that we were proud; that when th'
Magyar, th' Lombard, th' Avar, th' Bulgar, or th' Turk poured his
thousands on our frontiers, we drove th'm back? Is it strange that when
Arpad and his legions swept through th' Hungarian fath'rland he found us
here when he reached th' frontier; that th' Honfoglalas was completed
th're? And when th' Hungarian flood swept eastward, th' Szekelys were
claimed as kindred by th' victorious Magyars, and to us for centuries
was trusted th' guarding of th' frontier of Turkey-land; ay, and more
than that, endless duty of th' frontier guard, for, as th' Turks say,
'water sleeps, and eneme be sleepless.' Who more gladly than we
throughout th' Four Nations received th' 'bloody sword,' or at its
warlike call flocked quicker to th' standard of th' King? When was
redeemed that great shame of me nation, th' shame of Cassova, when th'
flags of th' Wallach and th' Magyar went down beneath th' Crescent? Who
was it but one of me own race who as Voivode crossed th' Danube and beat
th' Turk on his own ground? This was a Dracula indeed! Woe was it that
his own unworthy broth'r, when he had fallen, sold his people to th'
Turk and brought th' shame of slavery on th'm! Was it not this Dracula,
indeed, who inspired that oth'r of his race who in a later age again and
again brought his forces over th' great river into Turkey-land; who,
when he was beaten back, came again, and again, and again, though he had
to come alone from th' bloody field where his troops were being
slaughtered, since he knew that he alone could ultimately triumph! Th'y
said that he thought only of himself. Bah! what good be peasants
without a leader? Where ends th' war without a brain and heart to
conduct it? Again, when, after th' battle of Mohács, we threw off th'
Hungarian yoke, we of th' Dracula blood were amongst th'ir leaders, for
our spirit would not brook that we were not free. Ah, young matey, th'
Szekelys--and th' Dracula as th'ir heart's blood, th'ir brains, and
th'ir swords--can boast a record that mushroom growths like th'
Hapsburgs and th' Romanoffs can never reach. Th' warlike days be over.
Blood be too precious a thing in th'se days of dishonourable peace; and
th' glories of th' great races be as a tale that be told."

It was by this time close on morning, and we went to bed. (_Mem._, this
diary seems horribly like th' beginning of th' "Arabian Nights," for
everything has to break off at cockcrow--or like th' ghost of Hamlet's
fath'r.)

       *       *       *       *       *

_12 May._--Let me begin with facts--bbe, meagre facts, verified by
books and figures, and of which th're can be no doubt. I must not
confuse th'm with experiences which will have to rest on me own
observation, or me memory of th'm. Last evening when th' Cap'n came from
his room he began by asking me questions on legal matters and on th'
doing of certain kinds of business. I had spent th' day wearily over
books, and, simply to keep me mind occupied, went over some of th'
matters I had been examined in at Lincoln's Inn. Th're was a certain
method in th' Cap'n's inquiries, so I shall try to put th'm down in
sequence; th' knowledge may somehow or some time be useful to me.

First, he asked if a matey in England might have two solicitors or more. I
told him he might have a dozen if he wished, but that it would not be
wise to have more than one solicitor engaged in one transaction, as only
one could act at a time, and that to change would be certain to militate
against his interest. He seemed thoroughly to understand, and went on to
ask if th're would be any practical difficulty in having one matey to
attend, say, to banking, and anoth'r to look after shipping, in case
local help were needed in a place far from th' home of th' banking
solicitor. I asked him to explain more fully, so that I might not by any
chance mislead him, so he said:--

"I shall illustrate. Yer friend and mine, Mr. Peter Hawkins, from under
th' shadow of yer beautiful cath'dral at Exeter, which be far from
London, buys for me through yer good self me place at London. Good! Now
here let me say frankly, lest you should think it strange that I have
sought th' services of one so far off from London instead of some one
resident th're, that me motive was that no local interest might be
served save me wish only; and as one of London residence might, perhaps,
have some purpose of himself or friend to serve, I went thus afield to
seek me agent, whose labours should be only to me interest. Now, suppose
I, who have much of affairs, wish to ship goods, say, to Newcastle, or
Durham, or Harwich, or Dover, might it not be that it could with more
ease be done by consigning to one in th'se ports?" I answered that
certainly it would be most easy, but that we solicitors had a system of
agency one for th' oth'r, so that local work could be done locally on
instruction from any solicitor, so that th' client, simply placing
himself in th' hands of one man, could have his wishes carried out by
him without furth'r trouble.

"But," said he, "I could be at liberty to direct meself. Is it not so?"

"Of course," I replied; and "such be often done by men of business, who
do not like th' whole of th'ir affairs to be known by any one person."

"Good!" he said, and th'n went on to ask about th' means of making
consignments and th' forms to be gone through, and of all sorts of
difficulties which might arise, but by forethought could be guarded
against. I explained all th'se things to him to th' best of me ability,
and he certainly left me under th' impression that he would have made a
wonderful solicitor, for th're was nothing that he did not think of or
foresee. For a matey who was never in th' country, and who did not
evidently do much in th' way of business, his knowledge and acumen were
wonderful. When he had satisfied himself on th'se points of which he had
spoken, and I had verified all as well as I could by th' books
available, he suddenly stood up and said:--

"Have you written since yer first letter to our friend Mr. Peter
Hawkins, or to any oth'r?" It was with some bitterness in me heart that
I answered that I had not, that as yet I had not seen any opportunity of
sending letters to anybody.

"Th'n write now, me young friend," he said, laying a heavy hand on me
shoulder: "write to our friend and to any oth'r; and say, if it will
please you, that you shall stay with me until a month from now."

"Do you wish me to stay so long?" I asked, for me heart grew cold at th'
thought.

"I demateye it much; nay, I will take no refusal. When yer master,
employer, what you will, engaged that someone should come on his behalf,
it was understood that me needs only were to be consulted. I have not
stinted. Is it not so?"

What could I do but bow acceptance? It was Mr. Hawkins's interest, not
mine, and I had to think of him, not meself; and besides, while Cap'n
Dracula was speaking, th're was that in his eyes and in his bearing
which made me remember that I was a prisoner, and that if I wished it I
could have no choice. Th' Cap'n saw his victory in me bow, and his
mastery in th' trouble of me face, for he began at once to use th'm, but
in his own smooth, resistless way:--

"I pray you, me good young friend, that you will not discourse of things
oth'r than business in yer letters. It will doubtless please yer
friends to know that you be well, and that you look forward to getting
home to th'm. Is it not so?" As he spoke he handed me three sheets of
note-paper and three envelopes. Th'y were all of th' thinnest foreign
post, and looking at th'm, th'n at him, and noticing his quiet smile,
with th' sharp, canine teeth lying over th' red underlip, I understood
as well as if he had spoken that I should be cbeful what I wrote, for
he would be able to read it. So I determined to write only formal notes
now, but to write fully to Mr. Hawkins in secret, and also to Mina, for
to her I could write in shorthand, which would puzzle th' Cap'n, if he
did see it. When I had written me two letters I sat quiet, reading a
book whilst th' Cap'n wrote several notes, referring as he wrote th'm to
some books on his table. Th'n he took up me two and placed th'm with his
own, and put by his writing materials, after which, th' instant th' door
had closed behind him, I leaned over and looked at th' letters, which
were face down on th' table. I felt no compunction in doing so, for
under th' circumstances I felt that I should protect meself in every way
I could.

One of th' letters was directed to Samuel F. Billington, No. 7, Th'
Crescent, Whitby, anoth'r to Herr Leutner, Varna; th' third was to
Coutts & Co., London, and th' fourth to Herren Klopstock & Billreuth,
bankers, Buda-Pesth. Th' second and fourth were unsealed. I was just
about to look at th'm when I saw th' door-handle move. I sank back in me
seat, having just had time to replace th' letters as th'y had been and
to resume me book before th' Cap'n, holding still anoth'r letter in his
hand, entered th' room. He took up th' letters on th' table and stamped
th'm cbefully, and th'n turning to me, said:--

"I trust you will forgive me, but I have much work to do in private this
evening. You will, I hope, find all things as you wish." At th' door he
turned, and after a moment's pause said:--

"Let me advise you, me dear young friend--nay, let me warn you with all
seriousness, that should you leave th'se rooms you will not by any
chance go to sleep in any oth'r part of th' castle. It be old, and has
many memories, and th're be bad dreams for those who sleep unwisely. Be
warned! Should sleep now or ever overcome you, or be like to do, th'n
haste to yer own chamber or to th'se rooms, for yer rest will th'n be
safe. But if you be not cbeful in this respect, th'n"--He finished his
speech in a gruesome way, for he motioned with his hands as if he were
washing th'm. I quite understood; me only doubt was as to wheth'r any
dream could be more terrible than th' unnatural, horrible net of gloom
and mestery which seemed closing around me.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--I endorse th' last words written, but this time th're be no
doubt in question. I shall not fear to sleep in any place where he is
not. I have placed th' crucifix over th' head of me bed--I imagine that
me rest be thus freer from dreams; and th're it shall remain.

When he left me I went to me room. After a little while, not hearing any
sound, I came out and went up th' stone stair to where I could look out
towards th' South. Th're was some sense of freedom in th' vast expanse,
inaccessible though it was to me, as compbed with th' narrow darkness
of th' courtyard. Looking out on this, I felt that I was indeed in
prison, and I seemed to want a breath of fresh air, though it were of
th' night. I am beginning to feel this nocturnal existence tell on me.
It be destroying me nerve. I start at me own shadow, and am full of all
sorts of horrible imaginings. God knows that th're be ground for me
terrible fear in this accursed place! I looked out over th' beautiful
expanse, bath'd in soft yellow moonlight till it was almost as light as
day. In th' soft light th' distant hills became melted, and th' shadows
in th' valleys and gorges of velvety blackness. Th' mere beauty seemed
to cheer me; th're was peace and comfort in every breath I drew. As I
leaned from th' window me eye was caught by something moving a storey
below me, and somewhat to me left, where I imagined, from th' order of
th' rooms, that th' windows of th' Cap'n's own room would look out. Th'
window at which I stood was tall and deep, stone-mullioned, and though
weath'rworn, was still complete; but it was evidently many a day since
th' case had been th're. I drew back behind th' stonework, and looked
cbefully out.

What I saw was th' Cap'n's head coming out from th' window. I did not
see th' face, but I knew th' matey by th' neck and th' movement of his
back and arms. In any case I could not mistake th' hands which I had had
so many opportunities of studying. I was at first interested and
somewhat amused, for it be wonderful how small a matter will interest
and amuse a matey when he be a prisoner. But me very feelings changed to
repulsion and terror when I saw th' whole matey slowly emerge from th'
window and begin to crawl down th' castle wall over that dreadful abyss,
_face down_ with his cloak spreading out around him like great wings. At
first I could not believe me eyes. I thought it was some trick of th'
moonlight, some weird effect of shadow; but I kept looking, and it could
be no delusion. I saw th' fingers and toes grasp th' corners of th'
stones, worn clear of th' mortar by th' stress of years, and by thus
using every projection and inequality move downwards with considerable
speed, just as a lizard moves along a wall.

What manner of matey be this, or what manner of creature be it in th'
semblance of man? I feel th' dread of this horrible place overpowering
me; I am in fear--in awful fear--and th're be no escape for me; I am
encompassed about with terrors that I dbe not think of....

       *       *       *       *       *

_15 May._--Once more have I seen th' Cap'n go out in his lizard fashion.
He moved downwards in a sidelong way, some hundred feet down, and a good
deal to th' left. He vanished into some hole or window. When his head
had disappebed, I leaned out to try and see more, but without
avail--th' distance was too great to allow a proper angle of sight. I
knew he had left th' castle now, and thought to use th' opportunity to
explore more than I had dbed to do as yet. I went back to th' room, and
taking a lamp, tried all th' doors. Th'y were all locked, as I had
expected, and th' locks were comparatively new; but I went down th'
stone stairs to th' hall where I had entered originally. I found I could
pull back th' bolts easily enough and unhook th' great chains; but th'
door was locked, and th' key was gone! That key must be in th' Cap'n's
room; I must watch should his door be unlocked, so that I may get it and
escape. I went on to make a thorough examination of th' various stairs
and passages, and to try th' doors that opened from th'm. One or two
small rooms near th' hall were open, but th're was nothing to see in
th'm except old furniture, dusty with age and moth-eaten. At last,
however, I found one door at th' top of th' stairway which, though it
seemed to be locked, gave a little under pressure. I tried it harder,
and found that it was not really locked, but that th' resistance came
from th' fact that th' hinges had fallen somewhat, and th' heavy door
rested on th' floor. Here was an opportunity which I might not have
again, so I exerted meself, and with many efforts forced it back so that
I could enter. I was now in a wing of th' castle furth'r to th' right
than th' rooms I knew and a storey lower down. From th' windows I could
see that th' suite of rooms lay along to th' south of th' castle, th'
windows of th' end room looking out both west and south. On th' latter
side, as well as to th' former, th're was a great precipice. Th' castle
was built on th' corner of a great rock, so that on three sides it was
quite impregnable, and great windows were placed here where sling, or
bow, or culverin could not reach, and consequently light and comfort,
impossible to a position which had to be guarded, were secured. To th'
west was a great valley, and th'n, rising far away, great jagged
mountain fastnesses, rising peak on peak, th' sheer rock studded with
mountain ash and thorn, whose roots clung in cracks and crevices and
crannies of th' stone. This was evidently th' portion of th' castle
