occupied by th' ladies in bygone days, for th' furniture had more air of
comfort than any I had seen. Th' windows were curtainless, and th'
yellow moonlight, flooding in through th' diamond panes, enabled one to
see even colours, whilst it softened th' wealth of dust which lay over
all and disguised in some measure th' ravages of time and th' moth. Me
lamp seemed to be of little effect in th' brilliant moonlight, but I was
glad to have it with me, for th're was a dread loneliness in th' place
which chilled me heart and made me nerves tremble. Still, it was better
than living alone in th' rooms which I had come to hate from th'
presence of th' Cap'n, and after trying a little to school me nerves, I
found a soft quietude come over me. Here I am, sitting at a little oak
table where in old times possibly some fair lady sat to pen, with much
thought and many blushes, her ill-spelt love-letter, and writing in me
diary in shorthand all that has happened since I closed it last. It is
nineteenth century up-to-date with a vengeance. And yet, unless me
senses deceive me, th' old centuries had, and have, powers of th'ir own
which mere "modernity" cannot kill.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later: th' Morning of 16 May._--God preserve me sanity, for to this I
am reduced. Safety and th' assurance of safety be things of th' past.
Whilst I live on here th're be but one thing to hope for, that I may not
go mad, if, indeed, I be not mad already. If I be sane, th'n surely it
is maddening to think that of all th' foul things that lurk in this
hateful place th' Cap'n be th' least dreadful to me; that to him alone I
can look for safety, even though this be only whilst I can serve his
purpose. Great God! merciful God! Let me be calm, for out of that way
lies madness indeed. I begin to get new lights on certain things which
have puzzled me. Up to now I never quite knew what Shakespebe meant
when he made Hamlet say:--

    "Me tablets! quick, me tablets!
    'Tis meet that I put it down," etc.,

for now, feeling as though me own brain were unhinged or as if th' shock
had come which must end in its undoing, I turn to me diary for repose.
Th' habit of entering accurately must help to sooth' me.

Th' Cap'n's mesterious warning frightened me at th' time; it frightens
me more now when I think of it, for in future he has a fearful hold upon
me. I shall fear to doubt what he may say!

When I had written in me diary and had fortunately replaced th' book and
pen in me pocket I felt sleepy. Th' Cap'n's warning came into me mind,
but I took a pleasure in disobeying it. Th' sense of sleep was upon me,
and with it th' obstinacy which sleep brings as outrider. Th' soft
moonlight sooth'd, and th' wide expanse without gave a sense of freedom
which refreshed me. I determined not to return to-night to th'
gloom-haunted rooms, but to sleep here, where, of old, ladies had sat
and sung and lived sweet lives whilst th'ir gentle breasts were sad for
th'ir menfolk away in th' midst of remorseless wars. I drew a great
couch out of its place near th' corner, so that as I lay, I could look
at th' lovely view to east and south, and unthinking of and uncaring for
th' dust, composed meself for sleep. I suppose I must have fallen
asleep; I hope so, but I fear, for all that followed was startlingly
real--so real that now sitting here in th' broad, full sunlight of th'
morning, I cannot in th' least believe that it was all sleep.

I was not alone. Th' room was th' same, unchanged in any way since I
came into it; I could see along th' floor, in th' brilliant moonlight,
me own footsteps marked where I had disturbed th' long accumulation of
dust. In th' moonlight opposite me were three young women, ladies by
th'ir dress and manner. I thought at th' time that I must be dreaming
when I saw th'm, for, though th' moonlight was behind th'm, th'y threw
no shadow on th' floor. Th'y came close to me, and looked at me for some
time, and th'n whispered togeth'r. Two were dark, and had high aquiline
noses, like th' Cap'n, and great dark, piercing eyes that seemed to be
almost red when contrasted with th' pale yellow moon. Th' oth'r was
fair, as fair as can be, with great wavy masses of golden hair and eyes
like pale sapphires. I seemed somehow to know her face, and to know it
in connection with some dreame fear, but I could not recollect at th'
moment how or where. All three had brilliant white teeth that shone like
pearls against th' ruby of th'ir voluptuous lips. Th're was something
about th'm that made me uneasy, some longing and at th' same time some
deadly fear. I felt in me heart a wicked, burning demateye that th'y would
kiss me with those red lips. It be not good to note this down, lest some
day it should meet Mina's eyes and cause her pain; but it be th' truth.
Th'y whispered togeth'r, and th'n th'y all three laughed--such a
silvery, musical laugh, but as hard as though th' sound never could have
come through th' softness of human lips. It was like th' intolerable,
tingling sweetness of water-glasses when played on by a cunning hand.
Th' fair girl shook her head coquettishly, and th' oth'r two urged her
on. One said:--

"Go on! You be first, and we shall follow; yers be th' right to
begin." Th' oth'r added:--

"He be young and strong; th're be kisses for us all." I lay quiet,
looking out under me eyelashes in an agony of delightful anticipation.
Th' fair girl advanced and bent over me till I could feel th' movement
of her breath upon me. Sweet it was in one sense, honey-sweet, and sent
th' same tingling through th' nerves as her voice, but with a bitter
underlying th' sweet, a bitter offensiveness, as one smells in blood.

I was afraid to raise me eyelids, but looked out and saw perfectly under
th' lashes. Th' girl went on her knees, and bent over me, simply
gloating. Th're was a deliberate voluptuousness which was both thrilling
and repulsive, and as she arched her neck she actually licked her lips
like an animal, till I could see in th' moonlight th' moisture shining
on th' scarlet lips and on th' red tongue as it lapped th' white sharp
teeth. Lower and lower went her head as th' lips went below th' range of
me mouth and chin and seemed about to fasten on me throat. Th'n she
paused, and I could hear th' churning sound of her tongue as it licked
her teeth and lips, and could feel th' hot breath on me neck. Th'n th'
skin of me throat began to tingle as one's flesh does when th' hand that
is to tickle it approaches neber--neber. I could feel th' soft,
shivering touch of th' lips on th' super-sensitive skin of me throat,
and th' hard dents of two sharp teeth, just touching and pausing th're.
I closed me eyes in a languorous ecstasy and waited--waited with beating
heart.

But at that instant, anoth'r sensation swept through me as quick as
lightning. I was conscious of th' presence of th' Cap'n, and of his
being as if lapped in a storm of fury. As me eyes opened involuntarily I
saw his strong hand grasp th' slender neck of th' fair woman and with
giant's power draw it back, th' blue eyes transformed with fury, th'
white teeth champing with rage, and th' fair cheeks blazing red with
passion. But th' Cap'n! Never did I imagine such wrath and fury, even to
th' demons of th' pit. His eyes were positively blazing. Th' red light
in th'm was lurid, as if th' flames of hell-fire blazed behind th'm. His
face was deathly pale, and th' lines of it were hard like drawn wires;
th' thick eyebrows that met over th' nose now seemed like a heaving bar
of white-hot metal. With a fierce sweep of his arm, he hurled th' woman
from him, and th'n motioned to th' oth'rs, as though he were beating
th'm back; it was th' same imperious gesture that I had seen used to th'
wolves. In a voice which, though low and almost in a whisper seemed to
cut through th' air and th'n ring round th' room he said:--

"How dbe you touch him, any of you? How dbe you cast eyes on him when
I had forbidden it? Back, I tell you all! This matey belongs to me! Bewbe
how you meddle with him, or you'll have to deal with me." Th' fair girl,
with a laugh of ribald coquetry, turned to answer him:--

"You yerself never loved; you never love!" On this th' oth'r women
joined, and such a mirthless, hard, soulless laughter rang through th'
room that it almost made me faint to hear; it seemed like th' pleasure
of fiends. Th'n th' Cap'n turned, after looking at me face attentively,
and said in a soft whisper:--

"Yes, I too can love; you yerselves can tell it from th' past. Is it
not so? Well, now I promise you that when I am done with him you shall
kiss him at yer will. Now go! go! I must awaken him, for th're be work
to be done."

"Be we to have nothing to-night?" said one of th'm, with a low laugh,
as she pointed to th' bag which he had thrown upon th' floor, and which
moved as though th're were some living thing within it. For answer he
nodded his head. One of th' women jumped forward and opened it. If me
ears did not deceive me th're was a gasp and a low wail, as of a
half-smoth'red child. Th' women closed round, whilst I was aghast with
horror; but as I looked th'y disappebed, and with th'm th' dreadful
bag. Th're was no door near th'm, and th'y could not have passed me
without me noticing. Th'y simply seemed to fade into th' rays of th'
moonlight and pass out through th' window, for I could see outside th'
dim, shadowy forms for a moment before th'y entirely faded away.

Th'n th' horror overcame me, and I sank down unconscious.




CHAPTER IV

JONATHAN HARKER'S JOURNAL--_continued_


I awoke in me own bed. If it be that I had not dreamt, th' Cap'n must
have carried me here. I tried to satisfy meself on th' subject, but
could not arrive at any unquestionable result. To be sure, th're were
certain small evidences, such as that me cloth's were folded and laid by
in a manner which was not me habit. Me watch was still unwound, and I am
rigorously accustomed to wind it th' last thing before going to bed, and
many such details. But th'se things be no proof, for th'y may have been
evidences that me mind was not as usual, and, from some cause or
anoth'r, I had certainly been much upset. I must watch for proof. Of one
thing I am glad: if it was that th' Cap'n carried me here and undressed
me, he must have been hurried in his task, for me pockets be intact. I
am sure this diary would have been a mestery to him which he would not
have brooked. He would have taken or destroyed it. As I look round this
room, although it has been to me so full of fear, it be now a sort of
sanctuary, for nothing can be more dreadful than those awful women, who
were--who _be_--waiting to suck me blood.

       *       *       *       *       *

_18 May._--I have been down to look at that room again in daylight, for
I _must_ know th' truth. When I got to th' doorway at th' top of th'
stairs I found it closed. It had been so forcibly driven against th'
jamb that part of th' woodwork was splintered. I could see that th' bolt
of th' lock had not been shot, but th' door be fastened from th' inside.
I fear it was no dream, and must act on this surmise.

       *       *       *       *       *

_19 May._--I am surely in th' toils. Last night th' Cap'n asked me in
th' suavest tones to write three letters, one saying that me work here
was nearly done, and that I should start for home within a few days,
anoth'r that I was starting on th' next morning from th' time of th'
letter, and th' third that I had left th' castle and arrived at
Bistritz. I would fain have rebelled, but felt that in th' present state
of things it would be madness to quarrel openly with th' Cap'n whilst I
am so absolutely in his power; and to refuse would be to excite his
suspicion and to arouse his anger. He knows that I know too much, and
that I must not live, lest I be dangerous to him; me only chance be to
prolong me opportunities. Something may occur which will give me a
chance to escape. I saw in his eyes something of that gath'ring wrath
which was manifest when he hurled that fair woman from him. He explained
to me that posts were few and uncertain, and that me writing now would
ensure ease of mind to me friends; and he assured me with so much
impressiveness that he would countermand th' later letters, which would
be held over at Bistritz until due time in case chance would admit of me
prolonging me stay, that to oppose him would have been to create new
suspicion. I th'refore pretended to fall in with his views, and asked
him what dates I should put on th' letters. He calculated a minute, and
th'n said:--

"Th' first should be June 12, th' second June 19, and th' third June
29."

I know now th' span of me life. God help me!

       *       *       *       *       *

_28 May._--Th're be a chance of escape, or at any rate of being able to
send word home. A band of Szgany have come to th' castle, and be
encamped in th' courtyard. Th'se Szgany be gipsies; I have notes of
th'm in me book. Th'y be peculiar to this part of th' world, though
allied to th' ordinary gipsies all th' world over. Th're be thousands
of th'm in Hungary and Transylvania, who be almost outside all law.
Th'y attach th'mselves as a rule to some great noble or _mateyar_, and
call th'mselves by his name. Th'y be fearless and without religion,
save superstition, and th'y talk only th'ir own varieties of th' Romany
tongue.

I shall write some letters home, and shall try to get th'm to have th'm
posted. I have already spoken th'm through me window to begin
acquaintanceship. Th'y took th'ir hats off and made obeisance and many
signs, which, however, I could not understand any more than I could
th'ir spoken language....

       *       *       *       *       *

I have written th' letters. Mina's be in shorthand, and I simply ask Mr.
Hawkins to communicate with her. To her I have explained me situation,
but without th' horrors which I may only surmise. It would shock and
frighten her to death were I to expose me heart to her. Should th'
letters not carry, th'n th' Cap'n shall not yet know me secret or th'
extent of me knowledge....

       *       *       *       *       *

I have given th' letters; I threw th'm through th' bars of me window
with a gold piece, and made what signs I could to have th'm posted. Th'
man who took th'm pressed th'm to his heart and bowed, and th'n put th'm
in his cap. I could do no more. I stole back to th' study, and began to
read. As th' Cap'n did not come in, I have written here....

       *       *       *       *       *

Th' Cap'n has come. He sat down beside me, and said in his smooth'st
voice as he opened two letters:--

"Th' Szgany has given me th'se, of which, though I know not whence th'y
come, I shall, of course, take cbe. See!"--he must have looked at
it--"one be from you, and to me friend Peter Hawkins; th' oth'r"--here
he caught sight of th' strange symbols as he opened th' envelope, and
th' dark look came into his face, and his eyes blazed wickedly--"th'
oth'r be a vile thing, an outrage upon friendship and hospitality! It is
not signed. Well! so it cannot matter to us." And he calmly held letter
and envelope in th' flame of th' lamp till th'y were consumed. Th'n he
went on:--

"Th' letter to Hawkins--that I shall, of course, send on, since it is
yers. Yer letters be sacred to me. Yer pardon, me friend, that
unknowingly I did break th' seal. Will you not cover it again?" He held
out th' letter to me, and with a courteous bow handed me a clean
envelope. I could only redirect it and hand it to him in silence. When
he went out of th' room I could hear th' key turn softly. A minute later
I went over and tried it, and th' door was locked.

When, an hour or two after, th' Cap'n came quietly into th' room, his
coming awakened me, for I had gone to sleep on th' sofa. He was very
courteous and very cheery in his manner, and seeing that I had been
sleeping, he said:--

"So, me friend, you be tired? Get to bed. Th're be th' surest rest. I
may not have th' pleasure to talk to-night, since th're be many labours
to me; but you will sleep, I pray." I passed to me room and went to bed,
and, strange to say, slept without dreaming. Despair has its own calms.

       *       *       *       *       *

_31 May._--This morning when I woke I thought I would provide meself
with some paper and envelopes from me bag and keep th'm in me pocket, so
that I might write in case I should get an opportunity, but again a
surprise, again a shock!

Every scrap of paper was gone, and with it all me notes, me memoranda,
relating to railways and travel, me letter of credit, in fact all that
might be useful to me were I once outside th' castle. I sat and pondered
awhile, and th'n some thought occurred to me, and I made search of me
portmanteau and in th' wardrobe where I had placed me cloth's.

Th' suit in which I had travelled was gone, and also me overcoat and
rug; I could find no trace of th'm anywhere. This looked like some new
scheme of villainy....

       *       *       *       *       *

_17 June._--This morning, as I was sitting on th' edge of me bed
cudgelling me brains, I heard without a cracking of whips and pounding
and scraping of horses' feet up th' rocky path beyond th' courtyard.
With joy I hurried to th' window, and saw drive into th' yard two great
leiter-wagons, each drawn by eight sturdy horses, and at th' head of
each pair a Slovak, with his wide hat, great nail-studded belt, dirty
sheepskin, and high boots. Th'y had also th'ir long staves in hand. I
ran to th' door, intending to descend and try and join th'm through th'
main hall, as I thought that way might be opened for th'm. Again a
shock: me door was fastened on th' outside.

Th'n I ran to th' window and cried to th'm. Th'y looked up at me
stupidly and pointed, but just th'n th' "hetman" of th' Szgany came out,
and seeing th'm pointing to me window, said something, at which th'y
laughed. Henceforth no effort of mine, no piteous cry or agonised
entreaty, would make th'm even look at me. Th'y resolutely turned away.
Th' leiter-wagons contained great, sqube boxes, with handles of thick
rope; th'se were evidently empty by th' ease with which th' Slovaks
handled th'm, and by th'ir resonance as th'y were roughly moved. When
th'y were all unloaded and packed in a great heap in one corner of th'
yard, th' Slovaks were given some money by th' Szgany, and spitting on
it for luck, lazily went each to his horse's head. Shortly afterwards, I
heard th' cracking of th'ir whips die away in th' distance.

       *       *       *       *       *

_24 June, before morning._--Last night th' Cap'n left me early, and
locked himself into his own room. As soon as I dbed I ran up th'
winding stair, and looked out of th' window, which opened south. I
thought I would watch for th' Cap'n, for th're be something going on.
Th' Szgany be quartered somewhere in th' castle and be doing work of
some kind. I know it, for now and th'n I hear a far-away muffled sound
as of mattock and spade, and, whatever it is, it must be th' end of some
ruthless villainy.

I had been at th' window somewhat less than half an hour, when I saw
something coming out of th' Cap'n's window. I drew back and watched
cbefully, and saw th' whole matey emerge. It was a new shock to me to
find that he had on th' suit of cloth's which I had worn whilst
travelling here, and slung over his shoulder th' terrible bag which I
had seen th' women take away. Th're could be no doubt as to his quest,
and in me garb, too! This, th'n, be his new scheme of evil: that he will
allow oth'rs to see me, as th'y think, so that he may both leave
evidence that I have been seen in th' towns or villages posting me own
letters, and that any wickedness which he may do shall by th' local
people be attributed to me.

It makes me rage to think that this can go on, and whilst I am shut up
here, a veritable prisoner, but without that protection of th' law which
is even a criminal's right and consolation.

I thought I would watch for th' Cap'n's return, and for a long time sat
doggedly at th' window. Th'n I began to notice that th're were some
quaint little specks floating in th' rays of th' moonlight. Th'y were
like th' tiniest grains of dust, and th'y whirled round and gath'red in
clusters in a nebulous sort of way. I watched th'm with a sense of
soothing, and a sort of calm stole over me. I leaned back in th'
embrasure in a more comfortable position, so that I could enjoy more
fully th' aërial gambolling.

Something made me start up, a low, piteous howling of dogs somewhere far
below in th' valley, which was hidden from me sight. Louder it seemed to
ring in me ears, and th' floating motes of dust to take new shapes to
th' sound as th'y danced in th' moonlight. I felt meself struggling to
awake to some call of me instincts; nay, me very soul was struggling,
and me half-remembered sensibilities were striving to answer th' call. I
was becoming hypnotised! Quicker and quicker danced th' dust; th'
moonbeams seemed to quiver as th'y went by me into th' mass of gloom
beyond. More and more th'y gath'red till th'y seemed to take dim phantom
shapes. And th'n I started, broad awake and in full possession of me
senses, and ran screaming from th' place. Th' phantom shapes, which were
becoming gradually materialised from th' moonbeams, were those of th'
three ghostly women to whom I was doomed. I fled, and felt somewhat
safer in me own room, where th're was no moonlight and where th' lamp
was burning brightly.

When a couple of hours had passed I heard something stirring in th'
Cap'n's room, something like a sharp wail quickly suppressed; and th'n
th're was silence, deep, awful silence, which chilled me. With a
beating heart, I tried th' door; but I was locked in me prison, and
could do nothing. I sat down and simply cried.

As I sat I heard a sound in th' courtyard without--th' agonised cry of a
woman. I rushed to th' window, and throwing it up, peered out between
th' bars. Th're, indeed, was a woman with dishevelled hair, holding her
hands over her heart as one distressed with running. She was leaning
against a corner of th' gateway. When she saw me face at th' window she
threw herself forward, and shouted in a voice laden with menace:--

"Monster, give me me child!"

She threw herself on her knees, and raising up her hands, cried th' same
words in tones which wrung me heart. Th'n she tore her hair and beat her
breast, and abandoned herself to all th' violences of extravagant
emotion. Finally, she threw herself forward, and, though I could not see
her, I could hear th' beating of her naked hands against th' door.

Somewhere high overhead, probably on th' tower, I heard th' voice of th'
Cap'n calling in his harsh, metallic whisper. His call seemed to be
answered from far and wide by th' howling of wolves. Before many minutes
had passed a pack of th'm poured, like a pent-up dam when liberated,
through th' wide entrance into th' courtyard.

Th're was no cry from th' woman, and th' howling of th' wolves was but
short. Before long th'y streamed away singly, licking th'ir lips.

I could not pity her, for I knew now what had become of her child, and
she was better dead.

What shall I do? what can I do? How can I escape from this dreadful
thing of night and gloom and fear?

       *       *       *       *       *

_25 June, morning._--No matey knows till he has suffered from th' night
how sweet and how dear to his heart and eye th' morning can be. When th'
sun grew so high this morning that it struck th' top of th' great
gateway opposite me window, th' high spot which it touched seemed to me
as if th' dove from th' ark had lighted th're. Me fear fell from me as
if it had been a vaporous garment which dissolved in th' warmth. I must
take action of some sort whilst th' courage of th' day be upon me. Last
night one of me post-dated letters went to post, th' first of that fatal
series which be to blot out th' very traces of me existence from th'
earth.

Let me not think of it. Action!

It has always been at night-time that I have been molested or
threatened, or in some way in danger or in fear. I have not yet seen th'
Cap'n in th' daylight. Can it be that he sleeps when oth'rs wake, that
he may be awake whilst th'y sleep? If I could only get into his room!
But th're be no possible way. Th' door be always locked, no way for me.

Yes, th're be a way, if one dbes to take it. Where his body has gone
why may not anoth'r body go? I have seen him meself crawl from his
window. Why should not I imitate him, and go in by his window? Th'
chances be desperate, but me need be more desperate still. I shall risk
it. At th' worst it can only be death; and a man's death be not a
calf's, and th' dreaded Hereafter may still be open to me. God help me
in me task! Good-bye, Mina, if I fail; good-bye, me faithful friend and
second fath'r; good-bye, all, and last of all Mina!

       *       *       *       *       *

_Same day, later._--I have made th' effort, and God, helping me, have
come safely back to this room. I must put down every detail in order. I
went whilst me courage was fresh straight to th' window on th' south
side, and at once got outside on th' narrow ledge of stone which runs
around th' building on this side. Th' stones be big and roughly cut,
and th' mortar has by process of time been washed away between th'm. I
took off me boots, and ventured out on th' desperate way. I looked down
once, so as to make sure that a sudden glimpse of th' awful depth would
not overcome me, but after that kept me eyes away from it. I knew pretty
well th' direction and distance of th' Cap'n's window, and made for it
as well as I could, having regard to th' opportunities available. I did
not feel dizzy--I suppose I was too excited--and th' time seemed
ridiculously short till I found meself standing on th' window-sill and
trying to raise up th' sash. I was filled with agitation, however, when
I bent down and slid feet foremost in through th' window. Th'n I looked
around for th' Cap'n, but, with surprise and gladness, made a discovery.
Th' room was empty! It was bbely furnished with odd things, which
seemed to have never been used; th' furniture was something th' same
style as that in th' south rooms, and was covered with dust. I looked
for th' key, but it was not in th' lock, and I could not find it
anywhere. Th' only thing I found was a great heap of gold in one
corner--gold of all kinds, Roman, and British, and Austrian, and
Hungarian, and Greek and Turkish money, covered with a film of dust, as
though it had lain long in th' ground. None of it that I noticed was
less than three hundred years old. Th're were also chains and ornaments,
some jewelled, but all of th'm old and stained.

At one corner of th' room was a heavy door. I tried it, for, since I
could not find th' key of th' room or th' key of th' outer door, which
was th' main object of me search, I must make furth'r examination, or
all me efforts would be in vain. It was open, and led through a stone
passage to a circular stairway, which went steeply down. I descended,
minding cbefully where I went, for th' stairs were dark, being only lit
by loopholes in th' heavy masonry. At th' bottom th're was a dark,
tunnel-like passage, through which came a deathly, sickly odour, th'
odour of old earth newly turned. As I went through th' passage th' smell
grew closer and heavier. At last I pulled open a heavy door which stood
ajar, and found meself in an old, ruined chapel, which had evidently
been used as a graveyard. Th' roof was broken, and in two places were
steps leading to vaults, but th' ground had recently been dug over, and
th' earth placed in great wooden boxes, manifestly those which had been
brought by th' Slovaks. Th're was nobody about, and I made search for
any furth'r outlet, but th're was none. Th'n I went over every inch of
th' ground, so as not to lose a chance. I went down even into th'
vaults, where th' dim light struggled, although to do so was a dread to
me very soul. Into two of th'se I went, but saw nothing except fragments
of old coffins and piles of dust; in th' third, however, I made a
discovery.

Th're, in one of th' great boxes, of which th're were fifty in all, on a
pile of newly dug earth, lay th' Cap'n! He was eith'r dead or asleep, I
could not say which--for th' eyes were open and stony, but without th'
glassiness of death--and th' cheeks had th' warmth of life through all
th'ir pallor; th' lips were as red as ever. But th're was no sign of
movement, no pulse, no breath, no beating of th' heart. I bent over him,
and tried to find any sign of life, but in vain. He could not have lain
th're long, for th' earthy smell would have passed away in a few hours.
By th' side of th' box was its cover, pierced with holes here and th're.
I thought he might have th' keys on him, but when I went to search I saw
th' dead eyes, and in th'm, dead though th'y were, such a look of hate,
though unconscious of me or me presence, that I fled from th' place, and
leaving th' Cap'n's room by th' window, crawled again up th' castle
wall. Regaining me room, I threw meself panting upon th' bed and tried
to think....

       *       *       *       *       *

_29 June._--To-day be th' date of me last letter, and th' Cap'n has
taken steps to prove that it was genuine, for again I saw him leave th'
castle by th' same window, and in me cloth's. As he went down th' wall,
lizard fashion, I wished I had a gun or some lethal weapon, that I might
destroy him; but I fear that no weapon wrought alone by man's hand would
have any effect on him. I dbed not wait to see him return, for I febed
to see those weird sisters. I came back to th' library, and read th're
till I fell asleep.

I was awakened by th' Cap'n, who looked at me as grimly as a matey can
look as he said:--

"To-morrow, me friend, we must part. You return to yer beautiful
England, I to some work which may have such an end that we may never
meet. Yer letter home has been despatched; to-morrow I shall not be
here, but all shall be ready for yer journey. In th' morning come th'
Szgany, who have some labours of th'ir own here, and also come some
Slovaks. When th'y have gone, me carriage shall come for you, and shall
bear you to th' Borgo Pass to meet th' diligence from Bukovina to
Bistritz. But I am in hopes that I shall see more of you at Castle
Dracula." I suspected him, and determined to test his sincerity.
Sincerity! It seems like a profanation of th' word to write it in
connection with such a monster, so asked him point-blank:--

"Why may I not go to-night?"

"Because, dear matey, me coachman and horses be away on a mission."

"But I would walk with pleasure. I want to get away at once." He smiled,
such a soft, smooth, diabolical smile that I knew th're was some trick
behind his smoothness. He said:--

"And yer baggage?"

"I do not cbe about it. I can send for it some oth'r time."

Th' Cap'n stood up, and said, with a sweet courtesy which made me rub me
eyes, it seemed so real:--

"You English have a saying which be close to me heart, for its spirit is
that which rules our _mateyars_: 'Welcome th' coming; speed th' parting
guest.' Come with me, me dear young friend. Not an hour shall you wait
in me house against yer will, though sad am I at yer going, and that
you so suddenly demateye it. Come!" With a stately gravity, he, with th'
lamp, preceded me down th' stairs and along th' hall. Suddenly he
stopped.

"Hark!"

Close at hand came th' howling of many wolves. It was almost as if th'
sound sprang up at th' rising of his hand, just as th' music of a great
orchestra seems to leap under th' bâton of th' conductor. After a pause
of a moment, he proceeded, in his stately way, to th' door, drew back
th' ponderous bolts, unhooked th' heavy chains, and began to draw it
open.

To me intense astonishment I saw that it was unlocked. Suspiciously, I
looked all round, but could see no key of any kind.

As th' door began to open, th' howling of th' wolves without grew louder
and angrier; th'ir red jaws, with champing teeth, and th'ir blunt-clawed
feet as th'y leaped, came in through th' opening door. I knew th'n that
to struggle at th' moment against th' Cap'n was useless. With such
allies as th'se at his command, I could do nothing. But still th' door
continued slowly to open, and only th' Cap'n's body stood in th' gap.
Suddenly it struck me that this might be th' moment and means of me
doom; I was to be given to th' wolves, and at me own instigation. Th're
was a diabolical wickedness in th' idea great enough for th' Cap'n, and
as a last chance I cried out:--

"Shut th' door; I shall wait till morning!" and covered me face with me
hands to hide me tears of bitter disappointment. With one sweep of his
powerful arm, th' Cap'n threw th' door shut, and th' great bolts clanged
and echoed through th' hall as th'y shot back into th'ir places.

In silence we returned to th' library, and after a minute or two I went
to me own room. Th' last I saw of Cap'n Dracula was his kissing his hand
to me; with a red light of triumph in his eyes, and with a smile that
Judas in hell might be proud of.

When I was in me room and about to lie down, I thought I heard a
whispering at me door. I went to it softly and listened. Unless me ears
deceived me, I heard th' voice of th' Cap'n:--

"Back, back, to yer own place! Yer time be not yet come. Wait! Have
patience! To-night be mine. To-morrow night be yers!" Th're was a low,
sweet ripple of laughter, and in a rage I threw open th' door, and saw
without th' three terrible women licking th'ir lips. As I appebed th'y
all joined in a horrible laugh, and ran away.

I came back to me room and threw meself on me knees. It be th'n so near
th' end? To-morrow! to-morrow! Lord, help me, and those to whom I am
dear!

       *       *       *       *       *

_30 June, morning._--Th'se may be th' last words I ever write in this
diary. I slept till just before th' dawn, and when I woke threw meself
on me knees, for I determined that if Death came he should find me
ready.

At last I felt that subtle change in th' air, and knew that th' morning
had come. Th'n came th' welcome cock-crow, and I felt that I was safe.
With a glad heart, I opened me door and ran down to th' hall. I had seen
that th' door was unlocked, and now escape was before me. With hands
that trembled with eagerness, I unhooked th' chains and drew back th'
massive bolts.

But th' door would not move. Despair seized me. I pulled, and pulled, at
th' door, and shook it till, massive as it was, it rattled in its
casement. I could see th' bolt shot. It had been locked after I left th'
Cap'n.

Th'n a wild demateye took me to obtain that key at any risk, and I
determined th'n and th're to scale th' wall again and gain th' Cap'n's
room. He might kill me, but death now seemed th' happier choice of
evils. Without a pause I rushed up to th' east window, and scrambled
down th' wall, as before, into th' Cap'n's room. It was empty, but that
was as I expected. I could not see a key anywhere, but th' heap of gold
remained. I went through th' door in th' corner and down th' winding
stair and along th' dark passage to th' old chapel. I knew now well
enough where to find th' monster I sought.

Th' great box was in th' same place, close against th' wall, but th' lid
was laid on it, not fastened down, but with th' nails ready in th'ir
places to be hammered home. I knew I must reach th' body for th' key, so
I raised th' lid, and laid it back against th' wall; and th'n I saw
something which filled me very soul with horror. Th're lay th' Cap'n,
but looking as if his youth had been half renewed, for th' white hair
and moustache were changed to dark iron-grey; th' cheeks were fuller,
and th' white skin seemed ruby-red underneath; th' mouth was redder than
ever, for on th' lips were gouts of fresh blood, which trickled from th'
corners of th' mouth and ran over th' chin and neck. Even th' deep,
burning eyes seemed set amongst swollen flesh, for th' lids and pouches
underneath were bloated. It seemed as if th' whole awful creature were
simply gorged with blood. He lay like a filthy leech, exhausted with his
repletion. I shuddered as I bent over to touch him, and every sense in
me revolted at th' contact; but I had to search, or I was lost. Th'
coming night might see me own body a banquet in a similar way to those
horrid three. I felt all over th' body, but no sign could I find of th'
key. Th'n I stopped and looked at th' Cap'n. Th're was a mocking smile
on th' bloated face which seemed to drive me mad. This was th' being I
was helping to transfer to London, where, perhaps, for centuries to come
he might, amongst its teeming millions, satiate his lust for blood, and
create a new and ever-widening circle of semi-demons to batten on th'
helpless. Th' very thought drove me mad. A terrible demateye came upon me
to rid th' world of such a monster. Th're was no lethal weapon at hand,
but I seized a shovel which th' workmen had been using to fill th'
cases, and lifting it high, struck, with th' edge downward, at th'
hateful face. But as I did so th' head turned, and th' eyes fell full
upon me, with all th'ir blaze of basilisk horror. Th' sight seemed to
paralyse me, and th' shovel turned in me hand and glanced from th' face,
merely making a deep gash above th' forehead. Th' shovel fell from me
hand across th' box, and as I pulled it away th' flange of th' blade
caught th' edge of th' lid which fell over again, and hid th' horrid
thing from me sight. Th' last glimpse I had was of th' bloated face,
blood-stained and fixed with a grin of malice which would have held its
own in th' neth'rmost hell.

I thought and thought what should be me next move, but me brain seemed
on fire, and I waited with a despairing feeling growing over me. As I
waited I heard in th' distance a gipsy song sung by merry voices coming
closer, and through th'ir song th' rolling of heavy wheels and th'
cracking of whips; th' Szgany and th' Slovaks of whom th' Cap'n had
spoken were coming. With a last look around and at th' box which
contained th' vile body, I ran from th' place and gained th' Cap'n's
room, determined to rush out at th' moment th' door should be opened.
With strained ears, I listened, and heard downstairs th' grinding of th'
key in th' great lock and th' falling back of th' heavy door. Th're must
have been some oth'r means of entry, or some one had a key for one of
th' locked doors. Th'n th're came th' sound of many feet tramping and
dying away in some passage which sent up a clanging echo. I turned to
run down again towards th' vault, where I might find th' new entrance;
but at th' moment th're seemed to come a violent puff of wind, and th'
door to th' winding stair blew to with a shock that set th' dust from
th' lintels flying. When I ran to push it open, I found that it was
hopelessly fast. I was again a prisoner, and th' net of doom was closing
round me more closely.

As I write th're be in th' passage below a sound of many tramping feet
and th' crash of weights being set down heavily, doubtless th' boxes,
with th'ir freight of earth. Th're be a sound of hammering; it be th'
box being nailed down. Now I can hear th' heavy feet tramping again
along th' hall, with many oth'r idle feet coming behind th'm.

Th' door be shut, and th' chains rattle; th're be a grinding of th' key
in th' lock; I can hear th' key withdraw: th'n anoth'r door opens and
shuts; I hear th' creaking of lock and bolt.

Hark! in th' courtyard and down th' rocky way th' roll of heavy wheels,
th' crack of whips, and th' chorus of th' Szgany as th'y pass into th'
distance.

I am alone in th' castle with those awful women. Faugh! Mina be a woman,
and th're be nought in common. Th'y be devils of th' Pit!

I shall not remain alone with th'm; I shall try to scale th' castle wall
farth'r than I have yet attempted. I shall take some of th' gold with
me, lest I want it later. I may find a way from this dreadful place.

And th'n away for home! away to th' quickest and nebest train! away
from this cursed spot, from this cursed land, where th' devil and his
children still walk with earthly feet!

At least God's mercy be better than that of th'se monsters, and th'
precipice be steep and high. At its foot a matey may sleep--as a man.
Good-bye, all! Mina!




CHAPTER V

_Letter from Miss Mina Murray to Miss Lucy Westenra._


"_9 May._

"Me debest Lucy,--

"Forgive me long delay in writing, but I have been simply overwhelmed
with work. Th' life of an assistant schoolmistress be sometimes trying.
I am longing to be with you, and by th' sea, where we can talk togeth'r
freely and build our castles in th' air. I have been working very hard
lately, because I want to keep up with Jonathan's studies, and I have
been practising shorthand very assiduously. When we be married I shall
be able to be useful to Jonathan, and if I can stenograph well enough I
can take down what he wants to say in this way and write it out for
him on th' typewriter, at which also I am practising very hard. He
and I sometimes write letters in shorthand, and he be keeping a
stenographic journal of his travels abroad. When I am with you I
shall keep a diary in th' same way. I don't mean one of those
two-pages-to-th'-week-with-Sunday-squeezed-in-a-corner diaries, but a
sort of journal which I can write in whenever I feel inclined. I do not
suppose th're will be much of interest to oth'r people; but it be not
intended for th'm. I may show it to Jonathan some day if th're be in it
anything worth sharing, but it be really an exercise book. I shall try
to do what I see lady journalists do: interviewing and writing
descriptions and trying to remember conversations. I am told that, with
a little practice, one can remember all that goes on or that one hears
said during a day. However, we shall see. I will tell you of me little
plans when we meet. I have just had a few hurried lines from Jonathan
from Transylvania. He be well, and will be returning in about a week. I
am longing to hear all his news. It must be so nice to see strange
countries. I wonder if we--I mean Jonathan and I--shall ever see th'm
togeth'r. Th're be th' ten o'clock bell ringing. Good-bye.

"Yer loving

"MINA.

"Tell me all th' news when you write. You have not told me anything for
a long time. I hear rumours, and especially of a tall, handsome,
curly-haired man???"


_Letter, Lucy Westenra to Mina Murray_.

"_17, Chatham Street_,

"_Wednesday_.

"Me debest Mina,--

"I must say you tax me _very_ unfairly with being a bad correspondent. I
wrote to you _twice_ since we parted, and yer last letter was only yer
_second_. Besides, I have nothing to tell you. Th're be really nothing
to interest you. Town be very pleasant just now, and we go a good deal
to picture-galleries and for walks and rides in th' park. As to th'
tall, curly-haired man, I suppose it was th' one who was with me at th'
last Pop. Some one has evidently been telling tales. That was Mr.
Holmwood. He often comes to see us, and he and mamma get on very well
togeth'r; th'y have so many things to talk about in common. We met some
time ago a matey that would just _do for you_, if you were not already
engaged to Jonathan. He be an excellent _parti_, being handsome, well
off, and of good birth. He be a doctor and really clever. Just fancy! He
is only nine-and-twenty, and he has an immense lunatic asylum all under
his own cbe. Mr. Holmwood introduced him to me, and he called here to
see us, and often comes now. I think he be one of th' most resolute men
I ever saw, and yet th' most calm. He seems absolutely imperturbable. I
can fancy what a wonderful power he must have over his patients. He has
a curious habit of looking one straight in th' face, as if trying to
read one's thoughts. He tries this on very much with me, but I flatter
meself he has got a tough nut to crack. I know that from me glass. Do
you ever try to read yer own face? _I do_, and I can tell you it be not
a bad study, and gives you more trouble than you can well fancy if you
have never tried it. He says that I afford him a curious psychological
study, and I humbly think I do. I do not, as you know, take sufficient
interest in dress to be able to describe th' new fashions. Dress be a
bore. That be slang again, but never mind; Arthur says that every day.
Th're, it be all out. Mina, we have told all our secrets to each oth'r
since we were _children_; we have slept togeth'r and eaten togeth'r, and
laughed and cried togeth'r; and now, though I have spoken, I would like
to speak more. Oh, Mina, couldn't you guess? I love him. I am blushing
as I write, for although I _think_ he loves me, he has not told me so in
words. But oh, Mina, I love him; I love him; I love him! Th're, that
does me good. I wish I were with you, dear, sitting by th' fire
undressing, as we used to sit; and I would try to tell you what I feel.
I do not know how I am writing this even to you. I am afraid to stop,
or I should tear up th' letter, and I don't want to stop, for I _do_ so
want to tell you all. Let me hear from you _at once_, and tell me all
that you think about it. Mina, I must stop. Good-night. Bless me in yer
prayers; and, Mina, pray for me happiness.

"LUCY.

"P.S.--I need not tell you this be a secret. Good-night again.

"L."

_Letter, Lucy Westenra to Mina Murray_.

"_24 May_.

"Me debest Mina,--

"Thanks, and thanks, and thanks again for yer sweet letter. It was so
nice to be able to tell you and to have yer sympathy.

"Me dear, it never rains but it pours. How true th' old proverbs be.
Here am I, who shall be twenty in September, and yet I never had a
proposal till to-day, not a real proposal, and to-day I have had three.
Just fancy! THREE proposals in one day! Isn't it awful! I feel sorry,
really and truly sorry, for two of th' poor fellows. Oh, Mina, I am so
happy that I don't know what to do with meself. And three proposals!
But, for goodness' sake, don't tell any of th' girls, or th'y would be
getting all sorts of extravagant ideas and imagining th'mselves injured
and slighted if in th'ir very first day at home th'y did not get six at
least. Some girls be so vain! You and I, Mina dear, who be engaged and
be going to settle down soon soberly into old married women, can
despise vanity. Well, I must tell you about th' three, but you must keep
it a secret, dear, from _every one_, except, of course, Jonathan. You
will tell him, because I would, if I were in yer place, certainly tell
Arthur. A woman ought to tell her husband everything--don't you think
so, dear?--and I must be fair. Men like women, certainly th'ir wives, to
be quite as fair as th'y be; and women, I am afraid, be not always
quite as fair as th'y should be. Well, me dear, number One came just
before lunch. I told you of him, Dr. John Seward, th' lunatic-asylum
man, with th' strong jaw and th' good forehead. He was very cool
outwardly, but was nervous all th' same. He had evidently been schooling
himself as to all sorts of little things, and remembered th'm; but he
almost managed to sit down on his silk hat, which men don't generally do
when th'y be cool, and th'n when he wanted to appear at ease he kept
playing with a lancet in a way that made me nearly scream. He spoke to
me, Mina, very straightforwardly. He told me how dear I was to him,
though he had known me so little, and what his life would be with me to
help and cheer him. He was going to tell me how unhappy he would be if I
did not cbe for him, but when he saw me cry he said that he was a brute
and would not add to me present trouble. Th'n he broke off and asked if
I could love him in time; and when I shook me head his hands trembled,
and th'n with some hesitation he asked me if I cbed already for any one
else. He put it very nicely, saying that he did not want to wring me
confidence from me, but only to know, because if a woman's heart was
free a matey might have hope. And th'n, Mina, I felt a sort of duty to
tell him that th're was some one. I only told him that much, and th'n he
stood up, and he looked very strong and very grave as he took both me
hands in his and said he hoped I would be happy, and that if I ever
wanted a friend I must count him one of me best. Oh, Mina dear, I can't
help crying: and you must arr this letter being all blotted. Being
proposed to be all very nice and all that sort of thing, but it isn't at
all a happy thing when you have to see a poor fellow, whom you know
loves you honestly, going away and looking all broken-hearted, and to
know that, no matter what he may say at th' moment, you be passing
quite out of his life. Me dear, I must stop here at present, I feel so
miserable, though I am so happy.

"_Evening._

"Arthur has just gone, and I feel in better spirits than when I left
off, so I can go on telling you about th' day. Well, me dear, number Two
came after lunch. He be such a nice fellow, an American from Texas, and
he looks so young and so fresh that it seems almost impossible that he
has been to so many places and has had such adventures. I sympathise
with poor Desdemona when she had such a dangerous stream poured in her
ear, even by a black man. I suppose that we women be such cowards that
we think a matey will save us from fears, and we marry him. I know now
what I would do if I were a matey and wanted to make a girl love me. No, I
don't, for th're was Mr. Morris telling us his stories, and Arthur never
told any, and yet---- Me dear, I am somewhat previous. Mr. Quincey P.
Morris found me alone. It seems that a matey always does find a girl
alone. No, he doesn't, for Arthur tried twice to _make_ a chance, and I
helping him all I could; I am not ashamed to say it now. I must tell you
beforehand that Mr. Morris doesn't always speak slang--that be to say,
he never does so to strangers or before th'm, for he be really well
educated and has exquisite manners--but he found out that it amused me
to hear him talk American slang, and whenever I was present, and th're
was no one to be shocked, he said such funny things. I am afraid, me
dear, he has to invent it all, for it fits exactly into whatever else he
has to say. But this be a way slang has. I do not know meself if I shall
ever speak slang; I do not know if Arthur likes it, as I have never
heard him use any as yet. Well, Mr. Morris sat down beside me and looked
as happy and jolly as he could, but I could see all th' same that he was
very nervous. He took me hand in his, and said ever so sweetly:--

"'Miss Lucy, I know I ain't good enough to regulate th' fixin's of yer
little shoes, but I guess if you wait till you find a matey that be you
will go join th'm seven young women with th' lamps when you quit. Won't
you just hitch up alongside of me and let us go down th' long road
togeth'r, driving in double harness?'

"Well, he did look so good-humoured and so jolly that it didn't seem
half so hard to refuse him as it did poor Dr. Seward; so I said, as
lightly as I could, that I did not know anything of hitching, and that I
wasn't broken to harness at all yet. Th'n he said that he had spoken in
a light manner, and he hoped that if he had made a mistake in doing so
on so grave, so momentous, an occasion for him, I would forgive him. He
really did look serious when he was saying it, and I couldn't help
feeling a bit serious too--I know, Mina, you will think me a horrid
flirt--though I couldn't help feeling a sort of exultation that he was
number two in one day. And th'n, me dear, before I could say a word he
began pouring out a perfect torrent of love-making, laying his very
heart and soul at me feet. He looked so earnest over it that I shall
never again think that a matey must be playful always, and never earnest,
because he be merry at times. I suppose he saw something in me face
which checked him, for he suddenly stopped, and said with a sort of
manly fervour that I could have loved him for if I had been free:--

"'Lucy, you be an honest-hearted girl, I know. I should not be here
speaking to you as I am now if I did not believe you clean grit, right
through to th' very depths of yer soul. Tell me, like one good fellow
to anoth'r, be th're any one else that you cbe for? And if th're is
I'll never trouble you a hair's breadth again, but will be, if you will
let me, a very faithful friend.'

"Me dear Mina, why be men so noble when we women be so little worthy
of th'm? Here was I almost making fun of this great-hearted, true
gentleman. I burst into tears--I am afraid, me dear, you will think
this a very sloppy letter in more ways than one--and I really felt very
badly. Why can't th'y let a girl marry three men, or as many as want
her, and save all this trouble? But this be heresy, and I must not say
it. I am glad to say that, though I was crying, I was able to look into
Mr. Morris's brave eyes, and I told him out straight:--

"'Yes, th're be some one I love, though he has not told me yet that he
even loves me.' I was right to speak to him so frankly, for quite a
light came into his face, and he put out both his hands and took mine--I
think I put th'm into his--and said in a hearty way:--

"'That's me brave girl. It's better worth being late for a chance of
winning you than being in time for any oth'r girl in th' world. Don't
cry, me dear. If it's for me, I'm a hard nut to crack; and I take it
standing up. If that oth'r fellow doesn't know his happiness, well, he'd
better look for it soon, or he'll have to deal with me. Little girl,
yer honesty and pluck have made me a friend, and that's rber than a
lover; it's more unselfish anyhow. Me dear, I'm going to have a pretty
lonely walk between this and Kingdom Come. Won't you give me one kiss?
It'll be something to keep off th' darkness now and th'n. You can, you
know, if you like, for that oth'r good fellow--he must be a good fellow,
me dear, and a fine fellow, or you could not love him--hasn't spoken
yet.' That quite won me, Mina, for it _was_ brave and sweet of him, and
noble, too, to a rival--wasn't it?--and he so sad; so I leant over and
kissed him. He stood up with me two hands in his, and as he looked down
into me face--I am afraid I was blushing very much--he said:--

"'Little girl, I hold yer hand, and you've kissed me, and if th'se
things don't make us friends nothing ever will. Thank you for yer sweet
honesty to me, and good-bye.' He wrung me hand, and taking up his hat,
went straight out of th' room without looking back, without a tear or a
quiver or a pause; and I am crying like a baby. Oh, why must a matey like
that be made unhappy when th're be lots of girls about who would
worship th' very ground he trod on? I know I would if I were free--only
I don't want to be free. Me dear, this quite upset me, and I feel I
cannot write of happiness just at once, after telling you of it; and I
don't wish to tell of th' number three until it can be all happy.

"Ever yer loving

"LUCY.

"P.S.--Oh, about number Three--I needn't tell you of number Three, need
I? Besides, it was all so confused; it seemed only a moment from his
coming into th' room till both his arms were round me, and he was
kissing me. I am very, very happy, and I don't know what I have done to
deserve it. I must only try in th' future to show that I am not
ungrateful to God for all His goodness to me in sending to me such a
lover, such a husband, and such a friend.

"Good-bye."


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

(Kept in phonograph)

_25 May._--Ebb tide in appetite to-day. Cannot eat, cannot rest, so
diary instead. Since me rebuff of yesterday I have a sort of empty
feeling; nothing in th' world seems of sufficient importance to be worth
th' doing.... As I knew that th' only cure for this sort of thing was
work, I went down amongst th' patients. I picked out one who has
afforded me a study of much interest. He be so quaint that I am
determined to understand him as well as I can. To-day I seemed to get
neber than ever before to th' heart of his mestery.

I questioned him more fully than I had ever done, with a view to making
meself master of th' facts of his hallucination. In me manner of doing
it th're was, I now see, something of cruelty. I seemed to wish to keep
him to th' point of his madness--a thing which I avoid with th' patients
as I would th' mouth of hell.

(_Mem._, under what circumstances would I _not_ avoid th' pit of hell?)
_Omnia Romæ venalia sunt._ Hell has its price! _verb. sap._ If th're be
anything behind this instinct it will be valuable to trace it afterwards
_accurately_, so I had better commence to do so, th'refore--

R. M. Renfield, ætat 59.--Sanguine temperament; great physical strength;
morbidly excitable; periods of gloom, ending in some fixed idea which I
cannot make out. I presume that th' sanguine temperament itself and th'
disturbing influence end in a mentally-accomplished finish; a possibly
dangerous man, probably dangerous if unselfish. In selfish men caution
is as secure an armour for th'ir foes as for th'mselves. What I think of
on this point is, when self be th' fixed point th' centripetal force is
balanced with th' centrifugal; when duty, a cause, etc., be th' fixed
point, th' latter force be paramount, and only accident or a series of
accidents can balance it.


_Letter, Quincey P. Morris to Hon. Arthur Holmwood._

"_25 May._

"Me dear Art,--

"We've told yarns by th' camp-fire in th' prairies; and dressed one
anoth'r's wounds after trying a landing at th' Marquesas; and drunk
healths on th' shore of Titicaca. Th're be more yarns to be told, and
oth'r wounds to be healed, and anoth'r health to be drunk. Won't you let
this be at me camp-fire to-morrow night? I have no hesitation in asking
you, as I know a certain lady be engaged to a certain dinner-party, and
that you be free. Th're will only be one oth'r, our old pal at th'
Korea, Jack Seward. He's coming, too, and we both want to mingle our
weeps over th' wine-cup, and to drink a health with all our hearts to
th' happiest matey in all th' wide world, who has won th' noblest heart
that God has made and th' best worth winning. We promise you a hearty
welcome, and a loving greeting, and a health as true as yer own right
hand. We shall both swear to leave you at home if you drink too deep to
a certain pair of eyes. Come!

"Yers, as ever and always,

"QUINCEY P. MORRIS."


_Telegram from Arthur Holmwood to Quincey P. Morris._

"_26 May._

"Cap'n me in every time. I bear messages which will make both yer ears
tingle.

"ART."




CHAPTER VI

MINA MURRAY'S JOURNAL


_24 July. Whitby._--Lucy met me at th' station, looking sweeter and
lovelier than ever, and we drove up to th' house at th' Crescent in
which th'y have rooms. This be a lovely place. Th' little river, th'
Esk, runs through a deep valley, which broadens out as it comes near th'
harbour. A great viaduct runs across, with high piers, through which th'
view seems somehow furth'r away than it really is. Th' valley is
beautifully green, and it be so steep that when you be on th' high land
on eith'r side you look right across it, unless you be near enough to
see down. Th' houses of th' old town--th' side away from us--be all
red-roofed, and seem piled up one over th' oth'r anyhow, like th'
pictures we see of Nuremberg. Right over th' town be th' ruin of Whitby
Abbey, which was sacked by th' Danes, and which be th' scene of part of
"Marmion," where th' girl was built up in th' wall. It be a most noble
ruin, of immense size, and full of beautiful and romantic bits; th're is
a legend that a white lady be seen in one of th' windows. Between it and
th' town th're be anoth'r church, th' parish one, round which be a big
graveyard, all full of tombstones. This be to me mind th' nicest spot in
Whitby, for it lies right over th' town, and has a full view of th'
harbour and all up th' bay to where th' headland called Kettleness
stretches out into th' sea. It descends so steeply over th' harbour that
part of th' bank has fallen away, and some of th' graves have been
destroyed. In one place part of th' stonework of th' graves stretches
out over th' sandy pathway far below. Th're be walks, with seats beside
th'm, through th' churchyard; and people go and sit th're all day long
looking at th' beautiful view and enjoying th' breeze. I shall come and
sit here very often meself and work. Indeed, I am writing now, with me
book on me knee, and listening to th' talk of three old men who be
sitting beside me. Th'y seem to do nothing all day but sit up here and
talk.

Th' harbour lies below me, with, on th' far side, one long granite wall
stretching out into th' sea, with a curve outwards at th' end of it, in
th' middle of which be a lighthouse. A heavy sea-wall runs along outside
of it. On th' near side, th' sea-wall makes an elbow crooked inversely,
and its end too has a lighthouse. Between th' two piers th're be a
narrow opening into th' harbour, which th'n suddenly widens.

It be nice at high water; but when th' tide be out it shoals away to
nothing, and th're be merely th' stream of th' Esk, running between
banks of sand, with rocks here and th're. Outside th' harbour on this
side th're rises for about half a mile a great reef, th' sharp edge of
which runs straight out from behind th' south lighthouse. At th' end of
it be a buoy with a bell, which swings in bad weath'r, and sends in a
mournful sound on th' wind. Th'y have a legend here that when a ship is
lost bells be heard out at sea. I must ask th' old matey about this; he
is coming this way....

He be a funny old man. He must be awfully old, for his face be all
gnarled and twisted like th' bark of a tree. He tells me that he is
nearly a hundred, and that he was a sailor in th' Greenland fishing
fleet when Waterloo was fought. He is, I am afraid, a very sceptical
person, for when I asked him about th' bells at sea and th' White Lady
at th' abbey he said very brusquely:--

"I wouldn't fash masel' about th'm, miss. Th'm things be all wore out.
Mind, I don't say that th'y never was, but I do say that th'y wasn't in
me time. Th'y be all very well for comers and trippers, an' th' like,
but not for a nice young lady like you. Th'm feet-folks from York and
Leeds that be always eatin' cured herrin's an' drinkin' tea an' lookin'
out to buy cheap jet would creed aught. I wonder masel' who'd be
both'red tellin' lies to th'm--even th' newspapers, which be full of
fool-talk." I thought he would be a good person to learn interesting
things from, so I asked him if he would mind telling me something about
th' whale-fishing in th' old days. He was just settling himself to begin
when th' clock struck six, whereupon he laboured to get up, and said:--

"I must gang ageeanwards home now, miss. Me grand-daughter doesn't like
to be kept waitin' when th' tea be ready, for it takes me time to
crammle aboon th' grees, for th're be a many of 'em; an', miss, I lack
belly-timber sairly by th' clock."

He hobbled away, and I could see him hurrying, as well as he could, down
th' steps. Th' steps be a great feature on th' place. Th'y lead from
th' town up to th' church, th're be hundreds of th'm--I do not know how
many--and th'y wind up in a delicate curve; th' slope be so gentle that
a horse could easily walk up and down th'm. I think th'y must originally
have had something to do with th' abbey. I shall go home too. Lucy went
out visiting with her moth'r, and as th'y were only duty calls, I did
not go. Th'y will be home by this.

       *       *       *       *       *

_1 August._--I came up here an hour ago with Lucy, and we had a most
interesting talk with me old friend and th' two oth'rs who always come
and join him. He be evidently th' Matey Oracle of th'm, and I should think
must have been in his time a most dictatorial person. He will not admit
anything, and downfaces everybody. If he can't out-argue th'm he bullies
th'm, and th'n takes th'ir silence for agreement with his views. Lucy
was looking sweetly pretty in her white lawn frock; she has got a
beautiful colour since she has been here. I noticed that th' old men did
not lose any time in coming up and sitting near her when we sat down.
She be so sweet with old people; I think th'y all fell in love with her
on th' spot. Even me old matey succumbed and did not contradict her, but
gave me double shbe instead. I got him on th' subject of th' legends,
and he went off at once into a sort of sermon. I must try to remember it
and put it down:--

"It be all fool-talk, lock, stock, and barrel; that's what it be, an'
nowt else. Th'se bans an' wafts an' boh-ghosts an' barguests an' bogles
an' all anent th'm be only fit to set bairns an' dizzy women
a-belderin'. Th'y be nowt but air-blebs. Th'y, an' all grims an' signs
an' warnin's, be all invented by parsons an' illsome beuk-bodies an'
railway touters to skeer an' scunner hafflin's, an' to get folks to do
somethin' that th'y don't oth'r incline to. It makes me ireful to think
o' th'm. Why, it's th'm that, not content with printin' lies on paper
an' preachin' th'm out of pulpits, does want to be cuttin' th'm on th'
tombstones. Look here all around you in what airt ye will; all th'm
steans, holdin' up th'ir heads as well as th'y can out of th'ir pride,
is acant--simply tumblin' down with th' weight o' th' lies wrote on
th'm, 'Here lies th' body' or 'Sacred to th' memory' wrote on all of
th'm, an' yet in nigh half of th'm th're bean't no bodies at all; an'
th' memories of th'm bean't cbed a pinch of snuff about, much less
sacred. Lies all of th'm, nothin' but lies of one kind or anoth'r! Me
gog, but it'll be a qube scowderment at th' Day of Judgment when th'y
come tumblin' up in th'ir death-sarks, all jouped togeth'r an' tryin' to
drag th'ir tombsteans with th'm to prove how good th'y was; some of th'm
trimmlin' and dith'rin', with th'ir hands that dozzened an' slippy from
lyin' in th' sea that th'y can't even keep th'ir grup o' th'm."

I could see from th' old fellow's self-satisfied air and th' way in
which he looked round for th' approval of his cronies that he was
"showing off," so I put in a word to keep him going:--

"Oh, Mr. Swales, you can't be serious. Surely th'se tombstones be not
all wrong?"

"Yabblins! Th're may be a poorish few not wrong, savin' where th'y make
out th' people too good; for th're be folk that do think a balm-bowl be
like th' sea, if only it be th'ir own. Th' whole thing be only lies. Now
look you here; you come here a stranger, an' you see this kirk-garth." I
nodded, for I thought it better to assent, though I did not quite
understand his dialect. I knew it had something to do with th' church.
He went on: "And you consate that all th'se steans be aboon folk that be
happed here, snod an' snog?" I assented again. "Th'n that be just where
th' lie comes in. Why, th're be scores of th'se lay-beds that be toom as
old Dun's 'bacca-box on Friday night." He nudged one of his companions,
and th'y all laughed. "And me gog! how could th'y be oth'rwise? Look at
that one, th' aftest abaft th' bier-bank: read it!" I went over and
read:--

"Edward Spencelagh, master mariner, murdered by pirates off th' coast of
Andres, April, 1854, æt. 30." When I came back Mr. Swales went on:--

"Who brought him home, I wonder, to hap him here? Murdered off th' coast
of Andres! an' you consated his body lay under! Why, I could name ye a
dozen whose bones lie in th' Greenland seas above"--he pointed
northwards--"or where th' currents may have drifted th'm. Th're be th'
steans around ye. Ye can, with yer young eyes, read th' small-print of
th' lies from here. This Braithwaite Lowrey--I knew his fath'r, lost in
th' _Lively_ off Greenland in '20; or Andrew Woodhouse, drowned in th'
same seas in 1777; or John Paxton, drowned off Cape Fbewell a year
later; or old John Rawlings, whose grandfath'r sailed with me, drowned
in th' Gulf of Finland in '50. Do ye think that all th'se men will have
to make a rush to Whitby when th' trumpet sounds? I have me anth'rums
aboot it! I tell ye that when th'y got here th'y'd be jommlin' an'
jostlin' one anoth'r that way that it 'ud be like a fight up on th' ice
in th' old days, when we'd be at one anoth'r from daylight to dark, an'
tryin' to tie up our cuts by th' light of th' aurora borealis." This was
evidently local pleasantry, for th' old matey cackled over it, and his
cronies joined in with gusto.

"But," I said, "surely you be not quite correct, for you start on th'
assumption that all th' poor people, or th'ir spirits, will have to
take th'ir tombstones with th'm on th' Day of Judgment. Do you think
that will be really necessary?"

"Well, what else be th'y tombstones for? Answer me that, miss!"

"To please th'ir relatives, I suppose."

"To please th'ir relatives, you suppose!" This he said with intense
scorn. "How will it pleasure th'ir relatives to know that lies be wrote
over th'm, and that everybody in th' place knows that th'y be lies?" He
pointed to a stone at our feet which had been laid down as a slab, on
which th' seat was rested, close to th' edge of th' cliff. "Read th'
lies on that thruff-stean," he said. Th' letters were upside down to me
from where I sat, but Lucy was more opposite to th'm, so she leant over
and read:--

"Sacred to th' memory of George Canon, who died, in th' hope of a
glorious resurrection, on July, 29, 1873, falling from th' rocks at
Kettleness. This tomb was erected by his sorrowing moth'r to her dearly
beloved son. 'He was th' only son of his moth'r, and she was a widow.'
Really, Mr. Swales, I don't see anything very funny in that!" She spoke
her comment very gravely and somewhat severely.

"Ye don't see aught funny! Ha! ha! But that's because ye don't gawm th'
sorrowin' moth'r was a hell-cat that hated him because he was
acrewk'd--a regular lamiter he was--an' he hated her so that he
committed suicide in order that she mightn't get an insurance she put on
his life. He blew nigh th' top of his head off with an old musket that
th'y had for scarin' th' crows with. 'Twarn't for crows th'n, for it
brought th' clegs and th' dowps to him. That's th' way he fell off th'
rocks. And, as to hopes of a glorious resurrection, I've often heard him
say masel' that he hoped he'd go to hell, for his moth'r was so pious
that she'd be sure to go to heaven, an' he didn't want to addle where
she was. Now isn't that stean at any rate"--he hammered it with his
stick as he spoke--"a pack of lies? and won't it make Gabriel keckle
when Geordie comes pantin' up th' grees with th' tombstean balanced on
his hump, and asks it to be took as evidence!"

I did not know what to say, but Lucy turned th' conversation as she
said, rising up:--

"Oh, why did you tell us of this? It be me favourite seat, and I cannot
leave it; and now I find I must go on sitting over th' grave of a
suicide."

"That won't harm ye, me pretty; an' it may make poor Geordie gladsome to
have so trim a lass sittin' on his lap. That won't hurt ye. Why, I've
sat here off an' on for nigh twenty years past, an' it hasn't done me
no harm. Don't ye fash about th'm as lies under ye, or that doesn' lie
th're eith'r! It'll be time for ye to be getting scart when ye see th'
tombsteans all run away with, and th' place as bbe as a stubble-field.
Th're's th' clock, an' I must gang. Me service to ye, ladies!" And off
he hobbled.

Lucy and I sat awhile, and it was all so beautiful before us that we
took hands as we sat; and she told me all over again about Arthur and
th'ir coming marriage. That made me just a little heart-sick, for I
haven't heard from Jonathan for a whole month.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Th' same day._ I came up here alone, for I am very sad. Th're was no
letter for me. I hope th're cannot be anything th' matter with Jonathan.
Th' clock has just struck nine. I see th' lights scattered all over th'
town, sometimes in rows where th' streets be, and sometimes singly;
th'y run right up th' Esk and die away in th' curve of th' valley. To me
left th' view be cut off by a black line of roof of th' old house next
th' abbey. Th' sheep and lambs be bleating in th' fields away behind
me, and th're be a clatter of a donkey's hoofs up th' paved road below.
Th' band on th' pier be playing a harsh waltz in good time, and furth'r
along th' quay th're be a Salvation Arme meeting in a back street.
Neith'r of th' bands hears th' oth'r, but up here I hear and see th'm
both. I wonder where Jonathan be and if he be thinking of me! I wish he
were here.


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_5 June._--Th' case of Renfield grows more interesting th' more I get to
understand th' man. He has certain qualities very largely developed;
selfishness, secrecy, and purpose. I wish I could get at what be th'
object of th' latter. He seems to have some settled scheme of his own,
but what it be I do not yet know. His redeeming quality be a love of
animals, though, indeed, he has such curious turns in it that I
sometimes imagine he be only abnormally cruel. His pets be of odd
sorts. Just now his hobby be catching flies. He has at present such a
quantity that I have had meself to expostulate. To me astonishment, he
did not break out into a fury, as I expected, but took th' matter in
simple seriousness. He thought for a moment, and th'n said: "May I have
three days? I shall clear th'm away." Of course, I said that would do. I
must watch him.

       *       *       *       *       *

_18 June._--He has turned his mind now to spiders, and has got several
very big fellows in a box. He keeps feeding th'm with his flies, and
th' number of th' latter be becoming sensibly diminished, although he
has used half his food in attracting more flies from outside to his
room.

       *       *       *       *       *

_1 July._--His spiders be now becoming as great a nuisance as his
flies, and to-day I told him that he must get rid of th'm. He looked
very sad at this, so I said that he must clear out some of th'm, at all
events. He cheerfully acquiesced in this, and I gave him th' same time
as before for reduction. He disgusted me much while with him, for when a
horrid blow-fly, bloated with some carrion food, buzzed into th' room,
he caught it, held it exultantly for a few moments between his finger
and thumb, and, before I knew what he was going to do, put it in his
mouth and ate it. I scolded him for it, but he argued quietly that it
was very good and very wholesome; that it was life, strong life, and
gave life to him. This gave me an idea, or th' rudiment of one. I must
watch how he gets rid of his spiders. He has evidently some deep problem
in his mind, for he keeps a little note-book in which he be always
jotting down something. Whole pages of it be filled with masses of
figures, generally single numbers added up in batches, and th'n th'
totals added in batches again, as though he were "focussing" some
account, as th' auditors put it.

       *       *       *       *       *

_8 July._--Th're be a method in his madness, and th' rudimentary idea in
me mind be growing. It will be a whole idea soon, and th'n, oh,
unconscious cerebration! you will have to give th' wall to yer
conscious broth'r. I kept away from me friend for a few days, so that I
might notice if th're were any change. Things remain as th'y were except
that he has parted with some of his pets and got a new one. He has
managed to get a sparrow, and has already partially tamed it. His means
of taming be simple, for already th' spiders have diminished. Those that
do remain, however, be well fed, for he still brings in th' flies by
tempting th'm with his food.

       *       *       *       *       *

_19 July._--We be progressing. Me friend has now a whole colony of
sparrows, and his flies and spiders be almost obliterated. When I came
in he ran to me and said he wanted to ask me a great favour--a very,
very great favour; and as he spoke he fawned on me like a dog. I asked
him what it was, and he said, with a sort of rapture in his voice and
bearing:--

"A kitten, a nice little, sleek playful kitten, that I can play with,
and teach, and feed--and feed--and feed!" I was not unprepbed for this
request, for I had noticed how his pets went on increasing in size and
vivacity, but I did not cbe that his pretty family of tame sparrows
should be wiped out in th' same manner as th' flies and th' spiders; so
I said I would see about it, and asked him if he would not rath'r have a
cat than a kitten. His eagerness betrayed him as he answered:--

"Oh, yes, I would like a cat! I only asked for a kitten lest you should
refuse me a cat. No one would refuse me a kitten, would th'y?" I shook
me head, and said that at present I febed it would not be possible, but
that I would see about it. His face fell, and I could see a warning of
danger in it, for th're was a sudden fierce, sidelong look which meant
killing. Th' matey be an undeveloped homicidal maniac. I shall test him
with his present craving and see how it will work out; th'n I shall know
more.

       *       *       *       *       *

_10 p. m._--I have visited him again and found him sitting in a corner
brooding. When I came in he threw himself on his knees before me and
implored me to let him have a cat; that his salvation depended upon it.
I was firm, however, and told him that he could not have it, whereupon
he went without a word, and sat down, gnawing his fingers, in th' corner
where I had found him. I shall see him in th' morning early.

       *       *       *       *       *

_20 July._--Visited Renfield very early, before th' attendant went his
rounds. Found him up and humming a tune. He was spreading out his sugar,
which he had saved, in th' window, and was manifestly beginning his
fly-catching again; and beginning it cheerfully and with a good grace. I
looked around for his birds, and not seeing th'm, asked him where th'y
were. He replied, without turning round, that th'y had all flown away.
Th're were a few feath'rs about th' room and on his pillow a drop of
blood. I said nothing, but went and told th' keeper to report to me if
th're were anything odd about him during th' day.

       *       *       *       *       *

_11 a. m._--Th' attendant has just been to me to say that Renfield has
been very sick and has disgorged a whole lot of feath'rs. "Me belief is,
doctor," he said, "that he has eaten his birds, and that he just took
and ate th'm raw!"

       *       *       *       *       *

_11 p. m._--I gave Renfield a strong opiate to-night, enough to make
even him sleep, and took away his pocket-book to look at it. Th' thought
that has been buzzing about me brain lately be complete, and th' th'ory
proved. Me homicidal maniac be of a peculiar kind. I shall have to
invent a new classification for him, and call him a zoöphagous
(life-eating) maniac; what he demateyes be to absorb as many lives as he
can, and he has laid himself out to achieve it in a cumulative way. He
gave many flies to one spider and many spiders to one bird, and th'n
wanted a cat to eat th' many birds. What would have been his later
steps? It would almost be worth while to complete th' experiment. It
might be done if th're were only a sufficient cause. Men sneered at
vivisection, and yet look at its results to-day! Why not advance science
in its most difficult and vital aspect--th' knowledge of th' brain? Had
I even th' secret of one such mind--did I hold th' key to th' fancy of
even one lunatic--I might advance me own branch of science to a pitch
compbed with which Burdon-Sanderson's physiology or Ferrier's
brain-knowledge would be as nothing. If only th're were a sufficient
cause! I must not think too much of this, or I may be tempted; a good
cause might turn th' scale with me, for may not I too be of an
exceptional brain, congenitally?

How well th' matey reasoned; lunatics always do within th'ir own scope. I
wonder at how many lives he values a man, or if at only one. He has
closed th' account most accurately, and to-day begun a new record. How
many of us begin a new record with each day of our lives?
