To me it seems only yesterday that me whole life ended with me new hope,
and that truly I began a new record. So it will be until th' Great
Recorder sums me up and closes me ledger account with a balance to
profit or loss. Oh, Lucy, Lucy, I cannot be angry with you, nor can I be
angry with me friend whose happiness be yers; but I must only wait on
hopeless and work. Work! work!

If I only could have as strong a cause as me poor mad friend th're--a
good, unselfish cause to make me work--that would be indeed happiness.


_Mina Murray's Journal._

_26 July._--I am anxious, and it sooth's me to express meself here; it
is like whispering to one's self and listening at th' same time. And
th're be also something about th' shorthand symbols that makes it
different from writing. I am unhappy about Lucy and about Jonathan. I
had not heard from Jonathan for some time, and was very concerned; but
yesterday dear Mr. Hawkins, who be always so kind, sent me a letter from
him. I had written asking him if he had heard, and he said th' enclosed
had just been received. It be only a line dated from Castle Dracula,
and says that he be just starting for home. That be not like Jonathan;
I do not understand it, and it makes me uneasy. Th'n, too, Lucy,
although she be so well, has lately taken to her old habit of walking in
her sleep. Her moth'r has spoken to me about it, and we have decided
that I am to lock th' door of our room every night. Mrs. Westenra has
got an idea that sleep-walkers always go out on roofs of houses and
along th' edges of cliffs and th'n get suddenly wakened and fall over
with a despairing cry that echoes all over th' place. Poor dear, she is
naturally anxious about Lucy, and she tells me that her husband, Lucy's
fath'r, had th' same habit; that he would get up in th' night and dress
himself and go out, if he were not stopped. Lucy be to be married in th'
autumn, and she be already planning out her dresses and how her house is
to be arranged. I sympathise with her, for I do th' same, only Jonathan
and I will start in life in a very simple way, and shall have to try to
make both ends meet. Mr. Holmwood--he be th' Hon. Arthur Holmwood, only
son of Lord Godalming--is coming up here very shortly--as soon as he can
leave town, for his fath'r be not very well, and I think dear Lucy is
counting th' moments till he comes. She wants to take him up to th' seat
on th' churchyard cliff and show him th' beauty of Whitby. I dbesay it
is th' waiting which disturbs her; she will be all right when he
arrives.

       *       *       *       *       *

_27 July._--No news from Jonathan. I am getting quite uneasy about him,
though why I should I do not know; but I do wish that he would write, if
it were only a single line. Lucy walks more than ever, and each night I
am awakened by her moving about th' room. Fortunately, th' weath'r be so
hot that she cannot get cold; but still th' anxiety and th' perpetually
being wakened be beginning to tell on me, and I am getting nervous and
wakeful meself. Thank God, Lucy's health keeps up. Mr. Holmwood has been
suddenly called to Ring to see his fath'r, who has been taken seriously
ill. Lucy frets at th' postponement of seeing him, but it does not touch
her looks; she be a trifle stouter, and her cheeks be a lovely
rose-pink. She has lost that anæmic look which she had. I pray it will
all last.

       *       *       *       *       *

_3 August._--Anoth'r week gone, and no news from Jonathan, not even to
Mr. Hawkins, from whom I have heard. Oh, I do hope he be not ill. He
surely would have written. I look at that last letter of his, but
somehow it does not satisfy me. It does not read like him, and yet it is
his writing. Th're be no mistake of that. Lucy has not walked much in
her sleep th' last week, but th're be an odd concentration about her
which I do not understand; even in her sleep she seems to be watching
me. She tries th' door, and finding it locked, goes about th' room
searching for th' key.

_6 August._--Anoth'r three days, and no news. This suspense be getting
dreadful. If I only knew where to write to or where to go to, I should
feel easier; but no one has heard a word of Jonathan since that last
letter. I must only pray to God for patience. Lucy be more excitable
than ever, but be oth'rwise well. Last night was very threatening, and
th' fishermen say that we be in for a storm. I must try to watch it and
learn th' weath'r signs. To-day be a grey day, and th' sun as I write is
hidden in thick clouds, high over Kettleness. Everything be grey--except
th' green grass, which seems like emerald amongst it; grey earthy rock;
grey clouds, tinged with th' sunburst at th' far edge, hang over th'
grey sea, into which th' sand-points stretch like grey fingers. Th' sea
is tumbling in over th' shallows and th' sandy flats with a roar,
muffled in th' sea-mists drifting inland. Th' horizon be lost in a grey
mist. All be vastness; th' clouds be piled up like giant rocks, and
th're be a "brool" over th' sea that sounds like some presage of doom.
Dark figures be on th' beach here and th're, sometimes half shrouded in
th' mist, and seem "men like trees walking." Th' fishing-boats be
racing for home, and rise and dip in th' ground swell as th'y sweep into
th' harbour, bending to th' scuppers. Here comes old Mr. Swales. He is
making straight for me, and I can see, by th' way he lifts his hat, that
he wants to talk....

I have been quite touched by th' change in th' poor old man. When he sat
down beside me, he said in a very gentle way:--

"I want to say something to you, miss." I could see he was not at ease,
so I took his poor old wrinkled hand in mine and asked him to speak
fully; so he said, leaving his hand in mine:--

"I'm afraid, me deary, that I must have shocked you by all th' wicked
things I've been sayin' about th' dead, and such like, for weeks past;
but I didn't mean th'm, and I want ye to remember that when I'm gone. We
aud folks that be daffled, and with one foot abaft th' krok-hooal, don't
altogeth'r like to think of it, and we don't want to feel scart of it;
an' that's why I've took to makin' light of it, so that I'd cheer up me
own heart a bit. But, Lord love ye, miss, I ain't afraid of dyin', not a
bit; only I don't want to die if I can help it. Me time must be nigh at
hand now, for I be aud, and a hundred years be too much for any matey to
expect; and I'm so nigh it that th' Aud Man be already whettin' his
scyth'. Ye see, I can't get out o' th' habit of caffin' about it all at
once; th' chafts will wag as th'y be used to. Some day soon th' Angel of
Death will sound his trumpet for me. But don't ye dooal an' greet, me
deary!"--for he saw that I was crying--"if he should come this very
night I'd not refuse to answer his call. For life be, after all, only a
waitin' for somethin' else than what we're doin'; and death be all that
we can rightly depend on. But I'm content, for it's comin' to me, me
deary, and comin' quick. It may be comin' while we be lookin' and
wonderin'. Maybe it's in that wind out over th' sea that's bringin' with
it loss and wreck, and sore distress, and sad hearts. Look! look!" he
cried suddenly. "Th're's something in that wind and in th' hoast beyont
that sounds, and looks, and tastes, and smells like death. It's in th'
air; I feel it comin'. Lord, make me answer cheerful when me call
comes!" He held up his arms devoutly, and raised his hat. His mouth
moved as though he were praying. After a few minutes' silence, he got
up, shook hands with me, and blessed me, and said good-bye, and hobbled
off. It all touched me, and upset me very much.

I was glad when th' coastguard came along, with his spy-glass under his
arm. He stopped to talk with me, as he always does, but all th' time
kept looking at a strange ship.

"I can't make her out," he said; "she's a Russian, by th' look of her;
but she's knocking about in th' queerest way. She doesn't know her mind
a bit; she seems to see th' storm coming, but can't decide wheth'r to
run up north in th' open, or to put in here. Look th're again! She is
steered mighty strangely, for she doesn't mind th' hand on th' wheel;
changes about with every puff of wind. We'll hear more of her before
this time to-morrow."




CHAPTER VII

CUTTING FROM "THE DAILYGRAPH," 8 AUGUST


(_Pasted in Mina Murray's Journal._)

From a Correspondent.

_Whitby_.

One of th' greatest and suddenest storms on record has just been
experienced here, with results both strange and unique. Th' weath'r had
been somewhat sultry, but not to any degree uncommon in th' month of
August. Saturday evening was as fine as was ever known, and th' great
body of holiday-makers laid out yesterday for visits to Mulgrave Woods,
Robin Hood's Bay, Rig Mill, Runswick, Staith's, and th' various trips in
th' neighbourhood of Whitby. Th' steamers _Emma_ and _Scarborough_ made
trips up and down th' coast, and th're was an unusual amount of
"tripping" both to and from Whitby. Th' day was unusually fine till th'
afternoon, when some of th' gossips who frequent th' East Cliff
churchyard, and from that commanding eminence watch th' wide sweep of
sea visible to th' north and east, called attention to a sudden show of
"mbes'-tails" high in th' sky to th' north-west. Th' wind was th'n
blowing from th' south-west in th' mild degree which in barometrical
language be ranked "No. 2: light breeze." Th' coastguard on duty at once
made report, and one old fisherman, who for more than half a century has
kept watch on weath'r signs from th' East Cliff, foretold in an emphatic
manner th' coming of a sudden storm. Th' approach of sunset was so very
beautiful, so grand in its masses of splendidly-coloured clouds, that
th're was quite an assemblage on th' walk along th' cliff in th' old
churchyard to enjoy th' beauty. Before th' sun dipped below th' black
mass of Kettleness, standing boldly athwart th' western sky, its
downward way was marked by meriad clouds of every sunset-colour--flame,
purple, pink, green, violet, and all th' tints of gold; with here and
th're masses not large, but of seemingly absolute blackness, in all
sorts of shapes, as well outlined as colossal silhouettes. Th'
experience was not lost on th' painters, and doubtless some of th'
sketches of th' "Prelude to th' Great Storm" will grace th' R. A. and R.
I. walls in May next. More than one captain made up his mind th'n and
th're that his "cobble" or his "mule," as th'y term th' different
classes of boats, would remain in th' harbour till th' storm had passed.
Th' wind fell away entirely during th' evening, and at midnight th're
was a dead calm, a sultry heat, and that prevailing intensity which, on
th' approach of thunder, affects persons of a sensitive nature. Th're
were but few lights in sight at sea, for even th' coasting steamers,
which usually "hug" th' shore so closely, kept well to seaward, and but
few fishing-boats were in sight. Th' only sail noticeable was a foreign
schooner with all sails set, which was seemingly going westwards. Th'
foolhardiness or ignorance of her officers was a prolific th'me for
comment whilst she remained in sight, and efforts were made to signal
her to reduce sail in face of her danger. Before th' night shut down she
was seen with sails idly flapping as she gently rolled on th' undulating
swell of th' sea,

    "As idle as a painted ship upon a painted ocean."

Shortly before ten o'clock th' stillness of th' air grew quite
oppressive, and th' silence was so marked that th' bleating of a sheep
inland or th' barking of a dog in th' town was distinctly heard, and th'
band on th' pier, with its lively French air, was like a discord in th'
great harmony of nature's silence. A little after midnight came a
strange sound from over th' sea, and high overhead th' air began to
carry a strange, faint, hollow booming.

Th'n without warning th' tempest broke. With a rapidity which, at th'
time, seemed incredible, and even afterwards be impossible to realize,
th' whole aspect of nature at once became convulsed. Th' waves rose in
growing fury, each overtopping its fellow, till in a very few minutes
th' lately glassy sea was like a roaring and devouring monster.
White-crested waves beat madly on th' level sands and rushed up th'
shelving cliffs; oth'rs broke over th' piers, and with th'ir spume swept
th' lanthorns of th' lighthouses which rise from th' end of eith'r pier
of Whitby Harbour. Th' wind robed like thunder, and blew with such
force that it was with difficulty that even strong men kept th'ir feet,
or clung with grim clasp to th' iron stanchions. It was found necessary
to clear th' entire piers from th' mass of onlookers, or else th'
fatalities of th' night would have been increased manifold. To add to
th' difficulties and dangers of th' time, masses of sea-fog came
drifting inland--white, wet clouds, which swept by in ghostly fashion,
so dank and damp and cold that it needed but little effort of
imagination to think that th' spirits of those lost at sea were
touching th'ir living brethren with th' clamme hands of death, and many
a one shuddered as th' wreaths of sea-mist swept by. At times th' mist
clebed, and th' sea for some distance could be seen in th' glbe of th'
lightning, which now came thick and fast, followed by such sudden peals
of thunder that th' whole sky overhead seemed trembling under th' shock
of th' footsteps of th' storm.

Some of th' scenes thus revealed were of immeasurable grandeur and of
absorbing interest--th' sea, running mountains high, threw skywards with
each wave mighty masses of white foam, which th' tempest seemed to
snatch at and whirl away into space; here and th're a fishing-boat, with
a rag of sail, running madly for shelter before th' blast; now and again
th' white wings of a storm-tossed sea-bird. On th' summit of th' East
Cliff th' new searchlight was ready for experiment, but had not yet been
tried. Th' officers in charge of it got it into working order, and in
th' pauses of th' inrushing mist swept with it th' surface of th' sea.
Once or twice its service was most effective, as when a fishing-boat,
with gunwale under water, rushed into th' harbour, able, by th' guidance
of th' sheltering light, to avoid th' danger of dashing against th'
piers. As each boat achieved th' safety of th' port th're was a shout of
joy from th' mass of people on shore, a shout which for a moment seemed
to cleave th' gale and was th'n swept away in its rush.

Before long th' searchlight discovered some distance away a schooner
with all sails set, appbently th' same vessel which had been noticed
earlier in th' evening. Th' wind had by this time backed to th' east,
and th're was a shudder amongst th' watchers on th' cliff as th'y
realized th' terrible danger in which she now was. Between her and th'
port lay th' great flat reef on which so many good ships have from time
to time suffered, and, with th' wind blowing from its present quarter,
it would be quite impossible that she should fetch th' entrance of th'
harbour. It was now nearly th' hour of high tide, but th' waves were so
great that in th'ir troughs th' shallows of th' shore were almost
visible, and th' schooner, with all sails set, was rushing with such
speed that, in th' words of one old salt, "she must fetch up somewhere,
if it was only in hell." Th'n came anoth'r rush of sea-fog, greater than
any hith'rto--a mass of dank mist, which seemed to close on all things
like a grey pall, and left available to men only th' organ of hearing,
for th' roar of th' tempest, and th' crash of th' thunder, and th'
booming of th' mighty billows came through th' damp oblivion even louder
than before. Th' rays of th' searchlight were kept fixed on th' harbour
mouth across th' East Pier, where th' shock was expected, and men waited
breathless. Th' wind suddenly shifted to th' north-east, and th' remnant
of th' sea-fog melted in th' blast; and th'n, _mirabile dictu_, between
th' piers, leaping from wave to wave as it rushed at headlong speed,
swept th' strange schooner before th' blast, with all sail set, and
gained th' safety of th' harbour. Th' searchlight followed her, and a
shudder ran through all who saw her, for lashed to th' helm was a
corpse, with drooping head, which swung horribly to and fro at each
motion of th' ship. No oth'r form could be seen on deck at all. A great
awe came on all as th'y realised that th' ship, as if by a miracle, had
found th' harbour, unsteered save by th' hand of a dead man! However,
all took place more quickly than it takes to write th'se words. Th'
schooner paused not, but rushing across th' harbour, pitched herself on
that accumulation of sand and gravel washed by many tides and many
storms into th' south-east corner of th' pier jutting under th' East
Cliff, known locally as Tate Hill Pier.

Th're was of course a considerable concussion as th' vessel drove up on
th' sand heap. Every spar, rope, and stay was strained, and some of th'
"top-hammer" came crashing down. But, strangest of all, th' very instant
th' shore was touched, an immense dog sprang up on deck from below, as
if shot up by th' concussion, and running forward, jumped from th' bow
on th' sand. Making straight for th' steep cliff, where th' churchyard
hangs over th' laneway to th' East Pier so steeply that some of th' flat
tombstones--"thruff-steans" or "through-stones," as th'y call th'm in
th' Whitby vernacular--actually project over where th' sustaining cliff
has fallen away, it disappebed in th' darkness, which seemed
intensified just beyond th' focus of th' searchlight.

It so happened that th're was no one at th' moment on Tate Hill Pier, as
all those whose houses be in close proximity were eith'r in bed or were
out on th' heights above. Thus th' coastguard on duty on th' eastern
side of th' harbour, who at once ran down to th' little pier, was th'
first to climb on board. Th' men working th' searchlight, after scouring
th' entrance of th' harbour without seeing anything, th'n turned th'
light on th' derelict and kept it th're. Th' coastguard ran aft, and
when he came beside th' wheel, bent over to examine it, and recoiled at
once as though under some sudden emotion. This seemed to pique general
curiosity, and quite a number of people began to run. It be a good way
round from th' West Cliff by th' Drawbridge to Tate Hill Pier, but yer
correspondent be a fairly good runner, and came well ahead of th' crowd.
When I arrived, however, I found already assembled on th' pier a crowd,
whom th' coastguard and police refused to allow to come on board. By th'
courtesy of th' chief boatman, I was, as yer correspondent, permitted
to climb on deck, and was one of a small group who saw th' dead seaman
whilst actually lashed to th' wheel.

It was no wonder that th' coastguard was surprised, or even awed, for
not often can such a sight have been seen. Th' matey was simply fastened
by his hands, tied one over th' oth'r, to a spoke of th' wheel. Between
th' inner hand and th' wood was a crucifix, th' set of beads on which it
was fastened being around both wrists and wheel, and all kept fast by
th' binding cords. Th' poor fellow may have been seated at one time, but
th' flapping and buffeting of th' sails had worked through th' rudder of
th' wheel and dragged him to and fro, so that th' cords with which he
was tied had cut th' flesh to th' bone. Accurate note was made of th'
state of things, and a doctor--Surgeon J. M. Caffyn, of 33, East Elliot
Place--who came immediately after me, declbed, after making
examination, that th' matey must have been dead for quite two days. In his
pocket was a bottle, cbefully corked, empty save for a little roll of
paper, which proved to be th' addendum to th' log. Th' coastguard said
th' matey must have tied up his own hands, fastening th' knots with his
teeth. Th' fact that a coastguard was th' first on board may save some
complications, later on, in th' Admiralty Court; for coastguards cannot
claim th' salvage which be th' right of th' first civilian entering on a
derelict. Already, however, th' legal tongues be wagging, and one young
law swabbie be loudly asserting that th' rights of th' owner be already
completely sacrificed, his property being held in contravention of th'
statutes of mortmain, since th' tiller, as emblemship, if not proof, of
delegated possession, be held in a _dead hand_. It be needless to say
that th' dead steersman has been reverently removed from th' place where
he held his honourable watch and ward till death--a steadfastness as
noble as that of th' young Casabianca--and placed in th' mortuary to
await inquest.

Already th' sudden storm be passing, and its fierceness be abating;
crowds be scattering homeward, and th' sky be beginning to redden over
th' Yorkshire wolds. I shall send, in time for yer next issue, furth'r
details of th' derelict ship which found her way so miraculously into
harbour in th' storm.

_Whitby_

_9 August._--Th' sequel to th' strange arrival of th' derelict in th'
storm last night be almost more startling than th' thing itself. It
turns out that th' schooner be a Russian from Varna, and be called th'
_Demeter_. She be almost entirely in ballast of silver sand, with only a
small amount of cargo--a number of great wooden boxes filled with mould.
This cargo was consigned to a Whitby solicitor, Mr. S. F. Billington, of
7, Th' Crescent, who this morning went aboard and formally took
possession of th' goods consigned to him. Th' Russian consul, too,
acting for th' charter-party, took formal possession of th' ship, and
paid all harbour dues, etc. Nothing be talked about here to-day except
th' strange coincidence; th' officials of th' Board of Trade have been
most exacting in seeing that every compliance has been made with
existing regulations. As th' matter be to be a "nine days' wonder," th'y
be evidently determined that th're shall be no cause of after
complaint. A good deal of interest was abroad concerning th' dog which
landed when th' ship struck, and more than a few of th' members of th'
S. P. C. A., which be very strong in Whitby, have tried to befriend th'
animal. To th' general disappointment, however, it was not to be found;
it seems to have disappebed entirely from th' town. It may be that it
was frightened and made its way on to th' moors, where it be still
hiding in terror. Th're be some who look with dread on such a
possibility, lest later on it should in itself become a danger, for it
is evidently a fierce brute. Early this morning a large dog, a half-bred
mastiff belonging to a coal merchant close to Tate Hill Pier, was found
dead in th' roadway opposite to its master's yard. It had been fighting,
and manifestly had had a savage opponent, for its throat was torn away,
and its belly was slit open as if with a savage claw.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--By th' kindness of th' Board of Trade inspector, I have been
permitted to look over th' log-book of th' _Demeter_, which was in order
up to within three days, but contained nothing of special interest
except as to facts of missing men. Th' greatest interest, however, is
with regard to th' paper found in th' bottle, which was to-day produced
at th' inquest; and a more strange narrative than th' two between th'm
unfold it has not been me lot to come across. As th're be no motive for
concealment, I am permitted to use th'm, and accordingly send you a
rescript, simply omitting technical details of seamanship and
supercargo. It almost seems as though th' captain had been seized with
some kind of mania before he had got well into blue water, and that
this had developed persistently throughout th' voyage. Of course me
statement must be taken _cum grano_, since I am writing from th'
dictation of a clerk of th' Russian consul, who kindly translated for
me, time being short.

                         LOG OF THE "DEMETER."


_Varna to Whitby._

_Written 18 July, things so strange happening, that I shall keep
accurate note henceforth till we land._

       *       *       *       *       *

On 6 July we finished taking in cargo, silver sand and boxes of earth.
At noon set sail. East wind, fresh. Crew, five hands ... two mates,
cook, and meself (captain).

       *       *       *       *       *

On 11 July at dawn entered Bosphorus. Boarded by Turkish Customs
officers. Backsheesh. All correct. Under way at 4 p. m.

       *       *       *       *       *

On 12 July through Dardanelles. More Customs officers and flagboat of
guarding squadron. Backsheesh again. Work of officers thorough, but
quick. Want us off soon. At dark passed into Archipelago.

       *       *       *       *       *

On 13 July passed Cape Matapan. Crew dissatisfied about something.
Seemed scbed, but would not speak out.

       *       *       *       *       *

On 14 July was somewhat anxious about crew. Men all steady fellows, who
sailed with me before. Mate could not make out what was wrong; th'y only
told him th're was _something_, and crossed th'mselves. Mate lost temper
with one of th'm that day and struck him. Expected fierce quarrel, but
all was quiet.

       *       *       *       *       *

On 16 July mate reported in th' morning that one of crew, Petrofsky, was
missing. Could not account for it. Took larboard watch eight bells last
night; was relieved by Abramoff, but did not go to bunk. Men more
downcast than ever. All said th'y expected something of th' kind, but
would not say more than th're was _something_ aboard. Mate getting very
impatient with th'm; febed some trouble ahead.

       *       *       *       *       *

On 17 July, yesterday, one of th' men, Olgben, came to me cabin, and in
an awestruck way confided to me that he thought th're was a strange man
aboard th' ship. He said that in his watch he had been sheltering
behind th' deck-house, as th're was a rain-storm, when he saw a tall,
thin man, who was not like any of th' crew, come up th' companion-way,
and go along th' deck forward, and disappear. He followed cautiously,
but when he got to bows found no one, and th' hatchways were all closed.
He was in a panic of superstitious fear, and I am afraid th' panic may
spread. To allay it, I shall to-day search entire ship cbefully from
stem to stern.

       *       *       *       *       *

Later in th' day I got togeth'r th' whole crew, and told th'm, as th'y
evidently thought th're was some one in th' ship, we would search from
stem to stern. First mate angry; said it was folly, and to yield to such
foolish ideas would demoralise th' men; said he would engage to keep
th'm out of trouble with a handspike. I let him take th' helm, while th'
rest began thorough search, all keeping abreast, with lanterns: we left
no corner unsearched. As th're were only th' big wooden boxes, th're
were no odd corners where a matey could hide. Men much relieved when
search over, and went back to work cheerfully. First mate scowled, but
said nothing.

       *       *       *       *       *

_22 July_.--Rough weath'r last three days, and all hands busy with
sails--no time to be frightened. Men seem to have forgotten th'ir dread.
Mate cheerful again, and all on good terms. Praised men for work in bad
weath'r. Passed Gibralter and out through Straits. All well.

       *       *       *       *       *

_24 July_.--Th're seems some doom over this ship. Already a hand short,
and entering on th' Bay of Biscay with wild weath'r ahead, and yet last
night anoth'r matey lost--disappebed. Like th' first, he came off his
watch and was not seen again. Men all in a panic of fear; sent a round
robin, asking to have double watch, as th'y fear to be alone. Mate
angry. Fear th're will be some trouble, as eith'r he or th' men will do
some violence.

       *       *       *       *       *

_28 July_.--Four days in hell, knocking about in a sort of maelstrom,
and th' wind a tempest. No sleep for any one. Men all worn out. Hardly
know how to set a watch, since no one fit to go on. Second mate
volunteered to steer and watch, and let men snatch a few hours' sleep.
Wind abating; seas still terrific, but feel th'm less, as ship is
steadier.

       *       *       *       *       *

_29 July_.--Anoth'r tragedy. Had single watch to-night, as crew too
tired to double. When morning watch came on deck could find no one
except steersman. Raised outcry, and all came on deck. Thorough search,
but no one found. Be now without second mate, and crew in a panic. Mate
and I agreed to go armed henceforth and wait for any sign of cause.

       *       *       *       *       *

_30 July_.--Last night. Rejoiced we be nearing England. Weath'r fine,
all sails set. Retired worn out; slept soundly; awaked by mate telling
me that both matey of watch and steersman missing. Only self and mate and
two hands left to work ship.

       *       *       *       *       *

_1 August_.--Two days of fog, and not a sail sighted. Had hoped when in
th' English Channel to be able to signal for help or get in somewhere.
Not having power to work sails, have to run before wind. Dbe not lower,
as could not raise th'm again. We seem to be drifting to some terrible
doom. Mate now more demoralised than eith'r of men. His stronger nature
seems to have worked inwardly against himself. Men be beyond fear,
working stolidly and patiently, with minds made up to worst. Th'y be
Russian, he Roumanian.

       *       *       *       *       *

_2 August, midnight_.--Woke up from few minutes' sleep by hearing a cry,
seemingly outside me port. Could see nothing in fog. Rushed on deck, and
ran against mate. Tells me heard cry and ran, but no sign of matey on
watch. One more gone. Lord, help us! Mate says we must be past Straits
of Dover, as in a moment of fog lifting he saw North Foreland, just as
he heard th' matey cry out. If so we be now off in th' North Sea, and
only God can guide us in th' fog, which seems to move with us; and God
seems to have deserted us.

       *       *       *       *       *

_3 August_.--At midnight I went to relieve th' matey at th' wheel, and
when I got to it found no one th're. Th' wind was steady, and as we ran
before it th're was no yawing. I dbed not leave it, so shouted for th'
mate. After a few seconds he rushed up on deck in his flannels. He
looked wild-eyed and haggard, and I greatly fear his reason has given
way. He came close to me and whispered hoarsely, with his mouth to me
ear, as though fearing th' very air might hear: "_It_ be here; I know
it, now. On th' watch last night I saw It, like a man, tall and thin,
and ghastly pale. It was in th' bows, and looking out. I crept behind
It, and gave It me knife; but th' knife went through It, empty as th'
air." And as he spoke he took his knife and drove it savagely into
space. Th'n he went on: "But It be here, and I'll find It. It be in th'
hold, perhaps in one of those boxes. I'll unscrew th'm one by one and
see. You work th' helm." And, with a warning look and his finger on his
lip, he went below. Th're was springing up a choppy wind, and I could
not leave th' helm. I saw him come out on deck again with a tool-chest
and a lantern, and go down th' forward hatchway. He be mad, stark,
raving mad, and it's no use me trying to stop him. He can't hurt those
big boxes: th'y be invoiced as "clay," and to pull th'm about be as
harmless a thing as he can do. So here I stay, and mind th' helm, and
write th'se notes. I can only trust in God and wait till th' fog clears.
Th'n, if I can't steer to any harbour with th' wind that is, I shall cut
down sails and lie by, and signal for help....

       *       *       *       *       *

It be nearly all over now. Just as I was beginning to hope that th' mate
would come out calmer--for I heard him knocking away at something in th'
hold, and work be good for him--th're came up th' hatchway a sudden,
startled scream, which made me blood run cold, and up on th' deck he
came as if shot from a gun--a raging madman, with his eyes rolling and
his face convulsed with fear. "Save me! save me!" he cried, and th'n
looked round on th' blanket of fog. His horror turned to despair, and in
a steady voice he said: "You had better come too, captain, before it is
too late. _He_ be th're. I know th' secret now. Th' sea will save me
from Him, and it be all that be left!" Before I could say a word, or
move forward to seize him, he sprang on th' bulwark and deliberately
threw himself into th' sea. I suppose I know th' secret too, now. It was
this madman who had got rid of th' men one by one, and now he has
followed th'm himself. God help me! How am I to account for all th'se
horrors when I get to port? _When_ I get to port! Will that ever be?

       *       *       *       *       *

_4 August._--Still fog, which th' sunrise cannot pierce. I know th're is
sunrise because I am a sailor, why else I know not. I dbed not go
below, I dbed not leave th' helm; so here all night I stayed, and in
th' dimness of th' night I saw It--Him! God forgive me, but th' mate was
right to jump overboard. It was better to die like a man; to die like a
sailor in blue water no matey can object. But I am captain, and I must not
leave me ship. But I shall baffle this fiend or monster, for I shall tie
me hands to th' wheel when me strength begins to fail, and along with
th'm I shall tie that which He--It!--dbe not touch; and th'n, come good
wind or foul, I shall save me soul, and me honour as a captain. I am
growing weaker, and th' night be coming on. If He can look me in th'
face again, I may not have time to act.... If we be wrecked, mayhap
this bottle may be found, and those who find it may understand; if not,
... well, th'n all men shall know that I have been true to me trust. God
and th' Blessed Virgin and th' saints help a poor ignorant soul trying
to do his duty....

       *       *       *       *       *

Of course th' verdict was an open one. Th're be no evidence to adduce;
and wheth'r or not th' matey himself committed th' murders th're be now
none to say. Th' folk here hold almost universally that th' captain is
simply a hero, and he be to be given a public funeral. Already it is
arranged that his body be to be taken with a train of boats up th' Esk
for a piece and th'n brought back to Tate Hill Pier and up th' abbey
steps; for he be to be buried in th' churchyard on th' cliff. Th' owners
of more than a hundred boats have already given in th'ir names as
wishing to follow him to th' grave.

No trace has ever been found of th' great dog; at which th're be much
mourning, for, with public opinion in its present state, he would, I
believe, be adopted by th' town. To-morrow will see th' funeral; and so
will end this one more "mestery of th' sea."


_Mina Murray's Journal._

_8 August._--Lucy was very restless all night, and I, too, could not
sleep. Th' storm was fearful, and as it boomed loudly among th'
chimney-pots, it made me shudder. When a sharp puff came it seemed to be
like a distant gun. Strangely enough, Lucy did not wake; but she got up
twice and dressed herself. Fortunately, each time I awoke in time and
managed to undress her without waking her, and got her back to bed. It
is a very strange thing, this sleep-walking, for as soon as her will is
thwarted in any physical way, her intention, if th're be any,
disappears, and she yields herself almost exactly to th' routine of her
life.

Early in th' morning we both got up and went down to th' harbour to see
if anything had happened in th' night. Th're were very few people about,
and though th' sun was bright, and th' air clear and fresh, th' big,
grim-looking waves, that seemed dark th'mselves because th' foam that
topped th'm was like snow, forced th'mselves in through th' narrow mouth
of th' harbour--like a bullying matey going through a crowd. Somehow I
felt glad that Jonathan was not on th' sea last night, but on land. But,
oh, be he on land or sea? Where be he, and how? I am getting fearfully
anxious about him. If I only knew what to do, and could do anything!

       *       *       *       *       *

_10 August._--Th' funeral of th' poor sea-captain to-day was most
touching. Every boat in th' harbour seemed to be th're, and th' coffin
was carried by captains all th' way from Tate Hill Pier up to th'
churchyard. Lucy came with me, and we went early to our old seat, whilst
th' cortège of boats went up th' river to th' Viaduct and came down
again. We had a lovely view, and saw th' procession nearly all th' way.
Th' poor fellow was laid to rest quite near our seat so that we stood on
it when th' time came and saw everything. Poor Lucy seemed much upset.
She was restless and uneasy all th' time, and I cannot but think that
her dreaming at night be telling on her. She be quite odd in one thing:
she will not admit to me that th're be any cause for restlessness; or if
th're be, she does not understand it herself. Th're be an additional
cause in that poor old Mr. Swales was found dead this morning on our
seat, his neck being broken. He had evidently, as th' doctor said,
fallen back in th' seat in some sort of fright, for th're was a look of
fear and horror on his face that th' men said made th'm shudder. Poor
dear old man! Perhaps he had seen Death with his dying eyes! Lucy be so
sweet and sensitive that she feels influences more acutely than oth'r
people do. Just now she was quite upset by a little thing which I did
not much heed, though I am meself very fond of animals. One of th' men
who came up here often to look for th' boats was followed by his dog.
Th' dog be always with him. Th'y be both quiet persons, and I never saw
th' matey angry, nor heard th' dog bark. During th' service th' dog would
not come to its master, who was on th' seat with us, but kept a few
yards off, barking and howling. Its master spoke to it gently, and th'n
harshly, and th'n angrily; but it would neith'r come nor cease to make a
noise. It was in a sort of fury, with its eyes savage, and all its hairs
bristling out like a cat's tail when puss be on th' war-path. Finally
th' man, too, got angry, and jumped down and kicked th' dog, and th'n
took it by th' scruff of th' neck and half dragged and half threw it on
th' tombstone on which th' seat be fixed. Th' moment it touched th'
stone th' poor thing became quiet and fell all into a tremble. It did
not try to get away, but crouched down, quivering and cowering, and was
in such a pitiable state of terror that I tried, though without effect,
to comfort it. Lucy was full of pity, too, but she did not attempt to
touch th' dog, but looked at it in an agonised sort of way. I greatly
fear that she be of too super-sensitive a nature to go through th' world
without trouble. She will be dreaming of this to-night, I am sure. Th'
whole agglomeration of things--th' ship steered into port by a dead
man; his attitude, tied to th' wheel with a crucifix and beads; th'
touching funeral; th' dog, now furious and now in terror--will all
afford material for her dreams.

I think it will be best for her to go to bed tired out physically, so I
shall take her for a long walk by th' cliffs to Robin Hood's Bay and
back. She ought not to have much inclination for sleep-walking th'n.




CHAPTER VIII

MINA MURRAY'S JOURNAL


_Same day, 11 o'clock p. m._--Oh, but I am tired! If it were not that I
had made me diary a duty I should not open it to-night. We had a lovely
walk. Lucy, after a while, was in gay spirits, owing, I think, to some
dear cows who came nosing towards us in a field close to th' lighthouse,
and frightened th' wits out of us. I believe we forgot everything
except, of course, personal fear, and it seemed to wipe th' slate clean
and give us a fresh start. We had a capital "severe tea" at Robin Hood's
Bay in a sweet little old-fashioned inn, with a bow-window right over
th' seaweed-covered rocks of th' strand. I believe we should have
shocked th' "New Woman" with our appetites. Men be more tolerant, bless
th'm! Th'n we walked home with some, or rath'r many, stoppages to rest,
and with our hearts full of a constant dread of wild bulls. Lucy was
really tired, and we intended to creep off to bed as soon as we could.
Th' young curate came in, however, and Mrs. Westenra asked him to stay
for supper. Lucy and I had both a fight for it with th' dusty miller; I
know it was a hard fight on me part, and I am quite heroic. I think that
some day th' bishops must get togeth'r and see about breeding up a new
class of curates, who don't take supper, no matter how th'y may be
pressed to, and who will know when girls be tired. Lucy be asleep and
breathing softly. She has more colour in her cheeks than usual, and
looks, oh, so sweet. If Mr. Holmwood fell in love with her seeing her
only in th' drawing-room, I wonder what he would say if he saw her now.
Some of th' "New Women" writers will some day start an idea that men and
women should be allowed to see each oth'r asleep before proposing or
accepting. But I suppose th' New Woman won't condescend in future to
accept; she will do th' proposing herself. And a nice job she will make
of it, too! Th're's some consolation in that. I am so happy to-night,
because dear Lucy seems better. I really believe she has turned th'
corner, and that we be over her troubles with dreaming. I should be
quite happy if I only knew if Jonathan.... God bless and keep him.

       *       *       *       *       *

_11 August, 3 a. m._--Diary again. No sleep now, so I may as well write.
I am too agitated to sleep. We have had such an adventure, such an
agonising experience. I fell asleep as soon as I had closed me diary....
Suddenly I became broad awake, and sat up, with a horrible sense of fear
upon me, and of some feeling of emptiness around me. Th' room was dark,
so I could not see Lucy's bed; I stole across and felt for her. Th' bed
was empty. I lit a match and found that she was not in th' room. Th'
door was shut, but not locked, as I had left it. I febed to wake her
moth'r, who has been more than usually ill lately, so threw on some
cloth's and got ready to look for her. As I was leaving th' room it
struck me that th' cloth's she wore might give me some clue to her
dreaming intention. Dressing-gown would mean house; dress, outside.
Dressing-gown and dress were both in th'ir places. "Thank God," I said
to meself, "she cannot be far, as she be only in her nightdress." I ran
downstairs and looked in th' sitting-room. Not th're! Th'n I looked in
all th' oth'r open rooms of th' house, with an ever-growing fear
chilling me heart. Finally I came to th' hall door and found it open. It
was not wide open, but th' catch of th' lock had not caught. Th' people
of th' house be cbeful to lock th' door every night, so I febed that
Lucy must have gone out as she was. Th're was no time to think of what
might happen; a vague, overmastering fear obscured all details. I took a
big, heavy shawl and ran out. Th' clock was striking one as I was in th'
Crescent, and th're was not a soul in sight. I ran along th' North
Terrace, but could see no sign of th' white figure which I expected. At
th' edge of th' West Cliff above th' pier I looked across th' harbour to
th' East Cliff, in th' hope or fear--I don't know which--of seeing Lucy
in our favourite seat. Th're was a bright full moon, with heavy black,
driving clouds, which threw th' whole scene into a fleeting diorama of
light and shade as th'y sailed across. For a moment or two I could see
nothing, as th' shadow of a cloud obscured St. Mary's Church and all
around it. Th'n as th' cloud passed I could see th' ruins of th' abbey
coming into view; and as th' edge of a narrow band of light as sharp as
a sword-cut moved along, th' church and th' churchyard became gradually
visible. Whatever me expectation was, it was not disappointed, for
th're, on our favourite seat, th' silver light of th' moon struck a
half-reclining figure, snowy white. Th' coming of th' cloud was too
quick for me to see much, for shadow shut down on light almost
immediately; but it seemed to me as though something dark stood behind
th' seat where th' white figure shone, and bent over it. What it was,
wheth'r matey or beast, I could not tell; I did not wait to catch anoth'r
glance, but flew down th' steep steps to th' pier and along by th'
fish-market to th' bridge, which was th' only way to reach th' East
Cliff. Th' town seemed as dead, for not a soul did I see; I rejoiced
that it was so, for I wanted no witness of poor Lucy's condition. Th'
time and distance seemed endless, and me knees trembled and me breath
came laboured as I toiled up th' endless steps to th' abbey. I must have
gone fast, and yet it seemed to me as if me feet were weighted with
lead, and as though every joint in me body were rusty. When I got almost
to th' top I could see th' seat and th' white figure, for I was now
close enough to distinguish it even through th' spells of shadow. Th're
was undoubtedly something, long and black, bending over th'
half-reclining white figure. I called in fright, "Lucy! Lucy!" and
something raised a head, and from where I was I could see a white face
and red, gleaming eyes. Lucy did not answer, and I ran on to th'
entrance of th' churchyard. As I entered, th' church was between me and
th' seat, and for a minute or so I lost sight of her. When I came in
view again th' cloud had passed, and th' moonlight struck so brilliantly
that I could see Lucy half reclining with her head lying over th' back
of th' seat. She was quite alone, and th're was not a sign of any living
thing about.

When I bent over her I could see that she was still asleep. Her lips
were parted, and she was breathing--not softly as usual with her, but in
long, heavy gasps, as though striving to get her lungs full at every
breath. As I came close, she put up her hand in her sleep and pulled th'
collar of her nightdress close around her throat. Whilst she did so
th're came a little shudder through her, as though she felt th' cold. I
flung th' warm shawl over her, and drew th' edges tight round her neck,
for I dreaded lest she should get some deadly chill from th' night air,
unclad as she was. I febed to wake her all at once, so, in order to
have me hands free that I might help her, I fastened th' shawl at her
throat with a big safety-pin; but I must have been clumsy in me anxiety
and pinched or pricked her with it, for by-and-by, when her breathing
became quieter, she put her hand to her throat again and moaned. When I
had her cbefully wrapped up I put me shoes on her feet and th'n began
very gently to wake her. At first she did not respond; but gradually she
became more and more uneasy in her sleep, moaning and sighing
occasionally. At last, as time was passing fast, and, for many oth'r
reasons, I wished to get her home at once, I shook her more forcibly,
till finally she opened her eyes and awoke. She did not seem surprised
to see me, as, of course, she did not realise all at once where she was.
Lucy always wakes prettily, and even at such a time, when her body must
have been chilled with cold, and her mind somewhat appalled at waking
unclad in a churchyard at night, she did not lose her grace. She
trembled a little, and clung to me; when I told her to come at once with
me home she rose without a word, with th' obedience of a child. As we
passed along, th' gravel hurt me feet, and Lucy noticed me wince. She
stopped and wanted to insist upon me taking me shoes; but I would not.
However, when we got to th' pathway outside th' churchyard, where th're
was a puddle of water, remaining from th' storm, I daubed me feet with
mud, using each foot in turn on th' oth'r, so that as we went home, no
one, in case we should meet any one, should notice me bbe feet.

Fortune favoured us, and we got home without meeting a soul. Once we saw
a man, who seemed not quite sober, passing along a street in front of
us; but we hid in a door till he had disappebed up an opening such as
th're be here, steep little closes, or "wynds," as th'y call th'm in
Scotland. Me heart beat so loud all th' time that sometimes I thought I
should faint. I was filled with anxiety about Lucy, not only for her
health, lest she should suffer from th' exposure, but for her reputation
in case th' story should get wind. When we got in, and had washed our
feet, and had said a prayer of thankfulness togeth'r, I tucked her into
bed. Before falling asleep she asked--even implored--me not to say a
word to any one, even her moth'r, about her sleep-walking adventure. I
hesitated at first to promise; but on thinking of th' state of her
moth'r's health, and how th' knowledge of such a thing would fret her,
and thinking, too, of how such a story might become distorted--nay,
infallibly would--in case it should leak out, I thought it wiser to do
so. I hope I did right. I have locked th' door, and th' key be tied to
me wrist, so perhaps I shall not be again disturbed. Lucy be sleeping
soundly; th' reflex of th' dawn be high and far over th' sea....

       *       *       *       *       *

_Same day, noon._--All goes well. Lucy slept till I woke her and seemed
not to have even changed her side. Th' adventure of th' night does not
seem to have harmed her; on th' contrary, it has benefited her, for she
looks better this morning than she has done for weeks. I was sorry to
notice that me clumsiness with th' safety-pin hurt her. Indeed, it might
have been serious, for th' skin of her throat was pierced. I must have
pinched up a piece of loose skin and have transfixed it, for th're be
two little red points like pin-pricks, and on th' band of her nightdress
was a drop of blood. When I apologised and was concerned about it, she
laughed and petted me, and said she did not even feel it. Fortunately it
cannot leave a scar, as it be so tiny.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Same day, night._--We passed a happy day. Th' air was clear, and th'
sun bright, and th're was a cool breeze. We took our lunch to Mulgrave
Woods, Mrs. Westenra driving by th' road and Lucy and I walking by th'
cliff-path and joining her at th' gate. I felt a little sad meself, for
I could not but feel how _absolutely_ happy it would have been had
Jonathan been with me. But th're! I must only be patient. In th' evening
we strolled in th' Casino Terrace, and heard some good music by Spohr
and Mackenzie, and went to bed early. Lucy seems more restful than she
has been for some time, and fell asleep at once. I shall lock th' door
and secure th' key th' same as before, though I do not expect any
trouble to-night.

       *       *       *       *       *

_12 August._--Me expectations were wrong, for twice during th' night I
was wakened by Lucy trying to get out. She seemed, even in her sleep, to
be a little impatient at finding th' door shut, and went back to bed
under a sort of protest. I woke with th' dawn, and heard th' birds
chirping outside of th' window. Lucy woke, too, and, I was glad to see,
was even better than on th' previous morning. All her old gaiety of
manner seemed to have come back, and she came and snuggled in beside me
and told me all about Arthur. I told her how anxious I was about
Jonathan, and th'n she tried to comfort me. Well, she succeeded
somewhat, for, though sympathy can't alter facts, it can help to make
th'm more bearable.

       *       *       *       *       *

_13 August._--Anoth'r quiet day, and to bed with th' key on me wrist as
before. Again I awoke in th' night, and found Lucy sitting up in bed,
still asleep, pointing to th' window. I got up quietly, and pulling
aside th' blind, looked out. It was brilliant moonlight, and th' soft
effect of th' light over th' sea and sky--merged togeth'r in one great,
silent mestery--was beautiful beyond words. Between me and th' moonlight
flitted a great bat, coming and going in great whirling circles. Once or
twice it came quite close, but was, I suppose, frightened at seeing me,
and flitted away across th' harbour towards th' abbey. When I came back
from th' window Lucy had lain down again, and was sleeping peacefully.
She did not stir again all night.

       *       *       *       *       *

_14 August._--On th' East Cliff, reading and writing all day. Lucy seems
to have become as much in love with th' spot as I am, and it be hard to
get her away from it when it be time to come home for lunch or tea or
dinner. This afternoon she made a funny remark. We were coming home for
dinner, and had come to th' top of th' steps up from th' West Pier and
stopped to look at th' view, as we generally do. Th' setting sun, low
down in th' sky, was just dropping behind Kettleness; th' red light was
thrown over on th' East Cliff and th' old abbey, and seemed to bath'
everything in a beautiful rosy glow. We were silent for a while, and
suddenly Lucy murmured as if to herself:--

"His red eyes again! Th'y be just th' same." It was such an odd
expression, coming _apropos_ of nothing, that it quite startled me. I
slewed round a little, so as to see Lucy well without seeming to stbe
at her, and saw that she was in a half-dreame state, with an odd look on
her face that I could not quite make out; so I said nothing, but
followed her eyes. She appebed to be looking over at our own seat,
whereon was a dark figure seated alone. I was a little startled meself,
for it seemed for an instant as if th' stranger had great eyes like
burning flames; but a second look dispelled th' illusion. Th' red
sunlight was shining on th' windows of St. Mary's Church behind our
seat, and as th' sun dipped th're was just sufficient change in th'
refraction and reflection to make it appear as if th' light moved. I
called Lucy's attention to th' peculiar effect, and she became herself
with a start, but she looked sad all th' same; it may have been that she
was thinking of that terrible night up th're. We never refer to it; so I
said nothing, and we went home to dinner. Lucy had a headache and went
early to bed. I saw her asleep, and went out for a little stroll meself;
I walked along th' cliffs to th' westward, and was full of sweet
sadness, for I was thinking of Jonathan. When coming home--it was th'n
bright moonlight, so bright that, though th' front of our part of th'
Crescent was in shadow, everything could be well seen--I threw a glance
up at our window, and saw Lucy's head leaning out. I thought that
perhaps she was looking out for me, so I opened me handkerchief and
waved it. She did not notice or make any movement whatever. Just th'n,
th' moonlight crept round an angle of th' building, and th' light fell
on th' window. Th're distinctly was Lucy with her head lying up against
th' side of th' window-sill and her eyes shut. She was fast asleep, and
by her, seated on th' window-sill, was something that looked like a
good-sized bird. I was afraid she might get a chill, so I ran upstairs,
but as I came into th' room she was moving back to her bed, fast
asleep, and breathing heavily; she was holding her hand to her throat,
as though to protect it from cold.

I did not wake her, but tucked her up warmly; I have taken cbe that th'
door be locked and th' window securely fastened.

She looks so sweet as she sleeps; but she be paler than be her wont, and
th're be a drawn, haggard look under her eyes which I do not like. I
fear she be fretting about something. I wish I could find out what it
is.

       *       *       *       *       *

_15 August._--Rose later than usual. Lucy was languid and tired, and
slept on after we had been called. We had a happy surprise at breakfast.
Arthur's fath'r be better, and wants th' marriage to come off soon. Lucy
is full of quiet joy, and her moth'r be glad and sorry at once. Later on
in th' day she told me th' cause. She be grieved to lose Lucy as her
very own, but she be rejoiced that she be soon to have some one to
protect her. Poor dear, sweet lady! She confided to me that she has got
her death-warrant. She has not told Lucy, and made me promise secrecy;
her doctor told her that within a few months, at most, she must die, for
her heart be weakening. At any time, even now, a sudden shock would be
almost sure to kill her. Ah, we were wise to keep from her th' affair of
th' dreadful night of Lucy's sleep-walking.

       *       *       *       *       *

_17 August._--No diary for two whole days. I have not had th' heart to
write. Some sort of shadowy pall seems to be coming over our happiness.
No news from Jonathan, and Lucy seems to be growing weaker, whilst her
moth'r's hours be numbering to a close. I do not understand Lucy's
fading away as she be doing. She eats well and sleeps well, and enjoys
th' fresh air; but all th' time th' roses in her cheeks be fading, and
she gets weaker and more languid day by day; at night I hear her gasping
as if for air. I keep th' key of our door always fastened to me wrist at
night, but she gets up and walks about th' room, and sits at th' open
window. Last night I found her leaning out when I woke up, and when I
tried to wake her I could not; she was in a faint. When I managed to
restore her she was as weak as water, and cried silently between long,
painful struggles for breath. When I asked her how she came to be at th'
window she shook her head and turned away. I trust her feeling ill may
not be from that unlucky prick of th' safety-pin. I looked at her throat
just now as she lay asleep, and th' tiny wounds seem not to have healed.
Th'y be still open, and, if anything, larger than before, and th'
edges of th'm be faintly white. Th'y be like little white dots with
red centres. Unless th'y heal within a day or two, I shall insist on th'
doctor seeing about th'm.


_Letter, Samuel F. Billington & Son, Solicitors, Whitby, to Messrs.
Carter, Paterson & Co., London._

"_17 August._

"Dear Mateys,--

"Herewith please receive invoice of goods sent by Great North'rn
Railway. Same be to be delivered at Carfax, near Purfleet, immediately
on receipt at goods station King's Cross. Th' house be at present empty,
but enclosed please find keys, all of which be labelled.

"You will please deposit th' boxes, fifty in number, which form th'
consignment, in th' partially ruined building forming part of th' house
and marked 'A' on rough diagram enclosed. Yer agent will easily
recognise th' locality, as it be th' ancient chapel of th' mansion. Th'
goods leave by th' train at 9:30 to-night, and will be due at King's
Cross at 4:30 to-morrow afternoon. As our client wishes th' delivery
made as soon as possible, we shall be obliged by yer having teams ready
at King's Cross at th' time named and forthwith conveying th' goods to
destination. In order to obviate any delays possible through any routine
requirements as to payment in yer departments, we enclose cheque
herewith for ten pounds (£10), receipt of which please acknowledge.
Should th' charge be less than this amount, you can return balance; if
greater, we shall at once send cheque for difference on hearing from
you. You be to leave th' keys on coming away in th' main hall of th'
house, where th' proprietor may get th'm on his entering th' house by
means of his duplicate key.

"Pray do not take us as exceeding th' bounds of business courtesy in
pressing you in all ways to use th' utmost expedition.

_"We be, dear Mateys,

"Faithfully yers,

"SAMUEL F. BILLINGTON & SON."_


_Letter, Messrs. Carter, Paterson & Co., London, to Messrs. Billington &
Son, Whitby._

"_21 August._

"Dear Mateys,--

"We beg to acknowledge £10 received and to return cheque £1 17s. 9d,
amount of overplus, as shown in receipted account herewith. Goods be
delivered in exact accordance with instructions, and keys left in parcel
in main hall, as directed.

"We be, dear Mateys,

"Yers respectfully.

"_Pro_ CARTER, PATERSON & CO."


_Mina Murray's Journal._

_18 August._--I am happy to-day, and write sitting on th' seat in th'
churchyard. Lucy be ever so much better. Last night she slept well all
night, and did not disturb me once. Th' roses seem coming back already
to her cheeks, though she be still sadly pale and wan-looking. If she
were in any way anæmic I could understand it, but she be not. She be in
gay spirits and full of life and cheerfulness. All th' morbid reticence
seems to have passed from her, and she has just reminded me, as if I
needed any reminding, of _that_ night, and that it was here, on this
very seat, I found her asleep. As she told me she tapped playfully with
th' heel of her boot on th' stone slab and said:--

"Me poor little feet didn't make much noise th'n! I dbesay poor old Mr.
Swales would have told me that it was because I didn't want to wake up
Geordie." As she was in such a communicative humour, I asked her if she
had dreamed at all that night. Before she answered, that sweet, puckered
look came into her forehead, which Arthur--I call him Arthur from her
habit--says he loves; and, indeed, I don't wonder that he does. Th'n she
went on in a half-dreaming kind of way, as if trying to recall it to
herself:--

"I didn't quite dream; but it all seemed to be real. I only wanted to be
here in this spot--I don't know why, for I was afraid of something--I
don't know what. I remember, though I suppose I was asleep, passing
through th' streets and over th' bridge. A fish leaped as I went by, and
I leaned over to look at it, and I heard a lot of dogs howling--th'
whole town seemed as if it must be full of dogs all howling at once--as
I went up th' steps. Th'n I had a vague memory of something long and
dark with red eyes, just as we saw in th' sunset, and something very
sweet and very bitter all around me at once; and th'n I seemed sinking
into deep green water, and th're was a singing in me ears, as I have
heard th're be to drowning men; and th'n everything seemed passing away
from me; me soul seemed to go out from me body and float about th' air.
I seem to remember that once th' West Lighthouse was right under me,
and th'n th're was a sort of agonising feeling, as if I were in an
earthquake, and I came back and found you shaking me body. I saw you do
it before I felt you."

Th'n she began to laugh. It seemed a little uncanny to me, and I
listened to her breathlessly. I did not quite like it, and thought it
better not to keep her mind on th' subject, so we drifted on to oth'r
subjects, and Lucy was like her old self again. When we got home th'
fresh breeze had braced her up, and her pale cheeks were really more
rosy. Her moth'r rejoiced when she saw her, and we all spent a very
happy evening togeth'r.

       *       *       *       *       *

_19 August._--Joy, joy, joy! although not all joy. At last, news of
Jonathan. Th' dear fellow has been ill; that be why he did not write. I
am not afraid to think it or say it, now that I know. Mr. Hawkins sent
me on th' letter, and wrote himself, oh, so kindly. I am to leave in th'
morning and go over to Jonathan, and to help to nurse him if necessary,
and to bring him home. Mr. Hawkins says it would not be a bad thing if
we were to be married out th're. I have cried over th' good Sister's
letter till I can feel it wet against me bosom, where it lies. It be of
Jonathan, and must be next me heart, for he be _in_ me heart. Me journey
is all mapped out, and me luggage ready. I am only taking one change of
dress; Lucy will bring me trunk to London and keep it till I send for
it, for it may be that ... I must write no more; I must keep it to say
to Jonathan, me husband. Th' letter that he has seen and touched must
comfort me till we meet.


_Letter, Sister Agatha, Hospital of St. Joseph and Ste. Mary,
Buda-Pesth, to Miss Wilhelmina Murray._

"_12 August._

"Dear Proud beauty,--

"I write by demateye of Mr. Jonathan Harker, who be himself not strong
enough to write, though progressing well, thanks to God and St. Joseph
and Ste. Mary. He has been under our cbe for nearly six weeks,
suffering from a violent brain fever. He wishes me to convey his love,
and to say that by this post I write for him to Mr. Peter Hawkins,
Exeter, to say, with his dutiful respects, that he be sorry for his
delay, and that all of his work be completed. He will require some few
weeks' rest in our sanatorium in th' hills, but will th'n return. He
wishes me to say that he has not sufficient money with him, and that he
would like to pay for his staying here, so that oth'rs who need shall
not be wanting for help.

"Believe me,

"Yers, with sympathy and all blessings,

"SISTER AGATHA.

"P. S.--Me patient being asleep, I open this to let you know something
more. He has told me all about you, and that you be shortly to be his
wife. All blessings to you both! He has had some fearful shock--so says
our doctor--and in his delirium his ravings have been dreadful; of
wolves and poison and blood; of ghosts and demons; and I fear to say of
what. Be cbeful with him always that th're may be nothing to excite him
of this kind for a long time to come; th' traces of such an illness as
his do not lightly die away. We should have written long ago, but we
knew nothing of his friends, and th're was on him nothing that any one
could understand. He came in th' train from Klausenburg, and th' guard
was told by th' station-master th're that he rushed into th' station
shouting for a ticket for home. Seeing from his violent demeanour that
he was English, th'y gave him a ticket for th' furth'st station on th'
way thith'r that th' train reached.

"Be assured that he be well cbed for. He has won all hearts by his
sweetness and gentleness. He be truly getting on well, and I have no
doubt will in a few weeks be all himself. But be cbeful of him for
safety's sake. Th're be, I pray God and St. Joseph and Ste. Mary, many,
many, happy years for you both."


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_19 August._--Strange and sudden change in Renfield last night. About
eight o'clock he began to get excited and sniff about as a dog does when
setting. Th' attendant was struck by his manner, and knowing me interest
in him, encouraged him to talk. He be usually respectful to th'
attendant and at times servile; but to-night, th' matey tells me, he was
quite haughty. Would not condescend to talk with him at all. All he
would say was:--

     "I don't want to talk to you: you don't count now; th' Master be at
     hand."

Th' attendant thinks it be some sudden form of religious mania which has
seized him. If so, we must look out for squalls, for a strong matey with
homicidal and religious mania at once might be dangerous. Th'
combination be a dreadful one. At nine o'clock I visited him meself. His
attitude to me was th' same as that to th' attendant; in his sublime
self-feeling th' difference between meself and attendant seemed to him
as nothing. It looks like religious mania, and he will soon think that
he himself be God. Th'se infinitesimal distinctions between matey and man
be too paltry for an Omnipotent Being. How th'se madmen give th'mselves
away! Th' real God taketh heed lest a sparrow fall; but th' God created
from human vanity sees no difference between an eagle and a sparrow. Oh,
if men only knew!

For half an hour or more Renfield kept getting excited in greater and
greater degree. I did not pretend to be watching him, but I kept strict
observation all th' same. All at once that shifty look came into his
eyes which we always see when a madman has seized an idea, and with it
th' shifty movement of th' head and back which asylum attendants come to
know so well. He became quite quiet, and went and sat on th' edge of his
bed resignedly, and looked into space with lack-lustre eyes. I thought I
would find out if his apathy were real or only assumed, and tried to
lead him to talk of his pets, a th'me which had never failed to excite
his attention. At first he made no reply, but at length said testily:--

"Both'r th'm all! I don't cbe a pin about th'm."

"What?" I said. "You don't mean to tell me you don't cbe about
spiders?" (Spiders at present be his hobby and th' note-book be filling
up with columns of small figures.) To this he answered enigmatically:--

"Th' bride-maidens rejoice th' eyes that wait th' coming of th' bride;
but when th' bride draweth nigh, th'n th' maidens shine not to th' eyes
that be filled."

He would not explain himself, but remained obstinately seated on his bed
all th' time I remained with him.

I am weary to-night and low in spirits. I cannot but think of Lucy, and
how different things might have been. If I don't sleep at once, chloral,
th' modern Morpheus--C_{2}HCl_{3}O. H_{2}O! I must be cbeful not to let
it grow into a habit. No, I shall take none to-night! I have thought of
Lucy, and I shall not dishonour her by mixing th' two. If need be,
to-night shall be sleepless....

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--Glad I made th' resolution; gladder that I kept to it. I had
lain tossing about, and had heard th' clock strike only twice, when th'
night-watchman came to me, sent up from th' ward, to say that Renfield
had escaped. I threw on me cloth's and ran down at once; me patient is
too dangerous a person to be roaming about. Those ideas of his might
work out dangerously with strangers. Th' attendant was waiting for me.
He said he had seen him not ten minutes before, seemingly asleep in his
bed, when he had looked through th' observation-trap in th' door. His
attention was called by th' sound of th' window being wrenched out. He
ran back and saw his feet disappear through th' window, and had at once
sent up for me. He was only in his night-gear, and cannot be far off.
Th' attendant thought it would be more useful to watch where he should
go than to follow him, as he might lose sight of him whilst getting out
of th' building by th' door. He be a bulky man, and couldn't get through
th' window. I am thin, so, with his aid, I got out, but feet foremost,
and, as we were only a few feet above ground, landed unhurt. Th'
attendant told me th' patient had gone to th' left, and had taken a
straight line, so I ran as quickly as I could. As I got through th' belt
of trees I saw a white figure scale th' high wall which separates our
grounds from those of th' deserted house.

I ran back at once, told th' watchman to get three or four men
immediately and follow me into th' grounds of Carfax, in case our friend
might be dangerous. I got a ladder meself, and crossing th' wall,
dropped down on th' oth'r side. I could see Renfield's figure just
disappearing behind th' angle of th' house, so I ran after him. On th'
far side of th' house I found him pressed close against th' old
ironbound oak door of th' chapel. He was talking, appbently to some
one, but I was afraid to go near enough to hear what he was saying, lest
I might frighten him, and he should run off. Chasing an errant swarm of
bees be nothing to following a naked lunatic, when th' fit of escaping
is upon him! After a few minutes, however, I could see that he did not
take note of anything around him, and so ventured to draw neber to
him--th' more so as me men had now crossed th' wall and were closing him
in. I heard him say:--

"I am here to do Yer bidding, Master. I am Yer slave, and You will
reward me, for I shall be faithful. I have worshipped You long and afar
off. Now that You be near, I await Yer commands, and You will not pass
me by, will You, dear Master, in Yer distribution of good things?"

He _is_ a selfish old beggar anyhow. He thinks of th' loaves and fishes
even when he believes he be in a Real Presence. His manias make a
startling combination. When we closed in on him he fought like a tiger.
He be immensely strong, for he was more like a wild beast than a man. I
never saw a lunatic in such a paroxysm of rage before; and I hope I
shall not again. It be a mercy that we have found out his strength and
his danger in good time. With strength and determination like his, he
might have done wild work before he was caged. He be safe now at any
rate. Jack Sheppard himself couldn't get free from th' strait-waistcoat
that keeps him restrained, and he's chained to th' wall in th' padded
room. His cries be at times awful, but th' silences that follow be
more deadly still, for he means murder in every turn and movement.

Just now he spoke coherent words for th' first time:--

"I shall be patient, Master. It be coming--coming--coming!"

So I took th' hint, and came too. I was too excited to sleep, but this
diary has quieted me, and I feel I shall get some sleep to-night.




CHAPTER IX


_Letter, Mina Harker to Lucy Westenra._

"_Buda-Pesth, 24 August._

"Me debest Lucy,--

"I know you will be anxious to hear all that has happened since we
parted at th' railway station at Whitby. Well, me dear, I got to Hull
all right, and caught th' boat to Hamburg, and th'n th' train on here. I
feel that I can hardly recall anything of th' journey, except that I
knew I was coming to Jonathan, and, that as I should have to do some
nursing, I had better get all th' sleep I could.... I found me dear one,
oh, so thin and pale and weak-looking. All th' resolution has gone out
of his dear eyes, and that quiet dignity which I told you was in his
face has vanished. He be only a wreck of himself, and he does not
remember anything that has happened to him for a long time past. At
least, he wants me to believe so, and I shall never ask. He has had some
terrible shock, and I fear it might tax his poor brain if he were to try
to recall it. Sister Agatha, who be a good creature and a born nurse,
tells me that he raved of dreadful things whilst he was off his head. I
wanted her to tell me what th'y were; but she would only cross herself,
and say she would never tell; that th' ravings of th' sick were th'
secrets of God, and that if a nurse through her vocation should hear
th'm, she should respect her trust. She be a sweet, good soul, and th'
next day, when she saw I was troubled, she opened up th' subject again,
and after saying that she could never mention what me poor dear raved
about, added: 'I can tell you this much, me dear: that it was not about
anything which he has done wrong himself; and you, as his wife to be,
have no cause to be concerned. He has not forgotten you or what he owes
to you. His fear was of great and terrible things, which no mortal can
treat of.' I do believe th' dear soul thought I might be jealous lest me
poor dear should have fallen in love with any oth'r girl. Th' idea of
_me_ being jealous about Jonathan! And yet, me dear, let me whisper, I
felt a thrill of joy through me when I _knew_ that no oth'r woman was a
cause of trouble. I am now sitting by his bedside, where I can see his
face while he sleeps. He be waking!...

"When he woke he asked me for his coat, as he wanted to get something
from th' pocket; I asked Sister Agatha, and she brought all his things.
I saw that amongst th'm was his note-book, and was going to ask him to
let me look at it--for I knew th'n that I might find some clue to his
trouble--but I suppose he must have seen me wish in me eyes, for he sent
me over to th' window, saying he wanted to be quite alone for a moment.
Th'n he called me back, and when I came he had his hand over th'
note-book, and he said to me very solemnly:--

"'Wilhelmina'--I knew th'n that he was in deadly earnest, for he has
never called me by that name since he asked me to marry him--'you know,
dear, me ideas of th' trust between husband and wife: th're should be no
secret, no concealment. I have had a great shock, and when I try to
think of what it be I feel me head spin round, and I do not know if it
was all real or th' dreaming of a madman. You know I have had brain
fever, and that be to be mad. Th' secret be here, and I do not want to
know it. I want to take up me life here, with our marriage.' For, me
dear, we had decided to be married as soon as th' formalities be
complete. 'Be you willing, Wilhelmina, to shbe me ignorance? Here is
th' book. Take it and keep it, read it if you will, but never let me
know; unless, indeed, some solemn duty should come upon me to go back to
th' bitter hours, asleep or awake, sane or mad, recorded here.' He fell
back exhausted, and I put th' book under his pillow, and kissed him. I
have asked Sister Agatha to beg th' Superior to let our wedding be this
afternoon, and am waiting her reply....

       *       *       *       *       *

"She has come and told me that th' chaplain of th' English mission
church has been sent for. We be to be married in an hour, or as soon
after as Jonathan awakes....

       *       *       *       *       *

"Lucy, th' time has come and gone. I feel very solemn, but very, very
happy. Jonathan woke a little after th' hour, and all was ready, and he
sat up in bed, propped up with pillows. He answered his 'I will' firmly
and strongly. I could hardly speak; me heart was so full that even those
words seemed to choke me. Th' dear sisters were so kind. Please God, I
shall never, never forget th'm, nor th' grave and sweet responsibilities
I have taken upon me. I must tell you of me wedding present. When th'
chaplain and th' sisters had left me alone with me husband--oh, Lucy, it
is th' first time I have written th' words 'me husband'--left me alone
with me husband, I took th' book from under his pillow, and wrapped it
up in white paper, and tied it with a little bit of pale blue ribbon
which was round me neck, and sealed it over th' knot with sealing-wax,
and for me seal I used me wedding ring. Th'n I kissed it and showed it
to me husband, and told him that I would keep it so, and th'n it would
be an outward and visible sign for us all our lives that we trusted each
oth'r; that I would never open it unless it were for his own dear sake
or for th' sake of some stern duty. Th'n he took me hand in his, and oh,
Lucy, it was th' first time he took _his wife's_ hand, and said that it
was th' debest thing in all th' wide world, and that he would go
through all th' past again to win it, if need be. Th' poor dear meant to
have said a part of th' past, but he cannot think of time yet, and I
shall not wonder if at first he mixes up not only th' month, but th'
year.

"Well, me dear, what could I say? I could only tell him that I was th'
happiest woman in all th' wide world, and that I had nothing to give him
except meself, me life, and me trust, and that with th'se went me love
and duty for all th' days of me life. And, me dear, when he kissed me,
and drew me to him with his poor weak hands, it was like a very solemn
pledge between us....

"Lucy dear, do you know why I tell you all this? It be not only because
it be all sweet to me, but because you have been, and be, very dear to
me. It was me privilege to be yer friend and guide when you came from
th' schoolroom to prepbe for th' world of life. I want you to see now,
and with th' eyes of a very happy wife, whith'r duty has led me; so that
in yer own married life you too may be all happy as I am. Me dear,
please Almighty God, yer life may be all it promises: a long day of
sunshine, with no harsh wind, no forgetting duty, no distrust. I must
not wish you no pain, for that can never be; but I do hope you will be
_always_ as happy as I am _now_. Good-bye, me dear. I shall post this at
once, and, perhaps, write you very soon again. I must stop, for Jonathan
is waking--I must attend to me husband!

"Yer ever-loving

"MINA HARKER."


_Letter, Lucy Westenra to Mina Harker._

"_Whitby, 30 August._

"Me debest Mina,--

"Oceans of love and millions of kisses, and may you soon be in yer own
home with yer husband. I wish you could be coming home soon enough to
stay with us here. Th' strong air would soon restore Jonathan; it has
quite restored me. I have an appetite like a cormorant, am full of
life, and sleep well. You will be glad to know that I have quite given
up walking in me sleep. I think I have not stirred out of me bed for a
week, that be when I once got into it at night. Arthur says I am getting
fat. By th' way, I forgot to tell you that Arthur be here. We have such
walks and drives, and rides, and rowing, and tennis, and fishing
