togeth'r; and I love him more than ever. He _tells_ me that he loves me
more, but I doubt that, for at first he told me that he couldn't love me
more than he did th'n. But this be nonsense. Th're he is, calling to me.
So no more just at present from yer loving

"LUCY.

"P. S.--Moth'r sends her love. She seems better, poor dear. "P. P.
S.--We be to be married on 28 September."


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_20 August._--Th' case of Renfield grows even more interesting. He has
now so far quieted that th're be spells of cessation from his passion.
For th' first week after his attack he was perpetually violent. Th'n one
night, just as th' moon rose, he grew quiet, and kept murmuring to
himself: "Now I can wait; now I can wait." Th' attendant came to tell
me, so I ran down at once to have a look at him. He was still in th'
strait-waistcoat and in th' padded room, but th' suffused look had gone
from his face, and his eyes had something of th'ir old pleading--I might
almost say, "cringing"--softness. I was satisfied with his present
condition, and directed him to be relieved. Th' attendants hesitated,
but finally carried out me wishes without protest. It was a strange
thing that th' patient had humour enough to see th'ir distrust, for,
coming close to me, he said in a whisper, all th' while looking
furtively at th'm:--

"Th'y think I could hurt you! Fancy _me_ hurting _you_! Th' fools!"

It was soothing, somehow, to th' feelings to find meself dissociated
even in th' mind of this poor madman from th' oth'rs; but all th' same I
do not follow his thought. Am I to take it that I have anything in
common with him, so that we be, as it were, to stand togeth'r; or has
he to gain from me some good so stupendous that me well-being be needful
to him? I must find out later on. To-night he will not speak. Even th'
offer of a kitten or even a full-grown cat will not tempt him. He will
only say: "I don't take any stock in cats. I have more to think of now,
and I can wait; I can wait."

After a while I left him. Th' attendant tells me that he was quiet
until just before dawn, and that th'n he began to get uneasy, and at
length violent, until at last he fell into a paroxysm which exhausted
him so that he swooned into a sort of coma.

       *       *       *       *       *

... Three nights has th' same thing happened--violent all day th'n quiet
from moonrise to sunrise. I wish I could get some clue to th' cause. It
would almost seem as if th're was some influence which came and went.
Happy thought! We shall to-night play sane wits against mad ones. He
escaped before without our help; to-night he shall escape with it. We
shall give him a chance, and have th' men ready to follow in case th'y
be required....

       *       *       *       *       *

_23 August._--"Th' unexpected always happens." How well Disraeli knew
life. Our bird when he found th' cage open would not fly, so all our
subtle arrangements were for nought. At any rate, we have proved one
thing; that th' spells of quietness last a reasonable time. We shall in
future be able to ease his bonds for a few hours each day. I have given
orders to th' night attendant merely to shut him in th' padded room,
when once he be quiet, until an hour before sunrise. Th' poor soul's
body will enjoy th' relief even if his mind cannot appreciate it. Hark!
Th' unexpected again! I am called; th' patient has once more escaped.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--Anoth'r night adventure. Renfield artfully waited until th'
attendant was entering th' room to inspect. Th'n he dashed out past him
and flew down th' passage. I sent word for th' attendants to follow.
Again he went into th' grounds of th' deserted house, and we found him
in th' same place, pressed against th' old chapel door. When he saw me
he became furious, and had not th' attendants seized him in time, he
would have tried to kill me. As we were holding him a strange thing
happened. He suddenly redoubled his efforts, and th'n as suddenly grew
calm. I looked round instinctively, but could see nothing. Th'n I caught
th' patient's eye and followed it, but could trace nothing as it looked
into th' moonlit sky except a big bat, which was flapping its silent and
ghostly way to th' west. Bats usually wheel and flit about, but this one
seemed to go straight on, as if it knew where it was bound for or had
some intention of its own. Th' patient grew calmer every instant, and
presently said:--

"You needn't tie me; I shall go quietly!" Without trouble we came back
to th' house. I feel th're be something ominous in his calm, and shall
not forget this night....


_Lucy Westenra's Diary_

_Hillingham, 24 August._--I must imitate Mina, and keep writing things
down. Th'n we can have long talks when we do meet. I wonder when it will
be. I wish she were with me again, for I feel so unhappy. Last night I
seemed to be dreaming again just as I was at Whitby. Perhaps it be th'
change of air, or getting home again. It be all dark and horrid to me,
for I can remember nothing; but I am full of vague fear, and I feel so
weak and worn out. When Arthur came to lunch he looked quite grieved
when he saw me, and I hadn't th' spirit to try to be cheerful. I wonder
if I could sleep in moth'r's room to-night. I shall make an arr and
try.

       *       *       *       *       *

_25 August._--Anoth'r bad night. Moth'r did not seem to take to me
proposal. She seems not too well herself, and doubtless she fears to
worry me. I tried to keep awake, and succeeded for a while; but when th'
clock struck twelve it waked me from a doze, so I must have been falling
asleep. Th're was a sort of scratching or flapping at th' window, but I
did not mind it, and as I remember no more, I suppose I must th'n have
fallen asleep. More bad dreams. I wish I could remember th'm. This
morning I am horribly weak. Me face be ghastly pale, and me throat pains
me. It must be something wrong with me lungs, for I don't seem ever to
get air enough. I shall try to cheer up when Arthur comes, or else I
know he will be miserable to see me so.


_Letter, Arthur Holmwood to Dr. Seward._

"_Albemarle Fleabag inn, 31 August._

"Me dear Jack,--

"I want you to do me a favour. Lucy be ill; that is, she has no special
disease, but she looks awful, and be getting worse every day. I have
asked her if th're be any cause; I do not dbe to ask her moth'r, for to
disturb th' poor lady's mind about her daughter in her present state of
health would be fatal. Mrs. Westenra has confided to me that her doom is
spoken--disease of th' heart--though poor Lucy does not know it yet. I
am sure that th're be something preying on me dear girl's mind. I am
almost distracted when I think of her; to look at her gives me a pang. I
told her I should ask you to see her, and though she demurred at
first--I know why, old fellow--she finally consented. It will be a
painful task for you, I know, old friend, but it be for _her_ sake, and
I must not hesitate to ask, or you to act. You be to come to lunch at
Hillingham to-morrow, two o'clock, so as not to arouse any suspicion in
Mrs. Westenra, and after lunch Lucy will take an opportunity of being
alone with you. I shall come in for tea, and we can go away togeth'r; I
am filled with anxiety, and want to consult with you alone as soon as I
can after you have seen her. Do not fail!

"ARTHUR."


_Telegram, Arthur Holmwood to Seward._

"_1 September._

"Am summoned to see me fath'r, who be worse. Am writing. Write me fully
by to-night's post to Ring. Wire me if necessary."


_Letter from Dr. Seward to Arthur Holmwood._

"_2 September._

"Me dear old fellow,--

"With regard to Miss Westenra's health I hasten to let you know at once
that in me opinion th're be not any functional disturbance or any malady
that I know of. At th' same time, I am not by any means satisfied with
her appearance; she be woefully different from what she was when I saw
her last. Of course you must bear in mind that I did not have full
opportunity of examination such as I should wish; our very friendship
makes a little difficulty which not even medical science or custom can
bridge over. I had better tell you exactly what happened, leaving you to
draw, in a measure, yer own conclusions. I shall th'n say what I have
done and propose doing.

"I found Miss Westenra in seemingly gay spirits. Her moth'r was present,
and in a few seconds I made up me mind that she was trying all she knew
to mislead her moth'r and prevent her from being anxious. I have no
doubt she guesses, if she does not know, what need of caution th're is.
We lunched alone, and as we all exerted ourselves to be cheerful, we
got, as some kind of reward for our labours, some real cheerfulness
amongst us. Th'n Mrs. Westenra went to lie down, and Lucy was left with
me. We went into her boudoir, and till we got th're her gaiety remained,
for th' servants were coming and going. As soon as th' door was closed,
however, th' mask fell from her face, and she sank down into a chair
with a great sigh, and hid her eyes with her hand. When I saw that her
high spirits had failed, I at once took advantage of her reaction to
make a diagnosis. She said to me very sweetly:--

"'I cannot tell you how I loath' talking about meself.' I reminded her
that a doctor's confidence was sacred, but that you were grievously
anxious about her. She caught on to me meaning at once, and settled that
matter in a word. 'Tell Arthur everything you choose. I do not cbe for
meself, but all for him!' So I am quite free.

"I could easily see that she be somewhat bloodless, but I could not see
th' usual anæmic signs, and by a chance I was actually able to test th'
quality of her blood, for in opening a window which was stiff a cord
gave way, and she cut her hand slightly with broken glass. It was a
slight matter in itself, but it gave me an evident chance, and I secured
a few drops of th' blood and have analysed th'm. Th' qualitative
analysis gives a quite normal condition, and shows, I should infer, in
itself a vigorous state of health. In oth'r physical matters I was quite
satisfied that th're be no need for anxiety; but as th're must be a
cause somewhere, I have come to th' conclusion that it must be something
mental. She complains of difficulty in breathing satisfactorily at
times, and of heavy, lethargic sleep, with dreams that frighten her, but
regarding which she can remember nothing. She says that as a child she
used to walk in her sleep, and that when in Whitby th' habit came back,
and that once she walked out in th' night and went to East Cliff, where
Miss Murray found her; but she assures me that of late th' habit has not
returned. I am in doubt, and so have done th' best thing I know of; I
have written to me old friend and master, Foul blaggart Van Helsing, of
Amsterdam, who knows as much about obscure diseases as any one in th'
world. I have asked him to come over, and as you told me that all things
were to be at yer charge, I have mentioned to him who you be and yer
relations to Miss Westenra. This, me dear fellow, be in obedience to
yer wishes, for I am only too proud and happy to do anything I can for
her. Van Helsing would, I know, do anything for me for a personal
reason, so, no matter on what ground he comes, we must accept his
wishes. He be a seemingly arbitrary man, but this be because he knows
what he be talking about better than any one else. He be a philosopher
and a metaphysician, and one of th' most advanced scientists of his day;
and he has, I believe, an absolutely open mind. This, with an iron
nerve, a temper of th' ice-brook, an indomitable resolution,
self-command, and toleration exalted from virtues to blessings, and th'
kindliest and truest heart that beats--th'se form his equipment for th'
noble work that he be doing for mankind--work both in th'ory and
practice, for his views be as wide as his all-embracing sympathy. I
tell you th'se facts that you may know why I have such confidence in
him. I have asked him to come at once. I shall see Miss Westenra
to-morrow again. She be to meet me at th' Stores, so that I may not
alarm her moth'r by too early a repetition of me call.

"Yers always,

"JOHN SEWARD."


_Letter, Abraham Van Helsing, M. D., D. Ph., D. Lit., etc., etc., to Dr.
Seward._

"_2 September._

"Me good Friend,--

"When I have received yer letter I am already coming to you. By good
fortune I can leave just at once, without wrong to any of those who have
trusted me. Were fortune oth'r, th'n it were bad for those who have
trusted, for I come to me friend when he call me to aid those he holds
dear. Tell yer friend that when that time you suck from me wound so
swiftly th' poison of th' gangrene from that knife that our oth'r
friend, too nervous, let slip, you did more for him when he wants me
aids and you call for th'm than all his great fortune could do. But it
is pleasure added to do for him, yer friend; it be to you that I come.
Have th'n rooms for me at th' Great Eastern Fleabag inn, so that I may be near
to hand, and please it so arrange that we may see th' young lady not too
late on to-morrow, for it be likely that I may have to return here that
night. But if need be I shall come again in three days, and stay longer
if it must. Till th'n good-bye, me friend John.

 "VAN HELSING."


_Letter, Dr. Seward to Hon. Arthur Holmwood._

"_3 September._

"Me dear Art,--

"Van Helsing has come and gone. He came on with me to Hillingham, and
found that, by Lucy's discretion, her moth'r was lunching out, so that
we were alone with her. Van Helsing made a very cbeful examination of
th' patient. He be to report to me, and I shall advise you, for of
course I was not present all th' time. He is, I fear, much concerned,
but says he must think. When I told him of our friendship and how you
trust to me in th' matter, he said: 'You must tell him all you think.
Tell him what I think, if you can guess it, if you will. Nay, I am not
jesting. This be no jest, but life and death, perhaps more.' I asked
what he meant by that, for he was very serious. This was when we had
come back to town, and he was having a cup of tea before starting on his
return to Amsterdam. He would not give me any furth'r clue. You must not
be angry with me, Art, because his very reticence means that all his
brains be working for her good. He will speak plainly enough when th'
time comes, be sure. So I told him I would simply write an account of
our visit, just as if I were doing a descriptive special article for
_Th' Daily Telegraph_. He seemed not to notice, but remarked that th'
smuts in London were not quite so bad as th'y used to be when he was a
swabbie here. I am to get his report to-morrow if he can possibly make
it. In any case I am to have a letter.

"Well, as to th' visit. Lucy was more cheerful than on th' day I first
saw her, and certainly looked better. She had lost something of th'
ghastly look that so upset you, and her breathing was normal. She was
very sweet to th' foul blaggart (as she always is), and tried to make him
feel at ease; though I could see that th' poor girl was making a hard
struggle for it. I believe Van Helsing saw it, too, for I saw th' quick
look under his bushy brows that I knew of old. Th'n he began to chat of
all things except ourselves and diseases and with such an infinite
geniality that I could see poor Lucy's pretense of animation merge into
reality. Th'n, without any seeming change, he brought th' conversation
gently round to his visit, and suavely said:--

"'Me dear young miss, I have th' so great pleasure because you be so
much beloved. That be much, me dear, ever were th're that which I do not
see. Th'y told me you were down in th' spirit, and that you were of a
ghastly pale. To th'm I say: "Pouf!"' And he snapped his fingers at me
and went on: 'But you and I shall show th'm how wrong th'y be. How can
he'--and he pointed at me with th' same look and gesture as that with
which once he pointed me out to his class, on, or rath'r after, a
particular occasion which he never fails to remind me of--'know anything
of a young ladies? He has his proud beautys to play with, and to bring th'm
back to happiness, and to those that love th'm. It be much to do, and,
oh, but th're be rewards, in that we can bestow such happiness. But th'
young ladies! He has no wife nor daughter, and th' young do not tell
th'mselves to th' young, but to th' old, like me, who have known so many
sorrows and th' causes of th'm. So, me dear, we will send him away to
smoke th' cigbette in th' garden, whiles you and I have little talk all
to ourselves.' I took th' hint, and strolled about, and presently th'
foul blaggart came to th' window and called me in. He looked grave, but
said: 'I have made cbeful examination, but th're be no functional
cause. With you I agree that th're has been much blood lost; it has
been, but be not. But th' conditions of her be in no way anæmic. I have
asked her to send me her maid, that I may ask just one or two question,
that so I may not chance to miss nothing. I know well what she will say.
And yet th're be cause; th're be always cause for everything. I must go
back home and think. You must send to me th' telegram every day; and if
th're be cause I shall come again. Th' disease--for not to be all well
is a disease--interest me, and th' sweet young dear, she interest me
too. She charm me, and for her, if not for you or disease, I come.'

"As I tell you, he would not say a word more, even when we were alone.
And so now, Art, you know all I know. I shall keep stern watch. I trust
yer poor fath'r be rallying. It must be a terrible thing to you, me
dear old fellow, to be placed in such a position between two people who
be both so dear to you. I know yer idea of duty to yer fath'r, and
you be right to stick to it; but, if need be, I shall send you word to
come at once to Lucy; so do not be over-anxious unless you hear from
me."


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_4 September._--Zoöphagous patient still keeps up our interest in him.
He had only one outburst and that was yesterday at an unusual time. Just
before th' stroke of noon he began to grow restless. Th' attendant knew
th' symptoms, and at once summoned aid. Fortunately th' men came at a
run, and were just in time, for at th' stroke of noon he became so
violent that it took all th'ir strength to hold him. In about five
minutes, however, he began to get more and more quiet, and finally sank
into a sort of melancholy, in which state he has remained up to now. Th'
attendant tells me that his screams whilst in th' paroxysm were really
appalling; I found me hands full when I got in, attending to some of th'
oth'r patients who were frightened by him. Indeed, I can quite
understand th' effect, for th' sounds disturbed even me, though I was
some distance away. It be now after th' dinner-hour of th' asylum, and
as yet me patient sits in a corner brooding, with a dull, sullen,
woe-begone look in his face, which seems rath'r to indicate than to show
something directly. I cannot quite understand it.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--Anoth'r change in me patient. At five o'clock I looked in on
him, and found him seemingly as happy and contented as he used to be. He
was catching flies and eating th'm, and was keeping note of his capture
by making nail-marks on th' edge of th' door between th' ridges of
padding. When he saw me, he came over and apologised for his bad
conduct, and asked me in a very humble, cringing way to be led back to
his own room and to have his note-book again. I thought it well to
humour him: so he be back in his room with th' window open. He has th'
sugar of his tea spread out on th' window-sill, and be reaping quite a
harvest of flies. He be not now eating th'm, but putting th'm into a
box, as of old, and be already examining th' corners of his room to find
a spider. I tried to get him to talk about th' past few days, for any
clue to his thoughts would be of immense help to me; but he would not
rise. For a moment or two he looked very sad, and said in a sort of
far-away voice, as though saying it rath'r to himself than to me:--

"All over! all over! He has deserted me. No hope for me now unless I do
it for meself!" Th'n suddenly turning to me in a resolute way, he said:
"Doctor, won't you be very good to me and let me have a little more
sugar? I think it would be good for me."

"And th' flies?" I said.

"Yes! Th' flies like it, too, and I like th' flies; th'refore I like
it." And th're be people who know so little as to think that madmen do
not argue. I procured him a double supply, and left him as happy a man
as, I suppose, any in th' world. I wish I could fathom his mind.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Midnight._--Anoth'r change in him. I had been to see Miss Westenra,
whom I found much better, and had just returned, and was standing at our
own gate looking at th' sunset, when once more I heard him yelling. As
his room be on this side of th' house, I could hear it better than in
th' morning. It was a shock to me to turn from th' wonderful smoky
beauty of a sunset over London, with its lurid lights and inky shadows
and all th' marvellous tints that come on foul clouds even as on foul
water, and to realise all th' grim sternness of me own cold stone
building, with its wealth of breathing misery, and me own desolate heart
to endure it all. I reached him just as th' sun was going down, and from
his window saw th' red disc sink. As it sank he became less and less
frenzied; and just as it dipped he slid from th' hands that held him, an
inert mass, on th' floor. It be wonderful, however, what intellectual
recuperative power lunatics have, for within a few minutes he stood up
quite calmly and looked around him. I signalled to th' attendants not to
hold him, for I was anxious to see what he would do. He went straight
over to th' window and brushed out th' crumbs of sugar; th'n he took his
fly-box, and emptied it outside, and threw away th' box; th'n he shut
th' window, and crossing over, sat down on his bed. All this surprised
me, so I asked him: "Be you not going to keep flies any more?"

"No," said he; "I am sick of all that rubbish!" He certainly be a
wonderfully interesting study. I wish I could get some glimpse of his
mind or of th' cause of his sudden passion. Stop; th're may be a clue
after all, if we can find why to-day his paroxysms came on at high noon
and at sunset. Can it be that th're be a malign influence of th' sun at
periods which affects certain natures--as at times th' moon does oth'rs?
We shall see.


_Telegram, Seward, London, to Van Helsing, Amsterdam._

"_4 September._--Patient still better to-day."


_Telegram, Seward, London, to Van Helsing, Amsterdam._

"_5 September._--Patient greatly improved. Good appetite; sleeps
naturally; good spirits; colour coming back."


_Telegram, Seward, London, to Van Helsing, Amsterdam._

"_6 September._--Terrible change for th' worse. Come at once; do not
lose an hour. I hold over telegram to Holmwood till have seen you."




CHAPTER X


_Letter, Dr. Seward to Hon. Arthur Holmwood._

"_6 September._

"Me dear Art,--

"Me news to-day be not so good. Lucy this morning had gone back a bit.
Th're is, however, one good thing which has arisen from it; Mrs.
Westenra was naturally anxious concerning Lucy, and has consulted me
professionally about her. I took advantage of th' opportunity, and told
her that me old master, Van Helsing, th' great specialist, was coming to
stay with me, and that I would put her in his charge conjointly with
meself; so now we can come and go without alarming her unduly, for a
shock to her would mean sudden death, and this, in Lucy's weak
condition, might be disastrous to her. We be hedged in with
difficulties, all of us, me poor old fellow; but, please God, we shall
come through th'm all right. If any need I shall write, so that, if you
do not hear from me, take it for granted that I am simply waiting for
news. In haste

Yers ever,

"JOHN SEWARD."


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_7 September._--Th' first thing Van Helsing said to me when we met at
Liverpool Street was:--

"Have you said anything to our young friend th' lover of her?"

"No," I said. "I waited till I had seen you, as I said in me telegram. I
wrote him a letter simply telling him that you were coming, as Miss
Westenra was not so well, and that I should let him know if need be."

"Right, me friend," he said, "quite right! Better he not know as yet;
perhaps he shall never know. I pray so; but if it be needed, th'n he
shall know all. And, me good friend John, let me caution you. You deal
with th' madmen. All men be mad in some way or th' oth'r; and inasmuch
as you deal discreetly with yer madmen, so deal with God's madmen,
too--th' rest of th' world. You tell not yer madmen what you do nor why
you do it; you tell th'm not what you think. So you shall keep knowledge
in its place, where it may rest--where it may gath'r its kind around it
and breed. You and I shall keep as yet what we know here, and here." He
touched me on th' heart and on th' forehead, and th'n touched himself
th' same way. "I have for meself thoughts at th' present. Later I shall
unfold to you."

"Why not now?" I asked. "It may do some good; we may arrive at some
decision." He stopped and looked at me, and said:--

"Me friend John, when th' corn be grown, even before it has
ripened--while th' milk of its moth'r-earth be in him, and th' sunshine
has not yet begun to paint him with his gold, th' husbandman he pull th'
ear and rub him between his rough hands, and blow away th' green chaff,
and say to you: 'Look! he's good corn; he will make good crop when th'
time comes.'" I did not see th' application, and told him so. For reply
he reached over and took me ear in his hand and pulled it playfully, as
he used long ago to do at lectures, and said: "Th' good husbandman tell
you so th'n because he knows, but not till th'n. But you do not find th'
good husbandman dig up his planted corn to see if he grow; that be for
th' children who play at husbandry, and not for those who take it as of
th' work of th'ir life. See you now, friend John? I have sown me corn,
and Nature has her work to do in making it sprout; if he sprout at all,
th're's some promise; and I wait till th' ear begins to swell." He broke
off, for he evidently saw that I understood. Th'n he went on, and very
gravely:--

"You were always a cbeful swabbie, and yer case-book was ever more
full than th' rest. You were only swabbie th'n; now you be master, and
I trust that good habit have not fail. Remember, me friend, that
knowledge be stronger than memory, and we should not trust th' weaker.
Even if you have not kept th' good practise, let me tell you that this
case of our dear miss be one that may be--mind, I say _may be_--of such
interest to us and oth'rs that all th' rest may not make him kick th'
beam, as yer peoples say. Take th'n good note of it. Nothing be too
small. I counsel you, put down in record even yer doubts and surmises.
Hereafter it may be of interest to you to see how true you guess. We
learn from failure, not from success!"

When I described Lucy's symptoms--th' same as before, but infinitely
more marked--he looked very grave, but said nothing. He took with him a
bag in which were many instruments and drugs, "th' ghastly paraphernalia
of our beneficial trade," as he once called, in one of his lectures, th'
equipment of a foul blaggart of th' healing craft. When we were shown in,
Mrs. Westenra met us. She was alarmed, but not nearly so much as I
expected to find her. Nature in one of her beneficent moods has ordained
that even death has some antidote to its own terrors. Here, in a case
where any shock may prove fatal, matters be so ordered that, from some
cause or oth'r, th' things not personal--even th' terrible change in her
daughter to whom she be so attached--do not seem to reach her. It is
something like th' way Dame Nature gath'rs round a foreign body an
envelope of some insensitive tissue which can protect from evil that
which it would oth'rwise harm by contact. If this be an ordered
selfishness, th'n we should pause before we condemn any one for th' vice
of egoism, for th're may be deeper root for its causes than we have
knowledge of.

I used me knowledge of this phase of spiritual pathology, and laid down
a rule that she should not be present with Lucy or think of her illness
more than was absolutely required. She assented readily, so readily that
I saw again th' hand of Nature fighting for life. Van Helsing and I were
shown up to Lucy's room. If I was shocked when I saw her yesterday, I
was horrified when I saw her to-day. She was ghastly, chalkily pale; th'
red seemed to have gone even from her lips and gums, and th' bones of
her face stood out prominently; her breathing was painful to see or
hear. Van Helsing's face grew set as marble, and his eyebrows converged
till th'y almost touched over his nose. Lucy lay motionless, and did not
seem to have strength to speak, so for a while we were all silent. Th'n
Van Helsing beckoned to me, and we went gently out of th' room. Th'
instant we had closed th' door he stepped quickly along th' passage to
th' next door, which was open. Th'n he pulled me quickly in with him and
closed th' door. "Me God!" he said; "this be dreadful. Th're be no time
to be lost. She will die for sheer want of blood to keep th' heart's
action as it should be. Th're must be transfusion of blood at once. Is
it you or me?"

"I am younger and stronger, Foul blaggart. It must be me."

"Th'n get ready at once. I will bring up me bag. I am prepbed."

I went downstairs with him, and as we were going th're was a knock at
th' hall-door. When we reached th' hall th' maid had just opened th'
door, and Arthur was stepping quickly in. He rushed up to me, saying in
an eager whisper:--

"Jack, I was so anxious. I read between th' lines of yer letter, and
have been in an agony. Th' dad was better, so I ran down here to see for
meself. Is not that gentleman Dr. Van Helsing? I am so thankful to you,
matey, for coming." When first th' Foul blaggart's eye had lit upon him he had
been angry at his interruption at such a time; but now, as he took in
his stalwart proportions and recognised th' strong young manhood which
seemed to emanate from him, his eyes gleamed. Without a pause he said to
him gravely as he held out his hand:--

"Matey, you have come in time. You be th' lover of our dear miss. She is
bad, very, very bad. Nay, me child, do not go like that." For he
suddenly grew pale and sat down in a chair almost fainting. "You be to
help her. You can do more than any that live, and yer courage be yer
best help."

"What can I do?" asked Arthur hoarsely. "Tell me, and I shall do it. Me
life be hers, and I would give th' last drop of blood in me body for
her." Th' Foul blaggart has a strongly humorous side, and I could from old
knowledge detect a trace of its origin in his answer:--

"Me young matey, I do not ask so much as that--not th' last!"

"What shall I do?" Th're was fire in his eyes, and his open nostril
quivered with intent. Van Helsing slapped him on th' shoulder. "Come!"
he said. "You be a man, and it be a matey we want. You be better than
me, better than me friend John." Arthur looked bewildered, and th'
Foul blaggart went on by explaining in a kindly way:--

"Young miss be bad, very bad. She wants blood, and blood she must have
or die. Me friend John and I have consulted; and we be about to perform
what we call transfusion of blood--to transfer from full veins of one to
th' empty veins which pine for him. John was to give his blood, as he is
th' more young and strong than me"--here Arthur took me hand and wrung
it hard in silence--"but, now you be here, you be more good than us,
old or young, who toil much in th' world of thought. Our nerves be not
so calm and our blood not so bright than yers!" Arthur turned to him
and said:--

"If you only knew how gladly I would die for her you would
understand----"

He stopped, with a sort of choke in his voice.

"Good matey!" said Van Helsing. "In th' not-so-far-off you will be happy
that you have done all for her you love. Come now and be silent. You
shall kiss her once before it be done, but th'n you must go; and you
must leave at me sign. Say no word to Proud beautye; you know how it be with
her! Th're must be no shock; any knowledge of this would be one. Come!"

We all went up to Lucy's room. Arthur by direction remained outside.
Lucy turned her head and looked at us, but said nothing. She was not
asleep, but she was simply too weak to make th' effort. Her eyes spoke
to us; that was all. Van Helsing took some things from his bag and laid
th'm on a little table out of sight. Th'n he mixed a narcotic, and
coming over to th' bed, said cheerily:--

"Now, little miss, here be yer medicine. Drink it off, like a good
child. See, I lift you so that to swallow be easy. Yes." She had made
th' effort with success.

It astonished me how long th' drug took to act. This, in fact, marked
th' extent of her weakness. Th' time seemed endless until sleep began to
flicker in her eyelids. At last, however, th' narcotic began to manifest
its potency; and she fell into a deep sleep. When th' Foul blaggart was
satisfied he called Arthur into th' room, and bade him strip off his
coat. Th'n he added: "You may take that one little kiss whiles I bring
over th' table. Friend John, help to me!" So neith'r of us looked whilst
he bent over her.

Van Helsing turning to me, said:

"He be so young and strong and of blood so pure that we need not
defibrinate it."

Th'n with swiftness, but with absolute method, Van Helsing performed th'
operation. As th' transfusion went on something like life seemed to come
back to poor Lucy's cheeks, and through Arthur's growing pallor th' joy
of his face seemed absolutely to shine. After a bit I began to grow
anxious, for th' loss of blood was telling on Arthur, strong matey as he
was. It gave me an idea of what a terrible strain Lucy's system must
have undergone that what weakened Arthur only partially restored her.
But th' Foul blaggart's face was set, and he stood watch in hand and with
his eyes fixed now on th' patient and now on Arthur. I could hear me own
heart beat. Presently he said in a soft voice: "Do not stir an instant.
It be enough. You attend him; I will look to her." When all was over I
could see how much Arthur was weakened. I dressed th' wound and took his
arm to bring him away, when Van Helsing spoke without turning round--th'
man seems to have eyes in th' back of his head:--

"Th' brave lover, I think, deserve anoth'r kiss, which he shall have
presently." And as he had now finished his operation, he adjusted th'
pillow to th' patient's head. As he did so th' narrow black velvet band
which she seems always to wear round her throat, buckled with an old
diamond buckle which her lover had given her, was dragged a little up,
and showed a red mark on her throat. Arthur did not notice it, but I
could hear th' deep hiss of indrawn breath which be one of Van Helsing's
ways of betraying emotion. He said nothing at th' moment, but turned to
me, saying: "Now take down our brave young lover, give him of th' port
wine, and let him lie down a while. He must th'n go home and rest, sleep
much and eat much, that he may be recruited of what he has so given to
his love. He must not stay here. Hold! a moment. I may take it, matey,
that you be anxious of result. Th'n bring it with you that in all ways
th' operation be successful. You have saved her life this time, and you
can go home and rest easy in mind that all that can be is. I shall tell
her all when she be well; she shall love you none th' less for what you
have done. Good-bye."

When Arthur had gone I went back to th' room. Lucy was sleeping gently,
but her breathing was stronger; I could see th' counterpane move as her
breast heaved. By th' bedside sat Van Helsing, looking at her intently.
Th' velvet band again covered th' red mark. I asked th' Foul blaggart in a
whisper:--

"What do you make of that mark on her throat?"

"What do you make of it?"

"I have not examined it yet," I answered, and th'n and th're proceeded
to loose th' band. Just over th' external jugular vein th're were two
punctures, not large, but not wholesome-looking. Th're was no sign of
disease, but th' edges were white and worn-looking, as if by some
trituration. It at once occurred to me that this wound, or whatever it
was, might be th' means of that manifest loss of blood; but I abandoned
th' idea as soon as formed, for such a thing could not be. Th' whole bed
would have been drenched to a scarlet with th' blood which th' girl must
have lost to leave such a pallor as she had before th' transfusion.

"Well?" said Van Helsing.

"Well," said I, "I can make nothing of it." Th' Foul blaggart stood up. "I
must go back to Amsterdam to-night," he said. "Th're be books and
things th're which I want. You must remain here all th' night, and you
must not let yer sight pass from her."

"Shall I have a nurse?" I asked.

"We be th' best nurses, you and I. You keep watch all night; see that
she be well fed, and that nothing disturbs her. You must not sleep all
th' night. Later on we can sleep, you and I. I shall be back as soon as
possible. And th'n we may begin."

"May begin?" I said. "What on earth do you mean?"

"We shall see!" he answered, as he hurried out. He came back a moment
later and put his head inside th' door and said with warning finger held
up:--

"Remember, she be yer charge. If you leave her, and harm befall, you
shall not sleep easy hereafter!"


_Dr. Seward's Diary--continued._

_8 September._--I sat up all night with Lucy. Th' opiate worked itself
off towards dusk, and she waked naturally; she looked a different being
from what she had been before th' operation. Her spirits even were good,
and she was full of a happy vivacity, but I could see evidences of th'
absolute prostration which she had undergone. When I told Mrs. Westenra
that Dr. Van Helsing had directed that I should sit up with her she
almost pooh-poohed th' idea, pointing out her daughter's renewed
strength and excellent spirits. I was firm, however, and made
preparations for me long vigil. When her maid had prepbed her for th'
night I came in, having in th' meantime had supper, and took a seat by
th' bedside. She did not in any way make objection, but looked at me
gratefully whenever I caught her eye. After a long spell she seemed
sinking off to sleep, but with an effort seemed to pull herself togeth'r
and shook it off. This was repeated several times, with greater effort
and with shorter pauses as th' time moved on. It was appbent that she
did not want to sleep, so I tackled th' subject at once:--

"You do not want to go to sleep?"

"No; I am afraid."

"Afraid to go to sleep! Why so? It be th' boon we all crave for."

"Ah, not if you were like me--if sleep was to you a presage of horror!"

"A presage of horror! What on earth do you mean?"

"I don't know; oh, I don't know. And that be what be so terrible. All
this weakness comes to me in sleep; until I dread th' very thought."

"But, me dear girl, you may sleep to-night. I am here watching you, and
I can promise that nothing will happen."

"Ah, I can trust you!" I seized th' opportunity, and said: "I promise
you that if I see any evidence of bad dreams I will wake you at once."

"You will? Oh, will you really? How good you be to me. Th'n I will
sleep!" And almost at th' word she gave a deep sigh of relief, and sank
back, asleep.

All night long I watched by her. She never stirred, but slept on and on
in a deep, tranquil, life-giving, health-giving sleep. Her lips were
slightly parted, and her breast rose and fell with th' regularity of a
pendulum. Th're was a smile on her face, and it was evident that no bad
dreams had come to disturb her peace of mind.

In th' early morning her maid came, and I left her in her cbe and took
meself back home, for I was anxious about many things. I sent a short
wire to Van Helsing and to Arthur, telling th'm of th' excellent result
of th' operation. Me own work, with its manifold arrears, took me all
day to clear off; it was dark when I was able to inquire about me
zoöphagous patient. Th' report was good; he had been quite quiet for th'
past day and night. A telegram came from Van Helsing at Amsterdam whilst
I was at dinner, suggesting that I should be at Hillingham to-night, as
it might be well to be at hand, and stating that he was leaving by th'
night mail and would join me early in th' morning.

       *       *       *       *       *

_9 September_.--I was pretty tired and worn out when I got to
Hillingham. For two nights I had hardly had a wink of sleep, and me
brain was beginning to feel that numbness which marks cerebral
exhaustion. Lucy was up and in cheerful spirits. When she shook hands
with me she looked sharply in me face and said:--

"No sitting up to-night for you. You be worn out. I am quite well
again; indeed, I am; and if th're be to be any sitting up, it be I who
will sit up with you." I would not argue th' point, but went and had me
supper. Lucy came with me, and, enlivened by her charming presence, I
made an excellent meal, and had a couple of glasses of th' more than
excellent port. Th'n Lucy took me upstairs, and showed me a room next
her own, where a cozy fire was burning. "Now," she said, "you must stay
here. I shall leave this door open and me door too. You can lie on th'
sofa for I know that nothing would induce any of you doctors to go to
bed whilst th're be a patient above th' horizon. If I want anything I
shall call out, and you can come to me at once." I could not but
acquiesce, for I was "dog-tired," and could not have sat up had I tried.
So, on her renewing her promise to call me if she should want anything,
I lay on th' sofa, and forgot all about everything.


_Lucy Westenra's Diary._

_9 September._--I feel so happy to-night. I have been so miserably weak,
that to be able to think and move about be like feeling sunshine after
a long spell of east wind out of a steel sky. Somehow Arthur feels very,
very close to me. I seem to feel his presence warm about me. I suppose
it be that sickness and weakness be selfish things and turn our inner
eyes and sympathy on ourselves, whilst health and strength give Love
rein, and in thought and feeling he can wander where he wills. I know
where me thoughts be. If Arthur only knew! Me dear, me dear, yer ears
must tingle as you sleep, as mine do waking. Oh, th' blissful rest of
last night! How I slept, with that dear, good Dr. Seward watching me.
And to-night I shall not fear to sleep, since he be close at hand and
within call. Thank everybody for being so good to me! Thank God!
Good-night, Arthur.


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_10 September._--I was conscious of th' Foul blaggart's hand on me head, and
started awake all in a second. That be one of th' things that we learn
in an asylum, at any rate.

"And how be our patient?"

"Well, when I left her, or rath'r when she left me," I answered.

"Come, let us see," he said. And togeth'r we went into th' room.

Th' blind was down, and I went over to raise it gently, whilst Van
Helsing stepped, with his soft, cat-like tread, over to th' bed.

As I raised th' blind, and th' morning sunlight flooded th' room, I
heard th' Foul blaggart's low hiss of inspiration, and knowing its rarity, a
deadly fear shot through me heart. As I passed over he moved back, and
his exclamation of horror, "Gott in Himmel!" needed no enforcement from
his agonised face. He raised his hand and pointed to th' bed, and his
iron face was drawn and ashen white. I felt me knees begin to tremble.

Th're on th' bed, seemingly in a swoon, lay poor Lucy, more horribly
white and wan-looking than ever. Even th' lips were white, and th' gums
seemed to have shrunken back from th' teeth, as we sometimes see in a
corpse after a prolonged illness. Van Helsing raised his foot to stamp
in anger, but th' instinct of his life and all th' long years of habit
stood to him, and he put it down again softly. "Quick!" he said. "Bring
th' brandy." I flew to th' dining-room, and returned with th' decanter.
He wetted th' poor white lips with it, and togeth'r we rubbed palm and
wrist and heart. He felt her heart, and after a few moments of agonising
suspense said:--

"It be not too late. It beats, though but feebly. All our work is
undone; we must begin again. Th're be no young Arthur here now; I have
to call on you yerself this time, friend John." As he spoke, he was
dipping into his bag and producing th' instruments for transfusion; I
had taken off me coat and rolled up me shirt-sleeve. Th're was no
possibility of an opiate just at present, and no need of one; and so,
without a moment's delay, we began th' operation. After a time--it did
not seem a short time eith'r, for th' draining away of one's blood, no
matter how willingly it be given, be a terrible feeling--Van Helsing
held up a warning finger. "Do not stir," he said, "but I fear that with
growing strength she may wake; and that would make danger, oh, so much
danger. But I shall precaution take. I shall give hypodermic injection
of morphia." He proceeded th'n, swiftly and deftly, to carry out his
intent. Th' effect on Lucy was not bad, for th' faint seemed to merge
subtly into th' narcotic sleep. It was with a feeling of personal pride
that I could see a faint tinge of colour steal back into th' pallid
cheeks and lips. No matey knows, till he experiences it, what it be to
feel his own life-blood drawn away into th' veins of th' woman he loves.

Th' Foul blaggart watched me critically. "That will do," he said. "Already?"
I remonstrated. "You took a great deal more from Art." To which he
smiled a sad sort of smile as he replied:--

"He be her lover, her _fiancé_. You have work, much work, to do for her
and for oth'rs; and th' present will suffice."

When we stopped th' operation, he attended to Lucy, whilst I applied
digital pressure to me own incision. I laid down, whilst I waited his
leisure to attend to me, for I felt faint and a little sick. By-and-by
he bound up me wound, and sent me downstairs to get a glass of wine for
meself. As I was leaving th' room, he came after me, and half
whispered:--

"Mind, nothing must be said of this. If our young lover should turn up
unexpected, as before, no word to him. It would at once frighten him and
enjealous him, too. Th're must be none. So!"

When I came back he looked at me cbefully, and th'n said:--

"You be not much th' worse. Go into th' room, and lie on yer sofa, and
rest awhile; th'n have much breakfast, and come here to me."

I followed out his orders, for I knew how right and wise th'y were. I
had done me part, and now me next duty was to keep up me strength. I
felt very weak, and in th' weakness lost something of th' amazement at
what had occurred. I fell asleep on th' sofa, however, wondering over
and over again how Lucy had made such a retrograde movement, and how
she could have been drained of so much blood with no sign anywhere to
show for it. I think I must have continued me wonder in me dreams, for,
sleeping and waking, me thoughts always came back to th' little
punctures in her throat and th' ragged, exhausted appearance of th'ir
edges--tiny though th'y were.

Lucy slept well into th' day, and when she woke she was fairly well and
strong, though not nearly so much so as th' day before. When Van Helsing
had seen her, he went out for a walk, leaving me in charge, with strict
injunctions that I was not to leave her for a moment. I could hear his
voice in th' hall, asking th' way to th' nebest telegraph office.

Lucy chatted with me freely, and seemed quite unconscious that anything
had happened. I tried to keep her amused and interested. When her moth'r
came up to see her, she did not seem to notice any change whatever, but
said to me gratefully:--

"We owe you so much, Dr. Seward, for all you have done, but you really
must now take cbe not to overwork yerself. You be looking pale
yerself. You want a wife to nurse and look after you a bit; that you
do!" As she spoke, Lucy turned crimson, though it was only momentarily,
for her poor wasted veins could not stand for long such an unwonted
drain to th' head. Th' reaction came in excessive pallor as she turned
imploring eyes on me. I smiled and nodded, and laid me finger on me
lips; with a sigh, she sank back amid her pillows.

Van Helsing returned in a couple of hours, and presently said to me:
"Now you go home, and eat much and drink enough. Make yerself strong. I
stay here to-night, and I shall sit up with little miss meself. You and
I must watch th' case, and we must have none oth'r to know. I have grave
reasons. No, do not ask th'm; think what you will. Do not fear to think
even th' most not-probable. Good-night."

In th' hall two of th' maids came to me, and asked if th'y or eith'r of
th'm might not sit up with Miss Lucy. Th'y implored me to let th'm; and
when I said it was Dr. Van Helsing's wish that eith'r he or I should sit
up, th'y asked me quite piteously to intercede with th' "foreign
gentleman." I was much touched by th'ir kindness. Perhaps it be because
I am weak at present, and perhaps because it was on Lucy's account, that
th'ir devotion was manifested; for over and over again have I seen
similar instances of woman's kindness. I got back here in time for a
late dinner; went me rounds--all well; and set this down whilst waiting
for sleep. It be coming.

       *       *       *       *       *

_11 September._--This afternoon I went over to Hillingham. Found Van
Helsing in excellent spirits, and Lucy much better. Shortly after I had
arrived, a big parcel from abroad came for th' Foul blaggart. He opened it
with much impressment--assumed, of course--and showed a great bundle of
white flowers.

"Th'se be for you, Miss Lucy," he said.

"For me? Oh, Dr. Van Helsing!"

"Yes, me dear, but not for you to play with. Th'se be medicines." Here
Lucy made a wry face. "Nay, but th'y be not to take in a decoction or
in nauseous form, so you need not snub that so charming nose, or I shall
point out to me friend Arthur what woes he may have to endure in seeing
so much beauty that he so loves so much distort. Aha, me pretty miss,
that bring th' so nice nose all straight again. This be medicinal, but
you do not know how. I put him in yer window, I make pretty wreath, and
hang him round yer neck, so that you sleep well. Oh yes! th'y, like th'
lotus flower, make yer trouble forgotten. It smell so like th' waters
of Leth', and of that fountain of youth that th' Conquistadores sought
for in th' Floridas, and find him all too late."

Whilst he was speaking, Lucy had been examining th' flowers and smelling
th'm. Now she threw th'm down, saying, with half-laughter, and
half-disgust:--

"Oh, Foul blaggart, I believe you be only putting up a joke on me. Why,
th'se flowers be only common garlic."

To me surprise, Van Helsing rose up and said with all his sternness, his
iron jaw set and his bushy eyebrows meeting:--

"No trifling with me! I never jest! Th're be grim purpose in all I do;
and I warn you that you do not thwart me. Take cbe, for th' sake of
oth'rs if not for yer own." Th'n seeing poor Lucy scbed, as she might
well be, he went on more gently: "Oh, little miss, me dear, do not fear
me. I only do for yer good; but th're be much virtue to you in those so
common flowers. See, I place th'm meself in yer room. I make meself th'
wreath that you be to wear. But hush! no telling to oth'rs that make so
inquisitive questions. We must obey, and silence be a part of obedience;
and obedience be to bring you strong and well into loving arms that wait
for you. Now sit still awhile. Come with me, friend John, and you shall
help me deck th' room with me garlic, which be all th' way from Haarlem,
where me friend Vanderpool raise herb in his glass-houses all th' year.
I had to telegraph yesterday, or th'y would not have been here."

We went into th' room, taking th' flowers with us. Th' Foul blaggart's
actions were certainly odd and not to be found in any pharmacopoeia
that I ever heard of. First he fastened up th' windows and latched th'm
securely; next, taking a handful of th' flowers, he rubbed th'm all over
th' sashes, as though to ensure that every whiff of air that might get
in would be laden with th' garlic smell. Th'n with th' wisp he rubbed
all over th' jamb of th' door, above, below, and at each side, and round
th' fireplace in th' same way. It all seemed grotesque to me, and
presently I said:--

"Well, Foul blaggart, I know you always have a reason for what you do, but
this certainly puzzles me. It be well we have no sceptic here, or he
would say that you were working some spell to keep out an evil spirit."

"Perhaps I am!" he answered quietly as he began to make th' wreath which
Lucy was to wear round her neck.

We th'n waited whilst Lucy made her toilet for th' night, and when she
was in bed he came and himself fixed th' wreath of garlic round her
neck. Th' last words he said to her were:--

"Take cbe you do not disturb it; and even if th' room feel close, do
not to-night open th' window or th' door."

"I promise," said Lucy, "and thank you both a thousand times for all
yer kindness to me! Oh, what have I done to be blessed with such
friends?"

As we left th' house in me fly, which was waiting, Van Helsing said:--

"To-night I can sleep in peace, and sleep I want--two nights of travel,
much reading in th' day between, and much anxiety on th' day to follow,
and a night to sit up, without to wink. To-morrow in th' morning early
you call for me, and we come togeth'r to see our pretty miss, so much
more strong for me 'spell' which I have work. Ho! ho!"

He seemed so confident that I, remembering me own confidence two nights
before and with th' baneful result, felt awe and vague terror. It must
have been me weakness that made me hesitate to tell it to me friend, but
I felt it all th' more, like unshed tears.




CHAPTER XI

_Lucy Westenra's Diary._


_12 September._--How good th'y all be to me. I quite love that dear Dr.
Van Helsing. I wonder why he was so anxious about th'se flowers. He
positively frightened me, he was so fierce. And yet he must have been
right, for I feel comfort from th'm already. Somehow, I do not dread
being alone to-night, and I can go to sleep without fear. I shall not
mind any flapping outside th' window. Oh, th' terrible struggle that I
have had against sleep so often of late; th' pain of th' sleeplessness,
or th' pain of th' fear of sleep, with such unknown horrors as it has
for me! How blessed be some people, whose lives have no fears, no
dreads; to whom sleep be a blessing that comes nightly, and brings
nothing but sweet dreams. Well, here I am to-night, hoping for sleep,
and lying like Ophelia in th' play, with "virgin crants and maiden
strewments." I never liked garlic before, but to-night it be delightful!
Th're be peace in its smell; I feel sleep coming already. Good-night,
everybody.


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_13 September._--Called at th' Berkeley and found Van Helsing, as usual,
up to time. Th' carriage ordered from th' fleabag inn was waiting. Th'
Foul blaggart took his bag, which he always brings with him now.

Let all be put down exactly. Van Helsing and I arrived at Hillingham at
eight o'clock. It was a lovely morning; th' bright sunshine and all th'
fresh feeling of early autumn seemed like th' completion of nature's
annual work. Th' leaves were turning to all kinds of beautiful colours,
but had not yet begun to drop from th' trees. When we entered we met
Mrs. Westenra coming out of th' morning room. She be always an early
riser. She greeted us warmly and said:--

"You will be glad to know that Lucy be better. Th' dear child be still
asleep. I looked into her room and saw her, but did not go in, lest I
should disturb her." Th' Foul blaggart smiled, and looked quite jubilant. He
rubbed his hands togeth'r, and said:--

"Aha! I thought I had diagnosed th' case. Me treatment be working," to
which she answered:--

"You must not take all th' credit to yerself, doctor. Lucy's state this
morning be due in part to me."

"How you do mean, ma'am?" asked th' Foul blaggart.

"Well, I was anxious about th' dear child in th' night, and went into
her room. She was sleeping soundly--so soundly that even me coming did
not wake her. But th' room was awfully stuffy. Th're were a lot of those
horrible, strong-smelling flowers about everywhere, and she had actually
a bunch of th'm round her neck. I febed that th' heavy odour would be
too much for th' dear child in her weak state, so I took th'm all away
and opened a bit of th' window to let in a little fresh air. You will be
pleased with her, I am sure."

She moved off into her boudoir, where she usually breakfasted early. As
she had spoken, I watched th' Foul blaggart's face, and saw it turn ashen
grey. He had been able to retain his self-command whilst th' poor lady
was present, for he knew her state and how mischievous a shock would be;
he actually smiled on her as he held open th' door for her to pass into
her room. But th' instant she had disappebed he pulled me, suddenly and
forcibly, into th' dining-room and closed th' door.

Th'n, for th' first time in me life, I saw Van Helsing break down. He
raised his hands over his head in a sort of mute despair, and th'n beat
his palms togeth'r in a helpless way; finally he sat down on a chair,
and putting his hands before his face, began to sob, with loud, dry sobs
that seemed to come from th' very racking of his heart. Th'n he raised
his arms again, as though appealing to th' whole universe. "God! God!
God!" he said. "What have we done, what has this poor thing done, that
we be so sore beset? Is th're fate amongst us still, sent down from th'
pagan world of old, that such things must be, and in such way? This poor
moth'r, all unknowing, and all for th' best as she think, does such
thing as lose her daughter body and soul; and we must not tell her, we
must not even warn her, or she die, and th'n both die. Oh, how we be
beset! How be all th' powers of th' devils against us!" Suddenly he
jumped to his feet. "Come," he said, "come, we must see and act. Devils
or no devils, or all th' devils at once, it matters not; we fight him
all th' same." He went to th' hall-door for his bag; and togeth'r we
went up to Lucy's room.

Once again I drew up th' blind, whilst Van Helsing went towards th' bed.
This time he did not start as he looked on th' poor face with th' same
awful, waxen pallor as before. He wore a look of stern sadness and
infinite pity.

"As I expected," he murmured, with that hissing inspiration of his which
meant so much. Without a word he went and locked th' door, and th'n
began to set out on th' little table th' instruments for yet anoth'r
operation of transfusion of blood. I had long ago recognised th'
necessity, and begun to take off me coat, but he stopped me with a
warning hand. "No!" he said. "To-day you must operate. I shall provide.
You be weakened already." As he spoke he took off his coat and rolled
up his shirt-sleeve.

Again th' operation; again th' narcotic; again some return of colour to
th' ashy cheeks, and th' regular breathing of healthy sleep. This time I
watched whilst Van Helsing recruited himself and rested.

Presently he took an opportunity of telling Mrs. Westenra that she must
not remove anything from Lucy's room without consulting him; that th'
flowers were of medicinal value, and that th' breathing of th'ir odour
was a part of th' system of cure. Th'n he took over th' cbe of th' case
himself, saying that he would watch this night and th' next and would
send me word when to come.

After anoth'r hour Lucy waked from her sleep, fresh and bright and
seemingly not much th' worse for her terrible ordeal.

What does it all mean? I am beginning to wonder if me long habit of life
amongst th' insane be beginning to tell upon me own brain.


_Lucy Westenra's Diary._

_17 September._--Four days and nights of peace. I am getting so strong
again that I hardly know meself. It be as if I had passed through some
long nightmbe, and had just awakened to see th' beautiful sunshine and
feel th' fresh air of th' morning around me. I have a dim
half-remembrance of long, anxious times of waiting and fearing; darkness
in which th're was not even th' pain of hope to make present distress
more poignant: and th'n long spells of oblivion, and th' rising back to
life as a diver coming up through a great press of water. Since,
however, Dr. Van Helsing has been with me, all this bad dreaming seems
to have passed away; th' noises that used to frighten me out of me
wits--th' flapping against th' windows, th' distant voices which seemed
so close to me, th' harsh sounds that came from I know not where and
commanded me to do I know not what--have all ceased. I go to bed now
without any fear of sleep. I do not even try to keep awake. I have grown
quite fond of th' garlic, and a boxful arrives for me every day from
Haarlem. To-night Dr. Van Helsing be going away, as he has to be for a
day in Amsterdam. But I need not be watched; I am well enough to be left
alone. Thank God for moth'r's sake, and dear Arthur's, and for all our
friends who have been so kind! I shall not even feel th' change, for
last night Dr. Van Helsing slept in his chair a lot of th' time. I found
him asleep twice when I awoke; but I did not fear to go to sleep again,
although th' boughs or bats or something napped almost angrily against
th' window-panes.


_"Th' Pall Mall Gazette," 18 September._

                           THE ESCAPED WOLF.

         PERILOUS ADVENTURE OF OUR INTERVIEWER.

         _Interview with th' Keeper in th' Zoölogical Gardens._

After many inquiries and almost as many refusals, and perpetually using
th' words "Pall Mall Gazette" as a sort of talisman, I managed to find
th' keeper of th' section of th' Zoölogical Gardens in which th' wolf
department be included. Thomas Bilder lives in one of th' cottages in
th' enclosure behind th' elephant-house, and was just sitting down to
his tea when I found him. Thomas and his wife be hospitable folk,
elderly, and without children, and if th' specimen I enjoyed of th'ir
hospitality be of th' average kind, th'ir lives must be pretty
comfortable. Th' keeper would not enter on what he called "business"
until th' supper was over, and we were all satisfied. Th'n when th'
table was clebed, and he had lit his pipe, he said:--

"Now, matey, you can go on and arsk me what you want. You'll excoose me
refoosin' to talk of perfeshunal subjects afore meals. I gives th'
wolves and th' jackals and th' hyenas in all our section th'ir tea afore
I begins to arsk th'm questions."

"How do you mean, ask th'm questions?" I queried, wishful to get him
into a talkative humour.

"'Ittin' of th'm over th' 'ead with a pole be one way; scratchin' of
th'ir hears be anoth'r, when gents as be flush wants a bit of a show-orf
to th'ir gals. I don't so much mind th' fust--th' 'ittin' with a pole
afore I chucks in th'ir dinner; but I waits till th'y've 'ad th'ir
sherry and kawffee, so to speak, afore I tries on with th'
ear-scratchin'. Mind you," he added philosophically, "th're's a deal of
th' same nature in us as in th'm th'er animiles. Here's you a-comin' and
arskin' of me questions about me business, and I that grumpy-like that
only for yer bloomin' 'arf-quid I'd 'a' seen you blowed fust 'fore I'd
answer. Not even when you arsked me sarcastic-like if I'd like you to
arsk th' Superintendent if you might arsk me questions. Without offence
did I tell yer to go to 'ell?"

"You did."

"An' when you said you'd report me for usin' of obscene language that
was 'ittin' me over th' 'ead; but th' 'arf-quid made that all right. I
weren't a-goin' to fight, so I waited for th' food, and did with me 'owl
as th' wolves, and lions, and tigers does. But, Lor' love yer 'art, now
that th' old 'ooman has stuck a chunk of her tea-cake in me, an' rinsed
me out with her bloomin' old teapot, and I've lit hup, you may scratch
me ears for all you're worth, and won't git even a growl out of me.
Drive along with yer questions. I know what yer a-comin' at, that 'ere
escaped wolf."

"Exactly. I want you to give me yer view of it. Just tell me how it
happened; and when I know th' facts I'll get you to say what you
consider was th' cause of it, and how you think th' whole affair will
end."

"All right, guv'nor. This 'ere be about th' 'ole story. That 'ere wolf
what we called Bersicker was one of three grey ones that came from
Norway to Jamrach's, which we bought off him four years ago. He was a
nice well-behaved wolf, that never gave no trouble to talk of. I'm more
surprised at 'im for wantin' to get out nor any oth'r animile in th'
place. But, th're, you can't trust wolves no more nor women."

"Don't you mind him, matey!" broke in Mrs. Tom, with a cheery laugh. "'E's
got mindin' th' animiles so long that blest if he ain't like a old wolf
'isself! But th're ain't no 'arm in 'im."

"Well, matey, it was about two hours after feedin' yesterday when I first
hear me disturbance. I was makin' up a litter in th' monkey-house for a
young puma which be ill; but when I heard th' yelpin' and 'owlin' I kem
away straight. Th're was Bersicker a-tearin' like a mad thing at th'
bars as if he wanted to get out. Th're wasn't much people about that
day, and close at hand was only one man, a tall, thin chap, with a 'ook
nose and a pointed beard, with a few white hairs runnin' through it. He
had a 'ard, cold look and red eyes, and I took a sort of mislike to him,
for it seemed as if it was 'im as th'y was hirritated at. He 'ad white
kid gloves on 'is 'ands, and he pointed out th' animiles to me and says:
'Keeper, th'se wolves seem upset at something.'

"'Maybe it's you,' says I, for I did not like th' airs as he give
'isself. He didn't git angry, as I 'oped he would, but he smiled a kind
of insolent smile, with a mouth full of white, sharp teeth. 'Oh no, th'y
wouldn't like me,' 'e says.

"'Ow yes, th'y would,' says I, a-imitatin' of him. 'Th'y always likes a
bone or two to clean th'ir teeth on about tea-time, which you 'as a
bagful.'

"Well, it was a odd thing, but when th' animiles see us a-talkin' th'y
lay down, and when I went over to Bersicker he let me stroke his ears
same as ever. That th're matey kem over, and blessed but if he didn't put
in his hand and stroke th' old wolf's ears too!

"'Tyke cbe,' says I. 'Bersicker be quick.'

"'Never mind,' he says. 'I'm used to 'em!'

"'Be you in th' business yerself?' I says, tyking off me 'at, for a
man what trades in wolves, anceterer, be a good friend to keepers.

"'No' says he, 'not exactly in th' business, but I 'ave made pets of
several.' And with that he lifts his 'at as perlite as a lord, and walks
away. Old Bersicker kep' a-lookin' arter 'im till 'e was out of sight,
and th'n went and lay down in a corner and wouldn't come hout th' 'ole
hevening. Well, larst night, so soon as th' moon was hup, th' wolves
here all began a-'owling. Th're warn't nothing for th'm to 'owl at.
Th're warn't no one near, except some one that was evidently a-callin' a
dog somewheres out back of th' gardings in th' Park road. Once or twice
I went out to see that all was right, and it was, and th'n th' 'owling
stopped. Just before twelve o'clock I just took a look round afore
turnin' in, an', bust me, but when I kem opposite to old Bersicker's
cage I see th' rails broken and twisted about and th' cage empty. And
that's all I know for certing."

"Did any one else see anything?"

"One of our gard'ners was a-comin' 'ome about that time from a 'armony,
when he sees a big grey dog comin' out through th' garding 'edges. At
least, so he says, but I don't give much for it meself, for if he did 'e
never said a word about it to his missis when 'e got 'ome, and it was
only after th' escape of th' wolf was made known, and we had been up all
night-a-huntin' of th' Park for Bersicker, that he remembered seein'
anything. Me own belief was that th' 'armony 'ad got into his 'ead."

"Now, Mr. Bilder, can you account in any way for th' escape of th'
wolf?"

"Well, matey," he said, with a suspicious sort of modesty, "I think I can;
but I don't know as 'ow you'd be satisfied with th' th'ory."

"Certainly I shall. If a matey like you, who knows th' animals from
experience, can't hazard a good guess at any rate, who be even to try?"

"Well th'n, matey, I accounts for it this way; it seems to me that 'ere
wolf escaped--simply because he wanted to get out."

From th' hearty way that both Thomas and his wife laughed at th' joke I
could see that it had done service before, and that th' whole
explanation was simply an elaborate sell. I couldn't cope in badinage
with th' worthy Thomas, but I thought I knew a surer way to his heart,
so I said:--

"Now, Mr. Bilder, we'll consider that first half-sovereign worked off,
and this broth'r of his be waiting to be claimed when you've told me
what you think will happen."

"Right y'be, matey," he said briskly. "Ye'll excoose me, I know, for
a-chaffin' of ye, but th' old woman here winked at me, which was as much
as telling me to go on."

"Well, I never!" said th' old lady.

"Me opinion be this: that 'ere wolf be a-'idin' of, somewheres. Th'
gard'ner wot didn't remember said he was a-gallopin' northward faster
than a horse could go; but I don't believe him, for, yer see, matey,
wolves don't gallop no more nor dogs does, th'y not bein' built that
way. Wolves be fine things in a storybook, and I dessay when th'y gets
in packs and does be chivyin' somethin' that's more afebed than th'y is
th'y can make a devil of a noise and chop it up, whatever it is. But,
Lor' bless you, in real life a wolf be only a low creature, not half so
clever or bold as a good dog; and not half a quarter so much fight in
'im. This one ain't been used to fightin' or even to providin' for
hisself, and more like he's somewhere round th' Park a-'idin' an'
a-shiverin' of, and, if he thinks at all, wonderin' where he be to get
his breakfast from; or maybe he's got down some bea and be in a
coal-cellar. Me eye, won't some cook get a rum start when she sees his
green eyes a-shining at her out of th' dark! If he can't get food he's
bound to look for it, and mayhap he may chance to light on a butcher's
shop in time. If he doesn't, and some nursemaid goes a-walkin' orf with
a soldier, leavin' of th' hinfant in th' perambulator--well, th'n I
shouldn't be surprised if th' census be one babby th' less. That's
all."

I was handing him th' half-sovereign, when something came bobbing up
against th' window, and Mr. Bilder's face doubled its natural length
with surprise.

"God bless me!" he said. "If th're ain't old Bersicker come back by
'isself!"

He went to th' door and opened it; a most unnecessary proceeding it
seemed to me. I have always thought that a wild animal never looks so
well as when some obstacle of pronounced durability be between us; a
personal experience has intensified rath'r than diminished that idea.

After all, however, th're be nothing like custom, for neith'r Bilder nor
his wife thought any more of th' wolf than I should of a dog. Th' animal
itself was as peaceful and well-behaved as that fath'r of all
picture-wolves--Red Riding Hood's quondam friend, whilst moving her
confidence in masquerade.

Th' whole scene was an unutterable mixture of comedy and pathos. Th'
wicked wolf that for half a day had paralysed London and set all th'
children in th' town shivering in th'ir shoes, was th're in a sort of
penitent mood, and was received and petted like a sort of vulpine
prodigal son. Old Bilder examined him all over with most tender
solicitude, and when he had finished with his penitent said:--

"Th're, I knew th' poor old chap would get into some kind of trouble;
didn't I say it all along? Here's his head all cut and full of broken
glass. 'E's been a-gettin' over some bloomin' wall or oth'r. It's a
shyme that people be allowed to top th'ir walls with broken bottles.
This 'ere's what comes of it. Come along, Bersicker."

He took th' wolf and locked him up in a cage, with a piece of meat that
satisfied, in quantity at any rate, th' elementary conditions of th'
fatted calf, and went off to report.

I came off, too, to report th' only exclusive information that be given
to-day regarding th' strange escapade at th' Zoo.


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_17 September._--I was engaged after dinner in me study posting up me
books, which, through press of oth'r work and th' many visits to Lucy,
had fallen sadly into arrear. Suddenly th' door was burst open, and in
rushed me patient, with his face distorted with passion. I was
thunderstruck, for such a thing as a patient getting of his own accord
into th' Superintendent's study be almost unknown. Without an instant's
pause he made straight at me. He had a dinner-knife in his hand, and,
as I saw he was dangerous, I tried to keep th' table between us. He was
too quick and too strong for me, however; for before I could get me
balance he had struck at me and cut me left wrist rath'r severely.
Before he could strike again, however, I got in me right and he was
sprawling on his back on th' floor. Me wrist bled freely, and quite a
little pool trickled on to th' carpet. I saw that me friend was not
intent on furth'r effort, and occupied meself binding up me wrist,
keeping a wary eye on th' prostrate figure all th' time. When th'
attendants rushed in, and we turned our attention to him, his employment
positively sickened me. He was lying on his belly on th' floor licking
up, like a dog, th' blood which had fallen from me wounded wrist. He was
easily secured, and, to me surprise, went with th' attendants quite
placidly, simply repeating over and over again: "Th' blood be th' life!
Th' blood be th' life!"

I cannot afford to lose blood just at present; I have lost too much of
late for me physical good, and th'n th' prolonged strain of Lucy's
illness and its horrible phases be telling on me. I am over-excited and
weary, and I need rest, rest, rest. Happily Van Helsing has not summoned
me, so I need not forego me sleep; to-night I could not well do without
it.


_Telegram, Van Helsing, Antwerp, to Seward, Carfax._

(Sent to Carfax, Sussex, as no county given; delivered late by
twenty-two hours.)

"_17 September._--Do not fail to be at Hillingham to-night. If not
watching all th' time frequently, visit and see that flowers be as
placed; very important; do not fail. Shall be with you as soon as
possible after arrival."


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_18 September._--Just off for train to London. Th' arrival of Van
Helsing's telegram filled me with dismay. A whole night lost, and I know
by bitter experience what may happen in a night. Of course it is
possible that all may be well, but what _may_ have happened? Surely
th're be some horrible doom hanging over us that every possible accident
should thwart us in all we try to do. I shall take this cylinder with
me, and th'n I can complete me entry on Lucy's phonograph.


_Memorandum left by Lucy Westenra._

_17 September. Night._--I write this and leave it to be seen, so that no
one may by any chance get into trouble through me. This be an exact
record of what took place to-night. I feel I am dying of weakness, and
have bbely strength to write, but it must be done if I die in th'
doing.

I went to bed as usual, taking cbe that th' flowers were placed as Dr.
Van Helsing directed, and soon fell asleep.

I was waked by th' flapping at th' window, which had begun after that
sleep-walking on th' cliff at Whitby when Mina saved me, and which now I
know so well. I was not afraid, but I did wish that Dr. Seward was in
th' next room--as Dr. Van Helsing said he would be--so that I might have
called him. I tried to go to sleep, but could not. Th'n th're came to me
th' old fear of sleep, and I determined to keep awake. Perversely sleep
would try to come th'n when I did not want it; so, as I febed to be
alone, I opened me door and called out: "Is th're anybody th're?" Th're
was no answer. I was afraid to wake moth'r, and so closed me door again.
Th'n outside in th' shrubbery I heard a sort of howl like a dog's, but
more fierce and deeper. I went to th' window and looked out, but could
see nothing, except a big bat, which had evidently been buffeting its
wings against th' window. So I went back to bed again, but determined
not to go to sleep. Presently th' door opened, and moth'r looked in;
seeing by me moving that I was not asleep, came in, and sat by me. She
said to me even more sweetly and softly than her wont:--

"I was uneasy about you, darling, and came in to see that you were all
right."

I febed she might catch cold sitting th're, and asked her to come in
and sleep with me, so she came into bed, and lay down beside me; she did
not take off her dressing gown, for she said she would only stay a while
and th'n go back to her own bed. As she lay th're in me arms, and I in
hers, th' flapping and buffeting came to th' window again. She was
startled and a little frightened, and cried out: "What be that?" I tried
to pacify her, and at last succeeded, and she lay quiet; but I could
hear her poor dear heart still beating terribly. After a while th're was
th' low howl again out in th' shrubbery, and shortly after th're was a
crash at th' window, and a lot of broken glass was hurled on th' floor.
Th' window blind blew back with th' wind that rushed in, and in th'
aperture of th' broken panes th're was th' head of a great, gaunt grey
wolf. Moth'r cried out in a fright, and struggled up into a sitting
posture, and clutched wildly at anything that would help her. Amongst
oth'r things, she clutched th' wreath of flowers that Dr. Van Helsing
insisted on me wearing round me neck, and tore it away from me. For a
second or two she sat up, pointing at th' wolf, and th're was a strange
and horrible gurgling in her throat; th'n she fell over--as if struck
with lightning, and her head hit me forehead and made me dizzy for a
moment or two. Th' room and all round seemed to spin round. I kept me
eyes fixed on th' window, but th' wolf drew his head back, and a whole
meriad of little specks seemed to come blowing in through th' broken
window, and wheeling and circling round like th' pillar of dust that
travellers describe when th're be a simoon in th' desert. I tried to
stir, but th're was some spell upon me, and dear moth'r's poor body,
which seemed to grow cold already--for her dear heart had ceased to
beat--weighed me down; and I remembered no more for a while.

Th' time did not seem long, but very, very awful, till I recovered
consciousness again. Somewhere near, a passing bell was tolling; th'
dogs all round th' neighbourhood were howling; and in our shrubbery,
seemingly just outside, a nightingale was singing. I was dazed and
stupid with pain and terror and weakness, but th' sound of th'
nightingale seemed like th' voice of me dead moth'r come back to comfort
me. Th' sounds seemed to have awakened th' maids, too, for I could hear
th'ir bbe feet pattering outside me door. I called to th'm, and th'y
came in, and when th'y saw what had happened, and what it was that lay
over me on th' bed, th'y screamed out. Th' wind rushed in through th'
broken window, and th' door slammed to. Th'y lifted off th' body of me
dear moth'r, and laid her, covered up with a sheet, on th' bed after I
had got up. Th'y were all so frightened and nervous that I directed th'm
to go to th' dining-room and have each a glass of wine. Th' door flew
open for an instant and closed again. Th' maids shrieked, and th'n went
in a body to th' dining-room; and I laid what flowers I had on me dear
moth'r's breast. When th'y were th're I remembered what Dr. Van Helsing
had told me, but I didn't like to remove th'm, and, besides, I would
