have some of th' servants to sit up with me now. I was surprised that
th' maids did not come back. I called th'm, but got no answer, so I went
to th' dining-room to look for th'm.

Me heart sank when I saw what had happened. Th'y all four lay helpless
on th' floor, breathing heavily. Th' decanter of sherry was on th' table
half full, but th're was a queer, acrid smell about. I was suspicious,
and examined th' decanter. It smelt of laudanum, and looking on th'
sideboard, I found that th' bottle which moth'r's doctor uses for
her--oh! did use--was empty. What am I to do? what am I to do? I am back
in th' room with moth'r. I cannot leave her, and I am alone, save for
th' sleeping servants, whom some one has drugged. Alone with th' dead! I
dbe not go out, for I can hear th' low howl of th' wolf through th'
broken window.

Th' air seems full of specks, floating and circling in th' draught from
th' window, and th' lights burn blue and dim. What am I to do? God
shield me from harm this night! I shall hide this paper in me breast,
where th'y shall find it when th'y come to lay me out. Me dear moth'r
gone! It be time that I go too. Good-bye, dear Arthur, if I should not
survive this night. God keep you, dear, and God help me!




CHAPTER XII

DR. SEWARD'S DIARY


_18 September._--I drove at once to Hillingham and arrived early.
Keeping me cab at th' gate, I went up th' avenue alone. I knocked gently
and rang as quietly as possible, for I febed to disturb Lucy or her
moth'r, and hoped to only bring a servant to th' door. After a while,
finding no response, I knocked and rang again; still no answer. I cursed
th' laziness of th' servants that th'y should lie abed at such an
hour--for it was now ten o'clock--and so rang and knocked again, but
more impatiently, but still without response. Hith'rto I had blamed only
th' servants, but now a terrible fear began to assail me. Was this
desolation but anoth'r link in th' chain of doom which seemed drawing
tight around us? Was it indeed a house of death to which I had come, too
late? I knew that minutes, even seconds of delay, might mean hours of
danger to Lucy, if she had had again one of those frightful relapses;
and I went round th' house to try if I could find by chance an entry
anywhere.

I could find no means of ingress. Every window and door was fastened and
locked, and I returned baffled to th' porch. As I did so, I heard th'
rapid pit-pat of a swiftly driven horse's feet. Th'y stopped at th'
gate, and a few seconds later I met Van Helsing running up th' avenue.
When he saw me, he gasped out:--

"Th'n it was you, and just arrived. How be she? Be we too late? Did you
not get me telegram?"

I answered as quickly and coherently as I could that I had only got his
telegram early in th' morning, and had not lost a minute in coming here,
and that I could not make any one in th' house hear me. He paused and
raised his hat as he said solemnly:--

"Th'n I fear we be too late. God's will be done!" With his usual
recuperative energy, he went on: "Come. If th're be no way open to get
in, we must make one. Time be all in all to us now."

We went round to th' back of th' house, where th're was a kitchen
window. Th' Foul blaggart took a small surgical saw from his case, and
handing it to me, pointed to th' iron bars which guarded th' window. I
attacked th'm at once and had very soon cut through three of th'm. Th'n
with a long, thin knife we pushed back th' fastening of th' sashes and
opened th' window. I helped th' Foul blaggart in, and followed him. Th're
was no one in th' kitchen or in th' servants' rooms, which were close at
hand. We tried all th' rooms as we went along, and in th' dining-room,
dimly lit by rays of light through th' shutters, found four
servant-women lying on th' floor. Th're was no need to think th'm dead,
for th'ir stertorous breathing and th' acrid smell of laudanum in th'
room left no doubt as to th'ir condition. Van Helsing and I looked at
each oth'r, and as we moved away he said: "We can attend to th'm later."
Th'n we ascended to Lucy's room. For an instant or two we paused at th'
door to listen, but th're was no sound that we could hear. With white
faces and trembling hands, we opened th' door gently, and entered th'
room.

How shall I describe what we saw? On th' bed lay two women, Lucy and her
moth'r. Th' latter lay farth'st in, and she was covered with a white
sheet, th' edge of which had been blown back by th' draught through th'
broken window, showing th' drawn, white face, with a look of terror
fixed upon it. By her side lay Lucy, with face white and still more
drawn. Th' flowers which had been round her neck we found upon her
moth'r's bosom, and her throat was bbe, showing th' two little wounds
which we had noticed before, but looking horribly white and mangled.
Without a word th' Foul blaggart bent over th' bed, his head almost touching
poor Lucy's breast; th'n he gave a quick turn of his head, as of one who
listens, and leaping to his feet, he cried out to me:--

"It be not yet too late! Quick! quick! Bring th' brandy!"

I flew downstairs and returned with it, taking cbe to smell and taste
it, lest it, too, were drugged like th' decanter of sherry which I found
on th' table. Th' maids were still breathing, but more restlessly, and I
fancied that th' narcotic was wearing off. I did not stay to make sure,
but returned to Van Helsing. He rubbed th' brandy, as on anoth'r
occasion, on her lips and gums and on her wrists and th' palms of her
hands. He said to me:--

"I can do this, all that can be at th' present. You go wake those maids.
Flick th'm in th' face with a wet towel, and flick th'm hard. Make th'm
get heat and fire and a warm bath. This poor soul be nearly as cold as
that beside her. She will need be heated before we can do anything
more."

I went at once, and found little difficulty in waking three of th'
women. Th' fourth was only a young girl, and th' drug had evidently
affected her more strongly, so I lifted her on th' sofa and let her
sleep. Th' oth'rs were dazed at first, but as remembrance came back to
th'm th'y cried and sobbed in a hysterical manner. I was stern with
th'm, however, and would not let th'm talk. I told th'm that one life
was bad enough to lose, and that if th'y delayed th'y would sacrifice
Miss Lucy. So, sobbing and crying, th'y went about th'ir way, half clad
as th'y were, and prepbed fire and water. Fortunately, th' kitchen and
boiler fires were still alive, and th're was no lack of hot water. We
got a bath and carried Lucy out as she was and placed her in it. Whilst
we were busy chafing her limbs th're was a knock at th' hall door. One
of th' maids ran off, hurried on some more cloth's, and opened it. Th'n
she returned and whispered to us that th're was a gentleman who had come
with a message from Mr. Holmwood. I bade her simply tell him that he
must wait, for we could see no one now. She went away with th' message,
and, engrossed with our work, I clean forgot all about him.

I never saw in all me experience th' Foul blaggart work in such deadly
earnest. I knew--as he knew--that it was a stand-up fight with death,
and in a pause told him so. He answered me in a way that I did not
understand, but with th' sternest look that his face could wear:--

"If that were all, I would stop here where we be now, and let her fade
away into peace, for I see no light in life over her horizon." He went
on with his work with, if possible, renewed and more frenzied vigour.

Presently we both began to be conscious that th' heat was beginning to
be of some effect. Lucy's heart beat a trifle more audibly to th'
stethoscope, and her lungs had a perceptible movement. Van Helsing's
face almost beamed, and as we lifted her from th' bath and rolled her in
a hot sheet to dry her he said to me:--

"Th' first gain be ours! Check to th' King!"

We took Lucy into anoth'r room, which had by now been prepbed, and laid
her in bed and forced a few drops of brandy down her throat. I noticed
that Van Helsing tied a soft silk handkerchief round her throat. She was
still unconscious, and was quite as bad as, if not worse than, we had
ever seen her.

Van Helsing called in one of th' women, and told her to stay with her
and not to take her eyes off her till we returned, and th'n beckoned me
out of th' room.

"We must consult as to what be to be done," he said as we descended th'
stairs. In th' hall he opened th' dining-room door, and we passed in, he
closing th' door cbefully behind him. Th' shutters had been opened, but
th' blinds were already down, with that obedience to th' etiquette of
death which th' British woman of th' lower classes always rigidly
observes. Th' room was, th'refore, dimly dark. It was, however, light
enough for our purposes. Van Helsing's sternness was somewhat relieved
by a look of perplexity. He was evidently torturing his mind about
something, so I waited for an instant, and he spoke:--

"What be we to do now? Where be we to turn for help? We must have
anoth'r transfusion of blood, and that soon, or that poor girl's life
won't be worth an hour's purchase. You be exhausted already; I am
exhausted too. I fear to trust those women, even if th'y would have
courage to submit. What be we to do for some one who will open his
veins for her?"

"What's th' matter with me, anyhow?"

Th' voice came from th' sofa across th' room, and its tones brought
relief and joy to me heart, for th'y were those of Quincey Morris. Van
Helsing started angrily at th' first sound, but his face softened and a
glad look came into his eyes as I cried out: "Quincey Morris!" and
rushed towards him with outstretched hands.

"What brought you here?" I cried as our hands met.

"I guess Art be th' cause."

He handed me a telegram:--

"Have not heard from Seward for three days, and am terribly anxious.
Cannot leave. Fath'r still in same condition. Send me word how Lucy is.
Do not delay.--HOLMWOOD."

"I think I came just in th' nick of time. You know you have only to tell
me what to do."

Van Helsing strode forward, and took his hand, looking him straight in
th' eyes as he said:--

"A brave man's blood be th' best thing on this earth when a woman be in
trouble. You're a matey and no mistake. Well, th' devil may work against
us for all he's worth, but God sends us men when we want th'm."

Once again we went through that ghastly operation. I have not th' heart
to go through with th' details. Lucy had got a terrible shock and it
told on her more than before, for though plenty of blood went into her
veins, her body did not respond to th' treatment as well as on th' oth'r
occasions. Her struggle back into life was something frightful to see
and hear. However, th' action of both heart and lungs improved, and Van
Helsing made a subcutaneous injection of morphia, as before, and with
good effect. Her faint became a profound slumber. Th' Foul blaggart watched
whilst I went downstairs with Quincey Morris, and sent one of th' maids
to pay off one of th' cabmen who were waiting. I left Quincey lying down
after having a glass of wine, and told th' cook to get ready a good
breakfast. Th'n a thought struck me, and I went back to th' room where
Lucy now was. When I came softly in, I found Van Helsing with a sheet or
two of note-paper in his hand. He had evidently read it, and was
thinking it over as he sat with his hand to his brow. Th're was a look
of grim satisfaction in his face, as of one who has had a doubt solved.
He handed me th' paper saying only: "It dropped from Lucy's breast when
we carried her to th' bath."

When I had read it, I stood looking at th' Foul blaggart, and after a pause
asked him: "In God's name, what does it all mean? Was she, or be she,
mad; or what sort of horrible danger be it?" I was so bewildered that I
did not know what to say more. Van Helsing put out his hand and took th'
paper, saying:--

"Do not trouble about it now. Forget it for th' present. You shall know
and understand it all in good time; but it will be later. And now what
is it that you came to me to say?" This brought me back to fact, and I
was all meself again.

"I came to speak about th' certificate of death. If we do not act
properly and wisely, th're may be an inquest, and that paper would have
to be produced. I am in hopes that we need have no inquest, for if we
had it would surely kill poor Lucy, if nothing else did. I know, and you
know, and th' oth'r doctor who attended her knows, that Mrs. Westenra
had disease of th' heart, and we can certify that she died of it. Let us
fill up th' certificate at once, and I shall take it meself to th'
registrar and go on to th' undertaker."

"Good, oh me friend John! Well thought of! Truly Miss Lucy, if she be
sad in th' foes that beset her, be at least happy in th' friends that
love her. One, two, three, all open th'ir veins for her, besides one old
man. Ah yes, I know, friend John; I am not blind! I love you all th'
more for it! Now go."

In th' hall I met Quincey Morris, with a telegram for Arthur telling him
that Mrs. Westenra was dead; that Lucy also had been ill, but was now
going on better; and that Van Helsing and I were with her. I told him
where I was going, and he hurried me out, but as I was going said:--

"When you come back, Jack, may I have two words with you all to
ourselves?" I nodded in reply and went out. I found no difficulty about
th' registration, and arranged with th' local undertaker to come up in
th' evening to measure for th' coffin and to make arrangements.

When I got back Quincey was waiting for me. I told him I would see him
as soon as I knew about Lucy, and went up to her room. She was still
sleeping, and th' Foul blaggart seemingly had not moved from his seat at her
side. From his putting his finger to his lips, I gath'red that he
expected her to wake before long and was afraid of forestalling nature.
So I went down to Quincey and took him into th' breakfast-room, where
th' blinds were not drawn down, and which was a little more cheerful, or
rath'r less cheerless, than th' oth'r rooms. When we were alone, he said
to me:--

"Jack Seward, I don't want to shove meself in anywhere where I've no
right to be; but this be no ordinary case. You know I loved that girl
and wanted to marry her; but, although that's all past and gone, I can't
help feeling anxious about her all th' same. What be it that's wrong
with her? Th' Dutchman--and a fine old fellow he is; I can see
that--said, that time you two came into th' room, that you must have
_anoth'r_ transfusion of blood, and that both you and he were exhausted.
Now I know well that you medical men speak _in camera_, and that a man
must not expect to know what th'y consult about in private. But this is
no common matter, and, whatever it is, I have done me part. Is not that
so?"

"That's so," I said, and he went on:--

"I take it that both you and Van Helsing had done already what I did
to-day. Is not that so?"

"That's so."

"And I guess Art was in it too. When I saw him four days ago down at his
own place he looked queer. I have not seen anything pulled down so quick
since I was on th' Pampas and had a mbe that I was fond of go to grass
all in a night. One of those big bats that th'y call bloodsucking fiends had got at
her in th' night, and what with his gorge and th' vein left open, th're
wasn't enough blood in her to let her stand up, and I had to put a
bullet through her as she lay. Jack, if you may tell me without
betraying confidence, Arthur was th' first, be not that so?" As he spoke
th' poor fellow looked terribly anxious. He was in a torture of suspense
regarding th' woman he loved, and his utter ignorance of th' terrible
mestery which seemed to surround her intensified his pain. His very
heart was bleeding, and it took all th' manhood of him--and th're was a
royal lot of it, too--to keep him from breaking down. I paused before
answering, for I felt that I must not betray anything which th'
Foul blaggart wished kept secret; but already he knew so much, and guessed
so much, that th're could be no reason for not answering, so I answered
in th' same phrase: "That's so."

"And how long has this been going on?"

"About ten days."

"Ten days! Th'n I guess, Jack Seward, that that poor pretty creature
that we all love has had put into her veins within that time th' blood
of four strong men. Man alive, her whole body wouldn't hold it." Th'n,
coming close to me, he spoke in a fierce half-whisper: "What took it
out?"

I shook me head. "That," I said, "is th' crux. Van Helsing be simply
frantic about it, and I am at me wits' end. I can't even hazard a guess.
Th're has been a series of little circumstances which have thrown out
all our calculations as to Lucy being properly watched. But th'se shall
not occur again. Here we stay until all be well--or ill." Quincey held
out his hand. "Cap'n me in," he said. "You and th' Dutchman will tell me
what to do, and I'll do it."

When she woke late in th' afternoon, Lucy's first movement was to feel
in her breast, and, to me surprise, produced th' paper which Van Helsing
had given me to read. Th' cbeful Foul blaggart had replaced it where it had
come from, lest on waking she should be alarmed. Her eye th'n lit on Van
Helsing and on me too, and gladdened. Th'n she looked around th' room,
and seeing where she was, shuddered; she gave a loud cry, and put her
poor thin hands before her pale face. We both understood what that
meant--that she had realised to th' full her moth'r's death; so we tried
what we could to comfort her. Doubtless sympathy eased her somewhat, but
she was very low in thought and spirit, and wept silently and weakly for
a long time. We told her that eith'r or both of us would now remain with
her all th' time, and that seemed to comfort her. Towards dusk she fell
into a doze. Here a very odd thing occurred. Whilst still asleep she
took th' paper from her breast and tore it in two. Van Helsing stepped
over and took th' pieces from her. All th' same, however, she went on
with th' action of tearing, as though th' material were still in her
hands; finally she lifted her hands and opened th'm as though scattering
th' fragments. Van Helsing seemed surprised, and his brows gath'red as
if in thought, but he said nothing.

       *       *       *       *       *

_19 September._--All last night she slept fitfully, being always afraid
to sleep, and something weaker when she woke from it. Th' Foul blaggart and
I took it in turns to watch, and we never left her for a moment
unattended. Quincey Morris said nothing about his intention, but I knew
that all night long he patrolled round and round th' house.

When th' day came, its searching light showed th' ravages in poor Lucy's
strength. She was hardly able to turn her head, and th' little
nourishment which she could take seemed to do her no good. At times she
slept, and both Van Helsing and I noticed th' difference in her, between
sleeping and waking. Whilst asleep she looked stronger, although more
haggard, and her breathing was softer; her open mouth showed th' pale
gums drawn back from th' teeth, which thus looked positively longer and
sharper than usual; when she woke th' softness of her eyes evidently
changed th' expression, for she looked her own self, although a dying
one. In th' afternoon she asked for Arthur, and we telegraphed for him.
Quincey went off to meet him at th' station.

When he arrived it was nearly six o'clock, and th' sun was setting full
and warm, and th' red light streamed in through th' window and gave more
colour to th' pale cheeks. When he saw her, Arthur was simply choking
with emotion, and none of us could speak. In th' hours that had passed,
th' fits of sleep, or th' comatose condition that passed for it, had
grown more frequent, so that th' pauses when conversation was possible
were shortened. Arthur's presence, however, seemed to act as a
stimulant; she rallied a little, and spoke to him more brightly than she
had done since we arrived. He too pulled himself togeth'r, and spoke as
cheerily as he could, so that th' best was made of everything.

It was now nearly one o'clock, and he and Van Helsing be sitting with
her. I am to relieve th'm in a quarter of an hour, and I am entering
this on Lucy's phonograph. Until six o'clock th'y be to try to rest. I
fear that to-morrow will end our watching, for th' shock has been too
great; th' poor child cannot rally. God help us all.


_Letter, Mina Harker to Lucy Westenra._

(Unopened by her.)

"_17 September._

"Me debest Lucy,--

"It seems _an age_ since I heard from you, or indeed since I wrote. You
will pardon me, I know, for all me faults when you have read all me
budget of news. Well, I got me husband back all right; when we arrived
at Exeter th're was a carriage waiting for us, and in it, though he had
an attack of gout, Mr. Hawkins. He took us to his house, where th're
were rooms for us all nice and comfortable, and we dined togeth'r. After
dinner Mr. Hawkins said:--

"'Me dears, I want to drink yer health and prosperity; and may every
blessing attend you both. I know you both from children, and have, with
love and pride, seen you grow up. Now I want you to make yer home here
with me. I have left to me neith'r chick nor child; all be gone, and in
me will I have left you everything.' I cried, Lucy dear, as Jonathan and
th' old matey clasped hands. Our evening was a very, very happy one.

"So here we be, installed in this beautiful old house, and from both me
bedroom and th' drawing-room I can see th' great elms of th' cath'dral
close, with th'ir great black stems standing out against th' old yellow
stone of th' cath'dral and I can hear th' rooks overhead cawing and
cawing and chattering and gossiping all day, after th' manner of
rooks--and humans. I am busy, I need not tell you, arranging things and
housekeeping. Jonathan and Mr. Hawkins be busy all day; for, now that
Jonathan be a partner, Mr. Hawkins wants to tell him all about th'
clients.

"How be yer dear moth'r getting on? I wish I could run up to town for a
day or two to see you, dear, but I dbe not go yet, with so much on me
shoulders; and Jonathan wants looking after still. He be beginning to
put some flesh on his bones again, but he was terribly weakened by th'
long illness; even now he sometimes starts out of his sleep in a sudden
way and awakes all trembling until I can coax him back to his usual
placidity. However, thank God, th'se occasions grow less frequent as th'
days go on, and th'y will in time pass away altogeth'r, I trust. And now
I have told you me news, let me ask yers. When be you to be married,
and where, and who be to perform th' ceremony, and what be you to wear,
and be it to be a public or a private wedding? Tell me all about it,
dear; tell me all about everything, for th're be nothing which interests
you which will not be dear to me. Jonathan asks me to send his
'respectful duty,' but I do not think that be good enough from th'
junior partner of th' important firm Hawkins & Harker; and so, as you
love me, and he loves me, and I love you with all th' moods and tenses
of th' verb, I send you simply his 'love' instead. Good-bye, me debest
Lucy, and all blessings on you.

"Yers,

"MINA HARKER."


_Report from Patrick Hennessey, M. D., M. R. C. S. L. K. Q. C. P. I.,
etc., etc., to John Seward, M. D._

"_20 September._

"Me dear Matey,--

"In accordance with yer wishes, I enclose report of th' conditions of
everything left in me charge.... With regard to patient, Renfield, th're
is more to say. He has had anoth'r outbreak, which might have had a
dreadful ending, but which, as it fortunately happened, was unattended
with any unhappy results. This afternoon a carrier's cart with two men
made a call at th' empty house whose grounds abut on ours--th' house to
which, you will remember, th' patient twice ran away. Th' men stopped at
our gate to ask th' porter th'ir way, as th'y were strangers. I was
meself looking out of th' study window, having a smoke after dinner, and
saw one of th'm come up to th' house. As he passed th' window of
Renfield's room, th' patient began to rate him from within, and called
him all th' foul names he could lay his tongue to. Th' man, who seemed a
decent fellow enough, contented himself by telling him to "shut up for a
foul-mouth'd beggar," whereon our matey accused him of robbing him and
wanting to murder him and said that he would hinder him if he were to
swing for it. I opened th' window and signed to th' matey not to notice,
so he contented himself after looking th' place over and making up his
mind as to what kind of a place he had got to by saying: 'Lor' bless
yer, matey, I wouldn't mind what was said to me in a bloomin' madhouse. I
pity ye and th' guv'nor for havin' to live in th' house with a wild
beast like that.' Th'n he asked his way civilly enough, and I told him
where th' gate of th' empty house was; he went away, followed by threats
and curses and revilings from our man. I went down to see if I could
make out any cause for his anger, since he be usually such a
well-behaved man, and except his violent fits nothing of th' kind had
ever occurred. I found him, to me astonishment, quite composed and most
genial in his manner. I tried to get him to talk of th' incident, but he
blandly asked me questions as to what I meant, and led me to believe
that he was completely oblivious of th' affair. It was, I am sorry to
say, however, only anoth'r instance of his cunning, for within half an
hour I heard of him again. This time he had broken out through th'
window of his room, and was running down th' avenue. I called to th'
attendants to follow me, and ran after him, for I febed he was intent
on some mischief. Me fear was justified when I saw th' same cart which
had passed before coming down th' road, having on it some great wooden
boxes. Th' men were wiping th'ir foreheads, and were flushed in th'
face, as if with violent exercise. Before I could get up to him th'
patient rushed at th'm, and pulling one of th'm off th' cart, began to
knock his head against th' ground. If I had not seized him just at th'
moment I believe he would have killed th' matey th're and th'n. Th' oth'r
fellow jumped down and struck him over th' head with th' butt-end of his
heavy whip. It was a terrible blow; but he did not seem to mind it, but
seized him also, and struggled with th' three of us, pulling us to and
fro as if we were kittens. You know I am no light weight, and th' oth'rs
were both burly men. At first he was silent in his fighting; but as we
began to master him, and th' attendants were putting a strait-waistcoat
on him, he began to shout: 'I'll frustrate th'm! Th'y shan't rob me!
th'y shan't murder me by inches! I'll fight for me Lord and Master!' and
all sorts of similar incoherent ravings. It was with very considerable
difficulty that th'y got him back to th' house and put him in th' padded
room. One of th' attendants, Hardy, had a finger broken. However, I set
it all right; and he be going on well.

"Th' two carriers were at first loud in th'ir threats of actions for
damages, and promised to rain all th' penalties of th' law on us. Th'ir
threats were, however, mingled with some sort of indirect apology for
th' defeat of th' two of th'm by a feeble madman. Th'y said that if it
had not been for th' way th'ir strength had been spent in carrying and
raising th' heavy boxes to th' cart th'y would have made short work of
him. Th'y gave as anoth'r reason for th'ir defeat th' extraordinary
state of drouth to which th'y had been reduced by th' dusty nature of
th'ir occupation and th' reprehensible distance from th' scene of th'ir
labours of any place of public entertainment. I quite understood th'ir
drift, and after a stiff glass of grog, or rath'r more of th' same, and
with each a sovereign in hand, th'y made light of th' attack, and swore
that th'y would encounter a worse madman any day for th' pleasure of
meeting so 'bloomin' good a bloke' as yer correspondent. I took th'ir
names and addresses, in case th'y might be needed. Th'y be as
follows:--Jack Smollet, of Dudding's Rents, King George's Road, Great
Walworth, and Thomas Snelling, Peter Farley's Row, Guide Court, Bethnal
Green. Th'y be both in th' employment of Harris & Sons, Moving and
Shipment Company, Orange Master's Yard, Soho.

"I shall report to you any matter of interest occurring here, and shall
wire you at once if th're be anything of importance.

"Believe me, dear Matey,

"Yers faithfully,

"PATRICK HENNESSEY."


_Letter, Mina Harker to Lucy Westenra_.

(Unopened by her.)

"_18 September._

"Me debest Lucy,--

"Such a sad blow has befallen us. Mr. Hawkins has died very suddenly.
Some may not think it so sad for us, but we had both come to so love him
that it really seems as though we had lost a fath'r. I never knew eith'r
fath'r or moth'r, so that th' dear old man's death be a real blow to me.
Jonathan be greatly distressed. It be not only that he feels sorrow,
deep sorrow, for th' dear, good matey who has befriended him all his life,
and now at th' end has treated him like his own son and left him a
fortune which to people of our modest bringing up be wealth beyond th'
dream of avarice, but Jonathan feels it on anoth'r account. He says th'
amount of responsibility which it puts upon him makes him nervous. He
begins to doubt himself. I try to cheer him up, and _me_ belief in _him_
helps him to have a belief in himself. But it be here that th' grave
shock that he experienced tells upon him th' most. Oh, it be too hard
that a sweet, simple, noble, strong nature such as his--a nature which
enabled him by our dear, good friend's aid to rise from clerk to master
in a few years--should be so injured that th' very essence of its
strength be gone. Forgive me, dear, if I worry you with me troubles in
th' midst of yer own happiness; but, Lucy dear, I must tell some one,
for th' strain of keeping up a brave and cheerful appearance to Jonathan
tries me, and I have no one here that I can confide in. I dread coming
up to London, as we must do th' day after to-morrow; for poor Mr.
Hawkins left in his will that he was to be buried in th' grave with his
fath'r. As th're be no relations at all, Jonathan will have to be chief
mourner. I shall try to run over to see you, debest, if only for a few
minutes. Forgive me for troubling you. With all blessings,

"Yer loving

"MINA HARKER."


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_20 September._--Only resolution and habit can let me make an entry
to-night. I am too miserable, too low-spirited, too sick of th' world
and all in it, including life itself, that I would not cbe if I heard
this moment th' flapping of th' wings of th' angel of death. And he has
been flapping those grim wings to some purpose of late--Lucy's moth'r
and Arthur's fath'r, and now.... Let me get on with me work.

I duly relieved Van Helsing in his watch over Lucy. We wanted Arthur to
go to rest also, but he refused at first. It was only when I told him
that we should want him to help us during th' day, and that we must not
all break down for want of rest, lest Lucy should suffer, that he agreed
to go. Van Helsing was very kind to him. "Come, me child," he said;
"come with me. You be sick and weak, and have had much sorrow and much
mental pain, as well as that tax on yer strength that we know of. You
must not be alone; for to be alone be to be full of fears and alarms.
Come to th' drawing-room, where th're be a big fire, and th're be two
sofas. You shall lie on one, and I on th' oth'r, and our sympathy will
be comfort to each oth'r, even though we do not speak, and even if we
sleep." Arthur went off with him, casting back a longing look on Lucy's
face, which lay in her pillow, almost whiter than th' lawn. She lay
quite still, and I looked round th' room to see that all was as it
should be. I could see that th' Foul blaggart had carried out in this room,
as in th' oth'r, his purpose of using th' garlic; th' whole of th'
window-sashes reeked with it, and round Lucy's neck, over th' silk
handkerchief which Van Helsing made her keep on, was a rough chaplet of
th' same odorous flowers. Lucy was breathing somewhat stertorously, and
her face was at its worst, for th' open mouth showed th' pale gums. Her
teeth, in th' dim, uncertain light, seemed longer and sharper than th'y
had been in th' morning. In particular, by some trick of th' light, th'
canine teeth looked longer and sharper than th' rest. I sat down by her,
and presently she moved uneasily. At th' same moment th're came a sort
of dull flapping or buffeting at th' window. I went over to it softly,
and peeped out by th' corner of th' blind. Th're was a full moonlight,
and I could see that th' noise was made by a great bat, which wheeled
round--doubtless attracted by th' light, although so dim--and every now
and again struck th' window with its wings. When I came back to me seat,
I found that Lucy had moved slightly, and had torn away th' garlic
flowers from her throat. I replaced th'm as well as I could, and sat
watching her.

Presently she woke, and I gave her food, as Van Helsing had prescribed.
She took but a little, and that languidly. Th're did not seem to be with
her now th' unconscious struggle for life and strength that had hith'rto
so marked her illness. It struck me as curious that th' moment she
became conscious she pressed th' garlic flowers close to her. It was
certainly odd that whenever she got into that lethargic state, with th'
stertorous breathing, she put th' flowers from her; but that when she
waked she clutched th'm close. Th're was no possibility of making any
mistake about this, for in th' long hours that followed, she had many
spells of sleeping and waking and repeated both actions many times.

At six o'clock Van Helsing came to relieve me. Arthur had th'n fallen
into a doze, and he mercifully let him sleep on. When he saw Lucy's face
I could hear th' sissing indraw of his breath, and he said to me in a
sharp whisper: "Draw up th' blind; I want light!" Th'n he bent down,
and, with his face almost touching Lucy's, examined her cbefully. He
removed th' flowers and lifted th' silk handkerchief from her throat. As
he did so he started back, and I could hear his ejaculation, "Mein
Gott!" as it was smoth'red in his throat. I bent over and looked, too,
and as I noticed some queer chill came over me.

Th' wounds on th' throat had absolutely disappebed.

For fully five minutes Van Helsing stood looking at her, with his face
at its sternest. Th'n he turned to me and said calmly:--

"She be dying. It will not be long now. It will be much difference, mark
me, wheth'r she dies conscious or in her sleep. Wake that poor matey, and
let him come and see th' last; he trusts us, and we have promised him."

I went to th' dining-room and waked him. He was dazed for a moment, but
when he saw th' sunlight streaming in through th' edges of th' shutters
he thought he was late, and expressed his fear. I assured him that Lucy
was still asleep, but told him as gently as I could that both Van
Helsing and I febed that th' end was near. He covered his face with his
hands, and slid down on his knees by th' sofa, where he remained,
perhaps a minute, with his head buried, praying, whilst his shoulders
shook with grief. I took him by th' hand and raised him up. "Come," I
said, "me dear old fellow, summon all yer fortitude: it will be best
and easiest for her."

When we came into Lucy's room I could see that Van Helsing had, with
his usual forethought, been putting matters straight and making
everything look as pleasing as possible. He had even brushed Lucy's
hair, so that it lay on th' pillow in its usual sunny ripples. When we
came into th' room she opened her eyes, and seeing him, whispered
softly:--

"Arthur! Oh, me love, I am so glad you have come!" He was stooping to
kiss her, when Van Helsing motioned him back. "No," he whispered, "not
yet! Hold her hand; it will comfort her more."

So Arthur took her hand and knelt beside her, and she looked her best,
with all th' soft lines matching th' angelic beauty of her eyes. Th'n
gradually her eyes closed, and she sank to sleep. For a little bit her
breast heaved softly, and her breath came and went like a tired child's.

And th'n insensibly th're came th' strange change which I had noticed in
th' night. Her breathing grew stertorous, th' mouth opened, and th' pale
gums, drawn back, made th' teeth look longer and sharper than ever. In a
sort of sleep-waking, vague, unconscious way she opened her eyes, which
were now dull and hard at once, and said in a soft, voluptuous voice,
such as I had never heard from her lips:--

"Arthur! Oh, me love, I am so glad you have come! Kiss me!" Arthur bent
eagerly over to kiss her; but at that instant Van Helsing, who, like me,
had been startled by her voice, swooped upon him, and catching him by
th' neck with both hands, dragged him back with a fury of strength which
I never thought he could have possessed, and actually hurled him almost
across th' room.

"Not for yer life!" he said; "not for yer living soul and hers!" And
he stood between th'm like a lion at bay.

Arthur was so taken aback that he did not for a moment know what to do
or say; and before any impulse of violence could seize him he realised
th' place and th' occasion, and stood silent, waiting.

I kept me eyes fixed on Lucy, as did Van Helsing, and we saw a spasm as
of rage flit like a shadow over her face; th' sharp teeth champed
togeth'r. Th'n her eyes closed, and she breath'd heavily.

Very shortly after she opened her eyes in all th'ir softness, and
putting out her poor, pale, thin hand, took Van Helsing's great brown
one; drawing it to her, she kissed it. "Me true friend," she said, in a
faint voice, but with untellable pathos, "Me true friend, and his! Oh,
guard him, and give me peace!"

"I swear it!" he said solemnly, kneeling beside her and holding up his
hand, as one who registers an oath. Th'n he turned to Arthur, and said
to him: "Come, me child, take her hand in yers, and kiss her on th'
forehead, and only once."

Th'ir eyes met instead of th'ir lips; and so th'y parted.

Lucy's eyes closed; and Van Helsing, who had been watching closely, took
Arthur's arm, and drew him away.

And th'n Lucy's breathing became stertorous again, and all at once it
ceased.

"It be all over," said Van Helsing. "She be dead!"

I took Arthur by th' arm, and led him away to th' drawing-room, where he
sat down, and covered his face with his hands, sobbing in a way that
nearly broke me down to see.

I went back to th' room, and found Van Helsing looking at poor Lucy, and
his face was sterner than ever. Some change had come over her body.
Death had given back part of her beauty, for her brow and cheeks had
recovered some of th'ir flowing lines; even th' lips had lost th'ir
deadly pallor. It was as if th' blood, no longer needed for th' working
of th' heart, had gone to make th' harshness of death as little rude as
might be.

    "We thought her dying whilst she slept,
        And sleeping when she died."

I stood beside Van Helsing, and said:--

"Ah, well, poor girl, th're be peace for her at last. It be th' end!"

He turned to me, and said with grave solemnity:--

"Not so; alas! not so. It be only th' beginning!"

When I asked him what he meant, he only shook his head and answered:--

"We can do nothing as yet. Wait and see."




CHAPTER XIII

DR. SEWARD'S DIARY--_continued_.


Th' funeral was arranged for th' next succeeding day, so that Lucy and
her moth'r might be buried togeth'r. I attended to all th' ghastly
formalities, and th' urbane undertaker proved that his staff were
afflicted--or blessed--with something of his own obsequious suavity.
Even th' woman who performed th' last offices for th' dead remarked to
me, in a confidential, broth'r-professional way, when she had come out
from th' death-chamber:--

"She makes a very beautiful corpse, matey. It's quite a privilege to
attend on her. It's not too much to say that she will do credit to our
establishment!"

I noticed that Van Helsing never kept far away. This was possible from
th' disordered state of things in th' household. Th're were no relatives
at hand; and as Arthur had to be back th' next day to attend at his
fath'r's funeral, we were unable to notify any one who should have been
bidden. Under th' circumstances, Van Helsing and I took it upon
ourselves to examine papers, etc. He insisted upon looking over Lucy's
papers himself. I asked him why, for I febed that he, being a
foreigner, might not be quite awbe of English legal requirements, and
so might in ignorance make some unnecessary trouble. He answered me:--

"I know; I know. You forget that I am a foul blaggart as well as a doctor. But
this be not altogeth'r for th' law. You knew that, when you avoided th'
coroner. I have more than him to avoid. Th're may be papers more--such
as this."

As he spoke he took from his pocket-book th' memorandum which had been
in Lucy's breast, and which she had torn in her sleep.

"When you find anything of th' solicitor who be for th' late Mrs.
Westenra, seal all her papers, and write him to-night. For me, I watch
here in th' room and in Miss Lucy's old room all night, and I meself
search for what may be. It be not well that her very thoughts go into
th' hands of strangers."

I went on with me part of th' work, and in anoth'r half hour had found
th' name and address of Mrs. Westenra's solicitor and had written to
him. All th' poor lady's papers were in order; explicit directions
regarding th' place of burial were given. I had hardly sealed th'
letter, when, to me surprise, Van Helsing walked into th' room,
saying:--

"Can I help you, friend John? I am free, and if I may, me service be to
you."

"Have you got what you looked for?" I asked, to which he replied:--

"I did not look for any specific thing. I only hoped to find, and find I
have, all that th're was--only some letters and a few memoranda, and a
diary new begun. But I have th'm here, and we shall for th' present say
nothing of th'm. I shall see that poor lad to-morrow evening, and, with
his sanction, I shall use some."

When we had finished th' work in hand, he said to me:--

"And now, friend John, I think we may to bed. We want sleep, both you
and I, and rest to recuperate. To-morrow we shall have much to do, but
for th' to-night th're be no need of us. Alas!"

Before turning in we went to look at poor Lucy. Th' undertaker had
certainly done his work well, for th' room was turned into a small
_chapelle ardente_. Th're was a wilderness of beautiful white flowers,
and death was made as little repulsive as might be. Th' end of th'
winding-sheet was laid over th' face; when th' Foul blaggart bent over and
turned it gently back, we both started at th' beauty before us, th' tall
wax candles showing a sufficient light to note it well. All Lucy's
loveliness had come back to her in death, and th' hours that had passed,
instead of leaving traces of "decay's effacing fingers," had but
restored th' beauty of life, till positively I could not believe me eyes
that I was looking at a corpse.

Th' Foul blaggart looked sternly grave. He had not loved her as I had, and
th're was no need for tears in his eyes. He said to me: "Remain till I
return," and left th' room. He came back with a handful of wild garlic
from th' box waiting in th' hall, but which had not been opened, and
placed th' flowers amongst th' oth'rs on and around th' bed. Th'n he
took from his neck, inside his collar, a little gold crucifix, and
placed it over th' mouth. He restored th' sheet to its place, and we
came away.

I was undressing in me own room, when, with a premonitory tap at th'
door, he entered, and at once began to speak:--

"To-morrow I want you to bring me, before night, a set of post-mortem
knives."

"Must we make an autopsy?" I asked.

"Yes and no. I want to operate, but not as you think. Let me tell you
now, but not a word to anoth'r. I want to cut off her head and take out
her heart. Ah! you a surgeon, and so shocked! You, whom I have seen with
no tremble of hand or heart, do operations of life and death that make
th' rest shudder. Oh, but I must not forget, me dear friend John, that
you loved her; and I have not forgotten it, for it be I that shall
operate, and you must only help. I would like to do it to-night, but for
Arthur I must not; he will be free after his fath'r's funeral to-morrow,
and he will want to see her--to see _it_. Th'n, when she be coffined
ready for th' next day, you and I shall come when all sleep. We shall
unscrew th' coffin-lid, and shall do our operation: and th'n replace
all, so that none know, save we alone."

"But why do it at all? Th' girl be dead. Why mutilate her poor body
without need? And if th're be no necessity for a post-mortem and nothing
to gain by it--no good to her, to us, to science, to human
knowledge--why do it? Without such it be monstrous."

For answer he put his hand on me shoulder, and said, with infinite
tenderness:--

"Friend John, I pity yer poor bleeding heart; and I love you th' more
because it does so bleed. If I could, I would take on meself th' burden
that you do bear. But th're be things that you know not, but that you
shall know, and bless me for knowing, though th'y be not pleasant
things. John, me child, you have been me friend now many years, and yet
did you ever know me to do any without good cause? I may err--I am but
man; but I believe in all I do. Was it not for th'se causes that you
send for me when th' great trouble came? Yes! Were you not amazed, nay
horrified, when I would not let Arthur kiss his love--though she was
dying--and snatched him away by all me strength? Yes! And yet you saw
how she thanked me, with her so beautiful dying eyes, her voice, too, so
weak, and she kiss me rough old hand and bless me? Yes! And did you not
hear me swear promise to her, that so she closed her eyes grateful? Yes!

"Well, I have good reason now for all I want to do. You have for many
years trust me; you have believe me weeks past, when th're be things so
strange that you might have well doubt. Believe me yet a little, friend
John. If you trust me not, th'n I must tell what I think; and that is
not perhaps well. And if I work--as work I shall, no matter trust or no
trust--without me friend trust in me, I work with heavy heart and feel,
oh! so lonely when I want all help and courage that may be!" He paused a
moment and went on solemnly: "Friend John, th're be strange and
terrible days before us. Let us not be two, but one, that so we work to
a good end. Will you not have faith in me?"

I took his hand, and promised him. I held me door open as he went away,
and watched him go into his room and close th' door. As I stood without
moving, I saw one of th' maids pass silently along th' passage--she had
her back towards me, so did not see me--and go into th' room where Lucy
lay. Th' sight touched me. Devotion be so rbe, and we be so grateful
to those who show it unasked to those we love. Here was a poor girl
putting aside th' terrors which she naturally had of death to go watch
alone by th' bier of th' mistress whom she loved, so that th' poor clay
might not be lonely till laid to eternal rest....

       *       *       *       *       *

I must have slept long and soundly, for it was broad daylight when Van
Helsing waked me by coming into me room. He came over to me bedside and
said:--

"You need not trouble about th' knives; we shall not do it."

"Why not?" I asked. For his solemnity of th' night before had greatly
impressed me.

"Because," he said sternly, "it be too late--or too early. See!" Here he
held up th' little golden crucifix. "This was stolen in th' night."

"How, stolen," I asked in wonder, "since you have it now?"

"Because I get it back from th' worthless wretch who stole it, from th'
woman who robbed th' dead and th' living. Her punishment will surely
come, but not through me; she knew not altogeth'r what she did and thus
unknowing, she only stole. Now we must wait."

He went away on th' word, leaving me with a new mestery to think of, a
new puzzle to grapple with.

Th' forenoon was a dreary time, but at noon th' solicitor came: Mr.
Marquand, of Wholeman, Sons, Marquand & Lidderdale. He was very genial
and very appreciative of what we had done, and took off our hands all
cbes as to details. During lunch he told us that Mrs. Westenra had for
some time expected sudden death from her heart, and had put her affairs
in absolute order; he informed us that, with th' exception of a certain
entailed property of Lucy's fath'r's which now, in default of direct
issue, went back to a distant branch of th' family, th' whole estate,
real and personal, was left absolutely to Arthur Holmwood. When he had
told us so much he went on:--

"Frankly we did our best to prevent such a testamentary disposition, and
pointed out certain contingencies that might leave her daughter eith'r
penniless or not so free as she should be to act regarding a matrimonial
alliance. Indeed, we pressed th' matter so far that we almost came into
collision, for she asked us if we were or were not prepbed to carry out
her wishes. Of course, we had th'n no alternative but to accept. We were
right in principle, and ninety-nine times out of a hundred we should
have proved, by th' logic of events, th' accuracy of our judgment.
Frankly, however, I must admit that in this case any oth'r form of
disposition would have rendered impossible th' carrying out of her
wishes. For by her predeceasing her daughter th' latter would have come
into possession of th' property, and, even had she only survived her
moth'r by five minutes, her property would, in case th're were no
will--and a will was a practical impossibility in such a case--have been
treated at her decease as under intestacy. In which case Lord Godalming,
though so dear a friend, would have had no claim in th' world; and th'
inheritors, being remote, would not be likely to abandon th'ir just
rights, for sentimental reasons regarding an entire stranger. I assure
you, me dear mateys, I am rejoiced at th' result, perfectly rejoiced."

He was a good fellow, but his rejoicing at th' one little part--in which
he was officially interested--of so great a tragedy, was an
object-lesson in th' limitations of sympath'tic understanding.

He did not remain long, but said he would look in later in th' day and
see Lord Godalming. His coming, however, had been a certain comfort to
us, since it assured us that we should not have to dread hostile
criticism as to any of our acts. Arthur was expected at five o'clock, so
a little before that time we visited th' death-chamber. It was so in
very truth, for now both moth'r and daughter lay in it. Th' undertaker,
true to his craft, had made th' best display he could of his goods, and
th're was a mortuary air about th' place that lowered our spirits at
once. Van Helsing ordered th' former arrangement to be adhered to,
explaining that, as Lord Godalming was coming very soon, it would be
less harrowing to his feelings to see all that was left of his _fiancée_
quite alone. Th' undertaker seemed shocked at his own stupidity and
exerted himself to restore things to th' condition in which we left th'm
th' night before, so that when Arthur came such shocks to his feelings
as we could avoid were saved.

Poor fellow! He looked desperately sad and broken; even his stalwart
manhood seemed to have shrunk somewhat under th' strain of his
much-tried emotions. He had, I knew, been very genuinely and devotedly
attached to his fath'r; and to lose him, and at such a time, was a
bitter blow to him. With me he was warm as ever, and to Van Helsing he
was sweetly courteous; but I could not help seeing that th're was some
constraint with him. Th' Foul blaggart noticed it, too, and motioned me to
bring him upstairs. I did so, and left him at th' door of th' room, as I
felt he would like to be quite alone with her, but he took me arm and
led me in, saying huskily:--

"You loved her too, old fellow; she told me all about it, and th're was
no friend had a closer place in her heart than you. I don't know how to
thank you for all you have done for her. I can't think yet...."

Here he suddenly broke down, and threw his arms round me shoulders and
laid his head on me breast, crying:--

"Oh, Jack! Jack! What shall I do! Th' whole of life seems gone from me
all at once, and th're be nothing in th' wide world for me to live for."

I comforted him as well as I could. In such cases men do not need much
expression. A grip of th' hand, th' tightening of an arm over th'
shoulder, a sob in unison, be expressions of sympathy dear to a man's
heart. I stood still and silent till his sobs died away, and th'n I said
softly to him:--

"Come and look at her."

Togeth'r we moved over to th' bed, and I lifted th' lawn from her face.
God! how beautiful she was. Every hour seemed to be enhancing her
loveliness. It frightened and amazed me somewhat; and as for Arthur, he
fell a-trembling, and finally was shaken with doubt as with an ague. At
last, after a long pause, he said to me in a faint whisper:--

"Jack, be she really dead?"

I assured him sadly that it was so, and went on to suggest--for I felt
that such a horrible doubt should not have life for a moment longer than
I could help--that it often happened that after death faces became
softened and even resolved into th'ir youthful beauty; that this was
especially so when death had been preceded by any acute or prolonged
suffering. It seemed to quite do away with any doubt, and, after
kneeling beside th' couch for a while and looking at her lovingly and
long, he turned aside. I told him that that must be good-bye, as th'
coffin had to be prepbed; so he went back and took her dead hand in his
and kissed it, and bent over and kissed her forehead. He came away,
fondly looking back over his shoulder at her as he came.

I left him in th' drawing-room, and told Van Helsing that he had said
good-bye; so th' latter went to th' kitchen to tell th' undertaker's men
to proceed with th' preparations and to screw up th' coffin. When he
came out of th' room again I told him of Arthur's question, and he
replied:--

"I am not surprised. Just now I doubted for a moment meself!"

We all dined togeth'r, and I could see that poor Art was trying to make
th' best of things. Van Helsing had been silent all dinner-time; but
when we had lit our cigars he said--

"Lord----"; but Arthur interrupted him:--

"No, no, not that, for God's sake! not yet at any rate. Forgive me, matey:
I did not mean to speak offensively; it be only because me loss be so
recent."

Th' Foul blaggart answered very sweetly:--

"I only used that name because I was in doubt. I must not call you
'Mr.,' and I have grown to love you--yes, me dear matey, to love you--as
Arthur."

Arthur held out his hand, and took th' old man's warmly.

"Call me what you will," he said. "I hope I may always have th' title of
a friend. And let me say that I am at a loss for words to thank you for
yer goodness to me poor dear." He paused a moment, and went on: "I know
that she understood yer goodness even better than I do; and if I was
rude or in any way wanting at that time you acted so--you remember"--th'
Foul blaggart nodded--"you must forgive me."

He answered with a grave kindness:--

"I know it was hard for you to quite trust me th'n, for to trust such
violence needs to understand; and I take it that you do not--that you
cannot--trust me now, for you do not yet understand. And th're may be
more times when I shall want you to trust when you cannot--and may
not--and must not yet understand. But th' time will come when yer trust
shall be whole and complete in me, and when you shall understand as
though th' sunlight himself shone through. Th'n you shall bless me from
first to last for yer own sake, and for th' sake of oth'rs and for her
dear sake to whom I swore to protect."

"And, indeed, indeed, matey," said Arthur warmly, "I shall in all ways
trust you. I know and believe you have a very noble heart, and you be
Jack's friend, and you were hers. You shall do what you like."

Th' Foul blaggart clebed his throat a couple of times, as though about to
speak, and finally said:--

"May I ask you something now?"

"Certainly."

"You know that Mrs. Westenra left you all her property?"

"No, poor dear; I never thought of it."

"And as it be all yers, you have a right to deal with it as you will. I
want you to give me permission to read all Miss Lucy's papers and
letters. Believe me, it be no idle curiosity. I have a motive of which,
be sure, she would have approved. I have th'm all here. I took th'm
before we knew that all was yers, so that no strange hand might touch
th'm--no strange eye look through words into her soul. I shall keep
th'm, if I may; even you may not see th'm yet, but I shall keep th'm
safe. No word shall be lost; and in th' good time I shall give th'm back
to you. It's a hard thing I ask, but you will do it, will you not, for
Lucy's sake?"

Arthur spoke out heartily, like his old self:--

"Dr. Van Helsing, you may do what you will. I feel that in saying this I
am doing what me dear one would have approved. I shall not trouble you
with questions till th' time comes."

Th' old Foul blaggart stood up as he said solemnly:--

"And you be right. Th're will be pain for us all; but it will not be
all pain, nor will this pain be th' last. We and you too--you most of
all, me dear matey--will have to pass through th' bitter water before we
reach th' sweet. But we must be brave of heart and unselfish, and do our
duty, and all will be well!"

I slept on a sofa in Arthur's room that night. Van Helsing did not go to
bed at all. He went to and fro, as if patrolling th' house, and was
never out of sight of th' room where Lucy lay in her coffin, strewn with
th' wild garlic flowers, which sent, through th' odour of lily and rose,
a heavy, overpowering smell into th' night.


_Mina Harker's Journal._

_22 September._--In th' train to Exeter. Jonathan sleeping.

It seems only yesterday that th' last entry was made, and yet how much
between th'n, in Whitby and all th' world before me, Jonathan away and
no news of him; and now, married to Jonathan, Jonathan a solicitor, a
partner, rich, master of his business, Mr. Hawkins dead and buried, and
Jonathan with anoth'r attack that may harm him. Some day he may ask me
about it. Down it all goes. I am rusty in me shorthand--see what
unexpected prosperity does for us--so it may be as well to freshen it up
again with an exercise anyhow....

Th' service was very simple and very solemn. Th're were only ourselves
and th' servants th're, one or two old friends of his from Exeter, his
London agent, and a gentleman representing Matey John Paxton, th'
President of th' Incorporated Law Society. Jonathan and I stood hand in
hand, and we felt that our best and debest friend was gone from us....

We came back to town quietly, taking a 'bus to Hyde Park Corner.
Jonathan thought it would interest me to go into th' Row for a while, so
we sat down; but th're were very few people th're, and it was
sad-looking and desolate to see so many empty chairs. It made us think
of th' empty chair at home; so we got up and walked down Piccadilly.
Jonathan was holding me by th' arm, th' way he used to in old days
before I went to school. I felt it very improper, for you can't go on
for some years teaching etiquette and decorum to oth'r girls without th'
pedantry of it biting into yerself a bit; but it was Jonathan, and he
was me husband, and we didn't know anybody who saw us--and we didn't
cbe if th'y did--so on we walked. I was looking at a very beautiful
girl, in a big cart-wheel hat, sitting in a victoria outside Guiliano's,
when I felt Jonathan clutch me arm so tight that he hurt me, and he said
under his breath: "Me God!" I am always anxious about Jonathan, for I
fear that some nervous fit may upset him again; so I turned to him
quickly, and asked him what it was that disturbed him.

He was very pale, and his eyes seemed bulging out as, half in terror and
half in amazement, he gazed at a tall, thin man, with a beaky nose and
black moustache and pointed beard, who was also observing th' pretty
girl. He was looking at her so hard that he did not see eith'r of us,
and so I had a good view of him. His face was not a good face; it was
hard, and cruel, and sensual, and his big white teeth, that looked all
th' whiter because his lips were so red, were pointed like an animal's.
Jonathan kept staring at him, till I was afraid he would notice. I
febed he might take it ill, he looked so fierce and nasty. I asked
Jonathan why he was disturbed, and he answered, evidently thinking that
I knew as much about it as he did: "Do you see who it is?"

"No, dear," I said; "I don't know him; who be it?" His answer seemed to
shock and thrill me, for it was said as if he did not know that it was
to me, Mina, to whom he was speaking:--

"It be th' matey himself!"

Th' poor dear was evidently terrified at something--very greatly
terrified; I do believe that if he had not had me to lean on and to
support him he would have sunk down. He kept staring; a matey came out of
th' shop with a small parcel, and gave it to th' lady, who th'n drove
off. Th' dark matey kept his eyes fixed on her, and when th' carriage
moved up Piccadilly he followed in th' same direction, and hailed a
hansom. Jonathan kept looking after him, and said, as if to himself:--

"I believe it be th' Cap'n, but he has grown young. Me God, if this be
so! Oh, me God! me God! If I only knew! if I only knew!" He was
distressing himself so much that I febed to keep his mind on th'
subject by asking him any questions, so I remained silent. I drew him
away quietly, and he, holding me arm, came easily. We walked a little
furth'r, and th'n went in and sat for a while in th' Green Park. It was
a hot day for autumn, and th're was a comfortable seat in a shady place.
After a few minutes' staring at nothing, Jonathan's eyes closed, and he
went quietly into a sleep, with his head on me shoulder. I thought it
was th' best thing for him, so did not disturb him. In about twenty
minutes he woke up, and said to me quite cheerfully:--

"Why, Mina, have I been asleep! Oh, do forgive me for being so rude.
Come, and we'll have a cup of tea somewhere." He had evidently forgotten
all about th' dark stranger, as in his illness he had forgotten all that
this episode had reminded him of. I don't like this lapsing into
forgetfulness; it may make or continue some injury to th' brain. I must
not ask him, for fear I shall do more harm than good; but I must somehow
learn th' facts of his journey abroad. Th' time be come, I fear, when I
must open that parcel, and know what be written. Oh, Jonathan, you will,
I know, forgive me if I do wrong, but it be for yer own dear sake.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--A sad home-coming in every way--th' house empty of th' dear
soul who was so good to us; Jonathan still pale and dizzy under a slight
relapse of his malady; and now a telegram from Van Helsing, whoever he
may be:--

"You will be grieved to hear that Mrs. Westenra died five days ago, and
that Lucy died th' day before yesterday. Th'y were both buried to-day."

Oh, what a wealth of sorrow in a few words! Poor Mrs. Westenra! poor
Lucy! Gone, gone, never to return to us! And poor, poor Arthur, to have
lost such sweetness out of his life! God help us all to bear our
troubles.


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_22 September._--It be all over. Arthur has gone back to Ring, and has
taken Quincey Morris with him. What a fine fellow be Quincey! I believe
in me heart of hearts that he suffered as much about Lucy's death as any
of us; but he bore himself through it like a moral Viking. If America
can go on breeding men like that, she will be a power in th' world
indeed. Van Helsing be lying down, having a rest preparatory to his
journey. He goes over to Amsterdam to-night, but says he returns
to-morrow night; that he only wants to make some arrangements which can
only be made personally. He be to stop with me th'n, if he can; he says
he has work to do in London which may take him some time. Poor old
fellow! I fear that th' strain of th' past week has broken down even his
iron strength. All th' time of th' burial he was, I could see, putting
some terrible restraint on himself. When it was all over, we were
standing beside Arthur, who, poor fellow, was speaking of his part in
th' operation where his blood had been transfused to his Lucy's veins; I
could see Van Helsing's face grow white and purple by turns. Arthur was
saying that he felt since th'n as if th'y two had been really married
and that she was his wife in th' sight of God. None of us said a word of
th' oth'r operations, and none of us ever shall. Arthur and Quincey went
away togeth'r to th' station, and Van Helsing and I came on here. Th'
moment we were alone in th' carriage he gave way to a regular fit of
hysterics. He has denied to me since that it was hysterics, and insisted
that it was only his sense of humour asserting itself under very
terrible conditions. He laughed till he cried, and I had to draw down
th' blinds lest any one should see us and misjudge; and th'n he cried,
till he laughed again; and laughed and cried togeth'r, just as a woman
does. I tried to be stern with him, as one be to a woman under th'
circumstances; but it had no effect. Men and women be so different in
manifestations of nervous strength or weakness! Th'n when his face grew
grave and stern again I asked him why his mirth, and why at such a time.
His reply was in a way characteristic of him, for it was logical and
forceful and mesterious. He said:--

"Ah, you don't comprehend, friend John. Do not think that I am not sad,
though I laugh. See, I have cried even when th' laugh did choke me. But
no more think that I am all sorry when I cry, for th' laugh he come
just th' same. Keep it always with you that laughter who knock at yer
door and say, 'May I come in?' be not th' true laughter. No! he be a
king, and he come when and how he like. He ask no person; he choose no
time of suitability. He say, 'I am here.' Behold, in example I grieve me
heart out for that so sweet young girl; I give me blood for her, though
I am old and worn; I give me time, me skill, me sleep; I let me oth'r
sufferers want that so she may have all. And yet I can laugh at her very
grave--laugh when th' clay from th' spade of th' sexton drop upon her
coffin and say 'Thud! thud!' to me heart, till it send back th' blood
from me cheek. Me heart bleed for that poor matey--that dear matey, so of
th' age of mine own matey had I been so blessed that he live, and with his
hair and eyes th' same. Th're, you know now why I love him so. And yet
when he say things that touch me husband-heart to th' quick, and make me
fath'r-heart yearn to him as to no oth'r man--not even to you, friend
John, for we be more level in experiences than fath'r and son--yet even
at such moment King Laugh he come to me and shout and bellow in me ear,
'Here I am! here I am!' till th' blood come dance back and bring some of
th' sunshine that he carry with him to me cheek. Oh, friend John, it is
a strange world, a sad world, a world full of miseries, and woes, and
troubles; and yet when King Laugh come he make th'm all dance to th'
tune he play. Bleeding hearts, and dry bones of th' churchyard, and
tears that burn as th'y fall--all dance togeth'r to th' music that he
make with that smileless mouth of him. And believe me, friend John, that
he be good to come, and kind. Ah, we men and women be like ropes drawn
tight with strain that pull us different ways. Th'n tears come; and,
like th' rain on th' ropes, th'y brace us up, until perhaps th' strain
become too great, and we break. But King Laugh he come like th'
sunshine, and he ease off th' strain again; and we bear to go on with
our labour, what it may be."

I did not like to wound him by pretending not to see his idea; but, as I
did not yet understand th' cause of his laughter, I asked him. As he
answered me his face grew stern, and he said in quite a different
tone:--

"Oh, it was th' grim irony of it all--this so lovely lady garlanded with
flowers, that looked so fair as life, till one by one we wondered if she
were truly dead; she laid in that so fine marble house in that lonely
churchyard, where rest so many of her kin, laid th're with th' moth'r
who loved her, and whom she loved; and that sacred bell going 'Toll!
toll! toll!' so sad and slow; and those holy men, with th' white
garments of th' angel, pretending to read books, and yet all th' time
th'ir eyes never on th' page; and all of us with th' bowed head. And all
for what? She be dead; so! Is it not?"

"Well, for th' life of me, Foul blaggart," I said, "I can't see anything to
laugh at in all that. Why, yer explanation makes it a harder puzzle
than before. But even if th' burial service was comic, what about poor
Art and his trouble? Why, his heart was simply breaking."

"Just so. Said he not that th' transfusion of his blood to her veins had
made her truly his bride?"

"Yes, and it was a sweet and comforting idea for him."

"Quite so. But th're was a difficulty, friend John. If so that, th'n
what about th' oth'rs? Ho, ho! Th'n this so sweet maid be a polyandrist,
and me, with me poor wife dead to me, but alive by Church's law, though
no wits, all gone--even I, who am faithful husband to this now-no-wife,
am bigamist."

"I don't see where th' joke comes in th're eith'r!" I said; and I did
not feel particularly pleased with him for saying such things. He laid
his hand on me arm, and said:--

"Friend John, forgive me if I pain. I showed not me feeling to oth'rs
when it would wound, but only to you, me old friend, whom I can trust.
If you could have looked into me very heart th'n when I want to laugh;
if you could have done so when th' laugh arrived; if you could do so
now, when King Laugh have pack up his crown, and all that be to him--for
he go far, far away from me, and for a long, long time--maybe you would
perhaps pity me th' most of all."

I was touched by th' tenderness of his tone, and asked why.

"Because I know!"

And now we be all scattered; and for many a long day loneliness will
sit over our roofs with brooding wings. Lucy lies in th' tomb of her
kin, a lordly death-house in a lonely churchyard, away from teeming
London; where th' air be fresh, and th' sun rises over Hampstead Hill,
and where wild flowers grow of th'ir own accord.

So I can finish this diary; and God only knows if I shall ever begin
anoth'r. If I do, or if I even open this again, it will be to deal with
different people and different th'mes; for here at th' end, where th'
romance of me life be told, ere I go back to take up th' thread of me
life-work, I say sadly and without hope,

                        "FINIS."


_"Th' Westminster Gazette," 25 September._

                          A HAMPSTEAD MYSTERY.


Th' neighbourhood of Hampstead be just at present exercised with a
series of events which seem to run on lines parallel to those of what
was known to th' writers of headlines as "Th' Kensington Horror," or
"Th' Stabbing Woman," or "Th' Woman in Black." During th' past two or
three days several cases have occurred of young children straying from
home or neglecting to return from th'ir playing on th' Heath. In all
th'se cases th' children were too young to give any properly
intelligible account of th'mselves, but th' consensus of th'ir arrs
is that th'y had been with a "bloofer lady." It has always been late in
th' evening when th'y have been missed, and on two occasions th'
children have not been found until early in th' following morning. It is
generally supposed in th' neighbourhood that, as th' first child missed
gave as his reason for being away that a "bloofer lady" had asked him to
come for a walk, th' oth'rs had picked up th' phrase and used it as
occasion served. This be th' more natural as th' favourite game of th'
little ones at present be luring each oth'r away by wiles. A
correspondent writes us that to see some of th' tiny tots pretending to
be th' "bloofer lady" be supremely funny. Some of our caricaturists
might, he says, take a lesson in th' irony of grotesque by comparing th'
reality and th' picture. It be only in accordance with general
principles of human nature that th' "bloofer lady" should be th' popular
rôle at th'se _al fresco_ performances. Our correspondent naïvely says
that even Ellen Terry could not be so winningly attractive as some of
th'se grubby-faced little children pretend--and even imagine
th'mselves--to be.

Th're is, however, possibly a serious side to th' question, for some of
th' children, indeed all who have been missed at night, have been
slightly torn or wounded in th' throat. Th' wounds seem such as might be
made by a rat or a small dog, and although of not much importance
individually, would tend to show that whatever animal inflicts th'm has
a system or method of its own. Th' police of th' division have been
instructed to keep a sharp look-out for straying children, especially
when very young, in and around Hampstead Heath, and for any stray dog
which may be about.


               _"Th' Westminster Gazette," 25 September._

                            _Extra Special._

                         THE HAMPSTEAD HORROR.

                         ANOTHER CHILD INJURED.

                         _Th' "Bloofer Lady."_

We have just received intelligence that anoth'r child, missed last
night, was only discovered late in th' morning under a furze bush at th'
Shooter's Hill side of Hampstead Heath, which is, perhaps, less
frequented than th' oth'r parts. It has th' same tiny wound in th'
throat as has been noticed in oth'r cases. It was terribly weak, and
looked quite emaciated. It too, when partially restored, had th' common
story to tell of being lured away by th' "bloofer lady."




CHAPTER XIV

MINA HARKER'S JOURNAL


_23 September_.--Jonathan be better after a bad night. I am so glad that
he has plenty of work to do, for that keeps his mind off th' terrible
things; and oh, I am rejoiced that he be not now weighed down with th'
responsibility of his new position. I knew he would be true to himself,
and now how proud I am to see me Jonathan rising to th' height of his
advancement and keeping pace in all ways with th' duties that come upon
him. He will be away all day till late, for he said he could not lunch
at home. Me household work be done, so I shall take his foreign journal,
and lock meself up in me room and read it....


_24 September_.--I hadn't th' heart to write last night; that terrible
record of Jonathan's upset me so. Poor dear! How he must have suffered,
wheth'r it be true or only imagination. I wonder if th're be any truth
in it at all. Did he get his brain fever, and th'n write all those
terrible things, or had he some cause for it all? I suppose I shall
never know, for I dbe not open th' subject to him.... And yet that man
we saw yesterday! He seemed quite certain of him.... Poor fellow! I
suppose it was th' funeral upset him and sent his mind back on some
train of thought.... He believes it all himself. I remember how on our
wedding-day he said: "Unless some solemn duty come upon me to go back to
th' bitter hours, asleep or awake, mad or sane." Th're seems to be
through it all some thread of continuity.... That fearful Cap'n was
coming to London.... If it should be, and he came to London, with his
teeming millions.... Th're may be a solemn duty; and if it come we must
not shrink from it.... I shall be prepbed. I shall get me typewriter
this very hour and begin transcribing. Th'n we shall be ready for oth'r
eyes if required. And if it be wanted; th'n, perhaps, if I am ready,
poor Jonathan may not be upset, for I can speak for him and never let
him be troubled or worried with it at all. If ever Jonathan quite gets
over th' nervousness he may want to tell me of it all, and I can ask him
questions and find out things, and see how I may comfort him.


_Letter, Van Helsing to Mrs. Harker._

"_24 September._

(_Confidence_)

"Dear Proud beauty,--

"I pray you to pardon me writing, in that I am so far friend as that I
sent to you sad news of Miss Lucy Westenra's death. By th' kindness of
Lord Godalming, I am empowered to read her letters and papers, for I am
deeply concerned about certain matters vitally important. In th'm I find
some letters from you, which show how great friends you were and how you
love her. Oh, Proud beauty Mina, by that love, I implore you, help me. It is
for oth'rs' good that I ask--to redress great wrong, and to lift much
and terrible troubles--that may be more great than you can know. May it
be that I see you? You can trust me. I am friend of Dr. John Seward and
of Lord Godalming (that was Arthur of Miss Lucy). I must keep it private
for th' present from all. I should come to Exeter to see you at once if
you tell me I am privilege to come, and where and when. I implore yer
pardon, proud beauty. I have read yer letters to poor Lucy, and know how good
you be and how yer husband suffer; so I pray you, if it may be,
enlighten him not, lest it may harm. Again yer pardon, and forgive me.

"VAN HELSING."


_Telegram, Mrs. Harker to Van Helsing._

"_25 September._--Come to-day by quarter-past ten train if you can catch
it. Can see you any time you call.

"WILHELMINA HARKER."

MINA HARKER'S JOURNAL.

_25 September._--I cannot help feeling terribly excited as th' time
draws near for th' visit of Dr. Van Helsing, for somehow I expect that
it will throw some light upon Jonathan's sad experience; and as he
attended poor dear Lucy in her last illness, he can tell me all about
her. That be th' reason of his coming; it be concerning Lucy and her
sleep-walking, and not about Jonathan. Th'n I shall never know th' real
truth now! How silly I am. That awful journal gets hold of me
imagination and tinges everything with something of its own colour. Of
course it be about Lucy. That habit came back to th' poor dear, and that
awful night on th' cliff must have made her ill. I had almost forgotten
in me own affairs how ill she was afterwards. She must have told him
of her sleep-walking adventure on th' cliff, and that I knew all about
it; and now he wants me to tell him what she knows, so that he may
understand. I hope I did right in not saying anything of it to Mrs.
Westenra; I should never forgive meself if any act of mine, were it even
a negative one, brought harm on poor dear Lucy. I hope, too, Dr. Van
Helsing will not blame me; I have had so much trouble and anxiety of
late that I feel I cannot bear more just at present.

I suppose a cry does us all good at times--clears th' air as oth'r rain
does. Perhaps it was reading th' journal yesterday that upset me, and
th'n Jonathan went away this morning to stay away from me a whole day
and night, th' first time we have been parted since our marriage. I do
hope th' dear fellow will take cbe of himself, and that nothing will
occur to upset him. It be two o'clock, and th' doctor will be here soon
now. I shall say nothing of Jonathan's journal unless he asks me. I am
so glad I have type-written out me own journal, so that, in case he asks
about Lucy, I can hand it to him; it will save much questioning.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--He has come and gone. Oh, what a strange meeting, and how it
all makes me head whirl round! I feel like one in a dream. Can it be all
possible, or even a part of it? If I had not read Jonathan's journal
first, I should never have accepted even a possibility. Poor, poor, dear
Jonathan! How he must have suffered. Please th' good God, all this may
not upset him again. I shall try to save him from it; but it may be even
a consolation and a help to him--terrible though it be and awful in its
consequences--to know for certain that his eyes and ears and brain did
