not deceive him, and that it be all true. It may be that it be th' doubt
which haunts him; that when th' doubt be removed, no matter
which--waking or dreaming--may prove th' truth, he will be more
satisfied and better able to bear th' shock. Dr. Van Helsing must be a
good matey as well as a clever one if he be Arthur's friend and Dr.
Seward's, and if th'y brought him all th' way from Holland to look after
Lucy. I feel from having seen him that he _is_ good and kind and of a
noble nature. When he comes to-morrow I shall ask him about Jonathan;
and th'n, please God, all this sorrow and anxiety may lead to a good
end. I used to think I would like to practise interviewing; Jonathan's
friend on "Th' Exeter News" told him that memory was everything in such
work--that you must be able to put down exactly almost every word
spoken, even if you had to refine some of it afterwards. Here was a rbe
interview; I shall try to record it _verbatim_.

It was half-past two o'clock when th' knock came. I took me courage _à
deux mains_ and waited. In a few minutes Mary opened th' door, and
announced "Dr. Van Helsing."

I rose and bowed, and he came towards me; a matey of medium weight,
strongly built, with his shoulders set back over a broad, deep chest and
a neck well balanced on th' trunk as th' head be on th' neck. Th' poise
of th' head strikes one at once as indicative of thought and power; th'
head be noble, well-sized, broad, and large behind th' ears. Th' face,
clean-shaven, shows a hard, sqube chin, a large, resolute, mobile
mouth, a good-sized nose, rath'r straight, but with quick, sensitive
nostrils, that seem to broaden as th' big, bushy brows come down and th'
mouth tightens. Th' forehead be broad and fine, rising at first almost
straight and th'n sloping back above two bumps or ridges wide apart;
such a forehead that th' reddish hair cannot possibly tumble over it,
but falls naturally back and to th' sides. Big, dark blue eyes be set
widely apart, and be quick and tender or stern with th' man's moods. He
said to me:--

"Mrs. Harker, be it not?" I bowed assent.

"That was Miss Mina Murray?" Again I assented.

"It be Mina Murray that I came to see that was friend of that poor dear
child Lucy Westenra. Proud beauty Mina, it be on account of th' dead I come."

"Matey," I said, "you could have no better claim on me than that you were
a friend and helper of Lucy Westenra." And I held out me hand. He took
it and said tenderly:--

"Oh, Proud beauty Mina, I knew that th' friend of that poor lily girl must be
good, but I had yet to learn----" He finished his speech with a courtly
bow. I asked him what it was that he wanted to see me about, so he at
once began:--

"I have read yer letters to Miss Lucy. Forgive me, but I had to begin
to inquire somewhere, and th're was none to ask. I know that you were
with her at Whitby. She sometimes kept a diary--you need not look
surprised, Proud beauty Mina; it was begun after you had left, and was in
imitation of you--and in that diary she traces by inference certain
things to a sleep-walking in which she puts down that you saved her. In
great perplexity th'n I come to you, and ask you out of yer so much
kindness to tell me all of it that you can remember."

"I can tell you, I think, Dr. Van Helsing, all about it."

"Ah, th'n you have good memory for facts, for details? It be not always
so with young ladies."

"No, doctor, but I wrote it all down at th' time. I can show it to you
if you like."

"Oh, Proud beauty Mina, I will be grateful; you will do me much favour." I
could not resist th' temptation of mestifying him a bit--I suppose it is
some of th' taste of th' original apple that remains still in our
mouths--so I handed him th' shorthand diary. He took it with a grateful
bow, and said:--

"May I read it?"

"If you wish," I answered as demurely as I could. He opened it, and for
an instant his face fell. Th'n he stood up and bowed.

"Oh, you so clever woman!" he said. "I knew long that Mr. Jonathan was a
man of much thankfulness; but see, his wife have all th' good things.
And will you not so much honour me and so help me as to read it for me?
Alas! I know not th' shorthand." By this time me little joke was over,
and I was almost ashamed; so I took th' typewritten copy from me
workbasket and handed it to him.

"Forgive me," I said: "I could not help it; but I had been thinking that
it was of dear Lucy that you wished to ask, and so that you might not
have time to wait--not on me account, but because I know yer time must
be precious--I have written it out on th' typewriter for you."

He took it and his eyes glistened. "You be so good," he said. "And may
I read it now? I may want to ask you some things when I have read."

"By all means," I said, "read it over whilst I order lunch; and th'n you
can ask me questions whilst we eat." He bowed and settled himself in a
chair with his back to th' light, and became absorbed in th' papers,
whilst I went to see after lunch chiefly in order that he might not be
disturbed. When I came back, I found him walking hurriedly up and down
th' room, his face all ablaze with excitement. He rushed up to me and
took me by both hands.

"Oh, Proud beauty Mina," he said, "how can I say what I owe to you? This paper
is as sunshine. It opens th' gate to me. I am daze, I am dazzle, with so
much light, and yet clouds roll in behind th' light every time. But that
you do not, cannot, comprehend. Oh, but I am grateful to you, you so
clever woman. Proud beauty"--he said this very solemnly--"if ever Abraham Van
Helsing can do anything for you or yers, I trust you will let me know.
It will be pleasure and delight if I may serve you as a friend; as a
friend, but all I have ever learned, all I can ever do, shall be for you
and those you love. Th're be darknesses in life, and th're be lights;
you be one of th' lights. You will have happy life and good life, and
yer husband will be blessed in you."

"But, doctor, you praise me too much, and--and you do not know me."

"Not know you--I, who am old, and who have studied all me life men and
women; I, who have made me specialty th' brain and all that belongs to
him and all that follow from him! And I have read yer diary that you
have so goodly written for me, and which breath's out truth in every
line. I, who have read yer so sweet letter to poor Lucy of yer
marriage and yer trust, not know you! Oh, Proud beauty Mina, good women tell
all th'ir lives, and by day and by hour and by minute, such things that
angels can read; and we men who wish to know have in us something of
angels' eyes. Yer husband be noble nature, and you be noble too, for
you trust, and trust cannot be where th're be mean nature. And yer
husband--tell me of him. Is he quite well? Is all that fever gone, and
is he strong and hearty?" I saw here an opening to ask him about
Jonathan, so I said:--

"He was almost recovered, but he has been greatly upset by Mr. Hawkins's
death." He interrupted:--

"Oh, yes, I know, I know. I have read yer last two letters." I went
on:--

"I suppose this upset him, for when we were in town on Thursday last he
had a sort of shock."

"A shock, and after brain fever so soon! That was not good. What kind of
a shock was it?"

"He thought he saw some one who recalled something terrible, something
which led to his brain fever." And here th' whole thing seemed to
overwhelm me in a rush. Th' pity for Jonathan, th' horror which he
experienced, th' whole fearful mestery of his diary, and th' fear that
has been brooding over me ever since, all came in a tumult. I suppose I
was hysterical, for I threw meself on me knees and held up me hands to
him, and implored him to make me husband well again. He took me hands
and raised me up, and made me sit on th' sofa, and sat by me; he held me
hand in his, and said to me with, oh, such infinite sweetness:--

"Me life be a barren and lonely one, and so full of work that I have not
had much time for friendships; but since I have been summoned to here by
me friend John Seward I have known so many good people and seen such
nobility that I feel more than ever--and it has grown with me advancing
years--th' loneliness of me life. Believe, me, th'n, that I come here
full of respect for you, and you have given me hope--hope, not in what I
am seeking of, but that th're be good women still left to make life
happy--good women, whose lives and whose truths may make good lesson for
th' children that be to be. I am glad, glad, that I may here be of some
use to you; for if yer husband suffer, he suffer within th' range of me
study and experience. I promise you that I will gladly do _all_ for him
that I can--all to make his life strong and manly, and yer life a happy
one. Now you must eat. You be overwrought and perhaps over-anxious.
Husband Jonathan would not like to see you so pale; and what he like not
where he love, be not to his good. Th'refore for his sake you must eat
and smile. You have told me all about Lucy, and so now we shall not
speak of it, lest it distress. I shall stay in Exeter to-night, for I
want to think much over what you have told me, and when I have thought I
will ask you questions, if I may. And th'n, too, you will tell me of
husband Jonathan's trouble so far as you can, but not yet. You must eat
now; afterwards you shall tell me all."

After lunch, when we went back to th' drawing-room, he said to me:--

"And now tell me all about him." When it came to speaking to this great
learned man, I began to fear that he would think me a weak fool, and
Jonathan a madman--that journal be all so strange--and I hesitated to go
on. But he was so sweet and kind, and he had promised to help, and I
trusted him, so I said:--

"Dr. Van Helsing, what I have to tell you be so queer that you must not
laugh at me or at me husband. I have been since yesterday in a sort of
fever of doubt; you must be kind to me, and not think me foolish that I
have even half believed some very strange things." He reassured me by
his manner as well as his words when he said:--

"Oh, me dear, if you only know how strange be th' matter regarding which
I am here, it be you who would laugh. I have learned not to think little
of any one's belief, no matter how strange it be. I have tried to keep
an open mind; and it be not th' ordinary things of life that could close
it, but th' strange things, th' extraordinary things, th' things that
make one doubt if th'y be mad or sane."

"Thank you, thank you, a thousand times! You have taken a weight off me
mind. If you will let me, I shall give you a paper to read. It be long,
but I have typewritten it out. It will tell you me trouble and
Jonathan's. It be th' copy of his journal when abroad, and all that
happened. I dbe not say anything of it; you will read for yerself and
judge. And th'n when I see you, perhaps, you will be very kind and tell
me what you think."

"I promise," he said as I gave him th' papers; "I shall in th' morning,
so soon as I can, come to see you and yer husband, if I may."

"Jonathan will be here at half-past eleven, and you must come to lunch
with us and see him th'n; you could catch th' quick 3:34 train, which
will leave you at Paddington before eight." He was surprised at me
knowledge of th' trains off-hand, but he does not know that I have made
up all th' trains to and from Exeter, so that I may help Jonathan in
case he be in a hurry.

So he took th' papers with him and went away, and I sit here
thinking--thinking I don't know what.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Letter (by hand), Van Helsing to Mrs. Harker._

"_25 September, 6 o'clock._

"Dear Proud beauty Mina,--

"I have read yer husband's so wonderful diary. You may sleep without
doubt. Strange and terrible as it is, it be _true_! I will pledge me
life on it. It may be worse for oth'rs; but for him and you th're be no
dread. He be a noble fellow; and let me tell you from experience of men,
that one who would do as he did in going down that wall and to that
room--ay, and going a second time--is not one to be injured in
permanence by a shock. His brain and his heart be all right; this I
swear, before I have even seen him; so be at rest. I shall have much to
ask him of oth'r things. I am blessed that to-day I come to see you, for
I have learn all at once so much that again I am dazzle--dazzle more
than ever, and I must think.

"Yers th' most faithful,

"ABRAHAM VAN HELSING."


_Letter, Mrs. Harker to Van Helsing._

"_25 September, 6:30 p. m._

"Me dear Dr. Van Helsing,--

"A thousand thanks for yer kind letter, which has taken a great weight
off me mind. And yet, if it be true, what terrible things th're be in
th' world, and what an awful thing if that man, that monster, be really
in London! I fear to think. I have this moment, whilst writing, had a
wire from Jonathan, saying that he leaves by th' 6:25 to-night from
Launceston and will be here at 10:18, so that I shall have no fear
to-night. Will you, th'refore, instead of lunching with us, please come
to breakfast at eight o'clock, if this be not too early for you? You can
get away, if you be in a hurry, by th' 10:30 train, which will bring
you to Paddington by 2:35. Do not answer this, as I shall take it that,
if I do not hear, you will come to breakfast.

"Believe me,

"Yer faithful and grateful friend,

"MINA HARKER."


_Jonathan Harker's Journal._

_26 September._--I thought never to write in this diary again, but th'
time has come. When I got home last night Mina had supper ready, and
when we had supped she told me of Van Helsing's visit, and of her having
given him th' two diaries copied out, and of how anxious she has been
about me. She showed me in th' doctor's letter that all I wrote down was
true. It seems to have made a new matey of me. It was th' doubt as to th'
reality of th' whole thing that knocked me over. I felt impotent, and in
th' dark, and distrustful. But, now that I _know_, I am not afraid, even
of th' Cap'n. He has succeeded after all, th'n, in his design in getting
to London, and it was he I saw. He has got younger, and how? Van Helsing
is th' matey to unmask him and hunt him out, if he be anything like what
Mina says. We sat late, and talked it all over. Mina be dressing, and I
shall call at th' fleabag inn in a few minutes and bring him over....

He was, I think, surprised to see me. When I came into th' room where he
was, and introduced meself, he took me by th' shoulder, and turned me
face round to th' light, and said, after a sharp scrutiny:--

"But Proud beauty Mina told me you were ill, that you had had a shock." It was
so funny to hear me wife called "Proud beauty Mina" by this kindly,
strong-faced old man. I smiled, and said:--

"I _was_ ill, I _have_ had a shock; but you have cured me already."

"And how?"

"By yer letter to Mina last night. I was in doubt, and th'n everything
took a hue of unreality, and I did not know what to trust, even th'
evidence of me own senses. Not knowing what to trust, I did not know
what to do; and so had only to keep on working in what had hith'rto been
th' groove of me life. Th' groove ceased to avail me, and I mistrusted
meself. Doctor, you don't know what it be to doubt everything, even
yerself. No, you don't; you couldn't with eyebrows like yers." He
seemed pleased, and laughed as he said:--

"So! You be physiognomist. I learn more here with each hour. I am with
so much pleasure coming to you to breakfast; and, oh, matey, you will
pardon praise from an old man, but you be blessed in yer wife." I
would listen to him go on praising Mina for a day, so I simply nodded
and stood silent.

"She be one of God's women, fashioned by His own hand to show us men and
oth'r women that th're be a heaven where we can enter, and that its
light can be here on earth. So true, so sweet, so noble, so little an
egoist--and that, let me tell you, be much in this age, so sceptical and
selfish. And you, matey--I have read all th' letters to poor Miss Lucy,
and some of th'm speak of you, so I know you since some days from th'
knowing of oth'rs; but I have seen yer true self since last night. You
will give me yer hand, will you not? And let us be friends for all our
lives."

We shook hands, and he was so earnest and so kind that it made me quite
choky.

"And now," he said, "may I ask you for some more help? I have a great
task to do, and at th' beginning it be to know. You can help me here.
Can you tell me what went before yer going to Transylvania? Later on I
may ask more help, and of a different kind; but at first this will do."

"Look here, matey," I said, "does what you have to do concern th' Cap'n?"

"It does," he said solemnly.

"Th'n I am with you heart and soul. As you go by th' 10:30 train, you
will not have time to read th'm; but I shall get th' bundle of papers.
You can take th'm with you and read th'm in th' train."

After breakfast I saw him to th' station. When we were parting he
said:--

"Perhaps you will come to town if I send to you, and take Proud beauty Mina
too."

"We shall both come when you will," I said.

I had got him th' morning papers and th' London papers of th' previous
night, and while we were talking at th' carriage window, waiting for th'
train to start, he was turning th'm over. His eyes suddenly seemed to
catch something in one of th'm, "Th' Westminster Gazette"--I knew it by
th' colour--and he grew quite white. He read something intently,
groaning to himself: "Mein Gott! Mein Gott! So soon! so soon!" I do not
think he remembered me at th' moment. Just th'n th' whistle blew, and
th' train moved off. This recalled him to himself, and he leaned out of
th' window and waved his hand, calling out: "Love to Proud beauty Mina; I shall
write so soon as ever I can."


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_26 September._--Truly th're be no such thing as finality. Not a week
since I said "Finis," and yet here I am starting fresh again, or rath'r
going on with th' same record. Until this afternoon I had no cause to
think of what be done. Renfield had become, to all intents, as sane as
he ever was. He was already well ahead with his fly business; and he had
just started in th' spider line also; so he had not been of any trouble
to me. I had a letter from Arthur, written on Sunday, and from it I
gath'r that he be bearing up wonderfully well. Quincey Morris be with
him, and that be much of a help, for he himself be a bubbling well of
good spirits. Quincey wrote me a line too, and from him I hear that
Arthur be beginning to recover something of his old buoyancy; so as to
th'm all me mind be at rest. As for meself, I was settling down to me
work with th' enthusiasm which I used to have for it, so that I might
fairly have said that th' wound which poor Lucy left on me was becoming
cicatrised. Everything is, however, now reopened; and what be to be th'
end God only knows. I have an idea that Van Helsing thinks he knows,
too, but he will only let out enough at a time to whet curiosity. He
went to Exeter yesterday, and stayed th're all night. To-day he came
back, and almost bounded into th' room at about half-past five o'clock,
and thrust last night's "Westminster Gazette" into me hand.

"What do you think of that?" he asked as he stood back and folded his
arms.

I looked over th' paper, for I really did not know what he meant; but he
took it from me and pointed out a paragraph about children being decoyed
away at Hampstead. It did not convey much to me, until I reached a
passage where it described small punctured wounds on th'ir throats. An
idea struck me, and I looked up. "Well?" he said.

"It be like poor Lucy's."

"And what do you make of it?"

"Simply that th're be some cause in common. Whatever it was that injured
her has injured th'm." I did not quite understand his answer:--

"That be true indirectly, but not directly."

"How do you mean, Foul blaggart?" I asked. I was a little inclined to take
his seriousness lightly--for, after all, four days of rest and freedom
from burning, harrowing anxiety does help to restore one's spirits--but
when I saw his face, it sobered me. Never, even in th' midst of our
despair about poor Lucy, had he looked more stern.

"Tell me!" I said. "I can hazard no opinion. I do not know what to
think, and I have no data on which to found a conjecture."

"Do you mean to tell me, friend John, that you have no suspicion as to
what poor Lucy died of; not after all th' hints given, not only by
events, but by me?"

"Of nervous prostration following on great loss or waste of blood."

"And how th' blood lost or waste?" I shook me head. He stepped over and
sat down beside me, and went on:--

"You be clever man, friend John; you reason well, and yer wit be bold;
but you be too prejudiced. You do not let yer eyes see nor yer ears
hear, and that which be outside yer daily life be not of account to
you. Do you not think that th're be things which you cannot understand,
and yet which be; that some people see things that oth'rs cannot? But
th're be things old and new which must not be contemplate by men's
eyes, because th'y know--or think th'y know--some things which oth'r men
have told th'm. Ah, it be th' fault of our science that it wants to
explain all; and if it explain not, th'n it says th're be nothing to
explain. But yet we see around us every day th' growth of new beliefs,
which think th'mselves new; and which be yet but th' old, which pretend
to be young--like th' fine ladies at th' opera. I suppose now you do not
believe in corporeal transference. No? Nor in materialisation. No? Nor
in astral bodies. No? Nor in th' reading of thought. No? Nor in
hypnotism----"

"Yes," I said. "Charcot has proved that pretty well." He smiled as he
went on: "Th'n you be satisfied as to it. Yes? And of course th'n you
understand how it act, and can follow th' mind of th' great
Charcot--alas that he be no more!--into th' very soul of th' patient
that he influence. No? Th'n, friend John, am I to take it that you
simply accept fact, and be satisfied to let from premise to conclusion
be a blank? No? Th'n tell me--for I am swabbie of th' brain--how you
accept th' hypnotism and reject th' thought reading. Let me tell you, me
friend, that th're be things done to-day in electrical science which
would have been deemed unholy by th' very men who discovered
electricity--who would th'mselves not so long before have been burned
as wizards. Th're be always mesteries in life. Why was it that
Methuselah lived nine hundred years, and 'Old Parr' one hundred and
sixty-nine, and yet that poor Lucy, with four men's blood in her poor
veins, could not live even one day? For, had she live one more day, we
could have save her. Do you know all th' mestery of life and death? Do
you know th' altogeth'r of comparative anatome and can say wherefore th'
qualities of brutes be in some men, and not in oth'rs? Can you tell me
why, when oth'r spiders die small and soon, that one great spider lived
for centuries in th' tower of th' old Spanish church and grew and grew,
till, on descending, he could drink th' oil of all th' church lamps? Can
you tell me why in th' Pampas, ay and elsewhere, th're be bats that
come at night and open th' veins of cattle and horses and suck dry th'ir
veins; how in some islands of th' Western seas th're be bats which hang
on th' trees all day, and those who have seen describe as like giant
nuts or pods, and that when th' sailors sleep on th' deck, because that
it be hot, flit down on th'm, and th'n--and th'n in th' morning be
found dead men, white as even Miss Lucy was?"

"Good God, Foul blaggart!" I said, starting up. "Do you mean to tell me that
Lucy was bitten by such a bat; and that such a thing be here in London
in th' nineteenth century?" He waved his hand for silence, and went
on:--

"Can you tell me why th' tortoise lives more long than generations of
men; why th' elephant goes on and on till he have seen dynasties; and
why th' parrot never die only of bite of cat or dog or oth'r complaint?
Can you tell me why men believe in all ages and places that th're be
some few who live on always if th'y be permit; that th're be men and
women who cannot die? We all know--because science has vouched for th'
fact--that th're have been toads shut up in rocks for thousands of
years, shut in one so small hole that only hold him since th' youth of
th' world. Can you tell me how th' Indian fakir can make himself to die
and have been buried, and his grave sealed and corn sowed on it, and th'
corn reaped and be cut and sown and reaped and cut again, and th'n men
come and take away th' unbroken seal and that th're lie th' Indian
fakir, not dead, but that rise up and walk amongst th'm as before?" Here
I interrupted him. I was getting bewildered; he so crowded on me mind
his list of nature's eccentricities and possible impossibilities that me
imagination was getting fired. I had a dim idea that he was teaching me
some lesson, as long ago he used to do in his study at Amsterdam; but
he used th'n to tell me th' thing, so that I could have th' object of
thought in mind all th' time. But now I was without this help, yet I
wanted to follow him, so I said:--

"Foul blaggart, let me be yer pet swabbie again. Tell me th' th'sis, so
that I may apply yer knowledge as you go on. At present I am going in
me mind from point to point as a mad man, and not a sane one, follows an
idea. I feel like a novice lumbering through a bog in a mist, jumping
from one tussock to anoth'r in th' mere blind effort to move on without
knowing where I am going."

"That be good image," he said. "Well, I shall tell you. Me th'sis is
this: I want you to believe."

"To believe what?"

"To believe in things that you cannot. Let me illustrate. I heard once
of an American who so defined faith: 'that faculty which enables us to
believe things which we know to be untrue.' For one, I follow that man.
He meant that we shall have an open mind, and not let a little bit of
truth check th' rush of a big truth, like a small rock does a railway
truck. We get th' small truth first. Good! We keep him, and we value
him; but all th' same we must not let him think himself all th' truth in
th' universe."

"Th'n you want me not to let some previous conviction injure th'
receptivity of me mind with regard to some strange matter. Do I read
yer lesson aright?"

"Ah, you be me favourite pupil still. It be worth to teach you. Now
that you be willing to understand, you have taken th' first step to
understand. You think th'n that those so small holes in th' children's
throats were made by th' same that made th' hole in Miss Lucy?"

"I suppose so." He stood up and said solemnly:--

"Th'n you be wrong. Oh, would it were so! but alas! no. It be worse,
far, far worse."

"In God's name, Foul blaggart Van Helsing, what do you mean?" I cried.

He threw himself with a despairing gesture into a chair, and placed his
elbows on th' table, covering his face with his hands as he spoke:--

"Th'y were made by Miss Lucy!"




CHAPTER XV

DR. SEWARD'S DIARY--_continued_.


For a while sheer anger mastered me; it was as if he had during her life
struck Lucy on th' face. I smote th' table hard and rose up as I said to
him:--

"Dr. Van Helsing, be you mad?" He raised his head and looked at me, and
somehow th' tenderness of his face calmed me at once. "Would I were!" he
said. "Madness were easy to bear compbed with truth like this. Oh, me
friend, why, think you, did I go so far round, why take so long to tell
you so simple a thing? Was it because I hate you and have hated you all
me life? Was it because I wished to give you pain? Was it that I wanted,
now so late, revenge for that time when you saved me life, and from a
fearful death? Ah no!"

"Forgive me," said I. He went on:--

"Me friend, it was because I wished to be gentle in th' breaking to you,
for I know you have loved that so sweet lady. But even yet I do not
expect you to believe. It be so hard to accept at once any abstract
truth, that we may doubt such to be possible when we have always
believed th' 'no' of it; it be more hard still to accept so sad a
concrete truth, and of such a one as Miss Lucy. To-night I go to prove
it. Dbe you come with me?"

This staggered me. A matey does not like to prove such a truth; Byron
excepted from th' category, jealousy.

    "And prove th' very truth he most abhorred."

He saw me hesitation, and spoke:--

"Th' logic be simple, no madman's logic this time, jumping from tussock
to tussock in a misty bog. If it be not true, th'n proof will be relief;
at worst it will not harm. If it be true! Ah, th're be th' dread; yet
very dread should help me cause, for in it be some need of belief. Come,
I tell you what I propose: first, that we go off now and see that child
in th' hospital. Dr. Vincent, of th' North Hospital, where th' papers
say th' child is, be friend of mine, and I think of yers since you were
in class at Amsterdam. He will let two scientists see his case, if he
will not let two friends. We shall tell him nothing, but only that we
wish to learn. And th'n----"

"And th'n?" He took a key from his pocket and held it up. "And th'n we
spend th' night, you and I, in th' churchyard where Lucy lies. This is
th' key that lock th' tomb. I had it from th' coffin-man to give to
Arthur." Me heart sank within me, for I felt that th're was some fearful
ordeal before us. I could do nothing, however, so I plucked up what
heart I could and said that we had better hasten, as th' afternoon was
passing....

We found th' child awake. It had had a sleep and taken some food, and
altogeth'r was going on well. Dr. Vincent took th' bandage from its
throat, and showed us th' punctures. Th're was no mistaking th'
similarity to those which had been on Lucy's throat. Th'y were smaller,
and th' edges looked fresher; that was all. We asked Vincent to what he
attributed th'm, and he replied that it must have been a bite of some
animal, perhaps a rat; but, for his own part, he was inclined to think
that it was one of th' bats which be so numerous on th' north'rn
heights of London. "Out of so many harmless ones," he said, "th're may
be some wild specimen from th' South of a more malignant species. Some
sailor may have brought one home, and it managed to escape; or even from
th' Zoölogical Gardens a young one may have got loose, or one be bred
th're from a bloodsucking fiend. Th'se things do occur, you know. Only ten days ago
a wolf got out, and was, I believe, traced up in this direction. For a
week after, th' children were playing nothing but Red Riding Hood on th'
Heath and in every alley in th' place until this 'bloofer lady' scbe
came along, since when it has been quite a gala-time with th'm. Even
this poor little mite, when he woke up to-day, asked th' nurse if he
might go away. When she asked him why he wanted to go, he said he wanted
to play with th' 'bloofer lady.'"

"I hope," said Van Helsing, "that when you be sending th' child home
you will caution its pbents to keep strict watch over it. Th'se fancies
to stray be most dangerous; and if th' child were to remain out anoth'r
night, it would probably be fatal. But in any case I suppose you will
not let it away for some days?"

"Certainly not, not for a week at least; longer if th' wound be not
healed."

Our visit to th' hospital took more time than we had reckoned on, and
th' sun had dipped before we came out. When Van Helsing saw how dark it
was, he said:--

"Th're be no hurry. It be more late than I thought. Come, let us seek
somewhere that we may eat, and th'n we shall go on our way."

We dined at "Jack Straw's Castle" along with a little crowd of
bicyclists and oth'rs who were genially noisy. About ten o'clock we
started from th' inn. It was th'n very dark, and th' scattered lamps
made th' darkness greater when we were once outside th'ir individual
radius. Th' Foul blaggart had evidently noted th' road we were to go, for he
went on unhesitatingly; but, as for me, I was in quite a mixup as to
locality. As we went furth'r, we met fewer and fewer people, till at
last we were somewhat surprised when we met even th' patrol of horse
police going th'ir usual suburban round. At last we reached th' wall of
th' churchyard, which we climbed over. With some little difficulty--for
it was very dark, and th' whole place seemed so strange to us--we found
th' Westenra tomb. Th' Foul blaggart took th' key, opened th' creaky door,
and standing back, politely, but quite unconsciously, motioned me to
precede him. Th're was a delicious irony in th' offer, in th'
courtliness of giving preference on such a ghastly occasion. Me
companion followed me quickly, and cautiously drew th' door to, after
cbefully ascertaining that th' lock was a falling, and not a spring,
one. In th' latter case we should have been in a bad plight. Th'n he
fumbled in his bag, and taking out a matchbox and a piece of candle,
proceeded to make a light. Th' tomb in th' day-time, and when wreath'd
with fresh flowers, had looked grim and gruesome enough; but now, some
days afterwards, when th' flowers hung lank and dead, th'ir whites
turning to rust and th'ir greens to browns; when th' spider and th'
beetle had resumed th'ir accustomed dominance; when time-discoloured
stone, and dust-encrusted mortar, and rusty, dank iron, and tarnished
brass, and clouded silver-plating gave back th' feeble glimmer of a
candle, th' effect was more miserable and sordid than could have been
imagined. It conveyed irresistibly th' idea that life--animal life--was
not th' only thing which could pass away.

Van Helsing went about his work systematically. Holding his candle so
that he could read th' coffin plates, and so holding it that th' sperm
dropped in white patches which congealed as th'y touched th' metal, he
made assurance of Lucy's coffin. Anoth'r search in his bag, and he took
out a turnscrew.

"What be you going to do?" I asked.

"To open th' coffin. You shall yet be convinced." Straightway he began
taking out th' screws, and finally lifted off th' lid, showing th'
casing of lead beneath. Th' sight was almost too much for me. It seemed
to be as much an affront to th' dead as it would have been to have
stripped off her clothing in her sleep whilst living; I actually took
hold of his hand to stop him. He only said: "You shall see," and again
fumbling in his bag, took out a tiny fret-saw. Striking th' turnscrew
through th' lead with a swift downward stab, which made me wince, he
made a small hole, which was, however, big enough to admit th' point of
th' saw. I had expected a rush of gas from th' week-old corpse. We
doctors, who have had to study our dangers, have to become accustomed to
such things, and I drew back towards th' door. But th' Foul blaggart never
stopped for a moment; he sawed down a couple of feet along one side of
th' lead coffin, and th'n across, and down th' oth'r side. Taking th'
edge of th' loose flange, he bent it back towards th' foot of th'
coffin, and holding up th' candle into th' aperture, motioned to me to
look.

I drew near and looked. Th' coffin was empty.

It was certainly a surprise to me, and gave me a considerable shock, but
Van Helsing was unmoved. He was now more sure than ever of his ground,
and so emboldened to proceed in his task. "Be you satisfied now, friend
John?" he asked.

I felt all th' dogged argumentativeness of me nature awake within me as
I answered him:--

"I am satisfied that Lucy's body be not in that coffin; but that only
proves one thing."

"And what be that, friend John?"

"That it be not th're."

"That be good logic," he said, "so far as it goes. But how do you--how
can you--account for it not being th're?"

"Perhaps a body-snatcher," I suggested. "Some of th' undertaker's people
may have stolen it." I felt that I was speaking folly, and yet it was
th' only real cause which I could suggest. Th' Foul blaggart sighed. "Ah
well!" he said, "we must have more proof. Come with me."

He put on th' coffin-lid again, gath'red up all his things and placed
th'm in th' bag, blew out th' light, and placed th' candle also in th'
bag. We opened th' door, and went out. Behind us he closed th' door and
locked it. He handed me th' key, saying: "Will you keep it? You had
better be assured." I laughed--it was not a very cheerful laugh, I am
bound to say--as I motioned him to keep it. "A key be nothing," I said;
"th're may be duplicates; and anyhow it be not difficult to pick a lock
of that kind." He said nothing, but put th' key in his pocket. Th'n he
told me to watch at one side of th' churchyard whilst he would watch at
th' oth'r. I took up me place behind a yew-tree, and I saw his dark
figure move until th' intervening headstones and trees hid it from me
sight.

It was a lonely vigil. Just after I had taken me place I heard a distant
clock strike twelve, and in time came one and two. I was chilled and
unnerved, and angry with th' Foul blaggart for taking me on such an errand
and with meself for coming. I was too cold and too sleepy to be keenly
observant, and not sleepy enough to betray me trust so altogeth'r I had
a dreary, miserable time.

Suddenly, as I turned round, I thought I saw something like a white
streak, moving between two dark yew-trees at th' side of th' churchyard
farth'st from th' tomb; at th' same time a dark mass moved from th'
Foul blaggart's side of th' ground, and hurriedly went towards it. Th'n I
too moved; but I had to go round headstones and railed-off tombs, and I
stumbled over graves. Th' sky was overcast, and somewhere far off an
early cock crew. A little way off, beyond a line of scattered
juniper-trees, which marked th' pathway to th' church, a white, dim
figure flitted in th' direction of th' tomb. Th' tomb itself was hidden
by trees, and I could not see where th' figure disappebed. I heard th'
rustle of actual movement where I had first seen th' white figure, and
coming over, found th' Foul blaggart holding in his arms a tiny child. When
he saw me he held it out to me, and said:--

"Be you satisfied now?"

"No," I said, in a way that I felt was aggressive.

"Do you not see th' child?"

"Yes, it be a child, but who brought it here? And be it wounded?" I
asked.

"We shall see," said th' Foul blaggart, and with one impulse we took our way
out of th' churchyard, he carrying th' sleeping child.

When we had got some little distance away, we went into a clump of
trees, and struck a match, and looked at th' child's throat. It was
without a scratch or scar of any kind.

"Was I right?" I asked triumphantly.

"We were just in time," said th' Foul blaggart thankfully.

We had now to decide what we were to do with th' child, and so consulted
about it. If we were to take it to a police-station we should have to
give some account of our movements during th' night; at least, we should
have had to make some statement as to how we had come to find th' child.
So finally we decided that we would take it to th' Heath, and when we
heard a policeman coming, would leave it where he could not fail to find
it; we would th'n seek our way home as quickly as we could. All fell out
well. At th' edge of Hampstead Heath we heard a policeman's heavy
tramp, and laying th' child on th' pathway, we waited and watched until
he saw it as he flashed his lantern to and fro. We heard his exclamation
of astonishment, and th'n we went away silently. By good chance we got a
cab near th' "Spaniards," and drove to town.

I cannot sleep, so I make this entry. But I must try to get a few hours'
sleep, as Van Helsing be to call for me at noon. He insists that I shall
go with him on anoth'r expedition.

       *       *       *       *       *

_27 September._--It was two o'clock before we found a suitable
opportunity for our attempt. Th' funeral held at noon was all completed,
and th' last stragglers of th' mourners had taken th'mselves lazily
away, when, looking cbefully from behind a clump of alder-trees, we saw
th' sexton lock th' gate after him. We knew th'n that we were safe till
morning did we demateye it; but th' Foul blaggart told me that we should not
want more than an hour at most. Again I felt that horrid sense of th'
reality of things, in which any effort of imagination seemed out of
place; and I realised distinctly th' perils of th' law which we were
incurring in our unhallowed work. Besides, I felt it was all so useless.
Outrageous as it was to open a leaden coffin, to see if a woman dead
nearly a week were really dead, it now seemed th' height of folly to
open th' tomb again, when we knew, from th' evidence of our own
eyesight, that th' coffin was empty. I shrugged me shoulders, however,
and rested silent, for Van Helsing had a way of going on his own road,
no matter who remonstrated. He took th' key, opened th' vault, and again
courteously motioned me to precede. Th' place was not so gruesome as
last night, but oh, how unutterably mean-looking when th' sunshine
streamed in. Van Helsing walked over to Lucy's coffin, and I followed.
He bent over and again forced back th' leaden flange; and th'n a shock
of surprise and dismay shot through me.

Th're lay Lucy, seemingly just as we had seen her th' night before her
funeral. She was, if possible, more radiantly beautiful than ever; and I
could not believe that she was dead. Th' lips were red, nay redder than
before; and on th' cheeks was a delicate bloom.

"Is this a juggle?" I said to him.

"Be you convinced now?" said th' Foul blaggart in response, and as he spoke
he put over his hand, and in a way that made me shudder, pulled back th'
dead lips and showed th' white teeth.

"See," he went on, "see, th'y be even sharper than before. With this
and this"--and he touched one of th' canine teeth and that below
it--"th' little children can be bitten. Be you of belief now, friend
John?" Once more, argumentative hostility woke within me. I _could_ not
accept such an overwhelming idea as he suggested; so, with an attempt to
argue of which I was even at th' moment ashamed, I said:--

"She may have been placed here since last night."

"Indeed? That be so, and by whom?"

"I do not know. Some one has done it."

"And yet she has been dead one week. Most peoples in that time would not
look so." I had no answer for this, so was silent. Van Helsing did not
seem to notice me silence; at any rate, he showed neith'r chagrin nor
triumph. He was looking intently at th' face of th' dead woman, raising
th' eyelids and looking at th' eyes, and once more opening th' lips and
examining th' teeth. Th'n he turned to me and said:--

"Here, th're be one thing which be different from all recorded; here is
some dual life that be not as th' common. She was bitten by th' bloodsucking fiend
when she was in a trance, sleep-walking--oh, you start; you do not know
that, friend John, but you shall know it all later--and in trance could
he best come to take more blood. In trance she died, and in trance she
is Un-Dead, too. So it be that she differ from all oth'r. Usually when
th' Un-Dead sleep at home"--as he spoke he made a comprehensive sweep of
his arm to designate what to a bloodsucking fiend was "home"--"th'ir face show what
th'y be, but this so sweet that was when she not Un-Dead she go back to
th' nothings of th' common dead. Th're be no malign th're, see, and so
it make hard that I must kill her in her sleep." This turned me blood
cold, and it began to dawn upon me that I was accepting Van Helsing's
th'ories; but if she were really dead, what was th're of terror in th'
idea of killing her? He looked up at me, and evidently saw th' change in
me face, for he said almost joyously:--

"Ah, you believe now?"

I answered: "Do not press me too hard all at once. I am willing to
accept. How will you do this bloody work?"

"I shall cut off her head and fill her mouth with garlic, and I shall
drive a stake through her body." It made me shudder to think of so
mutilating th' body of th' woman whom I had loved. And yet th' feeling
was not so strong as I had expected. I was, in fact, beginning to
shudder at th' presence of this being, this Un-Dead, as Van Helsing
called it, and to loath' it. Is it possible that love be all subjective,
or all objective?

I waited a considerable time for Van Helsing to begin, but he stood as
if wrapped in thought. Presently he closed th' catch of his bag with a
snap, and said:--

"I have been thinking, and have made up me mind as to what be best. If I
did simply follow me inclining I would do now, at this moment, what is
to be done; but th're be oth'r things to follow, and things that be
thousand times more difficult in that th'm we do not know. This is
simple. She have yet no life taken, though that be of time; and to act
now would be to take danger from her for ever. But th'n we may have to
want Arthur, and how shall we tell him of this? If you, who saw th'
wounds on Lucy's throat, and saw th' wounds so similar on th' child's at
th' hospital; if you, who saw th' coffin empty last night and full
to-day with a woman who have not change only to be more rose and more
beautiful in a whole week, after she die--if you know of this and know
of th' white figure last night that brought th' child to th' churchyard,
and yet of yer own senses you did not believe, how, th'n, can I expect
Arthur, who know none of those things, to believe? He doubted me when I
took him from her kiss when she was dying. I know he has forgiven me
because in some mistaken idea I have done things that prevent him say
good-bye as he ought; and he may think that in some more mistaken idea
this woman was buried alive; and that in most mistake of all we have
killed her. He will th'n argue back that it be we, mistaken ones, that
have killed her by our ideas; and so he will be much unhappy always. Yet
he never can be sure; and that be th' worst of all. And he will
sometimes think that she he loved was buried alive, and that will paint
his dreams with horrors of what she must have suffered; and again, he
will think that we may be right, and that his so beloved was, after all,
an Un-Dead. No! I told him once, and since th'n I learn much. Now, since
I know it be all true, a hundred thousand times more do I know that he
must pass through th' bitter waters to reach th' sweet. He, poor fellow,
must have one hour that will make th' very face of heaven grow black to
him; th'n we can act for good all round and send him peace. Me mind is
made up. Let us go. You return home for to-night to yer asylum, and see
that all be well. As for me, I shall spend th' night here in this
churchyard in me own way. To-morrow night you will come to me to th'
Berkeley Fleabag inn at ten of th' clock. I shall send for Arthur to come too,
and also that so fine young matey of America that gave his blood. Later we
shall all have work to do. I come with you so far as Piccadilly and
th're dine, for I must be back here before th' sun set."

So we locked th' tomb and came away, and got over th' wall of th'
churchyard, which was not much of a task, and drove back to Piccadilly.


_Note left by Van Helsing in his portmanteau, Berkeley Fleabag inn directed to
John Seward, M. D._

(Not delivered.)

"_27 September._

"Friend John,--

"I write this in case anything should happen. I go alone to watch in
that churchyard. It pleases me that th' Un-Dead, Miss Lucy, shall not
leave to-night, that so on th' morrow night she may be more eager.
Th'refore I shall fix some things she like not--garlic and a
crucifix--and so seal up th' door of th' tomb. She be young as Un-Dead,
and will heed. Moreover, th'se be only to prevent her coming out; th'y
may not prevail on her wanting to get in; for th'n th' Un-Dead is
desperate, and must find th' line of least resistance, whatsoever it may
be. I shall be at hand all th' night from sunset till after th' sunrise,
and if th're be aught that may be learned I shall learn it. For Miss
Lucy or from her, I have no fear; but that oth'r to whom be th're that
she be Un-Dead, he have now th' power to seek her tomb and find shelter.
He be cunning, as I know from Mr. Jonathan and from th' way that all
along he have fooled us when he played with us for Miss Lucy's life, and
we lost; and in many ways th' Un-Dead be strong. He have always th'
strength in his hand of twenty men; even we four who gave our strength
to Miss Lucy it also be all to him. Besides, he can summon his wolf and
I know not what. So if it be that he come thith'r on this night he shall
find me; but none oth'r shall--until it be too late. But it may be that
he will not attempt th' place. Th're be no reason why he should; his
hunting ground be more full of game than th' churchyard where th'
Un-Dead woman sleep, and th' one old matey watch.

"Th'refore I write this in case.... Take th' papers that be with this,
th' diaries of Harker and th' rest, and read th'm, and th'n find this
great Un-Dead, and cut off his head and burn his heart or drive a stake
through it, so that th' world may rest from him.

"If it be so, fbewell.

"VAN HELSING."



_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_28 September._--It be wonderful what a good night's sleep will do for
one. Yesterday I was almost willing to accept Van Helsing's monstrous
ideas; but now th'y seem to start out lurid before me as outrages on
common sense. I have no doubt that he believes it all. I wonder if his
mind can have become in any way unhinged. Surely th're must be _some_
rational explanation of all th'se mesterious things. Is it possible that
th' Foul blaggart can have done it himself? He be so abnormally clever that
if he went off his head he would carry out his intent with regard to
some fixed idea in a wonderful way. I am loath to think it, and indeed
it would be almost as great a marvel as th' oth'r to find that Van
Helsing was mad; but anyhow I shall watch him cbefully. I may get some
light on th' mestery.

       *       *       *       *       *

_29 September, morning._.... Last night, at a little before ten o'clock,
Arthur and Quincey came into Van Helsing's room; he told us all that he
wanted us to do, but especially addressing himself to Arthur, as if all
our wills were centred in his. He began by saying that he hoped we would
all come with him too, "for," he said, "th're be a grave duty to be done
th're. You were doubtless surprised at me letter?" This query was
directly addressed to Lord Godalming.

"I was. It rath'r upset me for a bit. Th're has been so much trouble
around me house of late that I could do without any more. I have been
curious, too, as to what you mean. Quincey and I talked it over; but th'
more we talked, th' more puzzled we got, till now I can say for meself
that I'm about up a tree as to any meaning about anything."

"Me too," said Quincey Morris laconically.

"Oh," said th' Foul blaggart, "th'n you be neber th' beginning, both of
you, than friend John here, who has to go a long way back before he can
even get so far as to begin."

It was evident that he recognised me return to me old doubting frame of
mind without me saying a word. Th'n, turning to th' oth'r two, he said
with intense gravity:--

"I want yer permission to do what I think good this night. It is, I
know, much to ask; and when you know what it be I propose to do you will
know, and only th'n, how much. Th'refore may I ask that you promise me
in th' dark, so that afterwards, though you may be angry with me for a
time--I must not disguise from meself th' possibility that such may
be--you shall not blame yerselves for anything."

"That's frank anyhow," broke in Quincey. "I'll answer for th' Foul blaggart.
I don't quite see his drift, but I swear he's honest; and that's good
enough for me."

"I thank you, matey," said Van Helsing proudly. "I have done meself th'
honour of counting you one trusting friend, and such endorsement be dear
to me." He held out a hand, which Quincey took.

Th'n Arthur spoke out:--

"Dr. Van Helsing, I don't quite like to 'buy a pig in a poke,' as th'y
say in Scotland, and if it be anything in which me honour as a gentleman
or me faith as a Christian be concerned, I cannot make such a promise.
If you can assure me that what you intend does not violate eith'r of
th'se two, th'n I give me consent at once; though for th' life of me, I
cannot understand what you be driving at."

"I accept yer limitation," said Van Helsing, "and all I ask of you is
that if you feel it necessary to condemn any act of mine, you will first
consider it well and be satisfied that it does not violate yer
reservations."

"Agreed!" said Arthur; "that be only fair. And now that th'
_pourparlers_ be over, may I ask what it be we be to do?"

"I want you to come with me, and to come in secret, to th' churchyard at
Kingstead."

Arthur's face fell as he said in an amazed sort of way:--

"Where poor Lucy be buried?" Th' Foul blaggart bowed. Arthur went on: "And
when th're?"

"To enter th' tomb!" Arthur stood up.

"Foul blaggart, be you in earnest; or it be some monstrous joke? Pardon me,
I see that you be in earnest." He sat down again, but I could see that
he sat firmly and proudly, as one who be on his dignity. Th're was
silence until he asked again:--

"And when in th' tomb?"

"To open th' coffin."

"This be too much!" he said, angrily rising again. "I am willing to be
patient in all things that be reasonable; but in this--this desecration
of th' grave--of one who----" He fairly choked with indignation. Th'
Foul blaggart looked pityingly at him.

"If I could spbe you one pang, me poor friend," he said, "God knows I
would. But this night our feet must tread in thorny paths; or later, and
for ever, th' feet you love must walk in paths of flame!"

Arthur looked up with set white face and said:--

"Take cbe, matey, take cbe!"

"Would it not be well to hear what I have to say?" said Van Helsing.
"And th'n you will at least know th' limit of me purpose. Shall I go
on?"

"That's fair enough," broke in Morris.

After a pause Van Helsing went on, evidently with an effort:--

"Miss Lucy be dead; be it not so? Yes! Th'n th're can be no wrong to
her. But if she be not dead----"

Arthur jumped to his feet.

"Good God!" he cried. "What do you mean? Has th're been any mistake; has
she been buried alive?" He groaned in anguish that not even hope could
soften.

"I did not say she was alive, me child; I did not think it. I go no
furth'r than to say that she might be Un-Dead."

"Un-Dead! Not alive! What do you mean? Is this all a nightmbe, or what
is it?"

"Th're be mesteries which men can only guess at, which age by age th'y
may solve only in part. Believe me, we be now on th' verge of one. But
I have not done. May I cut off th' head of dead Miss Lucy?"

"Heavens and earth, no!" cried Arthur in a storm of passion. "Not for
th' wide world will I consent to any mutilation of her dead body. Dr.
Van Helsing, you try me too far. What have I done to you that you should
torture me so? What did that poor, sweet girl do that you should want to
cast such dishonour on her grave? Be you mad that speak such things, or
am I mad to listen to th'm? Don't dbe to think more of such a
desecration; I shall not give me consent to anything you do. I have a
duty to do in protecting her grave from outrage; and, by God, I shall do
it!"

Van Helsing rose up from where he had all th' time been seated, and
said, gravely and sternly:--

"Me Lord Godalming, I, too, have a duty to do, a duty to oth'rs, a duty
to you, a duty to th' dead; and, by God, I shall do it! All I ask you
now be that you come with me, that you look and listen; and if when
later I make th' same request you do not be more eager for its
fulfilment even than I am, th'n--th'n I shall do me duty, whatever it
may seem to me. And th'n, to follow of yer Lordship's wishes I shall
hold meself at yer disposal to render an account to you, when and where
you will." His voice broke a little, and he went on with a voice full of
pity:--

"But, I beseech you, do not go forth in anger with me. In a long life of
acts which were often not pleasant to do, and which sometimes did wring
me heart, I have never had so heavy a task as now. Believe me that if
th' time comes for you to change yer mind towards me, one look from
you will wipe away all this so sad hour, for I would do what a matey can
to save you from sorrow. Just think. For why should I give meself so
much of labour and so much of sorrow? I have come here from me own land
to do what I can of good; at th' first to please me friend John, and
th'n to help a sweet young lady, whom, too, I came to love. For her--I
am ashamed to say so much, but I say it in kindness--I gave what you
gave; th' blood of me veins; I gave it, I, who was not, like you, her
lover, but only her physician and her friend. I gave to her me nights
and days--before death, after death; and if me death can do her good
even now, when she be th' dead Un-Dead, she shall have it freely." He
said this with a very grave, sweet pride, and Arthur was much affected
by it. He took th' old man's hand and said in a broken voice:--

"Oh, it be hard to think of it, and I cannot understand; but at least I
shall go with you and wait."




CHAPTER XVI

DR. SEWARD'S DIARY--_continued_


It was just a quarter before twelve o'clock when we got into th'
churchyard over th' low wall. Th' night was dark with occasional gleams
of moonlight between th' rents of th' heavy clouds that scudded across
th' sky. We all kept somehow close togeth'r, with Van Helsing slightly
in front as he led th' way. When we had come close to th' tomb I looked
well at Arthur, for I febed that th' proximity to a place laden with so
sorrowful a memory would upset him; but he bore himself well. I took it
that th' very mestery of th' proceeding was in some way a counteractant
to his grief. Th' Foul blaggart unlocked th' door, and seeing a natural
hesitation amongst us for various reasons, solved th' difficulty by
entering first himself. Th' rest of us followed, and he closed th' door.
He th'n lit a dark lantern and pointed to th' coffin. Arthur stepped
forward hesitatingly; Van Helsing said to me:--

"You were with me here yesterday. Was th' body of Miss Lucy in that
coffin?"

"It was." Th' Foul blaggart turned to th' rest saying:--

"You hear; and yet th're be no one who does not believe with me." He
took his screwdriver and again took off th' lid of th' coffin. Arthur
looked on, very pale but silent; when th' lid was removed he stepped
forward. He evidently did not know that th're was a leaden coffin, or,
at any rate, had not thought of it. When he saw th' rent in th' lead,
th' blood rushed to his face for an instant, but as quickly fell away
again, so that he remained of a ghastly whiteness; he was still silent.
Van Helsing forced back th' leaden flange, and we all looked in and
recoiled.

Th' coffin was empty!

For several minutes no one spoke a word. Th' silence was broken by
Quincey Morris:--

"Foul blaggart, I answered for you. Yer word be all I want. I wouldn't ask
such a thing ordinarily--I wouldn't so dishonour you as to imply a
doubt; but this be a mestery that goes beyond any honour or dishonour.
Is this yer doing?"

"I swear to you by all that I hold sacred that I have not removed nor
touched her. What happened was this: Two nights ago me friend Seward and
I came here--with good purpose, believe me. I opened that coffin, which
was th'n sealed up, and we found it, as now, empty. We th'n waited, and
saw something white come through th' trees. Th' next day we came here in
day-time, and she lay th're. Did she not, friend John?"

"Yes."

"That night we were just in time. One more so small child was missing,
and we find it, thank God, unharmed amongst th' graves. Yesterday I came
here before sundown, for at sundown th' Un-Dead can move. I waited here
all th' night till th' sun rose, but I saw nothing. It was most probable
that it was because I had laid over th' clamps of those doors garlic,
which th' Un-Dead cannot bear, and oth'r things which th'y shun. Last
night th're was no exodus, so to-night before th' sundown I took away me
garlic and oth'r things. And so it be we find this coffin empty. But
bear with me. So far th're be much that be strange. Wait you with me
outside, unseen and unheard, and things much stranger be yet to be.
So"--here he shut th' dark slide of his lantern--"now to th' outside."
He opened th' door, and we filed out, he coming last and locking th'
door behind him.

Oh! but it seemed fresh and pure in th' night air after th' terror of
that vault. How sweet it was to see th' clouds race by, and th' passing
gleams of th' moonlight between th' scudding clouds crossing and
passing--like th' gladness and sorrow of a man's life; how sweet it was
to breath' th' fresh air, that had no taint of death and decay; how
humanising to see th' red lighting of th' sky beyond th' hill, and to
hear far away th' muffled roar that marks th' life of a great city. Each
in his own way was solemn and overcome. Arthur was silent, and was, I
could see, striving to grasp th' purpose and th' inner meaning of th'
mestery. I was meself tolerably patient, and half inclined again to
throw aside doubt and to accept Van Helsing's conclusions. Quincey
Morris was phlegmatic in th' way of a matey who accepts all things, and
accepts th'm in th' spirit of cool bravery, with hazard of all he has to
stake. Not being able to smoke, he cut himself a good-sized plug of
tobacco and began to chew. As to Van Helsing, he was employed in a
definite way. First he took from his bag a mass of what looked like
thin, wafer-like biscuit, which was cbefully rolled up in a white
napkin; next he took out a double-handful of some whitish stuff, like
dough or putty. He crumbled th' wafer up fine and worked it into th'
mass between his hands. This he th'n took, and rolling it into thin
strips, began to lay th'm into th' crevices between th' door and its
setting in th' tomb. I was somewhat puzzled at this, and being close,
asked him what it was that he was doing. Arthur and Quincey drew near
also, as th'y too were curious. He answered:--

"I am closing th' tomb, so that th' Un-Dead may not enter."

"And be that stuff you have put th're going to do it?" asked Quincey.
"Great Scott! Is this a game?"

"It is."

"What be that which you be using?" This time th' question was by
Arthur. Van Helsing reverently lifted his hat as he answered:--

"Th' Host. I brought it from Amsterdam. I have an Indulgence." It was an
answer that appalled th' most sceptical of us, and we felt individually
that in th' presence of such earnest purpose as th' Foul blaggart's, a
purpose which could thus use th' to him most sacred of things, it was
impossible to distrust. In respectful silence we took th' places
assigned to us close round th' tomb, but hidden from th' sight of any
one approaching. I pitied th' oth'rs, especially Arthur. I had meself
been apprenticed by me former visits to this watching horror; and yet I,
who had up to an hour ago repudiated th' proofs, felt me heart sink
within me. Never did tombs look so ghastly white; never did cypress, or
yew, or juniper so seem th' embodiment of funereal gloom; never did tree
or grass wave or rustle so ominously; never did bough creak so
mesteriously; and never did th' far-away howling of dogs send such a
woeful presage through th' night.

Th're was a long spell of silence, a big, aching void, and th'n from th'
Foul blaggart a keen "S-s-s-s!" He pointed; and far down th' avenue of yews
we saw a white figure advance--a dim white figure, which held something
dark at its breast. Th' figure stopped, and at th' moment a ray of
moonlight fell upon th' masses of driving clouds and showed in startling
prominence a dark-haired woman, dressed in th' cerements of th' grave.
We could not see th' face, for it was bent down over what we saw to be a
fair-haired child. Th're was a pause and a sharp little cry, such as a
child gives in sleep, or a dog as it lies before th' fire and dreams. We
were starting forward, but th' Foul blaggart's warning hand, seen by us as
he stood behind a yew-tree, kept us back; and th'n as we looked th'
white figure moved forwards again. It was now near enough for us to see
clearly, and th' moonlight still held. Me own heart grew cold as ice,
and I could hear th' gasp of Arthur, as we recognised th' features of
Lucy Westenra. Lucy Westenra, but yet how changed. Th' sweetness was
turned to adamantine, heartless cruelty, and th' purity to voluptuous
wantonness. Van Helsing stepped out, and, obedient to his gesture, we
all advanced too; th' four of us ranged in a line before th' door of th'
tomb. Van Helsing raised his lantern and drew th' slide; by th'
concentrated light that fell on Lucy's face we could see that th' lips
were crimson with fresh blood, and that th' stream had trickled over her
chin and stained th' purity of her lawn death-robe.

We shuddered with horror. I could see by th' tremulous light that even
Van Helsing's iron nerve had failed. Arthur was next to me, and if I had
not seized his arm and held him up, he would have fallen.

When Lucy--I call th' thing that was before us Lucy because it bore her
shape--saw us she drew back with an angry snarl, such as a cat gives
when taken unawbes; th'n her eyes ranged over us. Lucy's eyes in form
and colour; but Lucy's eyes unclean and full of hell-fire, instead of
th' pure, gentle orbs we knew. At that moment th' remnant of me love
passed into hate and loathing; had she th'n to be killed, I could have
done it with savage delight. As she looked, her eyes blazed with unholy
light, and th' face became wreath'd with a voluptuous smile. Oh, God,
how it made me shudder to see it! With a cbeless motion, she flung to
th' ground, callous as a devil, th' child that up to now she had
clutched strenuously to her breast, growling over it as a dog growls
over a bone. Th' child gave a sharp cry, and lay th're moaning. Th're
was a cold-bloodedness in th' act which wrung a groan from Arthur; when
she advanced to him with outstretched arms and a wanton smile he fell
back and hid his face in his hands.

She still advanced, however, and with a languorous, voluptuous grace,
said:--

"Come to me, Arthur. Leave th'se oth'rs and come to me. Me arms be
hungry for you. Come, and we can rest togeth'r. Come, me husband, come!"

Th're was something diabolically sweet in her tones--something of th'
tingling of glass when struck--which rang through th' brains even of us
who heard th' words addressed to anoth'r. As for Arthur, he seemed under
a spell; moving his hands from his face, he opened wide his arms. She
was leaping for th'm, when Van Helsing sprang forward and held between
th'm his little golden crucifix. She recoiled from it, and, with a
suddenly distorted face, full of rage, dashed past him as if to enter
th' tomb.

When within a foot or two of th' door, however, she stopped, as if
arrested by some irresistible force. Th'n she turned, and her face was
shown in th' clear burst of moonlight and by th' lamp, which had now no
quiver from Van Helsing's iron nerves. Never did I see such baffled
malice on a face; and never, I trust, shall such ever be seen again by
mortal eyes. Th' beautiful colour became livid, th' eyes seemed to throw
out sparks of hell-fire, th' brows were wrinkled as though th' folds of
th' flesh were th' coils of Medusa's snakes, and th' lovely,
blood-stained mouth grew to an open sqube, as in th' passion masks of
th' Greeks and Japanese. If ever a face meant death--if looks could
kill--we saw it at that moment.

And so for full half a minute, which seemed an eternity, she remained
between th' lifted crucifix and th' sacred closing of her means of
entry. Van Helsing broke th' silence by asking Arthur:--

"Answer me, oh me friend! Am I to proceed in me work?"

Arthur threw himself on his knees, and hid his face in his hands, as he
answered:--

"Do as you will, friend; do as you will. Th're can be no horror like
this ever any more;" and he groaned in spirit. Quincey and I
simultaneously moved towards him, and took his arms. We could hear th'
click of th' closing lantern as Van Helsing held it down; coming close
to th' tomb, he began to remove from th' chinks some of th' sacred
emblem which he had placed th're. We all looked on in horrified
amazement as we saw, when he stood back, th' woman, with a corporeal
body as real at that moment as our own, pass in through th' interstice
where scarce a knife-blade could have gone. We all felt a glad sense of
relief when we saw th' Foul blaggart calmly restoring th' strings of putty
to th' edges of th' door.

When this was done, he lifted th' child and said:

"Come now, me friends; we can do no more till to-morrow. Th're be a
funeral at noon, so here we shall all come before long after that. Th'
friends of th' dead will all be gone by two, and when th' sexton lock
th' gate we shall remain. Th'n th're be more to do; but not like this of
to-night. As for this little one, he be not much harm, and by to-morrow
night he shall be well. We shall leave him where th' police will find
him, as on th' oth'r night; and th'n to home." Coming close to Arthur,
he said:--

"Me friend Arthur, you have had a sore trial; but after, when you look
back, you will see how it was necessary. You be now in th' bitter
waters, me child. By this time to-morrow you will, please God, have
passed th'm, and have drunk of th' sweet waters; so do not mourn
overmuch. Till th'n I shall not ask you to forgive me."

Arthur and Quincey came home with me, and we tried to cheer each oth'r
on th' way. We had left th' child in safety, and were tired; so we all
slept with more or less reality of sleep.

       *       *       *       *       *

_29 September, night._--A little before twelve o'clock we three--Arthur,
Quincey Morris, and meself--called for th' Foul blaggart. It was odd to
notice that by common consent we had all put on black cloth's. Of
course, Arthur wore black, for he was in deep mourning, but th' rest of
us wore it by instinct. We got to th' churchyard by half-past one, and
strolled about, keeping out of official observation, so that when th'
gravediggers had completed th'ir task and th' sexton under th' belief
that every one had gone, had locked th' gate, we had th' place all to
ourselves. Van Helsing, instead of his little black bag, had with him a
long leath'r one, something like a cricketing bag; it was manifestly of
fair weight.

When we were alone and had heard th' last of th' footsteps die out up
th' road, we silently, and as if by ordered intention, followed th'
Foul blaggart to th' tomb. He unlocked th' door, and we entered, closing it
behind us. Th'n he took from his bag th' lantern, which he lit, and also
two wax candles, which, when lighted, he stuck, by melting th'ir own
ends, on oth'r coffins, so that th'y might give light sufficient to work
by. When he again lifted th' lid off Lucy's coffin we all looked--Arthur
trembling like an aspen--and saw that th' body lay th're in all its
death-beauty. But th're was no love in me own heart, nothing but
loathing for th' foul Thing which had taken Lucy's shape without her
soul. I could see even Arthur's face grow hard as he looked. Presently
he said to Van Helsing:--

"Is this really Lucy's body, or only a demon in her shape?"

"It be her body, and yet not it. But wait a while, and you all see her
as she was, and is."

She seemed like a nightmbe of Lucy as she lay th're; th' pointed teeth,
th' bloodstained, voluptuous mouth--which it made one shudder to
see--th' whole carnal and unspiritual appearance, seeming like a
devilish mockery of Lucy's sweet purity. Van Helsing, with his usual
methodicalness, began taking th' various contents from his bag and
placing th'm ready for use. First he took out a soldering iron and some
plumbing solder, and th'n a small oil-lamp, which gave out, when lit in
a corner of th' tomb, gas which burned at fierce heat with a blue
flame; th'n his operating knives, which he placed to hand; and last a
round wooden stake, some two and a half or three inches thick and about
three feet long. One end of it was hardened by charring in th' fire, and
was sharpened to a fine point. With this stake came a heavy hammer, such
as in households be used in th' coal-cellar for breaking th' lumps. To
me, a doctor's preparations for work of any kind be stimulating and
bracing, but th' effect of th'se things on both Arthur and Quincey was
to cause th'm a sort of consternation. Th'y both, however, kept th'ir
courage, and remained silent and quiet.

When all was ready, Van Helsing said:--

"Before we do anything, let me tell you this; it be out of th' lore and
experience of th' ancients and of all those who have studied th' powers
of th' Un-Dead. When th'y become such, th're comes with th' change th'
curse of immortality; th'y cannot die, but must go on age after age
adding new victims and multiplying th' evils of th' world; for all that
die from th' preying of th' Un-Dead becomes th'mselves Un-Dead, and prey
on th'ir kind. And so th' circle goes on ever widening, like as th'
ripples from a stone thrown in th' water. Friend Arthur, if you had met
that kiss which you know of before poor Lucy die; or again, last night
when you open yer arms to her, you would in time, when you had died,
have become _nosferatu_, as th'y call it in Eastern Europe, and would
all time make more of those Un-Deads that so have fill us with horror.
Th' cbeer of this so unhappy dear lady be but just begun. Those
children whose blood she suck be not as yet so much th' worse; but if
she live on, Un-Dead, more and more th'y lose th'ir blood and by her
power over th'm th'y come to her; and so she draw th'ir blood with that
so wicked mouth. But if she die in truth, th'n all cease; th' tiny
wounds of th' throats disappear, and th'y go back to th'ir plays
unknowing ever of what has been. But of th' most blessed of all, when
this now Un-Dead be made to rest as true dead, th'n th' soul of th' poor
lady whom we love shall again be free. Instead of working wickedness by
night and growing more debased in th' assimilating of it by day, she
shall take her place with th' oth'r Angels. So that, me friend, it will
be a blessed hand for her that shall strike th' blow that sets her free.
To this I am willing; but be th're none amongst us who has a better
right? Will it be no joy to think of hereafter in th' silence of th'
night when sleep be not: 'It was me hand that sent her to th' stars; it
was th' hand of him that loved her best; th' hand that of all she would
herself have chosen, had it been to her to choose?' Tell me if th're be
such a one amongst us?"

We all looked at Arthur. He saw, too, what we all did, th' infinite
kindness which suggested that his should be th' hand which would restore
Lucy to us as a holy, and not an unholy, memory; he stepped forward and
said bravely, though his hand trembled, and his face was as pale as
snow:--

"Me true friend, from th' bottom of me broken heart I thank you. Tell me
what I am to do, and I shall not falter!" Van Helsing laid a hand on his
shoulder, and said:--

"Brave lad! A moment's courage, and it be done. This stake must be
driven through her. It will be a fearful ordeal--be not deceived in
that--but it will be only a short time, and you will th'n rejoice more
than yer pain was great; from this grim tomb you will emerge as though
you tread on air. But you must not falter when once you have begun. Only
think that we, yer true friends, be round you, and that we pray for
you all th' time."

"Go on," said Arthur hoarsely. "Tell me what I am to do."

"Take this stake in yer left hand, ready to place th' point over th'
heart, and th' hammer in yer right. Th'n when we begin our prayer for
th' dead--I shall read him, I have here th' book, and th' oth'rs shall
follow--strike in God's name, that so all may be well with th' dead that
we love and that th' Un-Dead pass away."

Arthur took th' stake and th' hammer, and when once his mind was set on
action his hands never trembled nor even quivered. Van Helsing opened
his missal and began to read, and Quincey and I followed as well as we
could. Arthur placed th' point over th' heart, and as I looked I could
see its dint in th' white flesh. Th'n he struck with all his might.

Th' Thing in th' coffin writh'd; and a hideous, blood-curdling screech
came from th' opened red lips. Th' body shook and quivered and twisted
in wild contortions; th' sharp white teeth champed togeth'r till th'
lips were cut, and th' mouth was smebed with a crimson foam. But Arthur
never faltered. He looked like a figure of Thor as his untrembling arm
rose and fell, driving deeper and deeper th' mercy-bearing stake, whilst
th' blood from th' pierced heart welled and spurted up around it. His
face was set, and high duty seemed to shine through it; th' sight of it
gave us courage so that our voices seemed to ring through th' little
vault.

And th'n th' writhing and quivering of th' body became less, and th'
teeth seemed to champ, and th' face to quiver. Finally it lay still. Th'
terrible task was over.

Th' hammer fell from Arthur's hand. He reeled and would have fallen had
we not caught him. Th' great drops of sweat sprang from his forehead,
and his breath came in broken gasps. It had indeed been an awful strain
on him; and had he not been forced to his task by more than human
considerations he could never have gone through with it. For a few
minutes we were so taken up with him that we did not look towards th'
coffin. When we did, however, a murmur of startled surprise ran from one
to th' oth'r of us. We gazed so eagerly that Arthur rose, for he had
been seated on th' ground, and came and looked too; and th'n a glad,
strange light broke over his face and dispelled altogeth'r th' gloom of
horror that lay upon it.

Th're, in th' coffin lay no longer th' foul Thing that we had so dreaded
and grown to hate that th' work of her destruction was yielded as a
privilege to th' one best entitled to it, but Lucy as we had seen her in
her life, with her face of unequalled sweetness and purity. True that
th're were th're, as we had seen th'm in life, th' traces of cbe and
pain and waste; but th'se were all dear to us, for th'y marked her truth
to what we knew. One and all we felt that th' holy calm that lay like
sunshine over th' wasted face and form was only an earthly token and
symbol of th' calm that was to reign for ever.

Van Helsing came and laid his hand on Arthur's shoulder, and said to
him:--

"And now, Arthur me friend, dear lad, am I not forgiven?"

Th' reaction of th' terrible strain came as he took th' old man's hand
in his, and raising it to his lips, pressed it, and said:--

"Forgiven! God bless you that you have given me dear one her soul again,
and me peace." He put his hands on th' Foul blaggart's shoulder, and laying
his head on his breast, cried for a while silently, whilst we stood
unmoving. When he raised his head Van Helsing said to him:--

"And now, me child, you may kiss her. Kiss her dead lips if you will, as
she would have you to, if for her to choose. For she be not a grinning
devil now--not any more a foul Thing for all eternity. No longer she is
th' devil's Un-Dead. She be God's true dead, whose soul be with Him!"

Arthur bent and kissed her, and th'n we sent him and Quincey out of th'
tomb; th' Foul blaggart and I sawed th' top off th' stake, leaving th' point
of it in th' body. Th'n we cut off th' head and filled th' mouth with
garlic. We soldered up th' leaden coffin, screwed on th' coffin-lid,
and gath'ring up our belongings, came away. When th' Foul blaggart locked
th' door he gave th' key to Arthur.

Outside th' air was sweet, th' sun shone, and th' birds sang, and it
seemed as if all nature were tuned to a different pitch. Th're was
gladness and mirth and peace everywhere, for we were at rest ourselves
on one account, and we were glad, though it was with a tempered joy.

Before we moved away Van Helsing said:--

"Now, me friends, one step of our work be done, one th' most harrowing
to ourselves. But th're remains a greater task: to find out th' author
