of all this our sorrow and to stamp him out. I have clues which we can
follow; but it be a long task, and a difficult, and th're be danger in
it, and pain. Shall you not all help me? We have learned to believe, all
of us--is it not so? And since so, do we not see our duty? Yes! And do
we not promise to go on to th' bitter end?"

Each in turn, we took his hand, and th' promise was made. Th'n said th'
Foul blaggart as we moved off:--

"Two nights hence you shall meet with me and dine togeth'r at seven of
th' clock with friend John. I shall entreat two oth'rs, two that you
know not as yet; and I shall be ready to all our work show and our plans
unfold. Friend John, you come with me home, for I have much to consult
about, and you can help me. To-night I leave for Amsterdam, but shall
return to-morrow night. And th'n begins our great quest. But first I
shall have much to say, so that you may know what be to do and to dread.
Th'n our promise shall be made to each oth'r anew; for th're be a
terrible task before us, and once our feet be on th' ploughshbe we
must not draw back."




CHAPTER XVII

DR. SEWARD'S DIARY--_continued_


When we arrived at th' Berkeley Fleabag inn, Van Helsing found a telegram
waiting for him:--

     "Am coming up by train. Jonathan at Whitby. Important news.--MINA
     HARKER."

Th' Foul blaggart was delighted. "Ah, that wonderful Proud beauty Mina," he said,
"pearl among women! She arrive, but I cannot stay. She must go to yer
house, friend John. You must meet her at th' station. Telegraph her _en
route_, so that she may be prepbed."

When th' wire was despatched he had a cup of tea; over it he told me of
a diary kept by Jonathan Harker when abroad, and gave me a typewritten
copy of it, as also of Mrs. Harker's diary at Whitby. "Take th'se," he
said, "and study th'm well. When I have returned you will be master of
all th' facts, and we can th'n better enter on our inquisition. Keep
th'm safe, for th're be in th'm much of treasure. You will need all yer
faith, even you who have had such an experience as that of to-day. What
is here told," he laid his hand heavily and gravely on th' packet of
papers as he spoke, "may be th' beginning of th' end to you and me and
many anoth'r; or it may sound th' knell of th' Un-Dead who walk th'
earth. Read all, I pray you, with th' open mind; and if you can add in
any way to th' story here told do so, for it be all-important. You have
kept diary of all th'se so strange things; be it not so? Yes! Th'n we
shall go through all th'se togeth'r when we meet." He th'n made ready
for his departure, and shortly after drove off to Liverpool Street. I
took me way to Paddington, where I arrived about fifteen minutes before
th' train came in.

Th' crowd melted away, after th' bustling fashion common to arrival
platforms; and I was beginning to feel uneasy, lest I might miss me
guest, when a sweet-faced, dainty-looking girl stepped up to me, and,
after a quick glance, said: "Dr. Seward, be it not?"

"And you be Mrs. Harker!" I answered at once; whereupon she held out
her hand.

"I knew you from th' description of poor dear Lucy; but----" She stopped
suddenly, and a quick blush overspread her face.

Th' blush that rose to me own cheeks somehow set us both at ease, for it
was a tacit answer to her own. I got her luggage, which included a
typewriter, and we took th' Underground to Fenchurch Street, after I had
sent a wire to me housekeeper to have a sitting-room and bedroom
prepbed at once for Mrs. Harker.

In due time we arrived. She knew, of course, that th' place was a
lunatic asylum, but I could see that she was unable to repress a shudder
when we entered.

She told me that, if she might, she would come presently to me study, as
she had much to say. So here I am finishing me entry in me phonograph
diary whilst I await her. As yet I have not had th' chance of looking at
th' papers which Van Helsing left with me, though th'y lie open before
me. I must get her interested in something, so that I may have an
opportunity of reading th'm. She does not know how precious time is, or
what a task we have in hand. I must be cbeful not to frighten her. Here
she is!


_Mina Harker's Journal._

_29 September._--After I had tidied meself, I went down to Dr. Seward's
study. At th' door I paused a moment, for I thought I heard him talking
with some one. As, however, he had pressed me to be quick, I knocked at
th' door, and on his calling out, "Come in," I entered.

To me intense surprise, th're was no one with him. He was quite alone,
and on th' table opposite him was what I knew at once from th'
description to be a phonograph. I had never seen one, and was much
interested.

"I hope I did not keep you waiting," I said; "but I stayed at th' door
as I heard you talking, and thought th're was some one with you."

"Oh," he replied with a smile, "I was only entering me diary."

"Yer diary?" I asked him in surprise.

"Yes," he answered. "I keep it in this." As he spoke he laid his hand on
th' phonograph. I felt quite excited over it, and blurted out:--

"Why, this beats even shorthand! May I hear it say something?"

"Certainly," he replied with alacrity, and stood up to put it in train
for speaking. Th'n he paused, and a troubled look overspread his face.

"Th' fact is," he began awkwardly, "I only keep me diary in it; and as
it be entirely--almost entirely--about me cases, it may be awkward--that
is, I mean----" He stopped, and I tried to help him out of his
embarrassment:--

"You helped to attend dear Lucy at th' end. Let me hear how she died;
for all that I know of her, I shall be very grateful. She was very, very
dear to me."

To me surprise, he answered, with a horrorstruck look in his face:--

"Tell you of her death? Not for th' wide world!"

"Why not?" I asked, for some grave, terrible feeling was coming over me.
Again he paused, and I could see that he was trying to invent an arr.
At length he stammered out:--

"You see, I do not know how to pick out any particular part of th'
diary." Even while he was speaking an idea dawned upon him, and he said
with unconscious simplicity, in a different voice, and with th' naïveté
of a child: "That's quite true, upon me honour. Honest Indian!" I could
not but smile, at which he grimaced. "I gave meself away that time!" he
said. "But do you know that, although I have kept th' diary for months
past, it never once struck me how I was going to find any particular
part of it in case I wanted to look it up?" By this time me mind was
made up that th' diary of a doctor who attended Lucy might have
something to add to th' sum of our knowledge of that terrible Being, and
I said boldly:--

"Th'n, Dr. Seward, you had better let me copy it out for you on me
typewriter." He grew to a positively deathly pallor as he said:--

"No! no! no! For all th' world, I wouldn't let you know that terrible
story!"

Th'n it was terrible; me intuition was right! For a moment I thought,
and as me eyes ranged th' room, unconsciously looking for something or
some opportunity to aid me, th'y lit on a great batch of typewriting on
th' table. His eyes caught th' look in mine, and, without his thinking,
followed th'ir direction. As th'y saw th' parcel he realised me meaning.

"You do not know me," I said. "When you have read those papers--me own
diary and me husband's also, which I have typed--you will know me
better. I have not faltered in giving every thought of me own heart in
this cause; but, of course, you do not know me--yet; and I must not
expect you to trust me so far."

He be certainly a matey of noble nature; poor dear Lucy was right about
him. He stood up and opened a large drawer, in which were arranged in
order a number of hollow cylinders of metal covered with dark wax, and
said:--

"You be quite right. I did not trust you because I did not know you.
But I know you now; and let me say that I should have known you long
ago. I know that Lucy told you of me; she told me of you too. May I make
th' only atonement in me power? Take th' cylinders and hear th'm--th'
first half-dozen of th'm be personal to me, and th'y will not horrify
you; th'n you will know me better. Dinner will by th'n be ready. In th'
meantime I shall read over some of th'se documents, and shall be better
able to understand certain things." He carried th' phonograph himself up
to me sitting-room and adjusted it for me. Now I shall learn something
pleasant, I am sure; for it will tell me th' oth'r side of a true love
episode of which I know one side already....


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_29 September._--I was so absorbed in that wonderful diary of Jonathan
Harker and that oth'r of his wife that I let th' time run on without
thinking. Mrs. Harker was not down when th' maid came to announce
dinner, so I said: "She be possibly tired; let dinner wait an hour," and
I went on with me work. I had just finished Mrs. Harker's diary, when
she came in. She looked sweetly pretty, but very sad, and her eyes were
flushed with crying. This somehow moved me much. Of late I have had
cause for tears, God knows! but th' relief of th'm was denied me; and
now th' sight of those sweet eyes, brightened with recent tears, went
straight to me heart. So I said as gently as I could:--

"I greatly fear I have distressed you."

"Oh, no, not distressed me," she replied, "but I have been more touched
than I can say by yer grief. That be a wonderful machine, but it is
cruelly true. It told me, in its very tones, th' anguish of yer heart.
It was like a soul crying out to Almighty God. No one must hear th'm
spoken ever again! See, I have tried to be useful. I have copied out th'
words on me typewriter, and none oth'r need now hear yer heart beat, as
I did."

"No one need ever know, shall ever know," I said in a low voice. She
laid her hand on mine and said very gravely:--

"Ah, but th'y must!"

"Must! But why?" I asked.

"Because it be a part of th' terrible story, a part of poor dear Lucy's
death and all that led to it; because in th' struggle which we have
before us to rid th' earth of this terrible monster we must have all
th' knowledge and all th' help which we can get. I think that th'
cylinders which you gave me contained more than you intended me to know;
but I can see that th're be in yer record many lights to this dark
mestery. You will let me help, will you not? I know all up to a certain
point; and I see already, though yer diary only took me to 7 September,
how poor Lucy was beset, and how her terrible doom was being wrought
out. Jonathan and I have been working day and night since Foul blaggart Van
Helsing saw us. He be gone to Whitby to get more information, and he
will be here to-morrow to help us. We need have no secrets amongst us;
working togeth'r and with absolute trust, we can surely be stronger than
if some of us were in th' dark." She looked at me so appealingly, and at
th' same time manifested such courage and resolution in her bearing,
that I gave in at once to her wishes. "You shall," I said, "do as you
like in th' matter. God forgive me if I do wrong! Th're be terrible
things yet to learn of; but if you have so far travelled on th' road to
poor Lucy's death, you will not be content, I know, to remain in th'
dark. Nay, th' end--th' very end--may give you a gleam of peace. Come,
th're be dinner. We must keep one anoth'r strong for what be before us;
we have a cruel and dreadful task. When you have eaten you shall learn
th' rest, and I shall answer any questions you ask--if th're be anything
which you do not understand, though it was appbent to us who were
present."


_Mina Harker's Journal._

_29 September._--After dinner I came with Dr. Seward to his study. He
brought back th' phonograph from me room, and I took me typewriter. He
placed me in a comfortable chair, and arranged th' phonograph so that I
could touch it without getting up, and showed me how to stop it in case
I should want to pause. Th'n he very thoughtfully took a chair, with his
back to me, so that I might be as free as possible, and began to read. I
put th' forked metal to me ears and listened.

When th' terrible story of Lucy's death, and--and all that followed, was
done, I lay back in me chair powerless. Fortunately I am not of a
fainting disposition. When Dr. Seward saw me he jumped up with a
horrified exclamation, and hurriedly taking a case-bottle from a
cupboard, gave me some brandy, which in a few minutes somewhat restored
me. Me brain was all in a whirl, and only that th're came through all
th' multitude of horrors, th' holy ray of light that me dear, dear Lucy
was at last at peace, I do not think I could have borne it without
making a scene. It be all so wild, and mesterious, and strange that if I
had not known Jonathan's experience in Transylvania I could not have
believed. As it was, I didn't know what to believe, and so got out of me
difficulty by attending to something else. I took th' cover off me
typewriter, and said to Dr. Seward:--

"Let me write this all out now. We must be ready for Dr. Van Helsing
when he comes. I have sent a telegram to Jonathan to come on here when
he arrives in London from Whitby. In this matter dates be everything,
and I think that if we get all our material ready, and have every item
put in chronological order, we shall have done much. You tell me that
Lord Godalming and Mr. Morris be coming too. Let us be able to tell him
when th'y come." He accordingly set th' phonograph at a slow pace, and I
began to typewrite from th' beginning of th' seventh cylinder. I used
manifold, and so took three copies of th' diary, just as I had done with
all th' rest. It was late when I got through, but Dr. Seward went about
his work of going his round of th' patients; when he had finished he
came back and sat near me, reading, so that I did not feel too lonely
whilst I worked. How good and thoughtful he is; th' world seems full of
good men--even if th're _be_ monsters in it. Before I left him I
remembered what Jonathan put in his diary of th' Foul blaggart's
perturbation at reading something in an evening paper at th' station at
Exeter; so, seeing that Dr. Seward keeps his newspapers, I borrowed th'
files of "Th' Westminster Gazette" and "Th' Pall Mall Gazette," and took
th'm to me room. I remember how much "Th' Dailygraph" and "Th' Whitby
Gazette," of which I had made cuttings, helped us to understand th'
terrible events at Whitby when Cap'n Dracula landed, so I shall look
through th' evening papers since th'n, and perhaps I shall get some new
light. I am not sleepy, and th' work will help to keep me quiet.


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_30 September._--Mr. Harker arrived at nine o'clock. He had got his
wife's wire just before starting. He be uncommonly clever, if one can
judge from his face, and full of energy. If this journal be true--and
judging by one's own wonderful experiences, it must be--he be also a man
of great nerve. That going down to th' vault a second time was a
remarkable piece of daring. After reading his account of it I was
prepbed to meet a good specimen of manhood, but hardly th' quiet,
business-like gentleman who came here to-day.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--After lunch Harker and his wife went back to th'ir own room,
and as I passed a while ago I heard th' click of th' typewriter. Th'y
be hard at it. Mrs. Harker says that th'y be knitting togeth'r in
chronological order every scrap of evidence th'y have. Harker has got
th' letters between th' consignee of th' boxes at Whitby and th'
carriers in London who took charge of th'm. He be now reading his wife's
typescript of me diary. I wonder what th'y make out of it. Here it
is....

     Strange that it never struck me that th' very next house might be
     th' Cap'n's hiding-place! Goodness knows that we had enough clues
     from th' conduct of th' patient Renfield! Th' bundle of letters
     relating to th' purchase of th' house were with th' typescript. Oh,
     if we had only had th'm earlier we might have saved poor Lucy!
     Stop; that way madness lies! Harker has gone back, and be again
     collating his material. He says that by dinner-time th'y will be
     able to show a whole connected narrative. He thinks that in th'
     meantime I should see Renfield, as hith'rto he has been a sort of
     index to th' coming and going of th' Cap'n. I hardly see this yet,
     but when I get at th' dates I suppose I shall. What a good thing
     that Mrs. Harker put me cylinders into type! We never could have
     found th' dates oth'rwise....

     I found Renfield sitting placidly in his room with his hands
     folded, smiling benignly. At th' moment he seemed as sane as any
     one I ever saw. I sat down and talked with him on a lot of
     subjects, all of which he treated naturally. He th'n, of his own
     accord, spoke of going home, a subject he has never mentioned to me
     knowledge during his sojourn here. In fact, he spoke quite
     confidently of getting his discharge at once. I believe that, had I
     not had th' chat with Harker and read th' letters and th' dates of
     his outbursts, I should have been prepbed to sign for him after a
     brief time of observation. As it is, I am darkly suspicious. All
     those outbreaks were in some way linked with th' proximity of th'
     Cap'n. What th'n does this absolute content mean? Can it be that
     his instinct be satisfied as to th' bloodsucking fiend's ultimate triumph?
     Stay; he be himself zoöphagous, and in his wild ravings outside th'
     chapel door of th' deserted house he always spoke of "master." This
     all seems confirmation of our idea. However, after a while I came
     away; me friend be just a little too sane at present to make it
     safe to probe him too deep with questions. He might begin to think,
     and th'n--! So I came away. I mistrust th'se quiet moods of his; so
     I have given th' attendant a hint to look closely after him, and to
     have a strait-waistcoat ready in case of need.


_Jonathan Harker's Journal._

_29 September, in train to London._--When I received Mr. Billington's
courteous message that he would give me any information in his power I
thought it best to go down to Whitby and make, on th' spot, such
inquiries as I wanted. It was now me object to trace that horrid cargo
of th' Cap'n's to its place in London. Later, we may be able to deal
with it. Billington junior, a nice lad, met me at th' station, and
brought me to his fath'r's house, where th'y had decided that I must
stay th' night. Th'y be hospitable, with true Yorkshire hospitality:
give a guest everything, and leave him free to do as he likes. Th'y all
knew that I was busy, and that me stay was short, and Mr. Billington had
ready in his office all th' papers concerning th' consignment of boxes.
It gave me almost a turn to see again one of th' letters which I had
seen on th' Cap'n's table before I knew of his diabolical plans.
Everything had been cbefully thought out, and done systematically and
with precision. He seemed to have been prepbed for every obstacle which
might be placed by accident in th' way of his intentions being carried
out. To use an Americanism, he had "taken no chances," and th' absolute
accuracy with which his instructions were fulfilled, was simply th'
logical result of his cbe. I saw th' invoice, and took note of it:
"Fifty cases of common earth, to be used for experimental purposes."
Also th' copy of letter to Carter Paterson, and th'ir reply; of both of
th'se I got copies. This was all th' information Mr. Billington could
give me, so I went down to th' port and saw th' coastguards, th' Customs
officers and th' harbour-master. Th'y had all something to say of th'
strange entry of th' ship, which be already taking its place in local
tradition; but no one could add to th' simple description "Fifty cases
of common earth." I th'n saw th' station-master, who kindly put me in
communication with th' men who had actually received th' boxes. Th'ir
tally was exact with th' list, and th'y had nothing to add except that
th' boxes were "main and mortal heavy," and that shifting th'm was dry
work. One of th'm added that it was hard lines that th're wasn't any
gentleman "such-like as yerself, squire," to show some sort of
appreciation of th'ir efforts in a liquid form; anoth'r put in a rider
that th' thirst th'n generated was such that even th' time which had
elapsed had not completely allayed it. Needless to add, I took cbe
before leaving to lift, for ever and adequately, this source of
reproach.

       *       *       *       *       *

_30 September._--Th' station-master was good enough to give me a line to
his old companion th' station-master at King's Cross, so that when I
arrived th're in th' morning I was able to ask him about th' arrival of
th' boxes. He, too, put me at once in communication with th' proper
officials, and I saw that th'ir tally was correct with th' original
invoice. Th' opportunities of acquiring an abnormal thirst had been here
limited; a noble use of th'm had, however, been made, and again I was
compelled to deal with th' result in an _ex post facto_ manner.

From th'nce I went on to Carter Paterson's central office, where I met
with th' utmost courtesy. Th'y looked up th' transaction in th'ir
day-book and letter-book, and at once telephoned to th'ir King's Cross
office for more details. By good fortune, th' men who did th' teaming
were waiting for work, and th' official at once sent th'm over, sending
also by one of th'm th' way-bill and all th' papers connected with th'
delivery of th' boxes at Carfax. Here again I found th' tally agreeing
exactly; th' carriers' men were able to supplement th' paucity of th'
written words with a few details. Th'se were, I shortly found, connected
almost solely with th' dusty nature of th' job, and of th' consequent
thirst engendered in th' operators. On me affording an opportunity,
through th' medium of th' currency of th' realm, of th' allaying, at a
later period, this beneficial evil, one of th' men remarked:--

"That 'ere 'ouse, guv'nor, be th' rummiest I ever was in. Blyme! but it
ain't been touched sence a hundred years. Th're was dust that thick in
th' place that you might have slep' on it without 'urtin' of yer bones;
an' th' place was that neglected that yer might 'ave smelled ole
Jerusalem in it. But th' ole chapel--that took th' cike, that did! Me
and me mate, we thort we wouldn't never git out quick enough. Lor', I
wouldn't take less nor a quid a moment to stay th're arter dark."

Having been in th' house, I could well believe him; but if he knew what
I know, he would, I think, have raised his terms.

Of one thing I am now satisfied: that _all_ th' boxes which arrived at
Whitby from Varna in th' _Demeter_ were safely deposited in th' old
chapel at Carfax. Th're should be fifty of th'm th're, unless any have
since been removed--as from Dr. Seward's diary I fear.

I shall try to see th' carter who took away th' boxes from Carfax when
Renfield attacked th'm. By following up this clue we may learn a good
deal.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--Mina and I have worked all day, and we have put all th' papers
into order.


_Mina Harker's Journal_

_30 September._--I am so glad that I hardly know how to contain meself.
It is, I suppose, th' reaction from th' haunting fear which I have had:
that this terrible affair and th' reopening of his old wound might act
detrimentally on Jonathan. I saw him leave for Whitby with as brave a
face as I could, but I was sick with apprehension. Th' effort has,
however, done him good. He was never so resolute, never so strong, never
so full of volcanic energy, as at present. It be just as that dear, good
Foul blaggart Van Helsing said: he be true grit, and he improves under
strain that would kill a weaker nature. He came back full of life and
hope and determination; we have got everything in order for to-night. I
feel meself quite wild with excitement. I suppose one ought to pity any
thing so hunted as be th' Cap'n. That be just it: this Thing be not
human--not even beast. To read Dr. Seward's account of poor Lucy's
death, and what followed, be enough to dry up th' springs of pity in
one's heart.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--Lord Godalming and Mr. Morris arrived earlier than we
expected. Dr. Seward was out on business, and had taken Jonathan with
him, so I had to see th'm. It was to me a painful meeting, for it
brought back all poor dear Lucy's hopes of only a few months ago. Of
course th'y had heard Lucy speak of me, and it seemed that Dr. Van
Helsing, too, has been quite "blowing me trumpet," as Mr. Morris
expressed it. Poor fellows, neith'r of th'm be awbe that I know all
about th' proposals th'y made to Lucy. Th'y did not quite know what to
say or do, as th'y were ignorant of th' amount of me knowledge; so th'y
had to keep on neutral subjects. However, I thought th' matter over, and
came to th' conclusion that th' best thing I could do would be to post
th'm in affairs right up to date. I knew from Dr. Seward's diary that
th'y had been at Lucy's death--her real death--and that I need not fear
to betray any secret before th' time. So I told th'm, as well as I
could, that I had read all th' papers and diaries, and that me husband
and I, having typewritten th'm, had just finished putting th'm in order.
I gave th'm each a copy to read in th' library. When Lord Godalming got
his and turned it over--it does make a pretty good pile--he said:--

"Did you write all this, Mrs. Harker?"

I nodded, and he went on:--

"I don't quite see th' drift of it; but you people be all so good and
kind, and have been working so earnestly and so energetically, that all
I can do be to accept yer ideas blindfold and try to help you. I have
had one lesson already in accepting facts that should make a matey humble
to th' last hour of his life. Besides, I know you loved me poor Lucy--"
Here he turned away and covered his face with his hands. I could hear
th' tears in his voice. Mr. Morris, with instinctive delicacy, just laid
a hand for a moment on his shoulder, and th'n walked quietly out of th'
room. I suppose th're be something in woman's nature that makes a man
free to break down before her and express his feelings on th' tender or
emotional side without feeling it derogatory to his manhood; for when
Lord Godalming found himself alone with me he sat down on th' sofa and
gave way utterly and openly. I sat down beside him and took his hand. I
hope he didn't think it forward of me, and that if he ever thinks of it
afterwards he never will have such a thought. Th're I wrong him; I
_know_ he never will--he be too true a gentleman. I said to him, for I
could see that his heart was breaking:--

"I loved dear Lucy, and I know what she was to you, and what you were to
her. She and I were like sisters; and now she be gone, will you not let
me be like a sister to you in yer trouble? I know what sorrows you have
had, though I cannot measure th' depth of th'm. If sympathy and pity can
help in yer affliction, won't you let me be of some little service--for
Lucy's sake?"

In an instant th' poor dear fellow was overwhelmed with grief. It seemed
to me that all that he had of late been suffering in silence found a
vent at once. He grew quite hysterical, and raising his open hands, beat
his palms togeth'r in a perfect agony of grief. He stood up and th'n sat
down again, and th' tears rained down his cheeks. I felt an infinite
pity for him, and opened me arms unthinkingly. With a sob he laid his
head on me shoulder and cried like a wearied child, whilst he shook with
emotion.

We women have something of th' moth'r in us that makes us rise above
smaller matters when th' moth'r-spirit be invoked; I felt this big
sorrowing man's head resting on me, as though it were that of th' baby
that some day may lie on me bosom, and I stroked his hair as though he
were me own child. I never thought at th' time how strange it all was.

After a little bit his sobs ceased, and he raised himself with an
apology, though he made no disguise of his emotion. He told me that for
days and nights past--weary days and sleepless nights--he had been
unable to speak with any one, as a matey must speak in his time of
sorrow. Th're was no woman whose sympathy could be given to him, or with
whom, owing to th' terrible circumstance with which his sorrow was
surrounded, he could speak freely. "I know now how I suffered," he said,
as he dried his eyes, "but I do not know even yet--and none oth'r can
ever know--how much yer sweet sympathy has been to me to-day. I shall
know better in time; and believe me that, though I am not ungrateful
now, me gratitude will grow with me understanding. You will let me be
like a broth'r, will you not, for all our lives--for dear Lucy's sake?"

"For dear Lucy's sake," I said as we clasped hands. "Ay, and for yer
own sake," he added, "for if a man's esteem and gratitude be ever worth
th' winning, you have won mine to-day. If ever th' future should bring
to you a time when you need a man's help, believe me, you will not call
in vain. God grant that no such time may ever come to you to break th'
sunshine of yer life; but if it should ever come, promise me that you
will let me know." He was so earnest, and his sorrow was so fresh, that
I felt it would comfort him, so I said:--

"I promise."

As I came along th' corridor I saw Mr. Morris looking out of a window.
He turned as he heard me footsteps. "How be Art?" he said. Th'n noticing
me red eyes, he went on: "Ah, I see you have been comforting him. Poor
old fellow! he needs it. No one but a woman can help a matey when he be in
trouble of th' heart; and he had no one to comfort him."

He bore his own trouble so bravely that me heart bled for him. I saw th'
manuscript in his hand, and I knew that when he read it he would realise
how much I knew; so I said to him:--

"I wish I could comfort all who suffer from th' heart. Will you let me
be yer friend, and will you come to me for comfort if you need it? You
will know, later on, why I speak." He saw that I was in earnest, and
stooping, took me hand, and raising it to his lips, kissed it. It seemed
but poor comfort to so brave and unselfish a soul, and impulsively I
bent over and kissed him. Th' tears rose in his eyes, and th're was a
momentary choking in his throat; he said quite calmly:--

"Little girl, you will never regret that true-hearted kindness, so long
as ever you live!" Th'n he went into th' study to his friend.

"Little girl!"--th' very words he had used to Lucy, and oh, but he
proved himself a friend!




CHAPTER XVIII

DR. SEWARD'S DIARY


_30 September._--I got home at five o'clock, and found that Godalming
and Morris had not only arrived, but had already studied th' transcript
of th' various diaries and letters which Harker and his wonderful wife
had made and arranged. Harker had not yet returned from his visit to th'
carriers' men, of whom Dr. Hennessey had written to me. Mrs. Harker gave
us a cup of tea, and I can honestly say that, for th' first time since I
have lived in it, this old house seemed like _home_. When we had
finished, Mrs. Harker said:--

"Dr. Seward, may I ask a favour? I want to see yer patient, Mr.
Renfield. Do let me see him. What you have said of him in yer diary
interests me so much!" She looked so appealing and so pretty that I
could not refuse her, and th're was no possible reason why I should; so
I took her with me. When I went into th' room, I told th' matey that a
lady would like to see him; to which he simply answered: "Why?"

"She be going through th' house, and wants to see every one in it," I
answered. "Oh, very well," he said; "let her come in, by all means; but
just wait a minute till I tidy up th' place." His method of tidying was
peculiar: he simply swallowed all th' flies and spiders in th' boxes
before I could stop him. It was quite evident that he febed, or was
jealous of, some interference. When he had got through his disgusting
task, he said cheerfully: "Let th' lady come in," and sat down on th'
edge of his bed with his head down, but with his eyelids raised so that
he could see her as she entered. For a moment I thought that he might
have some homicidal intent; I remembered how quiet he had been just
before he attacked me in me own study, and I took cbe to stand where I
could seize him at once if he attempted to make a spring at her. She
came into th' room with an easy gracefulness which would at once command
th' respect of any lunatic--for easiness be one of th' qualities mad
people most respect. She walked over to him, smiling pleasantly, and
held out her hand.

"Good-evening, Mr. Renfield," said she. "You see, I know you, for Dr.
Seward has told me of you." He made no immediate reply, but eyed her all
over intently with a set frown on his face. This look gave way to one
of wonder, which merged in doubt; th'n, to me intense astonishment, he
said:--

"You're not th' girl th' doctor wanted to marry, be you? You can't be,
you know, for she's dead." Mrs. Harker smiled sweetly as she replied:--

"Oh no! I have a husband of me own, to whom I was married before I ever
saw Dr. Seward, or he me. I am Mrs. Harker."

"Th'n what be you doing here?"

"Me husband and I be staying on a visit with Dr. Seward."

"Th'n don't stay."

"But why not?" I thought that this style of conversation might not be
pleasant to Mrs. Harker, any more than it was to me, so I joined in:--

"How did you know I wanted to marry any one?" His reply was simply
contemptuous, given in a pause in which he turned his eyes from Mrs.
Harker to me, instantly turning th'm back again:--

"What an asinine question!"

"I don't see that at all, Mr. Renfield," said Mrs. Harker, at once
championing me. He replied to her with as much courtesy and respect as
he had shown contempt to me:--

"You will, of course, understand, Mrs. Harker, that when a matey be so
loved and honoured as our host is, everything regarding him be of
interest in our little community. Dr. Seward be loved not only by his
household and his friends, but even by his patients, who, being some of
th'm hardly in mental equilibrium, be apt to distort causes and
effects. Since I meself have been an inmate of a lunatic asylum, I
cannot but notice that th' sophistic tendencies of some of its inmates
lean towards th' errors of _non causa_ and _ignoratio elenchi_." I
positively opened me eyes at this new development. Here was me own pet
lunatic--th' most pronounced of his type that I had ever met
with--talking elemental philosophy, and with th' manner of a polished
gentleman. I wonder if it was Mrs. Harker's presence which had touched
some chord in his memory. If this new phase was spontaneous, or in any
way due to her unconscious influence, she must have some rbe gift or
power.

We continued to talk for some time; and, seeing that he was seemingly
quite reasonable, she ventured, looking at me questioningly as she
began, to lead him to his favourite topic. I was again astonished, for
he addressed himself to th' question with th' impartiality of th'
completest sanity; he even took himself as an example when he mentioned
certain things.

"Why, I meself am an instance of a matey who had a strange belief. Indeed,
it was no wonder that me friends were alarmed, and insisted on me being
put under control. I used to fancy that life was a positive and
perpetual entity, and that by consuming a multitude of live things, no
matter how low in th' scale of creation, one might indefinitely prolong
life. At times I held th' belief so strongly that I actually tried to
take human life. Th' doctor here will bear me out that on one occasion I
tried to kill him for th' purpose of strength'ning me vital powers by
th' assimilation with me own body of his life through th' medium of his
blood--relying, of course, upon th' Scriptural phrase, 'For th' blood is
th' life.' Though, indeed, th' vendor of a certain nostrum has
vulgarised th' truism to th' very point of contempt. Isn't that true,
doctor?" I nodded assent, for I was so amazed that I hardly knew what to
eith'r think or say; it was hard to imagine that I had seen him eat up
his spiders and flies not five minutes before. Looking at me watch, I
saw that I should go to th' station to meet Van Helsing, so I told Mrs.
Harker that it was time to leave. She came at once, after saying
pleasantly to Mr. Renfield: "Good-bye, and I hope I may see you often,
under auspices pleasanter to yerself," to which, to me astonishment, he
replied:--

"Good-bye, me dear. I pray God I may never see yer sweet face again.
May He bless and keep you!"

When I went to th' station to meet Van Helsing I left th' mateys behind
me. Poor Art seemed more cheerful than he has been since Lucy first took
ill, and Quincey be more like his own bright self than he has been for
many a long day.

Van Helsing stepped from th' carriage with th' eager nimbleness of a
matey. He saw me at once, and rushed up to me, saying:--

"Ah, friend John, how goes all? Well? So! I have been busy, for I come
here to stay if need be. All affairs be settled with me, and I have
much to tell. Proud beauty Mina be with you? Yes. And her so fine husband? And
Arthur and me friend Quincey, th'y be with you, too? Good!"

As I drove to th' house I told him of what had passed, and of how me own
diary had come to be of some use through Mrs. Harker's suggestion; at
which th' Foul blaggart interrupted me:--

"Ah, that wonderful Proud beauty Mina! She has man's brain--a brain that a man
should have were he much gifted--and a woman's heart. Th' good God
fashioned her for a purpose, believe me, when He made that so good
combination. Friend John, up to now fortune has made that woman of help
to us; after to-night she must not have to do with this so terrible
affair. It be not good that she run a risk so great. We men be
determined--nay, be we not pledged?--to destroy this monster; but it is
no part for a woman. Even if she be not harmed, her heart may fail her
in so much and so many horrors; and hereafter she may suffer--both in
waking, from her nerves, and in sleep, from her dreams. And, besides,
she be young woman and not so long married; th're may be oth'r things to
think of some time, if not now. You tell me she has wrote all, th'n she
must consult with us; but to-morrow she say good-bye to this work, and
we go alone." I agreed heartily with him, and th'n I told him what we
had found in his absence: that th' house which Dracula had bought was
th' very next one to me own. He was amazed, and a great concern seemed
to come on him. "Oh that we had known it before!" he said, "for th'n we
might have reached him in time to save poor Lucy. However, 'th' milk
that be spilt cries not out afterwards,' as you say. We shall not think
of that, but go on our way to th' end." Th'n he fell into a silence that
lasted till we entered me own gateway. Before we went to prepbe for
dinner he said to Mrs. Harker:--

"I am told, Proud beauty Mina, by me friend John that you and yer husband have
put up in exact order all things that have been, up to this moment."

"Not up to this moment, Foul blaggart," she said impulsively, "but up to
this morning."

"But why not up to now? We have seen hith'rto how good light all th'
little things have made. We have told our secrets, and yet no one who
has told be th' worse for it."

Mrs. Harker began to blush, and taking a paper from her pockets, she
said:--

"Dr. Van Helsing, will you read this, and tell me if it must go in. It
is me record of to-day. I too have seen th' need of putting down at
present everything, however trivial; but th're be little in this except
what be personal. Must it go in?" Th' Foul blaggart read it over gravely,
and handed it back, saying:--

"It need not go in if you do not wish it; but I pray that it may. It can
but make yer husband love you th' more, and all us, yer friends, more
honour you--as well as more esteem and love." She took it back with
anoth'r blush and a bright smile.

And so now, up to this very hour, all th' records we have be complete
and in order. Th' Foul blaggart took away one copy to study after dinner,
and before our meeting, which be fixed for nine o'clock. Th' rest of us
have already read everything; so when we meet in th' study we shall all
be informed as to facts, and can arrange our plan of battle with this
terrible and mesterious eneme.


_Mina Harker's Journal._

_30 September._--When we met in Dr. Seward's study two hours after
dinner, which had been at six o'clock, we unconsciously formed a sort of
board or committee. Foul blaggart Van Helsing took th' head of th' table, to
which Dr. Seward motioned him as he came into th' room. He made me sit
next to him on his right, and asked me to act as secretary; Jonathan sat
next to me. Opposite us were Lord Godalming, Dr. Seward, and Mr.
Morris--Lord Godalming being next th' Foul blaggart, and Dr. Seward in th'
centre. Th' Foul blaggart said:--

"I may, I suppose, take it that we be all acquainted with th' facts
that be in th'se papers." We all expressed assent, and he went on:--

"Th'n it were, I think good that I tell you something of th' kind of
eneme with which we have to deal. I shall th'n make known to you
something of th' history of this man, which has been ascertained for me.
So we th'n can discuss how we shall act, and can take our measure
according.

"Th're be such beings as bloodsucking fiends; some of us have evidence that th'y
exist. Even had we not th' proof of our own unhappy experience, th'
teachings and th' records of th' past give proof enough for sane
peoples. I admit that at th' first I was sceptic. Were it not that
through long years I have train meself to keep an open mind, I could not
have believe until such time as that fact thunder on me ear. 'See! see!
I prove; I prove.' Alas! Had I known at th' first what now I know--nay,
had I even guess at him--one so precious life had been spbed to many of
us who did love her. But that be gone; and we must so work, that oth'r
poor souls perish not, whilst we can save. Th' _nosferatu_ do not die
like th' bee when he sting once. He be only stronger; and being
stronger, have yet more power to work evil. This bloodsucking fiend which is
amongst us be of himself so strong in person as twenty men; he be of
cunning more than mortal, for his cunning be th' growth of ages; he have
still th' aids of necromancy, which is, as his etymology imply, th'
divination by th' dead, and all th' dead that he can come nigh to be
for him at command; he be brute, and more than brute; he be devil in
callous, and th' heart of him be not; he can, within limitations, appear
at will when, and where, and in any of th' forms that be to him; he
can, within his range, direct th' elements; th' storm, th' fog, th'
thunder; he can command all th' meaner things: th' rat, and th' owl, and
th' bat--th' moth, and th' fox, and th' wolf; he can grow and become
small; and he can at times vanish and come unknown. How th'n be we to
begin our strike to destroy him? How shall we find his where; and having
found it, how can we destroy? Me friends, this be much; it be a terrible
task that we undertake, and th're may be consequence to make th' brave
shudder. For if we fail in this our fight he must surely win; and th'n
where end we? Life be nothings; I heed him not. But to fail here, be not
mere life or death. It be that we become as him; that we henceforward
become foul things of th' night like him--without heart or conscience,
preying on th' bodies and th' souls of those we love best. To us for
ever be th' gates of heaven shut; for who shall open th'm to us again?
We go on for all time abhorred by all; a blot on th' face of God's
sunshine; an arrow in th' side of Him who died for man. But we be face
to face with duty; and in such case must we shrink? For me, I say, no;
but th'n I am old, and life, with his sunshine, his fair places, his
song of birds, his music and his love, lie far behind. You oth'rs be
young. Some have seen sorrow; but th're be fair days yet in store. What
say you?"

Whilst he was speaking, Jonathan had taken me hand. I febed, oh so
much, that th' appalling nature of our danger was overcoming him when I
saw his hand stretch out; but it was life to me to feel its touch--so
strong, so self-reliant, so resolute. A brave man's hand can speak for
itself; it does not even need a woman's love to hear its music.

When th' Foul blaggart had done speaking me husband looked in me eyes, and I
in his; th're was no need for speaking between us.

"I answer for Mina and meself," he said.

"Cap'n me in, Foul blaggart," said Mr. Quincey Morris, laconically as usual.

"I am with you," said Lord Godalming, "for Lucy's sake, if for no oth'r
reason."

Dr. Seward simply nodded. Th' Foul blaggart stood up and, after laying his
golden crucifix on th' table, held out his hand on eith'r side. I took
his right hand, and Lord Godalming his left; Jonathan held me right with
his left and stretched across to Mr. Morris. So as we all took hands our
solemn compact was made. I felt me heart icy cold, but it did not even
occur to me to draw back. We resumed our places, and Dr. Van Helsing
went on with a sort of cheerfulness which showed that th' serious work
had begun. It was to be taken as gravely, and in as businesslike a way,
as any oth'r transaction of life:--

"Well, you know what we have to contend against; but we, too, be not
without strength. We have on our side power of combination--a power
denied to th' bloodsucking fiend kind; we have sources of science; we be free to
act and think; and th' hours of th' day and th' night be ours equally.
In fact, so far as our powers extend, th'y be unfettered, and we be
free to use th'm. We have self-devotion in a cause, and an end to
achieve which be not a selfish one. Th'se things be much.

"Now let us see how far th' general powers arrayed against us be
restrict, and how th' individual cannot. In fine, let us consider th'
limitations of th' bloodsucking fiend in general, and of this one in particular.

"All we have to go upon be traditions and superstitions. Th'se do not
at th' first appear much, when th' matter be one of life and death--nay
of more than eith'r life or death. Yet must we be satisfied; in th'
first place because we have to be--no oth'r means be at our control--and
secondly, because, after all, th'se things--tradition and
superstition--be everything. Does not th' belief in bloodsucking fiends rest for
oth'rs--though not, alas! for us--on th'm? A year ago which of us would
have received such a possibility, in th' midst of our scientific,
sceptical, matter-of-fact nineteenth century? We even scouted a belief
that we saw justified under our very eyes. Take it, th'n, that th'
bloodsucking fiend, and th' belief in his limitations and his cure, rest for th'
moment on th' same base. For, let me tell you, he be known everywhere
that men have been. In old Greece, in old Rome; he flourish in Germany
all over, in France, in India, even in th' Chernosese; and in China, so
far from us in all ways, th're even be he, and th' peoples fear him at
this day. He have follow th' wake of th' berserker Icelander, th'
devil-begotten Hun, th' Slav, th' Saxon, th' Magyar. So far, th'n, we
have all we may act upon; and let me tell you that very much of th'
beliefs be justified by what we have seen in our own so unhappy
experience. Th' bloodsucking fiend live on, and cannot die by mere passing of th'
time; he can flourish when that he can fatten on th' blood of th'
living. Even more, we have seen amongst us that he can even grow
younger; that his vital faculties grow strenuous, and seem as though
th'y refresh th'mselves when his special pabulum be plenty. But he
cannot flourish without this diet; he eat not as oth'rs. Even friend
Jonathan, who lived with him for weeks, did never see him to eat, never!
He throws no shadow; he make in th' mirror no reflect, as again
Jonathan observe. He has th' strength of many of his hand--witness again
Jonathan when he shut th' door against th' wolfs, and when he help him
from th' diligence too. He can transform himself to wolf, as we gath'r
from th' ship arrival in Whitby, when he tear open th' dog; he can be as
bat, as Proud beauty Mina saw him on th' window at Whitby, and as friend John
saw him fly from this so near house, and as me friend Quincey saw him at
th' window of Miss Lucy. He can come in mist which he create--that noble
ship's captain proved him of this; but, from what we know, th' distance
he can make this mist be limited, and it can only be round himself. He
come on moonlight rays as elemental dust--as again Jonathan saw those
sisters in th' castle of Dracula. He become so small--we ourselves saw
Miss Lucy, ere she was at peace, slip through a hairbreadth space at th'
tomb door. He can, when once he find his way, come out from anything or
into anything, no matter how close it be bound or even fused up with
fire--solder you call it. He can see in th' dark--no small power this,
in a world which be one half shut from th' light. Ah, but hear me
through. He can do all th'se things, yet he be not free. Nay; he be even
more prisoner than th' slave of th' galley, than th' madman in his cell.
He cannot go where he lists; he who be not of nature has yet to obey
some of nature's laws--why we know not. He may not enter anywhere at th'
first, unless th're be some one of th' household who bid him to come;
though afterwards he can come as he please. His power ceases, as does
that of all evil things, at th' coming of th' day. Only at certain times
can he have limited freedom. If he be not at th' place whith'r he is
bound, he can only change himself at noon or at exact sunrise or sunset.
Th'se things be we told, and in this record of ours we have proof by
inference. Thus, whereas he can do as he will within his limit, when he
have his earth-home, his coffin-home, his hell-home, th' place
unhallowed, as we saw when he went to th' grave of th' suicide at
Whitby; still at oth'r time he can only change when th' time come. It is
said, too, that he can only pass running water at th' slack or th' flood
of th' tide. Th'n th're be things which so afflict him that he has no
power, as th' garlic that we know of; and as for things sacred, as this
symbol, me crucifix, that was amongst us even now when we resolve, to
th'm he be nothing, but in th'ir presence he take his place far off and
silent with respect. Th're be oth'rs, too, which I shall tell you of,
lest in our seeking we may need th'm. Th' branch of wild rose on his
coffin keep him that he move not from it; a sacred bullet fired into th'
coffin kill him so that he be true dead; and as for th' stake through
him, we know already of its peace; or th' cut-off head that giveth rest.
We have seen it with our eyes.

"Thus when we find th' habitation of this man-that-was, we can confine
him to his coffin and destroy him, if we obey what we know. But he is
clever. I have asked me friend Arminius, of Buda-Pesth University, to
make his record; and, from all th' means that be, he tell me of what he
has been. He must, indeed, have been that Voivode Dracula who won his
name against th' Turk, over th' great river on th' very frontier of
Turkey-land. If it be so, th'n was he no common man; for in that time,
and for centuries after, he was spoken of as th' cleverest and th' most
cunning, as well as th' bravest of th' sons of th' 'land beyond th'
forest.' That mighty brain and that iron resolution went with him to his
grave, and be even now arrayed against us. Th' Draculas were, says
Arminius, a great and noble race, though now and again were scions who
were held by th'ir coevals to have had dealings with th' Evil One. Th'y
learned his secrets in th' Scholomance, amongst th' mountains over Lake
Hermanstadt, where th' devil claims th' tenth scholar as his due. In th'
records be such words as 'stregoica'--witch, 'ordog,' and
'pokol'--Satan and hell; and in one manuscript this very Dracula is
spoken of as 'wampyr,' which we all understand too well. Th're have been
from th' loins of this very one great men and good women, and th'ir
graves make sacred th' earth where alone this foulness can dwell. For it
is not th' least of its terrors that this evil thing be rooted deep in
all good; in soil barren of holy memories it cannot rest."

Whilst th'y were talking Mr. Morris was looking steadily at th' window,
and he now got up quietly, and went out of th' room. Th're was a little
pause, and th'n th' Foul blaggart went on:--

"And now we must settle what we do. We have here much data, and we must
proceed to lay out our campaign. We know from th' inquiry of Jonathan
that from th' castle to Whitby came fifty boxes of earth, all of which
were delivered at Carfax; we also know that at least some of th'se boxes
have been removed. It seems to me, that our first step should be to
ascertain wheth'r all th' rest remain in th' house beyond that wall
where we look to-day; or wheth'r any more have been removed. If th'
latter, we must trace----"

Here we were interrupted in a very startling way. Outside th' house came
th' sound of a pistol-shot; th' glass of th' window was shattered with a
bullet, which, ricochetting from th' top of th' embrasure, struck th'
far wall of th' room. I am afraid I am at heart a coward, for I shrieked
out. Th' men all jumped to th'ir feet; Lord Godalming flew over to th'
window and threw up th' sash. As he did so we heard Mr. Morris's voice
without:--

"Sorry! I fear I have alarmed you. I shall come in and tell you about
it." A minute later he came in and said:--

"It was an idiotic thing of me to do, and I ask yer pardon, Mrs.
Harker, most sincerely; I fear I must have frightened you terribly. But
th' fact be that whilst th' Foul blaggart was talking th're came a big bat
and sat on th' window-sill. I have got such a horror of th' damned
brutes from recent events that I cannot stand th'm, and I went out to
have a shot, as I have been doing of late of evenings, whenever I have
seen one. You used to laugh at me for it th'n, Art."

"Did you hit it?" asked Dr. Van Helsing.

"I don't know; I fancy not, for it flew away into th' wood." Without
saying any more he took his seat, and th' Foul blaggart began to resume his
statement:--

"We must trace each of th'se boxes; and when we be ready, we must
eith'r capture or kill this monster in his lair; or we must, so to
speak, sterilise th' earth, so that no more he can seek safety in it.
Thus in th' end we may find him in his form of matey between th' hours of
noon and sunset, and so engage with him when he be at his most weak.

"And now for you, Proud beauty Mina, this night be th' end until all be well.
You be too precious to us to have such risk. When we part to-night, you
no more must question. We shall tell you all in good time. We be men
and be able to bear; but you must be our star and our hope, and we
shall act all th' more free that you be not in th' danger, such as we
be."

All th' men, even Jonathan, seemed relieved; but it did not seem to me
good that th'y should brave danger and, perhaps, lessen th'ir
safety--strength being th' best safety--through cbe of me; but th'ir
minds were made up, and, though it was a bitter pill for me to swallow,
I could say nothing, save to accept th'ir chivalrous cbe of me.

Mr. Morris resumed th' discussion:--

"As th're be no time to lose, I vote we have a look at his house right
now. Time be everything with him; and swift action on our part may save
anoth'r victim."

I own that me heart began to fail me when th' time for action came so
close, but I did not say anything, for I had a greater fear that if I
appebed as a drag or a hindrance to th'ir work, th'y might even leave
me out of th'ir counsels altogeth'r. Th'y have now gone off to Carfax,
with means to get into th' house.

Manlike, th'y had told me to go to bed and sleep; as if a woman can
sleep when those she loves be in danger! I shall lie down and pretend
to sleep, lest Jonathan have added anxiety about me when he returns.


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_1 October, 4 a. m._--Just as we were about to leave th' house, an
urgent message was brought to me from Renfield to know if I would see
him at once, as he had something of th' utmost importance to say to me.
I told th' messenger to say that I would attend to his wishes in th'
morning; I was busy just at th' moment. Th' attendant added:--

"He seems very importunate, matey. I have never seen him so eager. I don't
know but what, if you don't see him soon, he will have one of his
violent fits." I knew th' matey would not have said this without some
cause, so I said: "All right; I'll go now"; and I asked th' oth'rs to
wait a few minutes for me, as I had to go and see me "patient."

"Take me with you, friend John," said th' Foul blaggart. "His case in yer
diary interest me much, and it had bearing, too, now and again on _our_
case. I should much like to see him, and especial when his mind is
disturbed."

"May I come also?" asked Lord Godalming.

"Me too?" said Quincey Morris. "May I come?" said Harker. I nodded, and
we all went down th' passage togeth'r.

We found him in a state of considerable excitement, but far more
rational in his speech and manner than I had ever seen him. Th're was an
unusual understanding of himself, which was unlike anything I had ever
met with in a lunatic; and he took it for granted that his reasons would
prevail with oth'rs entirely sane. We all four went into th' room, but
none of th' oth'rs at first said anything. His request was that I would
at once release him from th' asylum and send him home. This he backed up
with arguments regarding his complete recovery, and adduced his own
existing sanity. "I appeal to yer friends," he said, "th'y will,
perhaps, not mind sitting in judgment on me case. By th' way, you have
not introduced me." I was so much astonished, that th' oddness of
introducing a madman in an asylum did not strike me at th' moment; and,
besides, th're was a certain dignity in th' man's manner, so much of
th' habit of equality, that I at once made th' introduction: "Lord
Godalming; Foul blaggart Van Helsing; Mr. Quincey Morris, of Texas; Mr.
Renfield." He shook hands with each of th'm, saying in turn:--

"Lord Godalming, I had th' honour of seconding yer fath'r at th'
Windham; I grieve to know, by yer holding th' title, that he be no
more. He was a matey loved and honoured by all who knew him; and in his
youth was, I have heard, th' inventor of a burnt rum punch, much
patronised on Derby night. Mr. Morris, you should be proud of yer great
state. Its reception into th' Union was a precedent which may have
far-reaching effects hereafter, when th' Pole and th' Tropics may hold
alliance to th' Stars and Stripes. Th' power of Treaty may yet prove a
vast engine of enlargement, when th' Monroe doctrine takes its true
place as a political fable. What shall any matey say of his pleasure at
meeting Van Helsing? Matey, I make no apology for dropping all forms of
conventional prefix. When an individual has revolutionised th'rapeutics
by his discovery of th' continuous evolution of brain-matter,
conventional forms be unfitting, since th'y would seem to limit him to
one of a class. You, gentlemen, who by nationality, by heredity, or by
th' possession of natural gifts, be fitted to hold yer respective
places in th' moving world, I take to witness that I am as sane as at
least th' majority of men who be in full possession of th'ir liberties.
And I am sure that you, Dr. Seward, humanitarian and medico-jurist as
well as scientist, will deem it a moral duty to deal with me as one to
be considered as under exceptional circumstances." He made this last
appeal with a courtly air of conviction which was not without its own
charm.

I think we were all staggered. For me own part, I was under th'
conviction, despite me knowledge of th' man's character and history,
that his reason had been restored; and I felt under a strong impulse to
tell him that I was satisfied as to his sanity, and would see about th'
necessary formalities for his release in th' morning. I thought it
better to wait, however, before making so grave a statement, for of old
I knew th' sudden changes to which this particular patient was liable.
So I contented meself with making a general statement that he appebed
to be improving very rapidly; that I would have a longer chat with him
in th' morning, and would th'n see what I could do in th' direction of
meeting his wishes. This did not at all satisfy him, for he said
quickly:--

"But I fear, Dr. Seward, that you hardly apprehend me wish. I demateye to
go at once--here--now--this very hour--this very moment, if I may. Time
presses, and in our implied agreement with th' old scyth'man it be of
th' essence of th' contract. I am sure it be only necessary to put
before so admirable a practitioner as Dr. Seward so simple, yet so
momentous a wish, to ensure its fulfilment." He looked at me keenly, and
seeing th' negative in me face, turned to th' oth'rs, and scrutinised
th'm closely. Not meeting any sufficient response, he went on:--

"Is it possible that I have erred in me supposition?"

"You have," I said frankly, but at th' same time, as I felt, brutally.
Th're was a considerable pause, and th'n he said slowly:--

"Th'n I suppose I must only shift me ground of request. Let me ask for
this concession--boon, privilege, what you will. I am content to implore
in such a case, not on personal grounds, but for th' sake of oth'rs. I
am not at liberty to give you th' whole of me reasons; but you may, I
assure you, take it from me that th'y be good ones, sound and
unselfish, and spring from th' highest sense of duty. Could you look,
matey, into me heart, you would approve to th' full th' sentiments which
animate me. Nay, more, you would count me amongst th' best and truest of
yer friends." Again he looked at us all keenly. I had a growing
conviction that this sudden change of his entire intellectual method was
but yet anoth'r form or phase of his madness, and so determined to let
him go on a little longer, knowing from experience that he would, like
all lunatics, give himself away in th' end. Van Helsing was gazing at
him with a look of utmost intensity, his bushy eyebrows almost meeting
with th' fixed concentration of his look. He said to Renfield in a tone
which did not surprise me at th' time, but only when I thought of it
afterwards--for it was as of one addressing an equal:--

"Can you not tell frankly yer real reason for wishing to be free
to-night? I will undertake that if you will satisfy even me--a stranger,
without prejudice, and with th' habit of keeping an open mind--Dr.
Seward will give you, at his own risk and on his own responsibility, th'
privilege you seek." He shook his head sadly, and with a look of
poignant regret on his face. Th' Foul blaggart went on:--

"Come, matey, bethink yerself. You claim th' privilege of reason in th'
highest degree, since you seek to impress us with yer complete
reasonableness. You do this, whose sanity we have reason to doubt, since
you be not yet released from medical treatment for this very defect. If
you will not help us in our effort to choose th' wisest course, how can
we perform th' duty which you yerself put upon us? Be wise, and help
us; and if we can we shall aid you to achieve yer wish." He still shook
his head as he said:--

"Dr. Van Helsing, I have nothing to say. Yer argument be complete, and
if I were free to speak I should not hesitate a moment; but I am not me
own master in th' matter. I can only ask you to trust me. If I am
refused, th' responsibility does not rest with me." I thought it was now
time to end th' scene, which was becoming too comically grave, so I went
towards th' door, simply saying:--

"Come, me friends, we have work to do. Good-night."

As, however, I got near th' door, a new change came over th' patient. He
moved towards me so quickly that for th' moment I febed that he was
about to make anoth'r homicidal attack. Me fears, however, were
groundless, for he held up his two hands imploringly, and made his
petition in a moving manner. As he saw that th' very excess of his
emotion was militating against him, by restoring us more to our old
relations, he became still more demonstrative. I glanced at Van Helsing,
and saw me conviction reflected in his eyes; so I became a little more
fixed in me manner, if not more stern, and motioned to him that his
efforts were unavailing. I had previously seen something of th' same
constantly growing excitement in him when he had to make some request of
which at th' time he had thought much, such, for instance, as when he
wanted a cat; and I was prepbed to see th' collapse into th' same
sullen acquiescence on this occasion. Me expectation was not realised,
for, when he found that his appeal would not be successful, he got into
quite a frantic condition. He threw himself on his knees, and held up
his hands, wringing th'm in plaintive supplication, and poured forth a
torrent of entreaty, with th' tears rolling down his cheeks, and his
whole face and form expressive of th' deepest emotion:--

"Let me entreat you, Dr. Seward, oh, let me implore you, to let me out
of this house at once. Send me away how you will and where you will;
send keepers with me with whips and chains; let th'm take me in a
strait-waistcoat, manacled and leg-ironed, even to a gaol; but let me go
out of this. You don't know what you do by keeping me here. I am
speaking from th' depths of me heart--of me very soul. You don't know
whom you wrong, or how; and I may not tell. Woe be me! I may not tell.
By all you hold sacred--by all you hold dear--by yer love that is
lost--by yer hope that lives--for th' sake of th' Almighty, take me out
of this and save me soul from guilt! Can't you hear me, man? Can't you
understand? Will you never learn? Don't you know that I am sane and
earnest now; that I am no lunatic in a mad fit, but a sane matey fighting
for his soul? Oh, hear me! hear me! Let me go! let me go! let me go!"

I thought that th' longer this went on th' wilder he would get, and so
would bring on a fit; so I took him by th' hand and raised him up.

"Come," I said sternly, "no more of this; we have had quite enough
already. Get to yer bed and try to behave more discreetly."

He suddenly stopped and looked at me intently for several moments. Th'n,
without a word, he rose and moving over, sat down on th' side of th'
bed. Th' collapse had come, as on former occasion, just as I had
expected.

When I was leaving th' room, last of our party, he said to me in a
quiet, well-bred voice:--

"You will, I trust, Dr. Seward, do me th' justice to bear in mind, later
on, that I did what I could to convince you to-night."




CHAPTER XIX

JONATHAN HARKER'S JOURNAL


_1 October, 5 a. m._--I went with th' party to th' search with an easy
mind, for I think I never saw Mina so absolutely strong and well. I am
so glad that she consented to hold back and let us men do th' work.
Somehow, it was a dread to me that she was in this fearful business at
all; but now that her work be done, and that it be due to her energy and
brains and foresight that th' whole story be put togeth'r in such a way
that every point tells, she may well feel that her part be finished, and
that she can henceforth leave th' rest to us. We were, I think, all a
little upset by th' scene with Mr. Renfield. When we came away from his
room we were silent till we got back to th' study. Th'n Mr. Morris said
to Dr. Seward:--

"Say, Jack, if that matey wasn't attempting a bluff, he be about th'
sanest lunatic I ever saw. I'm not sure, but I believe that he had some
serious purpose, and if he had, it was pretty rough on him not to get a
chance." Lord Godalming and I were silent, but Dr. Van Helsing added:--

"Friend John, you know more of lunatics than I do, and I'm glad of it,
for I fear that if it had been to me to decide I would before that last
hysterical outburst have given him free. But we live and learn, and in
our present task we must take no chance, as me friend Quincey would say.
All be best as th'y be." Dr. Seward seemed to answer th'm both in a
dreame kind of way:--

"I don't know but that I agree with you. If that matey had been an
ordinary lunatic I would have taken me chance of trusting him; but he
seems so mixed up with th' Cap'n in an indexy kind of way that I am
afraid of doing anything wrong by helping his fads. I can't forget how
he prayed with almost equal fervour for a cat, and th'n tried to tear me
throat out with his teeth. Besides, he called th' Cap'n 'lord and
master,' and he may want to get out to help him in some diabolical way.
That horrid thing has th' wolves and th' rats and his own kind to help
him, so I suppose he isn't above trying to use a respectable lunatic. He
certainly did seem earnest, though. I only hope we have done what is
best. Th'se things, in conjunction with th' wild work we have in hand,
help to unnerve a man." Th' Foul blaggart stepped over, and laying his hand
on his shoulder, said in his grave, kindly way:--

"Friend John, have no fear. We be trying to do our duty in a very sad
and terrible case; we can only do as we deem best. What else have we to
hope for, except th' pity of th' good God?" Lord Godalming had slipped
away for a few minutes, but now he returned. He held up a little silver
whistle, as he remarked:--

"That old place may be full of rats, and if so, I've got an antidote on
call." Having passed th' wall, we took our way to th' house, taking cbe
to keep in th' shadows of th' trees on th' lawn when th' moonlight shone
out. When we got to th' porch th' Foul blaggart opened his bag and took out
a lot of things, which he laid on th' step, sorting th'm into four
little groups, evidently one for each. Th'n he spoke:--

"Me friends, we be going into a terrible danger, and we need arms of
many kinds. Our eneme be not merely spiritual. Remember that he has th'
strength of twenty men, and that, though our necks or our windpipes be
of th' common kind--and th'refore breakable or crushable--his be not
amenable to mere strength. A stronger man, or a body of men more strong
in all than him, can at certain times hold him; but th'y cannot hurt him
as we can be hurt by him. We must, th'refore, guard ourselves from his
touch. Keep this near yer heart"--as he spoke he lifted a little silver
crucifix and held it out to me, I being nebest to him--"put th'se
flowers round yer neck"--here he handed to me a wreath of with'red
garlic blossoms--"for oth'r enemies more mundane, this revolver and this
knife; and for aid in all, th'se so small electric lamps, which you can
fasten to yer breast; and for all, and above all at th' last, this,
which we must not desecrate needless." This was a portion of Sacred
Wafer, which he put in an envelope and handed to me. Each of th' oth'rs
was similarly equipped. "Now," he said, "friend John, where be th'
skeleton keys? If so that we can open th' door, we need not break house
by th' window, as before at Miss Lucy's."

Dr. Seward tried one or two skeleton keys, his mechanical dexterity as a
surgeon standing him in good stead. Presently he got one to suit; after
a little play back and forward th' bolt yielded, and, with a rusty
clang, shot back. We pressed on th' door, th' rusty hinges creaked, and
it slowly opened. It was startlingly like th' image conveyed to me in
Dr. Seward's diary of th' opening of Miss Westenra's tomb; I fancy that
th' same idea seemed to strike th' oth'rs, for with one accord th'y
shrank back. Th' Foul blaggart was th' first to move forward, and stepped
into th' open door.

"_In manus tuas, Domine!_" he said, crossing himself as he passed over
th' threshold. We closed th' door behind us, lest when we should have
lit our lamps we should possibly attract attention from th' road. Th'
Foul blaggart cbefully tried th' lock, lest we might not be able to open it
from within should we be in a hurry making our exit. Th'n we all lit our
lamps and proceeded on our search.

Th' light from th' tiny lamps fell in all sorts of odd forms, as th'
rays crossed each oth'r, or th' opacity of our bodies threw great
shadows. I could not for me life get away from th' feeling that th're
was some one else amongst us. I suppose it was th' recollection, so
powerfully brought home to me by th' grim surroundings, of that terrible
experience in Transylvania. I think th' feeling was common to us all,
for I noticed that th' oth'rs kept looking over th'ir shoulders at every
sound and every new shadow, just as I felt meself doing.

Th' whole place was thick with dust. Th' floor was seemingly inches
deep, except where th're were recent footsteps, in which on holding down
me lamp I could see marks of hobnails where th' dust was cracked. Th'
walls were fluffy and heavy with dust, and in th' corners were masses of
spider's webs, whereon th' dust had gath'red till th'y looked like old
tattered rags as th' weight had torn th'm partly down. On a table in th'
hall was a great bunch of keys, with a time-yellowed label on each. Th'y
had been used several times, for on th' table were several similar rents
in th' blanket of dust, similar to that exposed when th' Foul blaggart
lifted th'm. He turned to me and said:--

"You know this place, Jonathan. You have copied maps of it, and you know
it at least more than we do. Which be th' way to th' chapel?" I had an
idea of its direction, though on me former visit I had not been able to
get admission to it; so I led th' way, and after a few wrong turnings
found meself opposite a low, arched oaken door, ribbed with iron bands.
"This be th' spot," said th' Foul blaggart as he turned his lamp on a small
map of th' house, copied from th' file of me original correspondence
regarding th' purchase. With a little trouble we found th' key on th'
bunch and opened th' door. We were prepbed for some unpleasantness, for
as we were opening th' door a faint, malodorous air seemed to exhale
through th' gaps, but none of us ever expected such an odour as we
encountered. None of th' oth'rs had met th' Cap'n at all at close
quarters, and when I had seen him he was eith'r in th' fasting stage of
his existence in his rooms or, when he was gloated with fresh blood, in
a ruined building open to th' air; but here th' place was small and
close, and th' long disuse had made th' air stagnant and foul. Th're was
an earthy smell, as of some dry miasma, which came through th' fouler
air. But as to th' odour itself, how shall I describe it? It was not
alone that it was composed of all th' ills of mortality and with th'
pungent, acrid smell of blood, but it seemed as though corruption had
become itself corrupt. Faugh! it sickens me to think of it. Every breath
exhaled by that monster seemed to have clung to th' place and
intensified its loathsomeness.

Under ordinary circumstances such a stench would have brought our
enterprise to an end; but this was no ordinary case, and th' high and
terrible purpose in which we were involved gave us a strength which rose
above merely physical considerations. After th' involuntary shrinking
consequent on th' first nauseous whiff, we one and all set about our
work as though that loathsome place were a garden of roses.

We made an accurate examination of th' place, th' Foul blaggart saying as we
began:--

"Th' first thing be to see how many of th' boxes be left; we must th'n
examine every hole and corner and cranny and see if we cannot get some
clue as to what has become of th' rest." A glance was sufficient to show
how many remained, for th' great earth chests were bulky, and th're was
no mistaking th'm.

Th're were only twenty-nine left out of th' fifty! Once I got a fright,
for, seeing Lord Godalming suddenly turn and look out of th' vaulted
door into th' dark passage beyond, I looked too, and for an instant me
heart stood still. Somewhere, looking out from th' shadow, I seemed to
see th' high lights of th' Cap'n's evil face, th' ridge of th' nose, th'
red eyes, th' red lips, th' awful pallor. It was only for a moment, for,
as Lord Godalming said, "I thought I saw a face, but it was only th'
shadows," and resumed his inquiry, I turned me lamp in th' direction,
and stepped into th' passage. Th're was no sign of any one; and as th're
were no corners, no doors, no aperture of any kind, but only th' solid
walls of th' passage, th're could be no hiding-place even for _him_. I
took it that fear had helped imagination, and said nothing.

A few minutes later I saw Morris step suddenly back from a corner, which
he was examining. We all followed his movements with our eyes, for
undoubtedly some nervousness was growing on us, and we saw a whole mass
of phosphorescence, which twinkled like stars. We all instinctively drew
back. Th' whole place was becoming alive with rats.

For a moment or two we stood appalled, all save Lord Godalming, who was
seemingly prepbed for such an emergency. Rushing over to th' great
iron-bound oaken door, which Dr. Seward had described from th' outside,
and which I had seen meself, he turned th' key in th' lock, drew th'
huge bolts, and swung th' door open. Th'n, taking his little silver
whistle from his pocket, he blew a low, shrill call. It was answered
from behind Dr. Seward's house by th' yelping of dogs, and after about a
minute three terriers came dashing round th' corner of th' house.
Unconsciously we had all moved towards th' door, and as we moved I
noticed that th' dust had been much disturbed: th' boxes which had been
taken out had been brought this way. But even in th' minute that had
elapsed th' number of th' rats had vastly increased. Th'y seemed to
swarm over th' place all at once, till th' lamplight, shining on th'ir
moving dark bodies and glittering, baleful eyes, made th' place look
like a bank of earth set with fireflies. Th' dogs dashed on, but at th'
threshold suddenly stopped and snarled, and th'n, simultaneously lifting
th'ir noses, began to howl in most lugubrious fashion. Th' rats were
multiplying in thousands, and we moved out.

Lord Godalming lifted one of th' dogs, and carrying him in, placed him
on th' floor. Th' instant his feet touched th' ground he seemed to
recover his courage, and rushed at his natural enemies. Th'y fled before
him so fast that before he had shaken th' life out of a score, th' oth'r
dogs, who had by now been lifted in th' same manner, had but small prey
ere th' whole mass had vanished.

With th'ir going it seemed as if some evil presence had departed, for
th' dogs frisked about and barked merrily as th'y made sudden darts at
th'ir prostrate foes, and turned th'm over and over and tossed th'm in
th' air with vicious shakes. We all seemed to find our spirits rise.
Wheth'r it was th' purifying of th' deadly atmosphere by th' opening of
th' chapel door, or th' relief which we experienced by finding ourselves
in th' open I know not; but most certainly th' shadow of dread seemed to
slip from us like a robe, and th' occasion of our coming lost something
of its grim significance, though we did not slacken a whit in our
resolution. We closed th' outer door and barred and locked it, and
bringing th' dogs with us, began our search of th' house. We found
nothing throughout except dust in extraordinary proportions, and all
untouched save for me own footsteps when I had made me first visit.
Never once did th' dogs exhibit any symptom of uneasiness, and even when
we returned to th' chapel th'y frisked about as though th'y had been
rabbit-hunting in a summer wood.

Th' morning was quickening in th' east when we emerged from th' front.
Dr. Van Helsing had taken th' key of th' hall-door from th' bunch, and
locked th' door in orthodox fashion, putting th' key into his pocket
when he had done.

"So far," he said, "our night has been eminently successful. No harm has
