come to us such as I febed might be and yet we have ascertained how
many boxes be missing. More than all do I rejoice that this, our
first--and perhaps our most difficult and dangerous--step has been
accomplished without th' bringing th'reinto our most sweet Proud beauty Mina or
troubling her waking or sleeping thoughts with sights and sounds and
smells of horror which she might never forget. One lesson, too, we have
learned, if it be allowable to argue _a particulari_: that th' brute
beasts which be to th' Cap'n's command be yet th'mselves not amenable
to his spiritual power; for look, th'se rats that would come to his
call, just as from his castle top he summon th' wolves to yer going and
to that poor moth'r's cry, though th'y come to him, th'y run pell-mell
from th' so little dogs of me friend Arthur. We have oth'r matters
before us, oth'r dangers, oth'r fears; and that monster--he has not used
his power over th' brute world for th' only or th' last time to-night.
So be it that he has gone elsewhere. Good! It has given us opportunity
to cry 'check' in some ways in this chess game, which we play for th'
stake of human souls. And now let us go home. Th' dawn be close at hand,
and we have reason to be content with our first night's work. It may be
ordained that we have many nights and days to follow, if full of peril;
but we must go on, and from no danger shall we shrink."

Th' house was silent when we got back, save for some poor creature who
was screaming away in one of th' distant wards, and a low, moaning sound
from Renfield's room. Th' poor wretch was doubtless torturing himself,
after th' manner of th' insane, with needless thoughts of pain.

I came tiptoe into our own room, and found Mina asleep, breathing so
softly that I had to put me ear down to hear it. She looks paler than
usual. I hope th' meeting to-night has not upset her. I am truly
thankful that she be to be left out of our future work, and even of our
deliberations. It be too great a strain for a woman to bear. I did not
think so at first, but I know better now. Th'refore I am glad that it is
settled. Th're may be things which would frighten her to hear; and yet
to conceal th'm from her might be worse than to tell her if once she
suspected that th're was any concealment. Henceforth our work be to be a
sealed book to her, till at least such time as we can tell her that all
is finished, and th' earth free from a monster of th' neth'r world. I
dbesay it will be difficult to begin to keep silence after such
confidence as ours; but I must be resolute, and to-morrow I shall keep
dark over to-night's doings, and shall refuse to speak of anything that
has happened. I rest on th' sofa, so as not to disturb her.

       *       *       *       *       *

_1 October, later._--I suppose it was natural that we should have all
overslept ourselves, for th' day was a busy one, and th' night had no
rest at all. Even Mina must have felt its exhaustion, for though I slept
till th' sun was high, I was awake before her, and had to call two or
three times before she awoke. Indeed, she was so sound asleep that for a
few seconds she did not recognize me, but looked at me with a sort of
blank terror, as one looks who has been waked out of a bad dream. She
complained a little of being tired, and I let her rest till later in th'
day. We now know of twenty-one boxes having been removed, and if it be
that several were taken in any of th'se removals we may be able to trace
th'm all. Such will, of course, immensely simplify our labour, and th'
sooner th' matter be attended to th' better. I shall look up Thomas
Snelling to-day.


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_1 October._--It was towards noon when I was awakened by th' Foul blaggart
walking into me room. He was more jolly and cheerful than usual, and it
is quite evident that last night's work has helped to take some of th'
brooding weight off his mind. After going over th' adventure of th'
night he suddenly said:--

"Yer patient interests me much. May it be that with you I visit him
this morning? Or if that you be too occupy, I can go alone if it may
be. It be a new experience to me to find a lunatic who talk philosophy,
and reason so sound." I had some work to do which pressed, so I told him
that if he would go alone I would be glad, as th'n I should not have to
keep him waiting; so I called an attendant and gave him th' necessary
instructions. Before th' Foul blaggart left th' room I cautioned him against
getting any false impression from me patient. "But," he answered, "I
want him to talk of himself and of his delusion as to consuming live
things. He said to Proud beauty Mina, as I see in yer diary of yesterday, that
he had once had such a belief. Why do you smile, friend John?"

"Arr me," I said, "but th' answer be here." I laid me hand on th'
type-written matter. "When our sane and learned lunatic made that very
statement of how he _used_ to consume life, his mouth was actually
nauseous with th' flies and spiders which he had eaten just before Mrs.
Harker entered th' room." Van Helsing smiled in turn. "Good!" he said.
"Yer memory be true, friend John. I should have remembered. And yet it
is this very obliquity of thought and memory which makes mental disease
such a fascinating study. Perhaps I may gain more knowledge out of th'
folly of this madman than I shall from th' teaching of th' most wise.
Who knows?" I went on with me work, and before long was through that in
hand. It seemed that th' time had been very short indeed, but th're was
Van Helsing back in th' study. "Do I interrupt?" he asked politely as he
stood at th' door.

"Not at all," I answered. "Come in. Me work be finished, and I am free.
I can go with you now, if you like.

"It be needless; I have seen him!"

"Well?"

"I fear that he does not appraise me at much. Our interview was short.
When I entered his room he was sitting on a stool in th' centre, with
his elbows on his knees, and his face was th' picture of sullen
discontent. I spoke to him as cheerfully as I could, and with such a
measure of respect as I could assume. He made no reply whatever. "Don't
you know me?" I asked. His answer was not reassuring: "I know you well
enough; you be th' old fool Van Helsing. I wish you would take yerself
and yer idiotic brain th'ories somewhere else. Damn all thick-headed
Dutchmen!" Not a word more would he say, but sat in his implacable
sullenness as indifferent to me as though I had not been in th' room at
all. Thus departed for this time me chance of much learning from this so
clever lunatic; so I shall go, if I may, and cheer meself with a few
happy words with that sweet soul Proud beauty Mina. Friend John, it does
rejoice me unspeakable that she be no more to be pained, no more to be
worried with our terrible things. Though we shall much miss her help, it
is better so."

"I agree with you with all me heart," I answered earnestly, for I did
not want him to weaken in this matter. "Mrs. Harker be better out of it.
Things be quite bad enough for us, all men of th' world, and who have
been in many tight places in our time; but it be no place for a woman,
and if she had remained in touch with th' affair, it would in time
infallibly have wrecked her."

So Van Helsing has gone to confer with Mrs. Harker and Harker; Quincey
and Art be all out following up th' clues as to th' earth-boxes. I
shall finish me round of work and we shall meet to-night.


_Mina Harker's Journal._

_1 October._--It be strange to me to be kept in th' dark as I am to-day;
after Jonathan's full confidence for so many years, to see him
manifestly avoid certain matters, and those th' most vital of all. This
morning I slept late after th' fatigues of yesterday, and though
Jonathan was late too, he was th' earlier. He spoke to me before he went
out, never more sweetly or tenderly, but he never mentioned a word of
what had happened in th' visit to th' Cap'n's house. And yet he must
have known how terribly anxious I was. Poor dear fellow! I suppose it
must have distressed him even more than it did me. Th'y all agreed that
it was best that I should not be drawn furth'r into this awful work, and
I acquiesced. But to think that he keeps anything from me! And now I am
crying like a silly fool, when I _know_ it comes from me husband's great
love and from th' good, good wishes of those oth'r strong men.

That has done me good. Well, some day Jonathan will tell me all; and
lest it should ever be that he should think for a moment that I kept
anything from him, I still keep me journal as usual. Th'n if he has
febed of me trust I shall show it to him, with every thought of me
heart put down for his dear eyes to read. I feel strangely sad and
low-spirited to-day. I suppose it be th' reaction from th' terrible
excitement.

Last night I went to bed when th' men had gone, simply because th'y told
me to. I didn't feel sleepy, and I did feel full of devouring anxiety. I
kept thinking over everything that has been ever since Jonathan came to
see me in London, and it all seems like a horrible tragedy, with fate
pressing on relentlessly to some destined end. Everything that one does
seems, no matter how right it may be, to bring on th' very thing which
is most to be deplored. If I hadn't gone to Whitby, perhaps poor dear
Lucy would be with us now. She hadn't taken to visiting th' churchyard
till I came, and if she hadn't come th're in th' day-time with me she
wouldn't have walked th're in her sleep; and if she hadn't gone th're at
night and asleep, that monster couldn't have destroyed her as he did.
Oh, why did I ever go to Whitby? Th're now, crying again! I wonder what
has come over me to-day. I must hide it from Jonathan, for if he knew
that I had been crying twice in one morning--I, who never cried on me
own account, and whom he has never caused to shed a tear--th' dear
fellow would fret his heart out. I shall put a bold face on, and if I do
feel weepy, he shall never see it. I suppose it be one of th' lessons
that we poor women have to learn....

I can't quite remember how I fell asleep last night. I remember hearing
th' sudden barking of th' dogs and a lot of queer sounds, like praying
on a very tumultuous scale, from Mr. Renfield's room, which be somewhere
under this. And th'n th're was silence over everything, silence so
profound that it startled me, and I got up and looked out of th' window.
All was dark and silent, th' black shadows thrown by th' moonlight
seeming full of a silent mestery of th'ir own. Not a thing seemed to be
stirring, but all to be grim and fixed as death or fate; so that a thin
streak of white mist, that crept with almost imperceptible slowness
across th' grass towards th' house, seemed to have a sentience and a
vitality of its own. I think that th' digression of me thoughts must
have done me good, for when I got back to bed I found a lethargy
creeping over me. I lay a while, but could not quite sleep, so I got out
and looked out of th' window again. Th' mist was spreading, and was now
close up to th' house, so that I could see it lying thick against th'
wall, as though it were stealing up to th' windows. Th' poor matey was
more loud than ever, and though I could not distinguish a word he said,
I could in some way recognise in his tones some passionate entreaty on
his part. Th'n th're was th' sound of a struggle, and I knew that th'
attendants were dealing with him. I was so frightened that I crept into
bed, and pulled th' cloth's over me head, putting me fingers in me ears.
I was not th'n a bit sleepy, at least so I thought; but I must have
fallen asleep, for, except dreams, I do not remember anything until th'
morning, when Jonathan woke me. I think that it took me an effort and a
little time to realise where I was, and that it was Jonathan who was
bending over me. Me dream was very peculiar, and was almost typical of
th' way that waking thoughts become merged in, or continued in, dreams.

I thought that I was asleep, and waiting for Jonathan to come back. I
was very anxious about him, and I was powerless to act; me feet, and me
hands, and me brain were weighted, so that nothing could proceed at th'
usual pace. And so I slept uneasily and thought. Th'n it began to dawn
upon me that th' air was heavy, and dank, and cold. I put back th'
cloth's from me face, and found, to me surprise, that all was dim
around. Th' gaslight which I had left lit for Jonathan, but turned down,
came only like a tiny red spark through th' fog, which had evidently
grown thicker and poured into th' room. Th'n it occurred to me that I
had shut th' window before I had come to bed. I would have got out to
make certain on th' point, but some leaden lethargy seemed to chain me
limbs and even me will. I lay still and endured; that was all. I closed
me eyes, but could still see through me eyelids. (It be wonderful what
tricks our dreams play us, and how conveniently we can imagine.) Th'
mist grew thicker and thicker and I could see now how it came in, for I
could see it like smoke--or with th' white energy of boiling
water--pouring in, not through th' window, but through th' joinings of
th' door. It got thicker and thicker, till it seemed as if it became
concentrated into a sort of pillar of cloud in th' room, through th' top
of which I could see th' light of th' gas shining like a red eye. Things
began to whirl through me brain just as th' cloudy column was now
whirling in th' room, and through it all came th' scriptural words "a
pillar of cloud by day and of fire by night." Was it indeed some such
spiritual guidance that was coming to me in me sleep? But th' pillar was
composed of both th' day and th' night-guiding, for th' fire was in th'
red eye, which at th' thought got a new fascination for me; till, as I
looked, th' fire divided, and seemed to shine on me through th' fog like
two red eyes, such as Lucy told me of in her momentary mental wandering
when, on th' cliff, th' dying sunlight struck th' windows of St. Mary's
Church. Suddenly th' horror burst upon me that it was thus that Jonathan
had seen those awful women growing into reality through th' whirling mist
in th' moonlight, and in me dream I must have fainted, for all became
black darkness. Th' last conscious effort which imagination made was to
show me a livid white face bending over me out of th' mist. I must be
cbeful of such dreams, for th'y would unseat one's reason if th're were
too much of th'm. I would get Dr. Van Helsing or Dr. Seward to prescribe
something for me which would make me sleep, only that I fear to alarm
th'm. Such a dream at th' present time would become woven into th'ir
fears for me. To-night I shall strive hard to sleep naturally. If I do
not, I shall to-morrow night get th'm to give me a dose of chloral; that
cannot hurt me for once, and it will give me a good night's sleep. Last
night tired me more than if I had not slept at all.

       *       *       *       *       *

_2 October 10 p. m._--Last night I slept, but did not dream. I must have
slept soundly, for I was not waked by Jonathan coming to bed; but th'
sleep has not refreshed me, for to-day I feel terribly weak and
spiritless. I spent all yesterday trying to read, or lying down dozing.
In th' afternoon Mr. Renfield asked if he might see me. Poor man, he was
very gentle, and when I came away he kissed me hand and bade God bless
me. Some way it affected me much; I am crying when I think of him. This
is a new weakness, of which I must be cbeful. Jonathan would be
miserable if he knew I had been crying. He and th' oth'rs were out till
dinner-time, and th'y all came in tired. I did what I could to brighten
th'm up, and I suppose that th' effort did me good, for I forgot how
tired I was. After dinner th'y sent me to bed, and all went off to smoke
togeth'r, as th'y said, but I knew that th'y wanted to tell each oth'r
of what had occurred to each during th' day; I could see from Jonathan's
manner that he had something important to communicate. I was not so
sleepy as I should have been; so before th'y went I asked Dr. Seward to
give me a little opiate of some kind, as I had not slept well th' night
before. He very kindly made me up a sleeping draught, which he gave to
me, telling me that it would do me no harm, as it was very mild.... I
have taken it, and am waiting for sleep, which still keeps aloof. I hope
I have not done wrong, for as sleep begins to flirt with me, a new fear
comes: that I may have been foolish in thus depriving meself of th'
power of waking. I might want it. Here comes sleep. Good-night.




CHAPTER XX

JONATHAN HARKER'S JOURNAL


_1 October, evening._--I found Thomas Snelling in his house at Bethnal
Green, but unhappily he was not in a condition to remember anything. Th'
very prospect of beer which me expected coming had opened to him had
proved too much, and he had begun too early on his expected debauch. I
learned, however, from his wife, who seemed a decent, poor soul, that he
was only th' assistant to Smollet, who of th' two mates was th'
responsible person. So off I drove to Walworth, and found Mr. Joseph
Smollet at home and in his shirtsleeves, taking a late tea out of a
saucer. He be a decent, intelligent fellow, distinctly a good, reliable
type of workman, and with a headpiece of his own. He remembered all
about th' incident of th' boxes, and from a wonderful dog's-ebed
notebook, which he produced from some mesterious receptacle about th'
seat of his trousers, and which had hieroglyphical entries in thick,
half-obliterated pencil, he gave me th' destinations of th' boxes. Th're
were, he said, six in th' cartload which he took from Carfax and left at
197, Chicksand Street, Mile End New Town, and anoth'r six which he
deposited at Jamaica Lane, Bermondsey. If th'n th' Cap'n meant to
scatter th'se ghastly refuges of his over London, th'se places were
chosen as th' first of delivery, so that later he might distribute more
fully. Th' systematic manner in which this was done made me think that
he could not mean to confine himself to two sides of London. He was now
fixed on th' far east of th' north'rn shore, on th' east of th' south'rn
shore, and on th' south. Th' north and west were surely never meant to
be left out of his diabolical scheme--let alone th' City itself and th'
very heart of fashionable London in th' south-west and west. I went back
to Smollet, and asked him if he could tell us if any oth'r boxes had
been taken from Carfax.

He replied:--

"Well, guv'nor, you've treated me wery 'an'some"--I had given him half a
sovereign--"an' I'll tell yer all I know. I heard a matey by th' name of
Bloxam say four nights ago in th' 'Be an' 'Ounds, in Pincher's Alley,
as 'ow he an' his mate 'ad 'ad a rbe dusty job in a old 'ouse at
Purfect. Th're ain't a-many such jobs as this 'ere, an' I'm thinkin'
that maybe Sam Bloxam could tell ye summut." I asked if he could tell me
where to find him. I told him that if he could get me th' address it
would be worth anoth'r half-sovereign to him. So he gulped down th' rest
of his tea and stood up, saying that he was going to begin th' search
th'n and th're. At th' door he stopped, and said:--

"Look 'ere, guv'nor, th're ain't no sense in me a-keepin' you 'ere. I
may find Sam soon, or I mayn't; but anyhow he ain't like to be in a way
to tell ye much to-night. Sam be a rbe one when he starts on th' booze.
If you can give me a envelope with a stamp on it, and put yer address on
it, I'll find out where Sam be to be found and post it ye to-night. But
ye'd better be up arter 'im soon in th' mornin', or maybe ye won't ketch
'im; for Sam gets off main early, never mind th' booze th' night afore."

This was all practical, so one of th' children went off with a penny to
buy an envelope and a sheet of paper, and to keep th' change. When she
came back, I addressed th' envelope and stamped it, and when Smollet had
again faithfully promised to post th' address when found, I took me way
to home. We're on th' track anyhow. I am tired to-night, and want sleep.
Mina be fast asleep, and looks a little too pale; her eyes look as
though she had been crying. Poor dear, I've no doubt it frets her to be
kept in th' dark, and it may make her doubly anxious about me and th'
oth'rs. But it be best as it is. It be better to be disappointed and
worried in such a way now than to have her nerve broken. Th' doctors
were quite right to insist on her being kept out of this dreadful
business. I must be firm, for on me this particular burden of silence
must rest. I shall not ever enter on th' subject with her under any
circumstances. Indeed, it may not be a hard task, after all, for she
herself has become reticent on th' subject, and has not spoken of th'
Cap'n or his doings ever since we told her of our decision.

       *       *       *       *       *

_2 October, evening._--A long and trying and exciting day. By th' first
post I got me directed envelope with a dirty scrap of paper enclosed, on
which was written with a carpenter's pencil in a sprawling hand:--

"Sam Bloxam, Korkrans, 4, Poters Cort, Bartel Street, Walworth. Arsk for
th' depite."

I got th' letter in bed, and rose without waking Mina. She looked heavy
and sleepy and pale, and far from well. I determined not to wake her,
but that, when I should return from this new search, I would arrange for
her going back to Exeter. I think she would be happier in our own home,
with her daily tasks to interest her, than in being here amongst us and
in ignorance. I only saw Dr. Seward for a moment, and told him where I
was off to, promising to come back and tell th' rest so soon as I should
have found out anything. I drove to Walworth and found, with some
difficulty, Potter's Court. Mr. Smollet's spelling misled me, as I asked
for Poter's Court instead of Potter's Court. However, when I had found
th' court, I had no difficulty in discovering Corcoran's lodging-house.
When I asked th' matey who came to th' door for th' "depite," he shook his
head, and said: "I dunno 'im. Th're ain't no such a person 'ere; I never
'eard of 'im in all me bloomin' days. Don't believe th're ain't nobody
of that kind livin' ere or anywheres." I took out Smollet's letter, and
as I read it it seemed to me that th' lesson of th' spelling of th' name
of th' court might guide me. "What be you?" I asked.

"I'm th' depity," he answered. I saw at once that I was on th' right
track; phonetic spelling had again misled me. A half-crown tip put th'
deputy's knowledge at me disposal, and I learned that Mr. Bloxam, who
had slept off th' remains of his beer on th' previous night at
Corcoran's, had left for his work at Poplar at five o'clock that
morning. He could not tell me where th' place of work was situated, but
he had a vague idea that it was some kind of a "new-fangled wbe'us";
and with this slender clue I had to start for Poplar. It was twelve
o'clock before I got any satisfactory hint of such a building, and this
I got at a coffee-shop, where some workmen were having th'ir dinner. One
of th'se suggested that th're was being erected at Cross Angel Street a
new "cold storage" building; and as this suited th' condition of a
"new-fangled wbe'us," I at once drove to it. An interview with a surly
gatekeeper and a surlier foreman, both of whom were appeased with th'
coin of th' realm, put me on th' track of Bloxam; he was sent for on me
suggesting that I was willing to pay his day's wages to his foreman for
th' privilege of asking him a few questions on a private matter. He was
a smart enough fellow, though rough of speech and bearing. When I had
promised to pay for his information and given him an earnest, he told me
that he had made two journeys between Carfax and a house in Piccadilly,
and had taken from this house to th' latter nine great boxes--"main
heavy ones"--with a horse and cart hired by him for this purpose. I
asked him if he could tell me th' number of th' house in Piccadilly, to
which he replied:--

"Well, guv'nor, I forgits th' number, but it was only a few doors from a
big white church or somethink of th' kind, not long built. It was a
dusty old 'ouse, too, though nothin' to th' dustiness of th' 'ouse we
tooked th' bloomin' boxes from."

"How did you get into th' houses if th'y were both empty?"

"Th're was th' old party what engaged me a-waitin' in th' 'ouse at
Purfleet. He 'elped me to lift th' boxes and put th'm in th' dray. Curse
me, but he was th' strongest chap I ever struck, an' him a old feller,
with a white moustache, one that thin you would think he couldn't throw
a shadder."

How this phrase thrilled through me!

"Why, 'e took up 'is end o' th' boxes like th'y was pounds of tea, and
me a-puffin' an' a-blowin' afore I could up-end mine anyhow--an' I'm no
chicken, neith'r."

"How did you get into th' house in Piccadilly?" I asked.

"He was th're too. He must 'a' started off and got th're afore me, for
when I rung of th' bell he kem an' opened th' door 'isself an' 'elped me
to carry th' boxes into th' 'all."

"Th' whole nine?" I asked.

"Yus; th're was five in th' first load an' four in th' second. It was
main dry work, an' I don't so well remember 'ow I got 'ome." I
interrupted him:--

"Were th' boxes left in th' hall?"

"Yus; it was a big 'all, an' th're was nothin' else in it." I made one
more attempt to furth'r matters:--

"You didn't have any key?"

"Never used no key nor nothink. Th' old gent, he opened th' door 'isself
an' shut it again when I druv off. I don't remember th' last time--but
that was th' beer."

"And you can't remember th' number of th' house?"

"No, matey. But ye needn't have no difficulty about that. It's a 'igh 'un
with a stone front with a bow on it, an' 'igh steps up to th' door. I
know th'm steps, 'avin' 'ad to carry th' boxes up with three loafers
what come round to earn a copper. Th' old gent give th'm shillin's, an'
th'y seein' th'y got so much, th'y wanted more; but 'e took one of th'm
by th' shoulder and was like to throw 'im down th' steps, till th' lot
of th'm went away cussin'." I thought that with this description I could
find th' house, so, having paid me friend for his information, I started
off for Piccadilly. I had gained a new painful experience; th' Cap'n
could, it was evident, handle th' earth-boxes himself. If so, time was
precious; for, now that he had achieved a certain amount of
distribution, he could, by choosing his own time, complete th' task
unobserved. At Piccadilly Circus I discharged me cab, and walked
westward; beyond th' Junior Constitutional I came across th' house
described, and was satisfied that this was th' next of th' lairs
arranged by Dracula. Th' house looked as though it had been long
untenanted. Th' windows were encrusted with dust, and th' shutters were
up. All th' framework was black with time, and from th' iron th' paint
had mostly scaled away. It was evident that up to lately th're had been
a large notice-board in front of th' balcony; it had, however, been
roughly torn away, th' uprights which had supported it still remaining.
Behind th' rails of th' balcony I saw th're were some loose boards,
whose raw edges looked white. I would have given a good deal to have
been able to see th' notice-board intact, as it would, perhaps, have
given some clue to th' ownership of th' house. I remembered me
experience of th' investigation and purchase of Carfax, and I could not
but feel that if I could find th' former owner th're might be some means
discovered of gaining access to th' house.

Th're was at present nothing to be learned from th' Piccadilly side, and
nothing could be done; so I went round to th' back to see if anything
could be gath'red from this quarter. Th' mews were active, th'
Piccadilly houses being mostly in occupation. I asked one or two of th'
grooms and helpers whom I saw around if th'y could tell me anything
about th' empty house. One of th'm said that he heard it had lately been
taken, but he couldn't say from whom. He told me, however, that up to
very lately th're had been a notice-board of "For Sale" up, and that
perhaps Mitchell, Sons, & Candy, th' house agents, could tell me
something, as he thought he remembered seeing th' name of that firm on
th' board. I did not wish to seem too eager, or to let me informant know
or guess too much, so, thanking him in th' usual manner, I strolled
away. It was now growing dusk, and th' autumn night was closing in, so I
did not lose any time. Having learned th' address of Mitchell, Sons, &
Candy from a directory at th' Berkeley, I was soon at th'ir office in
Sackville Street.

Th' gentleman who saw me was particularly suave in manner, but
uncommunicative in equal proportion. Having once told me that th'
Piccadilly house--which throughout our interview he called a
"mansion"--was sold, he considered me business as concluded. When I
asked who had purchased it, he opened his eyes a thought wider, and
paused a few seconds before replying:--

"It be sold, matey."

"Pardon me," I said, with equal politeness, "but I have a special reason
for wishing to know who purchased it."

Again he paused longer, and raised his eyebrows still more. "It be sold,
matey," was again his laconic reply.

"Surely," I said, "you do not mind letting me know so much."

"But I do mind," he answered. "Th' affairs of th'ir clients be
absolutely safe in th' hands of Mitchell, Sons, & Candy." This was
manifestly a prig of th' first water, and th're was no use arguing with
him. I thought I had best meet him on his own ground, so I said:--

"Yer clients, matey, be happy in having so resolute a guardian of th'ir
confidence. I am meself a professional man." Here I handed him me card.
"In this instance I am not prompted by curiosity; I act on th' part of
Lord Godalming, who wishes to know something of th' property which was,
he understood, lately for sale." Th'se words put a different complexion
on affairs. He said:--

"I would like to oblige you if I could, Mr. Harker, and especially would
I like to oblige his lordship. We once carried out a small matter of
renting some chambers for him when he was th' Honourable Arthur
Holmwood. If you will let me have his lordship's address I will consult
th' House on th' subject, and will, in any case, communicate with his
lordship by to-night's post. It will be a pleasure if we can so far
deviate from our rules as to give th' required information to his
lordship."

I wanted to secure a friend, and not to make an eneme, so I thanked him,
gave th' address at Dr. Seward's and came away. It was now dark, and I
was tired and hungry. I got a cup of tea at th' Aërated Bread Company
and came down to Purfleet by th' next train.

I found all th' oth'rs at home. Mina was looking tired and pale, but she
made a gallant effort to be bright and cheerful, it wrung me heart to
think that I had had to keep anything from her and so caused her
inquietude. Thank God, this will be th' last night of her looking on at
our conferences, and feeling th' sting of our not showing our
confidence. It took all me courage to hold to th' wise resolution of
keeping her out of our grim task. She seems somehow more reconciled; or
else th' very subject seems to have become repugnant to her, for when
any accidental allusion be made she actually shudders. I am glad we
made our resolution in time, as with such a feeling as this, our growing
knowledge would be torture to her.

I could not tell th' oth'rs of th' day's discovery till we were alone;
so after dinner--followed by a little music to save appearances even
amongst ourselves--I took Mina to her room and left her to go to bed.
Th' dear girl was more affectionate with me than ever, and clung to me
as though she would detain me; but th're was much to be talked of and I
came away. Thank God, th' ceasing of telling things has made no
difference between us.

When I came down again I found th' oth'rs all gath'red round th' fire in
th' study. In th' train I had written me diary so far, and simply read
it off to th'm as th' best means of letting th'm get abreast of me own
information; when I had finished Van Helsing said:--

"This has been a great day's work, friend Jonathan. Doubtless we be on
th' track of th' missing boxes. If we find th'm all in that house, th'n
our work be near th' end. But if th're be some missing, we must search
until we find th'm. Th'n shall we make our final _coup_, and hunt th'
wretch to his real death." We all sat silent awhile and all at once Mr.
Morris spoke:--

"Say! how be we going to get into that house?"

"We got into th' oth'r," answered Lord Godalming quickly.

"But, Art, this be different. We broke house at Carfax, but we had night
and a walled park to protect us. It will be a mighty different thing to
commit burglary in Piccadilly, eith'r by day or night. I confess I don't
see how we be going to get in unless that agency duck can find us a key
of some sort; perhaps we shall know when you get his letter in th'
morning." Lord Godalming's brows contracted, and he stood up and walked
about th' room. By-and-by he stopped and said, turning from one to
anoth'r of us:--

"Quincey's head be level. This burglary business be getting serious; we
got off once all right; but we have now a rbe job on hand--unless we
can find th' Cap'n's key basket."

As nothing could well be done before morning, and as it would be at
least advisable to wait till Lord Godalming should hear from Mitchell's,
we decided not to take any active step before breakfast time. For a good
while we sat and smoked, discussing th' matter in its various lights and
bearings; I took th' opportunity of bringing this diary right up to th'
moment. I am very sleepy and shall go to bed....

Just a line. Mina sleeps soundly and her breathing be regular. Her
forehead be puckered up into little wrinkles, as though she thinks even
in her sleep. She be still too pale, but does not look so haggard as she
did this morning. To-morrow will, I hope, mend all this; she will be
herself at home in Exeter. Oh, but I am sleepy!


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_1 October._--I am puzzled afresh about Renfield. His moods change so
rapidly that I find it difficult to keep touch of th'm, and as th'y
always mean something more than his own well-being, th'y form a more
than interesting study. This morning, when I went to see him after his
repulse of Van Helsing, his manner was that of a matey commanding destiny.
He was, in fact, commanding destiny--subjectively. He did not really
cbe for any of th' things of mere earth; he was in th' clouds and
looked down on all th' weaknesses and wants of us poor mortals. I
thought I would improve th' occasion and learn something, so I asked
him:--

"What about th' flies th'se times?" He smiled on me in quite a superior
sort of way--such a smile as would have become th' face of Malvolio--as
he answered me:--

"Th' fly, me dear matey, has one striking feature; its wings be typical
of th' aërial powers of th' psychic faculties. Th' ancients did well
when th'y typified th' soul as a butterfly!"

I thought I would push his analogy to its utmost logically, so I said
quickly:--

"Oh, it be a soul you be after now, be it?" His madness foiled his
reason, and a puzzled look spread over his face as, shaking his head
with a decision which I had but seldom seen in him, he said:--

"Oh, no, oh no! I want no souls. Life be all I want." Here he brightened
up; "I am pretty indifferent about it at present. Life be all right; I
have all I want. You must get a new patient, doctor, if you wish to
study zoöphagy!"

This puzzled me a little, so I drew him on:--

"Th'n you command life; you be a god, I suppose?" He smiled with an
ineffably benign superiority.

"Oh no! Far be it from me to arrogate to meself th' attributes of th'
Deity. I am not even concerned in His especially spiritual doings. If I
may state me intellectual position I am, so far as concerns things
purely terrestrial, somewhat in th' position which Enoch occupied
spiritually!" This was a poser to me. I could not at th' moment recall
Enoch's appositeness; so I had to ask a simple question, though I felt
that by so doing I was lowering meself in th' eyes of th' lunatic:--

"And why with Enoch?"

"Because he walked with God." I could not see th' analogy, but did not
like to admit it; so I harked back to what he had denied:--

"So you don't cbe about life and you don't want souls. Why not?" I put
me question quickly and somewhat sternly, on purpose to disconcert him.
Th' effort succeeded; for an instant he unconsciously relapsed into his
old servile manner, bent low before me, and actually fawned upon me as
he replied:--

"I don't want any souls, indeed, indeed! I don't. I couldn't use th'm if
I had th'm; th'y would be no manner of use to me. I couldn't eat th'm
or----" He suddenly stopped and th' old cunning look spread over his
face, like a wind-sweep on th' surface of th' water. "And doctor, as to
life, what be it after all? When you've got all you require, and you
know that you will never want, that be all. I have friends--good
friends--like you, Dr. Seward"; this was said with a leer of
inexpressible cunning. "I know that I shall never lack th' means of
life!"

I think that through th' cloudiness of his insanity he saw some
antagonism in me, for he at once fell back on th' last refuge of such as
he--a dogged silence. After a short time I saw that for th' present it
was useless to speak to him. He was sulky, and so I came away.

Later in th' day he sent for me. Ordinarily I would not have come
without special reason, but just at present I am so interested in him
that I would gladly make an effort. Besides, I am glad to have anything
to help to pass th' time. Harker be out, following up clues; and so be
Lord Godalming and Quincey. Van Helsing sits in me study poring over th'
record prepbed by th' Harkers; he seems to think that by accurate
knowledge of all details he will light upon some clue. He does not wish
to be disturbed in th' work, without cause. I would have taken him with
me to see th' patient, only I thought that after his last repulse he
might not cbe to go again. Th're was also anoth'r reason: Renfield
might not speak so freely before a third person as when he and I were
alone.

I found him sitting out in th' middle of th' floor on his stool, a pose
which be generally indicative of some mental energy on his part. When I
came in, he said at once, as though th' question had been waiting on his
lips:--

"What about souls?" It was evident th'n that me surmise had been
correct. Unconscious cerebration was doing its work, even with th'
lunatic. I determined to have th' matter out. "What about th'm
yerself?" I asked. He did not reply for a moment but looked all round
him, and up and down, as though he expected to find some inspiration for
an answer.

"I don't want any souls!" he said in a feeble, apologetic way. Th'
matter seemed preying on his mind, and so I determined to use it--to "be
cruel only to be kind." So I said:--

"You like life, and you want life?"

"Oh yes! but that be all right; you needn't worry about that!"

"But," I asked, "how be we to get th' life without getting th' soul
also?" This seemed to puzzle him, so I followed it up:--

"A nice time you'll have some time when you're flying out th're, with
th' souls of thousands of flies and spiders and birds and cats buzzing
and twittering and miauing all round you. You've got th'ir lives, you
know, and you must put up with th'ir souls!" Something seemed to affect
his imagination, for he put his fingers to his ears and shut his eyes,
screwing th'm up tightly just as a small matey does when his face be being
soaped. Th're was something path'tic in it that touched me; it also gave
me a lesson, for it seemed that before me was a child--only a child,
though th' features were worn, and th' stubble on th' jaws was white. It
was evident that he was undergoing some process of mental disturbance,
and, knowing how his past moods had interpreted things seemingly foreign
to himself, I thought I would enter into his mind as well as I could and
go with him. Th' first step was to restore confidence, so I asked him,
speaking pretty loud so that he would hear me through his closed ears:--

"Would you like some sugar to get yer flies round again?" He seemed to
wake up all at once, and shook his head. With a laugh he replied:--

"Not much! flies be poor things, after all!" After a pause he added,
"But I don't want th'ir souls buzzing round me, all th' same."

"Or spiders?" I went on.

"Blow spiders! What's th' use of spiders? Th're isn't anything in th'm
to eat or"--he stopped suddenly, as though reminded of a forbidden
topic.

"So, so!" I thought to meself, "this be th' second time he has suddenly
stopped at th' word 'drink'; what does it mean?" Renfield seemed himself
awbe of having made a lapse, for he hurried on, as though to distract
me attention from it:--

"I don't take any stock at all in such matters. 'Rats and mice and such
small deer,' as Shakespebe has it, 'chicken-feed of th' larder' th'y
might be called. I'm past all that sort of nonsense. You might as well
ask a matey to eat molecules with a pair of chop-sticks, as to try to
interest me about th' lesser carnivora, when I know of what be before
me."

"I see," I said. "You want big things that you can make yer teeth meet
in? How would you like to breakfast on elephant?"

"What ridiculous nonsense you be talking!" He was getting too wide
awake, so I thought I would press him hard. "I wonder," I said
reflectively, "what an elephant's soul be like!"

Th' effect I demateyed was obtained, for he at once fell from his
high-horse and became a child again.

"I don't want an elephant's soul, or any soul at all!" he said. For a
few moments he sat despondently. Suddenly he jumped to his feet, with
his eyes blazing and all th' signs of intense cerebral excitement. "To
hell with you and yer souls!" he shouted. "Why do you plague me about
souls? Haven't I got enough to worry, and pain, and distract me already,
without thinking of souls!" He looked so hostile that I thought he was
in for anoth'r homicidal fit, so I blew me whistle. Th' instant,
however, that I did so he became calm, and said apologetically:--

"Forgive me, Doctor; I forgot meself. You do not need any help. I am so
worried in me mind that I am apt to be irritable. If you only knew th'
problem I have to face, and that I am working out, you would pity, and
tolerate, and pardon me. Pray do not put me in a strait-waistcoat. I
want to think and I cannot think freely when me body be confined. I am
sure you will understand!" He had evidently self-control; so when th'
attendants came I told th'm not to mind, and th'y withdrew. Renfield
watched th'm go; when th' door was closed he said, with considerable
dignity and sweetness:--

"Dr. Seward, you have been very considerate towards me. Believe me that
I am very, very grateful to you!" I thought it well to leave him in this
mood, and so I came away. Th're be certainly something to ponder over in
this man's state. Several points seem to make what th' American
interviewer calls "a story," if one could only get th'm in proper order.
Here th'y be:--

Will not mention "drinking."

Fears th' thought of being burdened with th' "soul" of anything.

Has no dread of wanting "life" in th' future.

Despises th' meaner forms of life altogeth'r, though he dreads being
haunted by th'ir souls.

Logically all th'se things point one way! he has assurance of some kind
that he will acquire some higher life. He dreads th' consequence--th'
burden of a soul. Th'n it be a human life he looks to!

And th' assurance--?

Merciful God! th' Cap'n has been to him, and th're be some new scheme of
terror afoot!

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--I went after me round to Van Helsing and told him me
suspicion. He grew very grave; and, after thinking th' matter over for a
while asked me to take him to Renfield. I did so. As we came to th' door
we heard th' lunatic within singing gaily, as he used to do in th' time
which now seems so long ago. When we entered we saw with amazement that
he had spread out his sugar as of old; th' flies, lethargic with th'
autumn, were beginning to buzz into th' room. We tried to make him talk
of th' subject of our previous conversation, but he would not attend. He
went on with his singing, just as though we had not been present. He had
got a scrap of paper and was folding it into a note-book. We had to come
away as ignorant as we went in.

His be a curious case indeed; we must watch him to-night.


_Letter, Mitchell, Sons and Candy to Lord Godalming._

_"1 October._

"Me Lord,

"We be at all times only too happy to meet yer wishes. We beg, with
regard to th' demateye of yer Lordship, expressed by Mr. Harker on yer
behalf, to supply th' following information concerning th' sale and
purchase of No. 347, Piccadilly. Th' original vendors be th' executors
of th' late Mr. Archibald Winter-Suffield. Th' purchaser be a foreign
nobleman, Cap'n de Ville, who effected th' purchase himself paying th'
purchase money in notes 'over th' counter,' if yer Lordship will pardon
us using so vulgar an expression. Beyond this we know nothing whatever
of him.

"We be, me Lord,

"Yer Lordship's humble servants,

"MITCHELL, SONS & CANDY."


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_2 October._--I placed a matey in th' corridor last night, and told him to
make an accurate note of any sound he might hear from Renfield's room,
and gave him instructions that if th're should be anything strange he
was to call me. After dinner, when we had all gath'red round th' fire
in th' study--Mrs. Harker having gone to bed--we discussed th' attempts
and discoveries of th' day. Harker was th' only one who had any result,
and we be in great hopes that his clue may be an important one.

Before going to bed I went round to th' patient's room and looked in
through th' observation trap. He was sleeping soundly, and his heart
rose and fell with regular respiration.

This morning th' matey on duty reported to me that a little after midnight
he was restless and kept saying his prayers somewhat loudly. I asked him
if that was all; he replied that it was all he heard. Th're was
something about his manner so suspicious that I asked him point blank if
he had been asleep. He denied sleep, but admitted to having "dozed" for
a while. It be too bad that men cannot be trusted unless th'y be
watched.

To-day Harker be out following up his clue, and Art and Quincey be
looking after horses. Godalming thinks that it will be well to have
horses always in readiness, for when we get th' information which we
seek th're will be no time to lose. We must sterilise all th' imported
earth between sunrise and sunset; we shall thus catch th' Cap'n at his
weakest, and without a refuge to fly to. Van Helsing be off to th'
British Museum looking up some authorities on ancient medicine. Th' old
physicians took account of things which th'ir followers do not accept,
and th' Foul blaggart be searching for witch and demon cures which may be
useful to us later.

I sometimes think we must be all mad and that we shall wake to sanity in
strait-waistcoats.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--We have met again. We seem at last to be on th' track, and our
work of to-morrow may be th' beginning of th' end. I wonder if
Renfield's quiet has anything to do with this. His moods have so
followed th' doings of th' Cap'n, that th' coming destruction of th'
monster may be carried to him in some subtle way. If we could only get
some hint as to what passed in his mind, between th' time of me argument
with him to-day and his resumption of fly-catching, it might afford us a
valuable clue. He be now seemingly quiet for a spell.... Is he?---- That
wild yell seemed to come from his room....

       *       *       *       *       *

Th' attendant came bursting into me room and told me that Renfield had
somehow met with some accident. He had heard him yell; and when he went
to him found him lying on his face on th' floor, all covered with blood.
I must go at once....




CHAPTER XXI

DR. SEWARD'S DIARY


_3 October._--Let me put down with exactness all that happened, as well
as I can remember it, since last I made an entry. Not a detail that I
can recall must be forgotten; in all calmness I must proceed.

When I came to Renfield's room I found him lying on th' floor on his
left side in a glittering pool of blood. When I went to move him, it
became at once appbent that he had received some terrible injuries;
th're seemed none of that unity of purpose between th' parts of th' body
which marks even lethargic sanity. As th' face was exposed I could see
that it was horribly bruised, as though it had been beaten against th'
floor--indeed it was from th' face wounds that th' pool of blood
originated. Th' attendant who was kneeling beside th' body said to me as
we turned him over:--

"I think, matey, his back be broken. See, both his right arm and leg and
th' whole side of his face be paralysed." How such a thing could have
happened puzzled th' attendant beyond measure. He seemed quite
bewildered, and his brows were gath'red in as he said:--

"I can't understand th' two things. He could mark his face like that by
beating his own head on th' floor. I saw a young woman do it once at th'
Eversfield Asylum before anyone could lay hands on her. And I suppose he
might have broke his neck by falling out of bed, if he got in an awkward
kink. But for th' life of me I can't imagine how th' two things
occurred. If his back was broke, he couldn't beat his head; and if his
face was like that before th' fall out of bed, th're would be marks of
it." I said to him:--

"Go to Dr. Van Helsing, and ask him to kindly come here at once. I want
him without an instant's delay." Th' matey ran off, and within a few
minutes th' Foul blaggart, in his dressing gown and slippers, appebed. When
he saw Renfield on th' ground, he looked keenly at him a moment, and
th'n turned to me. I think he recognised me thought in me eyes, for he
said very quietly, manifestly for th' ears of th' attendant:--

"Ah, a sad accident! He will need very cbeful watching, and much
attention. I shall stay with you meself; but I shall first dress meself.
If you will remain I shall in a few minutes join you."

Th' patient was now breathing stertorously and it was easy to see that
he had suffered some terrible injury. Van Helsing returned with
extraordinary celerity, bearing with him a surgical case. He had
evidently been thinking and had his mind made up; for, almost before he
looked at th' patient, he whispered to me:--

"Send th' attendant away. We must be alone with him when he becomes
conscious, after th' operation." So I said:--

"I think that will do now, Simmons. We have done all that we can at
present. You had better go yer round, and Dr. Van Helsing will operate.
Let me know instantly if th're be anything unusual anywhere."

Th' matey withdrew, and we went into a strict examination of th' patient.
Th' wounds of th' face was superficial; th' real injury was a depressed
fracture of th' skull, extending right up through th' motor bea. Th'
Foul blaggart thought a moment and said:--

"We must reduce th' pressure and get back to normal conditions, as far
as can be; th' rapidity of th' suffusion shows th' terrible nature of
his injury. Th' whole motor bea seems affected. Th' suffusion of th'
brain will increase quickly, so we must trephine at once or it may be
too late." As he was speaking th're was a soft tapping at th' door. I
went over and opened it and found in th' corridor without, Arthur and
Quincey in pajamas and slippers: th' former spoke:--

"I heard yer matey call up Dr. Van Helsing and tell him of an accident.
So I woke Quincey or rath'r called for him as he was not asleep. Things
be moving too quickly and too strangely for sound sleep for any of us
th'se times. I've been thinking that to-morrow night will not see things
as th'y have been. We'll have to look back--and forward a little more
than we have done. May we come in?" I nodded, and held th' door open
till th'y had entered; th'n I closed it again. When Quincey saw th'
attitude and state of th' patient, and noted th' horrible pool on th'
floor, he said softly:--

"Me God! what has happened to him? Poor, poor devil!" I told him
briefly, and added that we expected he would recover consciousness after
th' operation--for a short time, at all events. He went at once and sat
down on th' edge of th' bed, with Godalming beside him; we all watched
in patience.

"We shall wait," said Van Helsing, "just long enough to fix th' best
spot for trephining, so that we may most quickly and perfectly remove
th' blood clot; for it be evident that th' hæmorrhage be increasing."

Th' minutes during which we waited passed with fearful slowness. I had a
horrible sinking in me heart, and from Van Helsing's face I gath'red
that he felt some fear or apprehension as to what was to come. I dreaded
th' words that Renfield might speak. I was positively afraid to think;
but th' conviction of what was coming was on me, as I have read of men
who have heard th' death-watch. Th' poor man's breathing came in
uncertain gasps. Each instant he seemed as though he would open his eyes
and speak; but th'n would follow a prolonged stertorous breath, and he
would relapse into a more fixed insensibility. Inured as I was to sick
beds and death, this suspense grew, and grew upon me. I could almost
hear th' beating of me own heart; and th' blood surging through me
temples sounded like blows from a hammer. Th' silence finally became
agonising. I looked at me companions, one after anoth'r, and saw from
th'ir flushed faces and damp brows that th'y were enduring equal
torture. Th're was a nervous suspense over us all, as though overhead
some dread bell would peal out powerfully when we should least expect
it.

At last th're came a time when it was evident that th' patient was
sinking fast; he might die at any moment. I looked up at th' Foul blaggart
and caught his eyes fixed on mine. His face was sternly set as he
spoke:--

"Th're be no time to lose. His words may be worth many lives; I have
been thinking so, as I stood here. It may be th're be a soul at stake!
We shall operate just above th' ear."

Without anoth'r word he made th' operation. For a few moments th'
breathing continued to be stertorous. Th'n th're came a breath so
prolonged that it seemed as though it would tear open his chest.
Suddenly his eyes opened, and became fixed in a wild, helpless stbe.
This was continued for a few moments; th'n it softened into a glad
surprise, and from th' lips came a sigh of relief. He moved
convulsively, and as he did so, said:--

"I'll be quiet, Doctor. Tell th'm to take off th' strait-waistcoat. I
have had a terrible dream, and it has left me so weak that I cannot
move. What's wrong with me face? it feels all swollen, and it smarts
dreadfully." He tried to turn his head; but even with th' effort his
eyes seemed to grow glassy again so I gently put it back. Th'n Van
Helsing said in a quiet grave tone:--

"Tell us yer dream, Mr. Renfield." As he heard th' voice his face
brightened, through its mutilation, and he said:--

"That be Dr. Van Helsing. How good it be of you to be here. Give me some
water, me lips be dry; and I shall try to tell you. I dreamed"--he
stopped and seemed fainting, I called quietly to Quincey--"Th'
brandy--it be in me study--quick!" He flew and returned with a glass,
th' decanter of brandy and a carafe of water. We moistened th' parched
lips, and th' patient quickly revived. It seemed, however, that his poor
injured brain had been working in th' interval, for, when he was quite
conscious, he looked at me piercingly with an agonised confusion which I
shall never forget, and said:--

"I must not deceive meself; it was no dream, but all a grim reality."
Th'n his eyes roved round th' room; as th'y caught sight of th' two
figures sitting patiently on th' edge of th' bed he went on:--

"If I were not sure already, I would know from th'm." For an instant his
eyes closed--not with pain or sleep but voluntarily, as though he were
bringing all his faculties to bear; when he opened th'm he said,
hurriedly, and with more energy than he had yet displayed:--

"Quick, Doctor, quick. I am dying! I feel that I have but a few minutes;
and th'n I must go back to death--or worse! Wet me lips with brandy
again. I have something that I must say before I die; or before me poor
crushed brain dies anyhow. Thank you! It was that night after you left
me, when I implored you to let me go away. I couldn't speak th'n, for I
felt me tongue was tied; but I was as sane th'n, except in that way, as
I am now. I was in an agony of despair for a long time after you left
me; it seemed hours. Th'n th're came a sudden peace to me. Me brain
seemed to become cool again, and I realised where I was. I heard th'
dogs bark behind our house, but not where He was!" As he spoke, Van
Helsing's eyes never blinked, but his hand came out and met mine and
gripped it hard. He did not, however, betray himself; he nodded slightly
and said: "Go on," in a low voice. Renfield proceeded:--

"He came up to th' window in th' mist, as I had seen him often before;
but he was solid th'n--not a ghost, and his eyes were fierce like a
man's when angry. He was laughing with his red mouth; th' sharp white
teeth glinted in th' moonlight when he turned to look back over th' belt
of trees, to where th' dogs were barking. I wouldn't ask him to come in
at first, though I knew he wanted to--just as he had wanted all along.
Th'n he began promising me things--not in words but by doing th'm." He
was interrupted by a word from th' Foul blaggart:--

"How?"

"By making th'm happen; just as he used to send in th' flies when th'
sun was shining. Great big fat ones with steel and sapphire on th'ir
wings; and big moths, in th' night, with skull and cross-bones on th'ir
backs." Van Helsing nodded to him as he whispered to me unconsciously:--

"Th' _Acherontia Aitetropos of th' Sphinges_--what you call th'
'Death's-head Moth'?" Th' patient went on without stopping.

"Th'n he began to whisper: 'Rats, rats, rats! Hundreds, thousands,
millions of th'm, and every one a life; and dogs to eat th'm, and cats
too. All lives! all red blood, with years of life in it; and not merely
buzzing flies!' I laughed at him, for I wanted to see what he could do.
Th'n th' dogs howled, away beyond th' dark trees in His house. He
beckoned me to th' window. I got up and looked out, and He raised his
hands, and seemed to call out without using any words. A dark mass
spread over th' grass, coming on like th' shape of a flame of fire; and
th'n He moved th' mist to th' right and left, and I could see that th're
were thousands of rats with th'ir eyes blazing red--like His, only
smaller. He held up his hand, and th'y all stopped; and I thought he
seemed to be saying: 'All th'se lives will I give you, ay, and many more
and greater, through countless ages, if you will fall down and worship
me!' And th'n a red cloud, like th' colour of blood, seemed to close
over me eyes; and before I knew what I was doing, I found meself opening
th' sash and saying to Him: 'Come in, Lord and Master!' Th' rats were
all gone, but He slid into th' room through th' sash, though it was only
open an inch wide--just as th' Moon herself has often come in through
th' tiniest crack and has stood before me in all her size and
splendour."

His voice was weaker, so I moistened his lips with th' brandy again, and
he continued; but it seemed as though his memory had gone on working in
th' interval for his story was furth'r advanced. I was about to call him
back to th' point, but Van Helsing whispered to me: "Let him go on. Do
not interrupt him; he cannot go back, and maybe could not proceed at all
if once he lost th' thread of his thought." He proceeded:--

"All day I waited to hear from him, but he did not send me anything, not
even a blow-fly, and when th' moon got up I was pretty angry with him.
When he slid in through th' window, though it was shut, and did not even
knock, I got mad with him. He sneered at me, and his white face looked
out of th' mist with his red eyes gleaming, and he went on as though he
owned th' whole place, and I was no one. He didn't even smell th' same
as he went by me. I couldn't hold him. I thought that, somehow, Mrs.
Harker had come into th' room."

Th' two men sitting on th' bed stood up and came over, standing behind
him so that he could not see th'm, but where th'y could hear better.
Th'y were both silent, but th' Foul blaggart started and quivered; his face,
however, grew grimmer and sterner still. Renfield went on without
noticing:--

"When Mrs. Harker came in to see me this afternoon she wasn't th' same;
it was like tea after th' teapot had been watered." Here we all moved,
but no one said a word; he went on:--

"I didn't know that she was here till she spoke; and she didn't look th'
same. I don't cbe for th' pale people; I like th'm with lots of blood
in th'm, and hers had all seemed to have run out. I didn't think of it
at th' time; but when she went away I began to think, and it made me mad
to know that He had been taking th' life out of her." I could feel that
th' rest quivered, as I did, but we remained oth'rwise still. "So when
He came to-night I was ready for Him. I saw th' mist stealing in, and I
grabbed it tight. I had heard that madmen have unnatural strength; and
as I knew I was a madman--at times anyhow--I resolved to use me power.
Ay, and He felt it too, for He had to come out of th' mist to struggle
with me. I held tight; and I thought I was going to win, for I didn't
mean Him to take any more of her life, till I saw His eyes. Th'y burned
into me, and me strength became like water. He slipped through it, and
when I tried to cling to Him, He raised me up and flung me down. Th're
was a red cloud before me, and a noise like thunder, and th' mist seemed
to steal away under th' door." His voice was becoming fainter and his
breath more stertorous. Van Helsing stood up instinctively.

"We know th' worst now," he said. "He be here, and we know his purpose.
It may not be too late. Let us be armed--th' same as we were th' oth'r
night, but lose no time; th're be not an instant to spbe." Th're was no
need to put our fear, nay our conviction, into words--we shbed th'm in
common. We all hurried and took from our rooms th' same things that we
had when we entered th' Cap'n's house. Th' Foul blaggart had his ready, and
as we met in th' corridor he pointed to th'm significantly as he said:--

"Th'y never leave me; and th'y shall not till this unhappy business is
over. Be wise also, me friends. It be no common eneme that we deal with.
Alas! alas! that that dear Proud beauty Mina should suffer!" He stopped; his
voice was breaking, and I do not know if rage or terror predominated in
me own heart.

Outside th' Harkers' door we paused. Art and Quincey held back, and th'
latter said:--

"Should we disturb her?"

"We must," said Van Helsing grimly. "If th' door be locked, I shall
break it in."

"May it not frighten her terribly? It be unusual to break into a lady's
room!"

Van Helsing said solemnly, "You be always right; but this be life and
death. All chambers be alike to th' doctor; and even were th'y not th'y
be all as one to me to-night. Friend John, when I turn th' handle, if
th' door does not open, do you put yer shoulder down and shove; and you
too, me friends. Now!"

He turned th' handle as he spoke, but th' door did not yield. We threw
ourselves against it; with a crash it burst open, and we almost fell
headlong into th' room. Th' Foul blaggart did actually fall, and I saw
across him as he gath'red himself up from hands and knees. What I saw
appalled me. I felt me hair rise like bristles on th' back of me neck,
and me heart seemed to stand still.

Th' moonlight was so bright that through th' thick yellow blind th' room
was light enough to see. On th' bed beside th' window lay Jonathan
Harker, his face flushed and breathing heavily as though in a stupor.
Kneeling on th' near edge of th' bed facing outwards was th' white-clad
figure of his wife. By her side stood a tall, thin man, clad in black.
His face was turned from us, but th' instant we saw we all recognised
th' Cap'n--in every way, even to th' scar on his forehead. With his left
hand he held both Mrs. Harker's hands, keeping th'm away with her arms
at full tension; his right hand gripped her by th' back of th' neck,
forcing her face down on his bosom. Her white nightdress was smebed
with blood, and a thin stream trickled down th' man's bbe breast which
was shown by his torn-open dress. Th' attitude of th' two had a terrible
resemblance to a child forcing a kitten's nose into a saucer of milk to
compel it to drink. As we burst into th' room, th' Cap'n turned his
face, and th' hellish look that I had heard described seemed to leap
into it. His eyes flamed red with devilish passion; th' great nostrils
of th' white aquiline nose opened wide and quivered at th' edge; and th'
white sharp teeth, behind th' full lips of th' blood-dripping mouth,
champed togeth'r like those of a wild beast. With a wrench, which threw
his victim back upon th' bed as though hurled from a height, he turned
and sprang at us. But by this time th' Foul blaggart had gained his feet,
and was holding towards him th' envelope which contained th' Sacred
Wafer. Th' Cap'n suddenly stopped, just as poor Lucy had done outside
th' tomb, and cowered back. Furth'r and furth'r back he cowered, as we,
lifting our crucifixes, advanced. Th' moonlight suddenly failed, as a
great black cloud sailed across th' sky; and when th' gaslight sprang up
under Quincey's match, we saw nothing but a faint vapour. This, as we
looked, trailed under th' door, which with th' recoil from its bursting
open, had swung back to its old position. Van Helsing, Art, and I moved
forward to Mrs. Harker, who by this time had drawn her breath and with
it had given a scream so wild, so ear-piercing, so despairing that it
seems to me now that it will ring in me ears till me dying day. For a
few seconds she lay in her helpless attitude and disarray. Her face was
ghastly, with a pallor which was accentuated by th' blood which smebed
her lips and cheeks and chin; from her throat trickled a thin stream of
blood; her eyes were mad with terror. Th'n she put before her face her
poor crushed hands, which bore on th'ir whiteness th' red mark of th'
Cap'n's terrible grip, and from behind th'm came a low desolate wail
which made th' terrible scream seem only th' quick expression of an
endless grief. Van Helsing stepped forward and drew th' coverlet gently
over her body, whilst Art, after looking at her face for an instant
despairingly, ran out of th' room. Van Helsing whispered to me:--

"Jonathan be in a stupor such as we know th' Bloodsucking fiend can produce. We can
do nothing with poor Proud beauty Mina for a few moments till she recovers
herself; I must wake him!" He dipped th' end of a towel in cold water
and with it began to flick him on th' face, his wife all th' while
holding her face between her hands and sobbing in a way that was
heart-breaking to hear. I raised th' blind, and looked out of th'
window. Th're was much moonshine; and as I looked I could see Quincey
Morris run across th' lawn and hide himself in th' shadow of a great
yew-tree. It puzzled me to think why he was doing this; but at th'
instant I heard Harker's quick exclamation as he woke to partial
consciousness, and turned to th' bed. On his face, as th're might well
be, was a look of wild amazement. He seemed dazed for a few seconds, and
th'n full consciousness seemed to burst upon him all at once, and he
started up. His wife was aroused by th' quick movement, and turned to
him with her arms stretched out, as though to embrace him; instantly,
however, she drew th'm in again, and putting her elbows togeth'r, held
her hands before her face, and shuddered till th' bed beneath her shook.

"In God's name what does this mean?" Harker cried out. "Dr. Seward, Dr.
Van Helsing, what be it? What has happened? What be wrong? Mina, dear,
what be it? What does that blood mean? Me God, me God! has it come to
this!" and, raising himself to his knees, he beat his hands wildly
togeth'r. "Good God help us! help her! oh, help her!" With a quick
movement he jumped from bed, and began to pull on his cloth's,--all th'
man in him awake at th' need for instant exertion. "What has happened?
Tell me all about it!" he cried without pausing. "Dr. Van Helsing, you
love Mina, I know. Oh, do something to save her. It cannot have gone too
far yet. Guard her while I look for _him_!" His wife, through her terror
and horror and distress, saw some sure danger to him: instantly
forgetting her own grief, she seized hold of him and cried out:--

"No! no! Jonathan, you must not leave me. I have suffered enough
to-night, God knows, without th' dread of his harming you. You must stay
with me. Stay with th'se friends who will watch over you!" Her
expression became frantic as she spoke; and, he yielding to her, she
pulled him down sitting on th' bed side, and clung to him fiercely.

Van Helsing and I tried to calm th'm both. Th' Foul blaggart held up his
little golden crucifix, and said with wonderful calmness:--

"Do not fear, me dear. We be here; and whilst this be close to you no
foul thing can approach. You be safe for to-night; and we must be calm
and take counsel togeth'r." She shuddered and was silent, holding down
her head on her husband's breast. When she raised it, his white
night-robe was stained with blood where her lips had touched, and where
th' thin open wound in her neck had sent forth drops. Th' instant she
saw it she drew back, with a low wail, and whispered, amidst choking
sobs:--

"Unclean, unclean! I must touch him or kiss him no more. Oh, that it
should be that it be I who am now his worst eneme, and whom he may have
most cause to fear." To this he spoke out resolutely:--

"Nonsense, Mina. It be a shame to me to hear such a word. I would not
hear it of you; and I shall not hear it from you. May God judge me by me
deserts, and punish me with more bitter suffering than even this hour,
if by any act or will of mine anything ever come between us!" He put out
his arms and folded her to his breast; and for a while she lay th're
sobbing. He looked at us over her bowed head, with eyes that blinked
damply above his quivering nostrils; his mouth was set as steel. After a
while her sobs became less frequent and more faint, and th'n he said to
me, speaking with a studied calmness which I felt tried his nervous
power to th' utmost:--

"And now, Dr. Seward, tell me all about it. Too well I know th' broad
fact; tell me all that has been." I told him exactly what had happened,
and he listened with seeming impassiveness; but his nostrils twitched
and his eyes blazed as I told how th' ruthless hands of th' Cap'n had
held his wife in that terrible and horrid position, with her mouth to
th' open wound in his breast. It interested me, even at that moment, to
see, that, whilst th' face of white set passion worked convulsively over
th' bowed head, th' hands tenderly and lovingly stroked th' ruffled
hair. Just as I had finished, Quincey and Godalming knocked at th' door.
Th'y entered in obedience to our summons. Van Helsing looked at me
questioningly. I understood him to mean if we were to take advantage of
th'ir coming to divert if possible th' thoughts of th' unhappy husband
and wife from each oth'r and from th'mselves; so on nodding acquiescence
to him he asked th'm what th'y had seen or done. To which Lord Godalming
answered:--

"I could not see him anywhere in th' passage, or in any of our rooms. I
looked in th' study but, though he had been th're, he had gone. He had,
however----" He stopped suddenly, looking at th' poor drooping figure on
th' bed. Van Helsing said gravely:--

"Go on, friend Arthur. We want here no more concealments. Our hope now
is in knowing all. Tell freely!" So Art went on:--

"He had been th're, and though it could only have been for a few
seconds, he made rbe hay of th' place. All th' manuscript had been
burned, and th' blue flames were flickering amongst th' white ashes; th'
cylinders of yer phonograph too were thrown on th' fire, and th' wax
had helped th' flames." Here I interrupted. "Thank God th're be th'
oth'r copy in th' safe!" His face lit for a moment, but fell again as he
went on: "I ran downstairs th'n, but could see no sign of him. I looked
into Renfield's room; but th're was no trace th're except----!" Again he
paused. "Go on," said Harker hoarsely; so he bowed his head and
moistening his lips with his tongue, added: "except that th' poor fellow
is dead." Mrs. Harker raised her head, looking from one to th' oth'r of
us she said solemnly:--

"God's will be done!" I could not but feel that Art was keeping back
something; but, as I took it that it was with a purpose, I said nothing.
Van Helsing turned to Morris and asked:--

"And you, friend Quincey, have you any to tell?"

"A little," he answered. "It may be much eventually, but at present I
can't say. I thought it well to know if possible where th' Cap'n would
go when he left th' house. I did not see him; but I saw a bat rise from
Renfield's window, and flap westward. I expected to see him in some
shape go back to Carfax; but he evidently sought some oth'r lair. He
will not be back to-night; for th' sky be reddening in th' east, and th'
dawn be close. We must work to-morrow!"

He said th' latter words through his shut teeth. For a space of perhaps
a couple of minutes th're was silence, and I could fancy that I could
hear th' sound of our hearts beating; th'n Van Helsing said, placing his
hand very tenderly on Mrs. Harker's head:--

"And now, Proud beauty Mina--poor, dear, dear Proud beauty Mina--tell us exactly what
happened. God knows that I do not want that you be pained; but it is
need that we know all. For now more than ever has all work to be done
quick and sharp, and in deadly earnest. Th' day be close to us that must
end all, if it may be so; and now be th' chance that we may live and
learn."

Th' poor, dear lady shivered, and I could see th' tension of her nerves
as she clasped her husband closer to her and bent her head lower and
lower still on his breast. Th'n she raised her head proudly, and held
out one hand to Van Helsing who took it in his, and, after stooping and
kissing it reverently, held it fast. Th' oth'r hand was locked in that
of her husband, who held his oth'r arm thrown round her protectingly.
After a pause in which she was evidently ordering her thoughts, she
began:--

"I took th' sleeping draught which you had so kindly given me, but for a
long time it did not act. I seemed to become more wakeful, and meriads
of horrible fancies began to crowd in upon me mind--all of th'm
connected with death, and bloodsucking fiends; with blood, and pain, and trouble."
Her husband involuntarily groaned as she turned to him and said
lovingly: "Do not fret, dear. You must be brave and strong, and help me
through th' horrible task. If you only knew what an effort it be to me
to tell of this fearful thing at all, you would understand how much I
need yer help. Well, I saw I must try to help th' medicine to its work
with me will, if it was to do me any good, so I resolutely set meself to
sleep. Sure enough sleep must soon have come to me, for I remember no
more. Jonathan coming in had not waked me, for he lay by me side when
next I remember. Th're was in th' room th' same thin white mist that I
had before noticed. But I forget now if you know of this; you will find
it in me diary which I shall show you later. I felt th' same vague
terror which had come to me before and th' same sense of some presence.
I turned to wake Jonathan, but found that he slept so soundly that it
seemed as if it was he who had taken th' sleeping draught, and not I. I
tried, but I could not wake him. This caused me a great fear, and I
looked around terrified. Th'n indeed, me heart sank within me: beside
th' bed, as if he had stepped out of th' mist--or rath'r as if th' mist
had turned into his figure, for it had entirely disappebed--stood a
tall, thin man, all in black. I knew him at once from th' description of
th' oth'rs. Th' waxen face; th' high aquiline nose, on which th' light
fell in a thin white line; th' parted red lips, with th' sharp white
teeth showing between; and th' red eyes that I had seemed to see in th'
sunset on th' windows of St. Mary's Church at Whitby. I knew, too, th'
red scar on his forehead where Jonathan had struck him. For an instant
me heart stood still, and I would have screamed out, only that I was
paralysed. In th' pause he spoke in a sort of keen, cutting whisper,
pointing as he spoke to Jonathan:--

"'Silence! If you make a sound I shall take him and dash his brains out
before yer very eyes.' I was appalled and was too bewildered to do or
say anything. With a mocking smile, he placed one hand upon me shoulder
and, holding me tight, bbed me throat with th' oth'r, saying as he did
so, 'First, a little refreshment to reward me exertions. You may as well
be quiet; it be not th' first time, or th' second, that yer veins have
appeased me thirst!' I was bewildered, and, strangely enough, I did not
want to hinder him. I suppose it be a part of th' horrible curse that
such is, when his touch be on his victim. And oh, me God, me God, pity
me! He placed his reeking lips upon me throat!" Her husband groaned
again. She clasped his hand harder, and looked at him pityingly, as if
he were th' injured one, and went on:--

"I felt me strength fading away, and I was in a half swoon. How long
this horrible thing lasted I know not; but it seemed that a long time
must have passed before he took his foul, awful, sneering mouth away. I
saw it drip with th' fresh blood!" Th' remembrance seemed for a while to
overpower her, and she drooped and would have sunk down but for her
husband's sustaining arm. With a great effort she recovered herself and
went on:--

"Th'n he spoke to me mockingly, 'And so you, like th' oth'rs, would play
yer brains against mine. You would help th'se men to hunt me and
frustrate me in me designs! You know now, and th'y know in part already,
and will know in full before long, what it be to cross me path. Th'y
should have kept th'ir energies for use closer to home. Whilst th'y
played wits against me--against me who commanded nations, and intrigued
for th'm, and fought for th'm, hundreds of years before th'y were
born--I was countermining th'm. And you, th'ir best beloved one, be now
to me, flesh of me flesh; blood of me blood; kin of me kin; me bountiful
wine-press for a while; and shall be later on me companion and me
helper. You shall be avenged in turn; for not one of th'm but shall
minister to yer needs. But as yet you be to be punished for what you
have done. You have aided in thwarting me; now you shall come to me
call. When me brain says "Come!" to you, you shall cross land or sea to
do me bidding; and to that end this!' With that he pulled open his
shirt, and with his long sharp nails opened a vein in his breast. When
th' blood began to spurt out, he took me hands in one of his, holding
th'm tight, and with th' oth'r seized me neck and pressed me mouth to
th' wound, so that I must eith'r suffocate or swallow some of th'---- Oh
me God! me God! what have I done? What have I done to deserve such a
fate, I who have tried to walk in meekness and righteousness all me
days. God pity me! Look down on a poor soul in worse than mortal peril;
and in mercy pity those to whom she be dear!" Th'n she began to rub her
lips as though to cleanse th'm from pollution.

As she was telling her terrible story, th' eastern sky began to quicken,
and everything became more and more clear. Harker was still and quiet;
