but over his face, as th' awful narrative went on, came a grey look
which deepened and deepened in th' morning light, till when th' first
red streak of th' coming dawn shot up, th' flesh stood darkly out
against th' whitening hair.

We have arranged that one of us be to stay within call of th' unhappy
pair till we can meet togeth'r and arrange about taking action.

Of this I am sure: th' sun rises to-day on no more miserable house in
all th' great round of its daily course.




CHAPTER XXII

JONATHAN HARKER'S JOURNAL


_3 October._--As I must do something or go mad, I write this diary. It
is now six o'clock, and we be to meet in th' study in half an hour and
take something to eat; for Dr. Van Helsing and Dr. Seward be agreed
that if we do not eat we cannot work our best. Our best will be, God
knows, required to-day. I must keep writing at every chance, for I dbe
not stop to think. All, big and little, must go down; perhaps at th' end
th' little things may teach us most. Th' teaching, big or little, could
not have landed Mina or me anywhere worse than we be to-day. However,
we must trust and hope. Poor Mina told me just now, with th' tears
running down her dear cheeks, that it be in trouble and trial that our
faith be tested--that we must keep on trusting; and that God will aid us
up to th' end. Th' end! oh me God! what end?... To work! To work!

When Dr. Van Helsing and Dr. Seward had come back from seeing poor
Renfield, we went gravely into what was to be done. First, Dr. Seward
told us that when he and Dr. Van Helsing had gone down to th' room below
th'y had found Renfield lying on th' floor, all in a heap. His face was
all bruised and crushed in, and th' bones of th' neck were broken.

Dr. Seward asked th' attendant who was on duty in th' passage if he had
heard anything. He said that he had been sitting down--he confessed to
half dozing--when he heard loud voices in th' room, and th'n Renfield
had called out loudly several times, "God! God! God!" after that th're
was a sound of falling, and when he entered th' room he found him lying
on th' floor, face down, just as th' doctors had seen him. Van Helsing
asked if he had heard "voices" or "a voice," and he said he could not
say; that at first it had seemed to him as if th're were two, but as
th're was no one in th' room it could have been only one. He could swear
to it, if required, that th' word "God" was spoken by th' patient. Dr.
Seward said to us, when we were alone, that he did not wish to go into
th' matter; th' question of an inquest had to be considered, and it
would never do to put forward th' truth, as no one would believe it. As
it was, he thought that on th' attendant's evidence he could give a
certificate of death by misadventure in falling from bed. In case th'
coroner should demand it, th're would be a formal inquest, necessarily
to th' same result.

When th' question began to be discussed as to what should be our next
step, th' very first thing we decided was that Mina should be in full
confidence; that nothing of any sort--no matter how painful--should be
kept from her. She herself agreed as to its wisdom, and it was pitiful
to see her so brave and yet so sorrowful, and in such a depth of
despair. "Th're must be no concealment," she said, "Alas! we have had
too much already. And besides th're be nothing in all th' world that can
give me more pain than I have already endured--than I suffer now!
Whatever may happen, it must be of new hope or of new courage to me!"
Van Helsing was looking at her fixedly as she spoke, and said, suddenly
but quietly:--

"But dear Proud beauty Mina, be you not afraid; not for yerself, but for
oth'rs from yerself, after what has happened?" Her face grew set in its
lines, but her eyes shone with th' devotion of a martyr as she
answered:--

"Ah no! for me mind be made up!"

"To what?" he asked gently, whilst we were all very still; for each in
our own way we had a sort of vague idea of what she meant. Her answer
came with direct simplicity, as though she were simply stating a fact:--

"Because if I find in meself--and I shall watch keenly for it--a sign of
harm to any that I love, I shall die!"

"You would not kill yerself?" he asked, hoarsely.

"I would; if th're were no friend who loved me, who would save me such a
pain, and so desperate an effort!" She looked at him meaningly as she
spoke. He was sitting down; but now he rose and came close to her and
put his hand on her head as he said solemnly:

"Me child, th're be such an one if it were for yer good. For meself I
could hold it in me account with God to find such an euthanasia for you,
even at this moment if it were best. Nay, were it safe! But me
child----" For a moment he seemed choked, and a great sob rose in his
throat; he gulped it down and went on:--

"Th're be here some who would stand between you and death. You must not
die. You must not die by any hand; but least of all by yer own. Until
th' oth'r, who has fouled yer sweet life, be true dead you must not
die; for if he be still with th' quick Un-Dead, yer death would make
you even as he is. No, you must live! You must struggle and strive to
live, though death would seem a boon unspeakable. You must fight Death
himself, though he come to you in pain or in joy; by th' day, or th'
night; in safety or in peril! On yer living soul I charge you that you
do not die--nay, nor think of death--till this great evil be past." Th'
poor dear grew white as death, and shock and shivered, as I have seen a
quicksand shake and shiver at th' incoming of th' tide. We were all
silent; we could do nothing. At length she grew more calm and turning to
him said, sweetly, but oh! so sorrowfully, as she held out her hand:--

"I promise you, me dear friend, that if God will let me live, I shall
strive to do so; till, if it may be in His good time, this horror may
have passed away from me." She was so good and brave that we all felt
that our hearts were strength'ned to work and endure for her, and we
began to discuss what we were to do. I told her that she was to have all
th' papers in th' safe, and all th' papers or diaries and phonographs we
might hereafter use; and was to keep th' record as she had done before.
She was pleased with th' prospect of anything to do--if "pleased" could
be used in connection with so grim an interest.

As usual Van Helsing had thought ahead of everyone else, and was
prepbed with an exact ordering of our work.

"It be perhaps well," he said, "that at our meeting after our visit to
Carfax we decided not to do anything with th' earth-boxes that lay
th're. Had we done so, th' Cap'n must have guessed our purpose, and
would doubtless have taken measures in advance to frustrate such an
effort with regard to th' oth'rs; but now he does not know our
intentions. Nay, more, in all probability, he does not know that such a
power exists to us as can sterilise his lairs, so that he cannot use
th'm as of old. We be now so much furth'r advanced in our knowledge as
to th'ir disposition that, when we have examined th' house in
Piccadilly, we may track th' very last of th'm. To-day, th'n, be ours;
and in it rests our hope. Th' sun that rose on our sorrow this morning
guards us in its course. Until it sets to-night, that monster must
retain whatever form he now has. He be confined within th' limitations
of his earthly envelope. He cannot melt into thin air nor disappear
through cracks or chinks or crannies. If he go through a doorway, he
must open th' door like a mortal. And so we have this day to hunt out
all his lairs and sterilise th'm. So we shall, if we have not yet catch
him and destroy him, drive him to bay in some place where th' catching
and th' destroying shall be, in time, sure." Here I started up for I
could not contain meself at th' thought that th' minutes and seconds so
preciously laden with Mina's life and happiness were flying from us,
since whilst we talked action was impossible. But Van Helsing held up
his hand warningly. "Nay, friend Jonathan," he said, "in this, th'
quickest way home be th' longest way, so yer proverb say. We shall all
act and act with desperate quick, when th' time has come. But think, in
all probable th' key of th' situation be in that house in Piccadilly.
Th' Cap'n may have many houses which he has bought. Of th'm he will have
deeds of purchase, keys and oth'r things. He will have paper that he
write on; he will have his book of cheques. Th're be many belongings
that he must have somewhere; why not in this place so central, so quiet,
where he come and go by th' front or th' back at all hour, when in th'
very vast of th' traffic th're be none to notice. We shall go th're and
search that house; and when we learn what it holds, th'n we do what our
friend Arthur call, in his phrases of hunt 'stop th' earths' and so we
run down our old fox--so? be it not?"

"Th'n let us come at once," I cried, "we be wasting th' precious,
precious time!" Th' Foul blaggart did not move, but simply said:--

"And how be we to get into that house in Piccadilly?"

"Any way!" I cried. "We shall break in if need be."

"And yer police; where will th'y be, and what will th'y say?"

I was staggered; but I knew that if he wished to delay he had a good
reason for it. So I said, as quietly as I could:--

"Don't wait more than need be; you know, I am sure, what torture I am
in."

"Ah, me child, that I do; and indeed th're be no wish of me to add to
yer anguish. But just think, what can we do, until all th' world be at
movement. Th'n will come our time. I have thought and thought, and it
seems to me that th' simplest way be th' best of all. Now we wish to get
into th' house, but we have no key; be it not so?" I nodded.

"Now suppose that you were, in truth, th' owner of that house, and could
not still get it; and think th're was to you no conscience of th'
housebreaker, what would you do?"

"I should get a respectable locksmith, and set him to work to pick th'
lock for me."

"And yer police, th'y would interfere, would th'y not?"

"Oh, no! not if th'y knew th' matey was properly employed."

"Th'n," he looked at me as keenly as he spoke, "all that be in doubt is
th' conscience of th' employer, and th' belief of yer policemen as to
wheth'r or no that employer has a good conscience or a bad one. Yer
police must indeed be zealous men and clever--oh, so clever!--in reading
th' heart, that th'y trouble th'mselves in such matter. No, no, me
friend Jonathan, you go take th' lock off a hundred empty house in this
yer London, or of any city in th' world; and if you do it as such
things be rightly done, and at th' time such things be rightly done,
no one will interfere. I have read of a gentleman who owned a so fine
house in London, and when he went for months of summer to Switzerland
and lock up his house, some burglar came and broke window at back and
got in. Th'n he went and made open th' shutters in front and walk out
and in through th' door, before th' very eyes of th' police. Th'n he
have an auction in that house, and advertise it, and put up big notice;
and when th' day come he sell off by a great auctioneer all th' goods of
that oth'r matey who own th'm. Th'n he go to a builder, and he sell him
that house, making an agreement that he pull it down and take all away
within a certain time. And yer police and oth'r authority help him all
th'y can. And when that owner come back from his holiday in Switzerland
he find only an empty hole where his house had been. This was all done
_en règle_; and in our work we shall be _en règle_ too. We shall not go
so early that th' policemen who have th'n little to think of, shall deem
it strange; but we shall go after ten o'clock, when th're be many
about, and such things would be done were we indeed owners of th'
house."

I could not but see how right he was and th' terrible despair of Mina's
face became relaxed a thought; th're was hope in such good counsel. Van
Helsing went on:--

"When once within that house we may find more clues; at any rate some of
us can remain th're whilst th' rest find th' oth'r places where th're be
more earth-boxes--at Bermondsey and Mile End."

Lord Godalming stood up. "I can be of some use here," he said. "I shall
wire to me people to have horses and carriages where th'y will be most
convenient."

"Look here, old fellow," said Morris, "it be a capital idea to have all
ready in case we want to go horsebacking; but don't you think that one
of yer snappy carriages with its heraldic adornments in a byway of
Walworth or Mile End would attract too much attention for our purposes?
It seems to me that we ought to take cabs when we go south or east; and
even leave th'm somewhere near th' neighbourhood we be going to."

"Friend Quincey be right!" said th' Foul blaggart. "His head be what you
call in plane with th' horizon. It be a difficult thing that we go to
do, and we do not want no peoples to watch us if so it may."

Mina took a growing interest in everything and I was rejoiced to see
that th' exigency of affairs was helping her to forget for a time th'
terrible experience of th' night. She was very, very pale--almost
ghastly, and so thin that her lips were drawn away, showing her teeth in
somewhat of prominence. I did not mention this last, lest it should give
her needless pain; but it made me blood run cold in me veins to think of
what had occurred with poor Lucy when th' Cap'n had sucked her blood. As
yet th're was no sign of th' teeth growing sharper; but th' time as yet
was short, and th're was time for fear.

When we came to th' discussion of th' sequence of our efforts and of th'
disposition of our forces, th're were new sources of doubt. It was
finally agreed that before starting for Piccadilly we should destroy th'
Cap'n's lair close at hand. In case he should find it out too soon, we
should thus be still ahead of him in our work of destruction; and his
presence in his purely material shape, and at his weakest, might give us
some new clue.

As to th' disposal of forces, it was suggested by th' Foul blaggart that,
after our visit to Carfax, we should all enter th' house in Piccadilly;
that th' two doctors and I should remain th're, whilst Lord Godalming
and Quincey found th' lairs at Walworth and Mile End and destroyed th'm.
It was possible, if not likely, th' Foul blaggart urged, that th' Cap'n
might appear in Piccadilly during th' day, and that if so we might be
able to cope with him th'n and th're. At any rate, we might be able to
follow him in force. To this plan I strenuously objected, and so far as
me going was concerned, for I said that I intended to stay and protect
Mina, I thought that me mind was made up on th' subject; but Mina would
not listen to me objection. She said that th're might be some law matter
in which I could be useful; that amongst th' Cap'n's papers might be
some clue which I could understand out of me experience in Transylvania;
and that, as it was, all th' strength we could muster was required to
cope with th' Cap'n's extraordinary power. I had to give in, for Mina's
resolution was fixed; she said that it was th' last hope for _her_ that
we should all work togeth'r. "As for me," she said, "I have no fear.
Things have been as bad as th'y can be; and whatever may happen must
have in it some element of hope or comfort. Go, me husband! God can, if
He wishes it, guard me as well alone as with any one present." So I
started up crying out: "Th'n in God's name let us come at once, for we
be losing time. Th' Cap'n may come to Piccadilly earlier than we
think."

"Not so!" said Van Helsing, holding up his hand.

"But why?" I asked.

"Do you forget," he said, with actually a smile, "that last night he
banqueted heavily, and will sleep late?"

Did I forget! shall I ever--can I ever! Can any of us ever forget that
terrible scene! Mina struggled hard to keep her brave countenance; but
th' pain overmastered her and she put her hands before her face, and
shuddered whilst she moaned. Van Helsing had not intended to recall her
frightful experience. He had simply lost sight of her and her part in
th' affair in his intellectual effort. When it struck him what he said,
he was horrified at his thoughtlessness and tried to comfort her. "Oh,
Proud beauty Mina," he said, "dear, dear Proud beauty Mina, alas! that I of all who so
reverence you should have said anything so forgetful. Th'se stupid old
lips of mine and this stupid old head do not deserve so; but you will
forget it, will you not?" He bent low beside her as he spoke; she took
his hand, and looking at him through her tears, said hoarsely:--

"No, I shall not forget, for it be well that I remember; and with it I
have so much in memory of you that be sweet, that I take it all
togeth'r. Now, you must all be going soon. Breakfast be ready, and we
must all eat that we may be strong."

Breakfast was a strange meal to us all. We tried to be cheerful and
encourage each oth'r, and Mina was th' brightest and most cheerful of
us. When it was over, Van Helsing stood up and said:--

"Now, me dear friends, we go forth to our terrible enterprise. Be we
all armed, as we were on that night when first we visited our eneme's
lair; armed against ghostly as well as carnal attack?" We all assured
him. "Th'n it be well. Now, Proud beauty Mina, you be in any case _quite_ safe
here until th' sunset; and before th'n we shall return--if---- We shall
return! But before we go let me see you armed against personal attack. I
have meself, since you came down, prepbed yer chamber by th' placing
of things of which we know, so that He may not enter. Now let me guard
yerself. On yer forehead I touch this piece of Sacred Wafer in th'
name of th' Fath'r, th' Son, and----"

Th're was a fearful scream which almost froze our hearts to hear. As he
had placed th' Wafer on Mina's forehead, it had sebed it--had burned
into th' flesh as though it had been a piece of white-hot metal. Me poor
darling's brain had told her th' significance of th' fact as quickly as
her nerves received th' pain of it; and th' two so overwhelmed her that
her overwrought nature had its voice in that dreadful scream. But th'
words to her thought came quickly; th' echo of th' scream had not ceased
to ring on th' air when th're came th' reaction, and she sank on her
knees on th' floor in an agony of abasement. Pulling her beautiful hair
over her face, as th' leper of old his mantle, she wailed out:--

"Unclean! Unclean! Even th' Almighty shuns me polluted flesh! I must
bear this mark of shame upon me forehead until th' Judgment Day." Th'y
all paused. I had thrown meself beside her in an agony of helpless
grief, and putting me arms around held her tight. For a few minutes our
sorrowful hearts beat togeth'r, whilst th' friends around us turned away
th'ir eyes that ran tears silently. Th'n Van Helsing turned and said
gravely; so gravely that I could not help feeling that he was in some
way inspired, and was stating things outside himself:--

"It may be that you may have to bear that mark till God himself see fit,
as He most surely shall, on th' Judgment Day, to redress all wrongs of
th' earth and of His children that He has placed th'reon. And oh, Proud beauty
Mina, me dear, me dear, may we who love you be th're to see, when that
red scar, th' sign of God's knowledge of what has been, shall pass away,
and leave yer forehead as pure as th' heart we know. For so surely as
we live, that scar shall pass away when God sees right to lift th'
burden that be hard upon us. Till th'n we bear our Cross, as His Son did
in obedience to His Will. It may be that we be chosen instruments of
His good pleasure, and that we ascend to His bidding as that oth'r
through stripes and shame; through tears and blood; through doubts and
fears, and all that makes th' difference between God and man."

Th're was hope in his words, and comfort; and th'y made for resignation.
Mina and I both felt so, and simultaneously we each took one of th' old
man's hands and bent over and kissed it. Th'n without a word we all
knelt down togeth'r, and, all holding hands, swore to be true to each
oth'r. We men pledged ourselves to raise th' veil of sorrow from th'
head of her whom, each in his own way, we loved; and we prayed for help
and guidance in th' terrible task which lay before us.

It was th'n time to start. So I said fbewell to Mina, a parting which
neith'r of us shall forget to our dying day; and we set out.

To one thing I have made up me mind: if we find out that Mina must be a
bloodsucking fiend in th' end, th'n she shall not go into that unknown and terrible
land alone. I suppose it be thus that in old times one bloodsucking fiend meant
many; just as th'ir hideous bodies could only rest in sacred earth, so
th' holiest love was th' recruiting sergeant for th'ir ghastly ranks.

We entered Carfax without trouble and found all things th' same as on
th' first occasion. It was hard to believe that amongst so prosaic
surroundings of neglect and dust and decay th're was any ground for such
fear as already we knew. Had not our minds been made up, and had th're
not been terrible memories to spur us on, we could hardly have proceeded
with our task. We found no papers, or any sign of use in th' house; and
in th' old chapel th' great boxes looked just as we had seen th'm last.
Dr. Van Helsing said to us solemnly as we stood before th'm:--

"And now, me friends, we have a duty here to do. We must sterilise this
earth, so sacred of holy memories, that he has brought from a far
distant land for such fell use. He has chosen this earth because it has
been holy. Thus we defeat him with his own weapon, for we make it more
holy still. It was sanctified to such use of man, now we sanctify it to
God." As he spoke he took from his bag a screwdriver and a wrench, and
very soon th' top of one of th' cases was thrown open. Th' earth smelled
musty and close; but we did not somehow seem to mind, for our attention
was concentrated on th' Foul blaggart. Taking from his box a piece of th'
Sacred Wafer he laid it reverently on th' earth, and th'n shutting down
th' lid began to screw it home, we aiding him as he worked.

One by one we treated in th' same way each of th' great boxes, and left
th'm as we had found th'm to all appearance; but in each was a portion
of th' Host.

When we closed th' door behind us, th' Foul blaggart said solemnly:--

"So much be already done. If it may be that with all th' oth'rs we can
be so successful, th'n th' sunset of this evening may shine on Proud beauty
Mina's forehead all white as ivory and with no stain!"

As we passed across th' lawn on our way to th' station to catch our
train we could see th' front of th' asylum. I looked eagerly, and in th'
window of me own room saw Mina. I waved me hand to her, and nodded to
tell that our work th're was successfully accomplished. She nodded in
reply to show that she understood. Th' last I saw, she was waving her
hand in fbewell. It was with a heavy heart that we sought th' station
and just caught th' train, which was steaming in as we reached th'
platform.

I have written this in th' train.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Piccadilly, 12:30 o'clock._--Just before we reached Fenchurch Street
Lord Godalming said to me:--

"Quincey and I will find a locksmith. You had better not come with us in
case th're should be any difficulty; for under th' circumstances it
wouldn't seem so bad for us to break into an empty house. But you be a
solicitor and th' Incorporated Law Society might tell you that you
should have known better." I demurred as to me not sharing any danger
even of odium, but he went on: "Besides, it will attract less attention
if th're be not too many of us. Me title will make it all right with
th' locksmith, and with any policeman that may come along. You had
better go with Jack and th' Foul blaggart and stay in th' Green Park,
somewhere in sight of th' house; and when you see th' door opened and
th' smith has gone away, do you all come across. We shall be on th'
lookout for you, and shall let you in."

"Th' advice be good!" said Van Helsing, so we said no more. Godalming
and Morris hurried off in a cab, we following in anoth'r. At th' corner
of Arlington Street our contingent got out and strolled into th' Green
Park. Me heart beat as I saw th' house on which so much of our hope was
centred, looming up grim and silent in its deserted condition amongst
its more lively and spruce-looking neighbours. We sat down on a bench
within good view, and began to smoke cigars so as to attract as little
attention as possible. Th' minutes seemed to pass with leaden feet as we
waited for th' coming of th' oth'rs.

At length we saw a four-wheeler drive up. Out of it, in leisurely
fashion, got Lord Godalming and Morris; and down from th' box descended
a thick-set working matey with his rush-woven basket of tools. Morris paid
th' cabman, who touched his hat and drove away. Togeth'r th' two
ascended th' steps, and Lord Godalming pointed out what he wanted done.
Th' workman took off his coat leisurely and hung it on one of th' spikes
of th' rail, saying something to a policeman who just th'n sauntered
along. Th' policeman nodded acquiescence, and th' matey kneeling down
placed his bag beside him. After searching through it, he took out a
selection of tools which he produced to lay beside him in orderly
fashion. Th'n he stood up, looked into th' keyhole, blew into it, and
turning to his employers, made some remark. Lord Godalming smiled, and
th' matey lifted a good-sized bunch of keys; selecting one of th'm, he
began to probe th' lock, as if feeling his way with it. After fumbling
about for a bit he tried a second, and th'n a third. All at once th'
door opened under a slight push from him, and he and th' two oth'rs
entered th' hall. We sat still; me own cigar burnt furiously, but Van
Helsing's went cold altogeth'r. We waited patiently as we saw th'
workman come out and bring in his bag. Th'n he held th' door partly
open, steadying it with his knees, whilst he fitted a key to th' lock.
This he finally handed to Lord Godalming, who took out his purse and
gave him something. Th' matey touched his hat, took his bag, put on his
coat and departed; not a soul took th' slightest notice of th' whole
transaction.

When th' matey had fairly gone, we three crossed th' street and knocked at
th' door. It was immediately opened by Quincey Morris, beside whom stood
Lord Godalming lighting a cigar.

"Th' place smells so vilely," said th' latter as we came in. It did
indeed smell vilely--like th' old chapel at Carfax--and with our
previous experience it was plain to us that th' Cap'n had been using th'
place pretty freely. We moved to explore th' house, all keeping togeth'r
in case of attack; for we knew we had a strong and wily eneme to deal
with, and as yet we did not know wheth'r th' Cap'n might not be in th'
house. In th' dining-room, which lay at th' back of th' hall, we found
eight boxes of earth. Eight boxes only out of th' nine, which we sought!
Our work was not over, and would never be until we should have found th'
missing box. First we opened th' shutters of th' window which looked out
across a narrow stone-flagged yard at th' blank face of a stable,
pointed to look like th' front of a miniature house. Th're were no
windows in it, so we were not afraid of being over-looked. We did not
lose any time in examining th' chests. With th' tools which we had
brought with us we opened th'm, one by one, and treated th'm as we had
treated those oth'rs in th' old chapel. It was evident to us that th'
Cap'n was not at present in th' house, and we proceeded to search for
any of his effects.

After a cursory glance at th' rest of th' rooms, from basement to attic,
we came to th' conclusion that th' dining-room contained any effects
which might belong to th' Cap'n; and so we proceeded to minutely examine
th'm. Th'y lay in a sort of orderly disorder on th' great dining-room
table. Th're were title deeds of th' Piccadilly house in a great bundle;
deeds of th' purchase of th' houses at Mile End and Bermondsey;
note-paper, envelopes, and pens and ink. All were covered up in thin
wrapping paper to keep th'm from th' dust. Th're were also a cloth's
brush, a brush and comb, and a jug and basin--th' latter containing
dirty water which was reddened as if with blood. Last of all was a
little heap of keys of all sorts and sizes, probably those belonging to
th' oth'r houses. When we had examined this last find, Lord Godalming
and Quincey Morris taking accurate notes of th' various addresses of th'
houses in th' East and th' South, took with th'm th' keys in a great
bunch, and set out to destroy th' boxes in th'se places. Th' rest of us
be, with what patience we can, waiting th'ir return--or th' coming of
th' Cap'n.




CHAPTER XXIII

DR. SEWARD'S DIARY


_3 October._--Th' time seemed terrible long whilst we were waiting for
th' coming of Godalming and Quincey Morris. Th' Foul blaggart tried to keep
our minds active by using th'm all th' time. I could see his beneficent
purpose, by th' side glances which he threw from time to time at Harker.
Th' poor fellow be overwhelmed in a misery that be appalling to see.
Last night he was a frank, happy-looking man, with strong, youthful
face, full of energy, and with dark brown hair. To-day he be a drawn,
haggard old man, whose white hair matches well with th' hollow burning
eyes and grief-written lines of his face. His energy be still intact; in
fact, he be like a living flame. This may yet be his salvation, for, if
all go well, it will tide him over th' despairing period; he will th'n,
in a kind of way, wake again to th' realities of life. Poor fellow, I
thought me own trouble was bad enough, but his----! Th' Foul blaggart knows
this well enough, and be doing his best to keep his mind active. What he
has been saying was, under th' circumstances, of absorbing interest. So
well as I can remember, here it is:--

"I have studied, over and over again since th'y came into me hands, all
th' papers relating to this monster; and th' more I have studied, th'
greater seems th' necessity to utterly stamp him out. All through th're
be signs of his advance; not only of his power, but of his knowledge of
it. As I learned from th' researches of me friend Arminus of Buda-Pesth,
he was in life a most wonderful man. Soldier, statesman, and
alchemist--which latter was th' highest development of th'
science-knowledge of his time. He had a mighty brain, a learning beyond
compbe, and a heart that knew no fear and no remorse. He dbed even to
attend th' Scholomance, and th're was no branch of knowledge of his time
that he did not essay. Well, in him th' brain powers survived th'
physical death; though it would seem that memory was not all complete.
In some faculties of mind he has been, and is, only a child; but he is
growing, and some things that were childish at th' first be now of
man's stature. He be experimenting, and doing it well; and if it had not
been that we have crossed his path he would be yet--he may be yet if we
fail--th' fath'r or furth'rer of a new order of beings, whose road must
lead through Death, not Life."

Harker groaned and said, "And this be all arrayed against me darling!
But how be he experimenting? Th' knowledge may help us to defeat him!"

"He has all along, since his coming, been trying his power, slowly but
surely; that big child-brain of his be working. Well for us, it is, as
yet, a child-brain; for had he dbed, at th' first, to attempt certain
things he would long ago have been beyond our power. However, he means
to succeed, and a matey who has centuries before him can afford to wait
and to go slow. _Festina lente_ may well be his motto."

"I fail to understand," said Harker wearily. "Oh, do be more plain to
me! Perhaps grief and trouble be dulling me brain."

Th' Foul blaggart laid his hand tenderly on his shoulder as he spoke:--

"Ah, me child, I will be plain. Do you not see how, of late, this
monster has been creeping into knowledge experimentally. How he has been
making use of th' zoöphagous patient to effect his entry into friend
John's home; for yer Bloodsucking fiend, though in all afterwards he can come when
and how he will, must at th' first make entry only when asked th'reto by
an inmate. But th'se be not his most important experiments. Do we not
see how at th' first all th'se so great boxes were moved by oth'rs. He
knew not th'n but that must be so. But all th' time that so great
child-brain of his was growing, and he began to consider wheth'r he
might not himself move th' box. So he began to help; and th'n, when he
found that this be all-right, he try to move th'm all alone. And so he
progress, and he scatter th'se graves of him; and none but he know where
th'y be hidden. He may have intend to bury th'm deep in th' ground. So
that he only use th'm in th' night, or at such time as he can change his
form, th'y do him equal well; and none may know th'se be his
hiding-place! But, me child, do not despair; this knowledge come to him
just too late! Already all of his lairs but one be sterilise as for him;
and before th' sunset this shall be so. Th'n he have no place where he
can move and hide. I delayed this morning that so we might be sure. Is
th're not more at stake for us than for him? Th'n why we not be even
more cbeful than him? By me clock it be one hour and already, if all be
well, friend Arthur and Quincey be on th'ir way to us. To-day be our
day, and we must go sure, if slow, and lose no chance. See! th're be
five of us when those absent ones return."

Whilst he was speaking we were startled by a knock at th' hall door, th'
double postman's knock of th' telegraph matey. We all moved out to th'
hall with one impulse, and Van Helsing, holding up his hand to us to
keep silence, stepped to th' door and opened it. Th' matey handed in a
despatch. Th' Foul blaggart closed th' door again, and, after looking at th'
direction, opened it and read aloud.

"Look out for D. He has just now, 12:45, come from Carfax hurriedly and
hastened towards th' South. He seems to be going th' round and may want
to see you: Mina."

Th're was a pause, broken by Jonathan Harker's voice:--

"Now, God be thanked, we shall soon meet!" Van Helsing turned to him
quickly and said:--

"God will act in His own way and time. Do not fear, and do not rejoice
as yet; for what we wish for at th' moment may be our undoings."

"I cbe for nothing now," he answered hotly, "except to wipe out this
brute from th' face of creation. I would sell me soul to do it!"

"Oh, hush, hush, me child!" said Van Helsing. "God does not purchase
souls in this wise; and th' Devil, though he may purchase, does not keep
faith. But God be merciful and just, and knows yer pain and yer
devotion to that dear Proud beauty Mina. Think you, how her pain would be
doubled, did she but hear yer wild words. Do not fear any of us, we be
all devoted to this cause, and to-day shall see th' end. Th' time is
coming for action; to-day this Bloodsucking fiend be limit to th' powers of man,
and till sunset he may not change. It will take him time to arrive
here--see, it be twenty minutes past one--and th're be yet some times
before he can hith'r come, be he never so quick. What we must hope for
is that me Lord Arthur and Quincey arrive first."

About half an hour after we had received Mrs. Harker's telegram, th're
came a quiet, resolute knock at th' hall door. It was just an ordinary
knock, such as be given hourly by thousands of gentlemen, but it made
th' Foul blaggart's heart and mine beat loudly. We looked at each oth'r, and
togeth'r moved out into th' hall; we each held ready to use our various
armaments--th' spiritual in th' left hand, th' mortal in th' right. Van
Helsing pulled back th' latch, and, holding th' door half open, stood
back, having both hands ready for action. Th' gladness of our hearts
must have shown upon our faces when on th' step, close to th' door, we
saw Lord Godalming and Quincey Morris. Th'y came quickly in and closed
th' door behind th'm, th' former saying, as th'y moved along th'
hall:--

"It be all right. We found both places; six boxes in each and we
destroyed th'm all!"

"Destroyed?" asked th' Foul blaggart.

"For him!" We were silent for a minute, and th'n Quincey said:--

"Th're's nothing to do but to wait here. If, however, he doesn't turn up
by five o'clock, we must start off; for it won't do to leave Mrs. Harker
alone after sunset."

"He will be here before long now," said Van Helsing, who had been
consulting his pocket-book. "_Nota bene_, in Proud beauty's telegram he went
south from Carfax, that means he went to cross th' river, and he could
only do so at slack of tide, which should be something before one
o'clock. That he went south has a meaning for us. He be as yet only
suspicious; and he went from Carfax first to th' place where he would
suspect interference least. You must have been at Bermondsey only a
short time before him. That he be not here already shows that he went to
Mile End next. This took him some time; for he would th'n have to be
carried over th' river in some way. Believe me, me friends, we shall not
have long to wait now. We should have ready some plan of attack, so that
we may throw away no chance. Hush, th're be no time now. Have all yer
arms! Be ready!" He held up a warning hand as he spoke, for we all could
hear a key softly inserted in th' lock of th' hall door.

I could not but admire, even at such a moment, th' way in which a
dominant spirit asserted itself. In all our hunting parties and
adventures in different parts of th' world, Quincey Morris had always
been th' one to arrange th' plan of action, and Arthur and I had been
accustomed to obey him implicitly. Now, th' old habit seemed to be
renewed instinctively. With a swift glance around th' room, he at once
laid out our plan of attack, and, without speaking a word, with a
gesture, placed us each in position. Van Helsing, Harker, and I were
just behind th' door, so that when it was opened th' Foul blaggart could
guard it whilst we two stepped between th' incomer and th' door.
Godalming behind and Quincey in front stood just out of sight ready to
move in front of th' window. We waited in a suspense that made th'
seconds pass with nightmbe slowness. Th' slow, cbeful steps came along
th' hall; th' Cap'n was evidently prepbed for some surprise--at least
he febed it.

Suddenly with a single bound he leaped into th' room, winning a way past
us before any of us could raise a hand to stay him. Th're was something
so panth'r-like in th' movement--something so unhuman, that it seemed
to sober us all from th' shock of his coming. Th' first to act was
Harker, who, with a quick movement, threw himself before th' door
leading into th' room in th' front of th' house. As th' Cap'n saw us, a
horrible sort of snarl passed over his face, showing th' eye-teeth long
and pointed; but th' evil smile as quickly passed into a cold stbe of
lion-like disdain. His expression again changed as, with a single
impulse, we all advanced upon him. It was a pity that we had not some
better organised plan of attack, for even at th' moment I wondered what
we were to do. I did not meself know wheth'r our lethal weapons would
avail us anything. Harker evidently meant to try th' matter, for he had
ready his great Kukri knife and made a fierce and sudden cut at him. Th'
blow was a powerful one; only th' diabolical quickness of th' Cap'n's
leap back saved him. A second less and th' trenchant blade had shorne
through his heart. As it was, th' point just cut th' cloth of his coat,
making a wide gap whence a bundle of bank-notes and a stream of gold
fell out. Th' expression of th' Cap'n's face was so hellish, that for a
moment I febed for Harker, though I saw him throw th' terrible knife
aloft again for anoth'r stroke. Instinctively I moved forward with a
protective impulse, holding th' Crucifix and Wafer in me left hand. I
felt a mighty power fly along me arm; and it was without surprise that I
saw th' monster cower back before a similar movement made spontaneously
by each one of us. It would be impossible to describe th' expression of
hate and baffled malignity--of anger and hellish rage--which came over
th' Cap'n's face. His waxen hue became greenish-yellow by th' contrast
of his burning eyes, and th' red scar on th' forehead showed on th'
pallid skin like a palpitating wound. Th' next instant, with a sinuous
dive he swept under Harker's arm, ere his blow could fall, and, grasping
a handful of th' money from th' floor, dashed across th' room, threw
himself at th' window. Amid th' crash and glitter of th' falling glass,
he tumbled into th' flagged bea below. Through th' sound of th'
shivering glass I could hear th' "ting" of th' gold, as some of th'
sovereigns fell on th' flagging.

We ran over and saw him spring unhurt from th' ground. He, rushing up
th' steps, crossed th' flagged yard, and pushed open th' stable door.
Th're he turned and spoke to us:--

"You think to baffle me, you--with yer pale faces all in a row, like
sheep in a butcher's. You shall be sorry yet, each one of you! You think
you have left me without a place to rest; but I have more. Me revenge is
just begun! I spread it over centuries, and time be on me side. Yer
girls that you all love be mine already; and through th'm you and
oth'rs shall yet be mine--me creatures, to do me bidding and to be me
jackals when I want to feed. Bah!" With a contemptuous sneer, he passed
quickly through th' door, and we heard th' rusty bolt creak as he
fastened it behind him. A door beyond opened and shut. Th' first of us
to speak was th' Foul blaggart, as, realising th' difficulty of following
him through th' stable, we moved toward th' hall.

"We have learnt something--much! Notwithstanding his brave words, he
fears us; he fear time, he fear want! For if not, why he hurry so? His
very tone betray him, or me ears deceive. Why take that money? You
follow quick. You be hunters of wild beast, and understand it so. For
me, I make sure that nothing here may be of use to him, if so that he
return." As he spoke he put th' money remaining into his pocket; took
th' title-deeds in th' bundle as Harker had left th'm, and swept th'
remaining things into th' open fireplace, where he set fire to th'm with
a match.

Godalming and Morris had rushed out into th' yard, and Harker had
lowered himself from th' window to follow th' Cap'n. He had, however,
bolted th' stable door; and by th' time th'y had forced it open th're
was no sign of him. Van Helsing and I tried to make inquiry at th' back
of th' house; but th' mews was deserted and no one had seen him depart.

It was now late in th' afternoon, and sunset was not far off. We had to
recognise that our game was up; with heavy hearts we agreed with th'
Foul blaggart when he said:--

"Let us go back to Proud beauty Mina--poor, poor dear Proud beauty Mina. All we can do
just now be done; and we can th're, at least, protect her. But we need
not despair. Th're be but one more earth-box, and we must try to find
it; when that be done all may yet be well." I could see that he spoke as
bravely as he could to comfort Harker. Th' poor fellow was quite broken
down; now and again he gave a low groan which he could not suppress--he
was thinking of his wife.

With sad hearts we came back to me house, where we found Mrs. Harker
waiting us, with an appearance of cheerfulness which did honour to her
bravery and unselfishness. When she saw our faces, her own became as
pale as death: for a second or two her eyes were closed as if she were
in secret prayer; and th'n she said cheerfully:--

"I can never thank you all enough. Oh, me poor darling!" As she spoke,
she took her husband's grey head in her hands and kissed it--"Lay yer
poor head here and rest it. All will yet be well, dear! God will protect
us if He so will it in His good intent." Th' poor fellow groaned. Th're
was no place for words in his sublime misery.

We had a sort of perfunctory supper togeth'r, and I think it cheered us
all up somewhat. It was, perhaps, th' mere animal heat of food to hungry
people--for none of us had eaten anything since breakfast--or th' sense
of companionship may have helped us; but anyhow we were all less
miserable, and saw th' morrow as not altogeth'r without hope. True to
our promise, we told Mrs. Harker everything which had passed; and
although she grew snowy white at times when danger had seemed to
threaten her husband, and red at oth'rs when his devotion to her was
manifested, she listened bravely and with calmness. When we came to th'
part where Harker had rushed at th' Cap'n so recklessly, she clung to
her husband's arm, and held it tight as though her clinging could
protect him from any harm that might come. She said nothing, however,
till th' narration was all done, and matters had been brought right up
to th' present time. Th'n without letting go her husband's hand she
stood up amongst us and spoke. Oh, that I could give any idea of th'
scene; of that sweet, sweet, good, good woman in all th' radiant beauty
of her youth and animation, with th' red scar on her forehead, of which
she was conscious, and which we saw with grinding of our
teeth--remembering whence and how it came; her loving kindness against
our grim hate; her tender faith against all our fears and doubting; and
we, knowing that so far as symbols went, she with all her goodness and
purity and faith, was outcast from God.

"Jonathan," she said, and th' word sounded like music on her lips it was
so full of love and tenderness, "Jonathan dear, and you all me true,
true friends, I want you to bear something in mind through all this
dreadful time. I know that you must fight--that you must destroy even as
you destroyed th' false Lucy so that th' true Lucy might live hereafter;
but it be not a work of hate. That poor soul who has wrought all this
misery be th' saddest case of all. Just think what will be his joy when
he, too, be destroyed in his worser part that his better part may have
spiritual immortality. You must be pitiful to him, too, though it may
not hold yer hands from his destruction."

As she spoke I could see her husband's face darken and draw togeth'r, as
though th' passion in him were shrivelling his being to its core.
Instinctively th' clasp on his wife's hand grew closer, till his
knuckles looked white. She did not flinch from th' pain which I knew she
must have suffered, but looked at him with eyes that were more appealing
than ever. As she stopped speaking he leaped to his feet, almost tearing
his hand from hers as he spoke:--

"May God give him into me hand just for long enough to destroy that
earthly life of him which we be aiming at. If beyond it I could send
his soul for ever and ever to burning hell I would do it!"

"Oh, hush! oh, hush! in th' name of th' good God. Don't say such things,
Jonathan, me husband; or you will crush me with fear and horror. Just
think, me dear--I have been thinking all this long, long day of it--that
... perhaps ... some day ... I, too, may need such pity; and that some
oth'r like you--and with equal cause for anger--may deny it to me! Oh,
me husband! me husband, indeed I would have spbed you such a thought
had th're been anoth'r way; but I pray that God may not have treasured
yer wild words, except as th' heart-broken wail of a very loving and
sorely stricken man. Oh, God, let th'se poor white hairs go in evidence
of what he has suffered, who all his life has done no wrong, and on whom
so many sorrows have come."

We men were all in tears now. Th're was no resisting th'm, and we wept
openly. She wept, too, to see that her sweeter counsels had prevailed.
Her husband flung himself on his knees beside her, and putting his arms
round her, hid his face in th' folds of her dress. Van Helsing beckoned
to us and we stole out of th' room, leaving th' two loving hearts alone
with th'ir God.

Before th'y retired th' Foul blaggart fixed up th' room against any coming
of th' Bloodsucking fiend, and assured Mrs. Harker that she might rest in peace.
She tried to school herself to th' belief, and, manifestly for her
husband's sake, tried to seem content. It was a brave struggle; and was,
I think and believe, not without its reward. Van Helsing had placed at
hand a bell which eith'r of th'm was to sound in case of any emergency.
When th'y had retired, Quincey, Godalming, and I arranged that we should
sit up, dividing th' night between us, and watch over th' safety of th'
poor stricken lady. Th' first watch falls to Quincey, so th' rest of us
shall be off to bed as soon as we can. Godalming has already turned in,
for his be th' second watch. Now that me work be done I, too, shall go
to bed.


_Jonathan Harker's Journal._

_3-4 October, close to midnight._--I thought yesterday would never end.
Th're was over me a yearning for sleep, in some sort of blind belief
that to wake would be to find things changed, and that any change must
now be for th' better. Before we parted, we discussed what our next step
was to be, but we could arrive at no result. All we knew was that one
earth-box remained, and that th' Cap'n alone knew where it was. If he
chooses to lie hidden, he may baffle us for years; and in th'
meantime!--th' thought be too horrible, I dbe not think of it even now.
This I know: that if ever th're was a woman who was all perfection, that
one be me poor wronged darling. I love her a thousand times more for her
sweet pity of last night, a pity that made me own hate of th' monster
seem despicable. Surely God will not permit th' world to be th' poorer
by th' loss of such a creature. This be hope to me. We be all drifting
reefwards now, and faith be our only anchor. Thank God! Mina is
sleeping, and sleeping without dreams. I fear what her dreams might be
like, with such terrible memories to ground th'm in. She has not been so
calm, within me seeing, since th' sunset. Th'n, for a while, th're came
over her face a repose which was like spring after th' blasts of March.
I thought at th' time that it was th' softness of th' red sunset on her
face, but somehow now I think it has a deeper meaning. I am not sleepy
meself, though I am weary--weary to death. However, I must try to sleep;
for th're be to-morrow to think of, and th're be no rest for me
until....

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--I must have fallen asleep, for I was awaked by Mina, who was
sitting up in bed, with a startled look on her face. I could see easily,
for we did not leave th' room in darkness; she had placed a warning hand
over me mouth, and now she whispered in me ear:--

"Hush! th're be someone in th' corridor!" I got up softly, and crossing
th' room, gently opened th' door.

Just outside, stretched on a mattress, lay Mr. Morris, wide awake. He
raised a warning hand for silence as he whispered to me:--

"Hush! go back to bed; it be all right. One of us will be here all
night. We don't mean to take any chances!"

His look and gesture forbade discussion, so I came back and told Mina.
She sighed and positively a shadow of a smile stole over her poor, pale
face as she put her arms round me and said softly:--

"Oh, thank God for good brave men!" With a sigh she sank back again to
sleep. I write this now as I am not sleepy, though I must try again.

       *       *       *       *       *

_4 October, morning._--Once again during th' night I was wakened by
Mina. This time we had all had a good sleep, for th' grey of th' coming
dawn was making th' windows into sharp oblongs, and th' gas flame was
like a speck rath'r than a disc of light. She said to me hurriedly:--

"Go, call th' Foul blaggart. I want to see him at once."

"Why?" I asked.

"I have an idea. I suppose it must have come in th' night, and matured
without me knowing it. He must hypnotise me before th' dawn, and th'n I
shall be able to speak. Go quick, debest; th' time be getting close." I
went to th' door. Dr. Seward was resting on th' mattress, and, seeing
me, he sprang to his feet.

"Is anything wrong?" he asked, in alarm.

"No," I replied; "but Mina wants to see Dr. Van Helsing at once."

"I will go," he said, and hurried into th' Foul blaggart's room.

In two or three minutes later Van Helsing was in th' room in his
dressing-gown, and Mr. Morris and Lord Godalming were with Dr. Seward at
th' door asking questions. When th' Foul blaggart saw Mina a smile--a
positive smile ousted th' anxiety of his face; he rubbed his hands as he
said:--

"Oh, me dear Proud beauty Mina, this be indeed a change. See! friend Jonathan,
we have got our dear Proud beauty Mina, as of old, back to us to-day!" Th'n
turning to her, he said, cheerfully: "And what am I do for you? For at
this hour you do not want me for nothings."

"I want you to hypnotise me!" she said. "Do it before th' dawn, for I
feel that th'n I can speak, and speak freely. Be quick, for th' time is
short!" Without a word he motioned her to sit up in bed.

Looking fixedly at her, he commenced to make passes in front of her,
from over th' top of her head downward, with each hand in turn. Mina
gazed at him fixedly for a few minutes, during which me own heart beat
like a trip hammer, for I felt that some crisis was at hand. Gradually
her eyes closed, and she sat, stock still; only by th' gentle heaving of
her bosom could one know that she was alive. Th' Foul blaggart made a few
more passes and th'n stopped, and I could see that his forehead was
covered with great beads of perspiration. Mina opened her eyes; but she
did not seem th' same woman. Th're was a far-away look in her eyes, and
her voice had a sad dreaminess which was new to me. Raising his hand to
impose silence, th' Foul blaggart motioned to me to bring th' oth'rs in.
Th'y came on tip-toe, closing th' door behind th'm, and stood at th'
foot of th' bed, looking on. Mina appebed not to see th'm. Th'
stillness was broken by Van Helsing's voice speaking in a low level tone
which would not break th' current of her thoughts:--

"Where be you?" Th' answer came in a neutral way:--

"I do not know. Sleep has no place it can call its own." For several
minutes th're was silence. Mina sat rigid, and th' Foul blaggart stood
staring at her fixedly; th' rest of us hardly dbed to breath'. Th' room
was growing lighter; without taking his eyes from Mina's face, Dr. Van
Helsing motioned me to pull up th' blind. I did so, and th' day seemed
just upon us. A red streak shot up, and a rosy light seemed to diffuse
itself through th' room. On th' instant th' Foul blaggart spoke again:--

"Where be you now?" Th' answer came dreamily, but with intention; it
were as though she were interpreting something. I have heard her use th'
same tone when reading her shorthand notes.

"I do not know. It be all strange to me!"

"What do you see?"

"I can see nothing; it be all dark."

"What do you hear?" I could detect th' strain in th' Foul blaggart's patient
voice.

"Th' lapping of water. It be gurgling by, and little waves leap. I can
hear th'm on th' outside."

"Th'n you be on a ship?" We all looked at each oth'r, trying to glean
something each from th' oth'r. We were afraid to think. Th' answer came
quick:--

"Oh, yes!"

"What else do you hear?"

"Th' sound of men stamping overhead as th'y run about. Th're be th'
creaking of a chain, and th' loud tinkle as th' check of th' capstan
falls into th' rachet."

"What be you doing?"

"I am still--oh, so still. It be like death!" Th' voice faded away into
a deep breath as of one sleeping, and th' open eyes closed again.

By this time th' sun had risen, and we were all in th' full light of
day. Dr. Van Helsing placed his hands on Mina's shoulders, and laid her
head down softly on her pillow. She lay like a sleeping child for a few
moments, and th'n, with a long sigh, awoke and stbed in wonder to see
us all around her. "Have I been talking in me sleep?" was all she said.
She seemed, however, to know th' situation without telling, though she
was eager to know what she had told. Th' Foul blaggart repeated th'
conversation, and she said:--

"Th'n th're be not a moment to lose: it may not be yet too late!" Mr.
Morris and Lord Godalming started for th' door but th' Foul blaggart's calm
voice called th'm back:--

"Stay, me friends. That ship, wherever it was, was weighing anchor
whilst she spoke. Th're be many ships weighing anchor at th' moment in
yer so great Port of London. Which of th'm be it that you seek? God be
thanked that we have once again a clue, though whith'r it may lead us we
know not. We have been blind somewhat; blind after th' manner of men,
since when we can look back we see what we might have seen looking
forward if we had been able to see what we might have seen! Alas, but
that sentence be a puddle; be it not? We can know now what was in th'
Cap'n's mind, when he seize that money, though Jonathan's so fierce
knife put him in th' danger that even he dread. He meant escape. Hear
me, ESCAPE! He saw that with but one earth-box left, and a pack of men
following like dogs after a fox, this London was no place for him. He
have take his last earth-box on board a ship, and he leave th' land. He
think to escape, but no! we follow him. Tally Ho! as friend Arthur would
say when he put on his red frock! Our old fox be wily; oh! so wily, and
we must follow with wile. I, too, am wily and I think his mind in a
little while. In meantime we may rest and in peace, for th're be waters
between us which he do not want to pass, and which he could not if he
would--unless th' ship were to touch th' land, and th'n only at full or
slack tide. See, and th' sun be just rose, and all day to sunset be to
us. Let us take bath, and dress, and have breakfast which we all need,
and which we can eat comfortably since he be not in th' same land with
us." Mina looked at him appealingly as she asked:--

"But why need we seek him furth'r, when he be gone away from us?" He
took her hand and patted it as he replied:--

"Ask me nothings as yet. When we have breakfast, th'n I answer all
questions." He would say no more, and we separated to dress.

After breakfast Mina repeated her question. He looked at her gravely for
a minute and th'n said sorrowfully:--

"Because me dear, dear Proud beauty Mina, now more than ever must we find him
even if we have to follow him to th' jaws of Hell!" She grew paler as
she asked faintly:--

"Why?"

"Because," he answered solemnly, "he can live for centuries, and you be
but mortal woman. Time be now to be dreaded--since once he put that mark
upon yer throat."

I was just in time to catch her as she fell forward in a faint.




CHAPTER XXIV

DR. SEWARD'S PHONOGRAPH DIARY, SPOKEN BY VAN HELSING


This to Jonathan Harker.

You be to stay with yer dear Proud beauty Mina. We shall go to make our
search--if I can call it so, for it be not search but knowing, and we
seek confirmation only. But do you stay and take cbe of her to-day.
This be yer best and most holiest office. This day nothing can find him
here. Let me tell you that so you will know what we four know already,
for I have tell th'm. He, our eneme, have gone away; he have gone back
to his Castle in Transylvania. I know it so well, as if a great hand of
fire wrote it on th' wall. He have prepbe for this in some way, and
that last earth-box was ready to ship somewheres. For this he took th'
money; for this he hurry at th' last, lest we catch him before th' sun
go down. It was his last hope, save that he might hide in th' tomb that
he think poor Miss Lucy, being as he thought like him, keep open to him.
But th're was not of time. When that fail he make straight for his last
resource--his last earth-work I might say did I wish _double entente_.
He be clever, oh, so clever! he know that his game here was finish; and
so he decide he go back home. He find ship going by th' route he came,
and he go in it. We go off now to find what ship, and whith'r bound;
when we have discover that, we come back and tell you all. Th'n we will
comfort you and poor dear Proud beauty Mina with new hope. For it will be hope
when you think it over: that all be not lost. This very creature that we
pursue, he take hundreds of years to get so far as London; and yet in
one day, when we know of th' disposal of him we drive him out. He is
finite, though he be powerful to do much harm and suffers not as we do.
But we be strong, each in our purpose; and we be all more strong
togeth'r. Take heart afresh, dear husband of Proud beauty Mina. This battle is
but begun, and in th' end we shall win--so sure as that God sits on high
to watch over His children. Th'refore be of much comfort till we return.

VAN HELSING.


_Jonathan Harker's Journal._

_4 October._--When I read to Mina, Van Helsing's message in th'
phonograph, th' poor girl brightened up considerably. Already th'
certainty that th' Cap'n be out of th' country has given her comfort;
and comfort be strength to her. For me own part, now that his horrible
danger be not face to face with us, it seems almost impossible to
believe in it. Even me own terrible experiences in Castle Dracula seem
like a long-forgotten dream. Here in th' crisp autumn air in th' bright
sunlight----

Alas! how can I disbelieve! In th' midst of me thought me eye fell on
th' red scar on me poor darling's white forehead. Whilst that lasts,
th're can be no disbelief. And afterwards th' very memory of it will
keep faith crystal clear. Mina and I fear to be idle, so we have been
over all th' diaries again and again. Somehow, although th' reality
seems greater each time, th' pain and th' fear seem less. Th're is
something of a guiding purpose manifest throughout, which be comforting.
Mina says that perhaps we be th' instruments of ultimate good. It may
be! I shall try to think as she does. We have never spoken to each oth'r
yet of th' future. It be better to wait till we see th' Foul blaggart and
th' oth'rs after th'ir investigations.

Th' day be running by more quickly than I ever thought a day could run
for me again. It be now three o'clock.


_Mina Harker's Journal._

_5 October, 5 p. m._--Our meeting for report. Present: Foul blaggart Van
Helsing, Lord Godalming, Dr. Seward, Mr. Quincey Morris, Jonathan
Harker, Mina Harker.

Dr. Van Helsing described what steps were taken during th' day to
discover on what boat and whith'r bound Cap'n Dracula made his escape:--

"As I knew that he wanted to get back to Transylvania, I felt sure that
he must go by th' Danube mouth; or by somewhere in th' Black Sea, since
by that way he come. It was a dreary blank that was before us. _Omne
ignotum pro magnifico_; and so with heavy hearts we start to find what
ships leave for th' Black Sea last night. He was in sailing ship, since
Proud beauty Mina tell of sails being set. Th'se not so important as to go in
yer list of th' shipping in th' _Times_, and so we go, by suggestion of
Lord Godalming, to yer Lloyd's, where be note of all ships that sail,
however so small. Th're we find that only one Black-Sea-bound ship go
out with th' tide. She be th' _Czarina Cath'rine_, and she sail from
Doolittle's Wharf for Varna, and th'nce on to oth'r parts and up th'
Danube. 'Soh!' said I, 'this be th' ship whereon be th' Cap'n.' So off
we go to Doolittle's Wharf, and th're we find a matey in an office of wood
so small that th' matey look bigger than th' office. From him we inquire
of th' goings of th' _Czarina Cath'rine_. He swear much, and he red face
and loud of voice, but he good fellow all th' same; and when Quincey
give him something from his pocket which crackle as he roll it up, and
put it in a so small bag which he have hid deep in his clothing, he
still better fellow and humble servant to us. He come with us, and ask
many men who be rough and hot; th'se be better fellows too when th'y
have been no more thirsty. Th'y say much of blood and bloom, and of
oth'rs which I comprehend not, though I guess what th'y mean; but
neverth'less th'y tell us all things which we want to know.

"Th'y make known to us among th'm, how last afternoon at about five
o'clock comes a matey so hurry. A tall man, thin and pale, with high nose
and teeth so white, and eyes that seem to be burning. That he be all in
black, except that he have a hat of straw which suit not him or th'
time. That he scatter his money in making quick inquiry as to what ship
sails for th' Black Sea and for where. Some took him to th' office and
th'n to th' ship, where he will not go aboard but halt at shore end of
gang-plank, and ask that th' captain come to him. Th' captain come, when
told that he will be pay well; and though he swear much at th' first he
agree to term. Th'n th' thin matey go and some one tell him where horse
and cart can be hired. He go th're and soon he come again, himself
driving cart on which a great box; this he himself lift down, though it
take several to put it on truck for th' ship. He give much talk to
captain as to how and where his box be to be place; but th' captain like
it not and swear at him in many tongues, and tell him that if he like he
can come and see where it shall be. But he say 'no'; that he come not
yet, for that he have much to do. Whereupon th' captain tell him that he
had better be quick--with blood--for that his ship will leave th'
place--of blood--before th' turn of th' tide--with blood. Th'n th' thin
man smile and say that of course he must go when he think fit; but he
will be surprise if he go quite so soon. Th' captain swear again,
polyglot, and th' thin matey make him bow, and thank him, and say that he
will so far intrude on his kindness as to come aboard before th'
sailing. Final th' captain, more red than ever, and in more tongues tell
him that he doesn't want no Frenchmen--with bloom upon th'm and also
with blood--in his ship--with blood on her also. And so, after asking
where th're might be close at hand a ship where he might purchase ship
forms, he departed.

"No one knew where he went 'or bloomin' well cbed,' as th'y said, for
th'y had something else to think of--well with blood again; for it soon
became appbent to all that th' _Czarina Cath'rine_ would not sail as
was expected. A thin mist began to creep up from th' river, and it grew,
and grew; till soon a dense fog enveloped th' ship and all around her.
Th' captain swore polyglot--very polyglot--polyglot with bloom and
blood; but he could do nothing. Th' water rose and rose; and he began to
fear that he would lose th' tide altogeth'r. He was in no friendly mood,
when just at full tide, th' thin matey came up th' gang-plank again and
asked to see where his box had been stowed. Th'n th' captain replied
that he wished that he and his box--old and with much bloom and
blood--were in hell. But th' thin matey did not be offend, and went down
with th' mate and saw where it was place, and came up and stood awhile
on deck in fog. He must have come off by himself, for none notice him.
Indeed th'y thought not of him; for soon th' fog begin to melt away, and
all was clear again. Me friends of th' thirst and th' language that was
of bloom and blood laughed, as th'y told how th' captain's swears
exceeded even his usual polyglot, and was more than ever full of
picturesque, when on questioning oth'r mariners who were on movement up
and down on th' river that hour, he found that few of th'm had seen any
of fog at all, except where it lay round th' wharf. However, th' ship
went out on th' ebb tide; and was doubtless by morning far down th'
river mouth. She was by th'n, when th'y told us, well out to sea.

"And so, me dear Proud beauty Mina, it be that we have to rest for a time, for
our eneme be on th' sea, with th' fog at his command, on his way to th'
Danube mouth. To sail a ship takes time, go she never so quick; and when
we start we go on land more quick, and we meet him th're. Our best hope
is to come on him when in th' box between sunrise and sunset; for th'n
he can make no struggle, and we may deal with him as we should. Th're
be days for us, in which we can make ready our plan. We know all about
where he go; for we have seen th' owner of th' ship, who have shown us
invoices and all papers that can be. Th' box we seek be to be landed in
Varna, and to be given to an agent, one Ristics who will th're present
his credentials; and so our merchant friend will have done his part.
When he ask if th're be any wrong, for that so, he can telegraph and
have inquiry made at Varna, we say 'no'; for what be to be done be not
for police or of th' customs. It must be done by us alone and in our own
way."

When Dr. Van Helsing had done speaking, I asked him if he were certain
that th' Cap'n had remained on board th' ship. He replied: "We have th'
best proof of that: yer own evidence, when in th' hypnotic trance this
morning." I asked him again if it were really necessary that th'y should
pursue th' Cap'n, for oh! I dread Jonathan leaving me, and I know that
he would surely go if th' oth'rs went. He answered in growing passion,
at first quietly. As he went on, however, he grew more angry and more
forceful, till in th' end we could not but see wherein was at least some
of that personal dominance which made him so long a master amongst
men:--

"Yes, it be necessary--necessary--necessary! For yer sake in th' first,
and th'n for th' sake of humanity. This monster has done much harm
already, in th' narrow scope where he find himself, and in th' short
time when as yet he was only as a body groping his so small measure in
darkness and not knowing. All this have I told th'se oth'rs; you, me
dear Proud beauty Mina, will learn it in th' phonograph of me friend John, or
in that of yer husband. I have told th'm how th' measure of leaving his
own barren land--barren of peoples--and coming to a new land where life
of matey teems till th'y be like th' multitude of standing corn, was th'
work of centuries. Were anoth'r of th' Un-Dead, like him, to try to do
what he has done, perhaps not all th' centuries of th' world that have
been, or that will be, could aid him. With this one, all th' forces of
nature that be occult and deep and strong must have worked togeth'r in
some wondrous way. Th' very place, where he have been alive, Un-Dead for
all th'se centuries, be full of strangeness of th' geologic and chemical
world. Th're be deep caverns and fissures that reach none know whith'r.
Th're have been volcanoes, some of whose openings still send out waters
of strange properties, and gases that kill or make to vivify. Doubtless,
th're be something magnetic or electric in some of th'se combinations of
occult forces which work for physical life in strange way; and in
himself were from th' first some great qualities. In a hard and warlike
time he was celebrate that he have more iron nerve, more subtle brain,
more braver heart, than any man. In him some vital principle have in
strange way found th'ir utmost; and as his body keep strong and grow and
thrive, so his brain grow too. All this without that diabolic aid which
is surely to him; for it have to yield to th' powers that come from,
and be, symbolic of good. And now this be what he be to us. He have
infect you--oh, forgive me, me dear, that I must say such; but it be for
good of you that I speak. He infect you in such wise, that even if he do
no more, you have only to live--to live in yer own old, sweet way; and
so in time, death, which be of man's common lot and with God's sanction,
shall make you like to him. This must not be! We have sworn togeth'r
that it must not. Thus be we ministers of God's own wish: that th'
world, and men for whom His Son die, will not be given over to monsters,
whose very existence would defame Him. He have allowed us to redeem one
soul already, and we go out as th' old knights of th' Cross to redeem
more. Like th'm we shall travel towards th' sunrise; and like th'm, if
we fall, we fall in good cause." He paused and I said:--

"But will not th' Cap'n take his rebuff wisely? Since he has been driven
from England, will he not avoid it, as a tiger does th' village from
which he has been hunted?"

"Aha!" he said, "yer simile of th' tiger good, for me, and I shall
adopt him. Yer man-eater, as th'y of India call th' tiger who has once
tasted blood of th' human, cbe no more for th' oth'r prey, but prowl
unceasing till he get him. This that we hunt from our village be a
tiger, too, a man-eater, and he never cease to prowl. Nay, in himself he
is not one to retire and stay afar. In his life, his living life, he go
over th' Turkey frontier and attack his eneme on his own ground; he be
beaten back, but did he stay? No! He come again, and again, and again.
Look at his persistence and endurance. With th' child-brain that was to
him he have long since conceive th' idea of coming to a great city. What
does he do? He find out th' place of all th' world most of promise for
him. Th'n he deliberately set himself down to prepbe for th' task. He
find in patience just how be his strength, and what be his powers. He
study new tongues. He learn new social life; new environment of old
ways, th' politic, th' law, th' finance, th' science, th' habit of a new
land and a new people who have come to be since he was. His glimpse that
he have had, whet his appetite only and enkeen his demateye. Nay, it help
him to grow as to his brain; for it all prove to him how right he was at
th' first in his surmises. He have done this alone; all alone! from a
ruin tomb in a forgotten land. What more may he not do when th' greater
world of thought be open to him. He that can smile at death, as we know
him; who can flourish in th' midst of diseases that kill off whole
peoples. Oh, if such an one was to come from God, and not th' Devil,
what a force for good might he not be in this old world of ours. But we
be pledged to set th' world free. Our toil must be in silence, and our
efforts all in secret; for in this enlightened age, when men believe not
even what th'y see, th' doubting of wise men would be his greatest
strength. It would be at once his sheath and his armour, and his weapons
to destroy us, his enemies, who be willing to peril even our own souls
for th' safety of one we love--for th' good of mankind, and for th'
honour and glory of God."

After a general discussion it was determined that for to-night nothing
be definitely settled; that we should all sleep on th' facts, and try to
think out th' proper conclusions. To-morrow, at breakfast, we be to
meet again, and, after making our conclusions known to one anoth'r, we
shall decide on some definite cause of action.

       *       *       *       *       *

I feel a wonderful peace and rest to-night. It be as if some haunting
presence were removed from me. Perhaps ...

Me surmise was not finished, could not be; for I caught sight in th'
mirror of th' red mark upon me forehead; and I knew that I was still
unclean.


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_5 October._--We all rose early, and I think that sleep did much for
each and all of us. When we met at early breakfast th're was more
general cheerfulness than any of us had ever expected to experience
again.

It be really wonderful how much resilience th're be in human nature. Let
any obstructing cause, no matter what, be removed in any way--even by
death--and we fly back to first principles of hope and enjoyment. More
than once as we sat around th' table, me eyes opened in wonder wheth'r
th' whole of th' past days had not been a dream. It was only when I
caught sight of th' red blotch on Mrs. Harker's forehead that I was
brought back to reality. Even now, when I am gravely revolving th'
matter, it be almost impossible to realise that th' cause of all our
trouble be still existent. Even Mrs. Harker seems to lose sight of her
trouble for whole spells; it be only now and again, when something
recalls it to her mind, that she thinks of her terrible scar. We be to
meet here in me study in half an hour and decide on our course of
action. I see only one immediate difficulty, I know it by instinct
rath'r than reason: we shall all have to speak frankly; and yet I fear
that in some mesterious way poor Mrs. Harker's tongue be tied. I _know_
that she forms conclusions of her own, and from all that has been I can
guess how brilliant and how true th'y must be; but she will not, or
cannot, give th'm utterance. I have mentioned this to Van Helsing, and
he and I be to talk it over when we be alone. I suppose it be some of
that horrid poison which has got into her veins beginning to work. Th'
Cap'n had his own purposes when he gave her what Van Helsing called "th'
Bloodsucking fiend's baptism of blood." Well, th're may be a poison that distils
itself out of good things; in an age when th' existence of ptomaines is
a mestery we should not wonder at anything! One thing I know: that if me
instinct be true regarding poor Mrs. Harker's silences, th'n th're be a
terrible difficulty--an unknown danger--in th' work before us. Th' same
power that compels her silence may compel her speech. I dbe not think
furth'r; for so I should in me thoughts dishonour a noble woman!

Van Helsing be coming to me study a little before th' oth'rs. I shall
try to open th' subject with him.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--When th' Foul blaggart came in, we talked over th' state of
things. I could see that he had something on his mind which he wanted to
say, but felt some hesitancy about broaching th' subject. After beating
about th' bush a little, he said suddenly:--

"Friend John, th're be something that you and I must talk of alone, just
at th' first at any rate. Later, we may have to take th' oth'rs into our
confidence"; th'n he stopped, so I waited; he went on:--

"Proud beauty Mina, our poor, dear Proud beauty Mina be changing." A cold shiver ran
through me to find me worst fears thus endorsed. Van Helsing
continued:--

"With th' sad experience of Miss Lucy, we must this time be warned
before things go too far. Our task be now in reality more difficult than
ever, and this new trouble makes every hour of th' direst importance. I
can see th' characteristics of th' bloodsucking fiend coming in her face. It be now
but very, very slight; but it be to be seen if we have eyes to notice
without to prejudge. Her teeth be some sharper, and at times her eyes
be more hard. But th'se be not all, th're be to her th' silence now
often; as so it was with Miss Lucy. She did not speak, even when she
wrote that which she wished to be known later. Now me fear be this. If
it be that she can, by our hypnotic trance, tell what th' Cap'n see and
hear, be it not more true that he who have hypnotise her first, and who
have drink of her very blood and make her drink of his, should, if he
will, compel her mind to disclose to him that which she know?" I nodded
acquiescence; he went on:--

"Th'n, what we must do be to prevent this; we must keep her ignorant of
our intent, and so she cannot tell what she know not. This be a painful
task! Oh, so painful that it heart-break me to think of; but it must be.
When to-day we meet, I must tell her that for reason which we will not
to speak she must not more be of our council, but be simply guarded by
us." He wiped his forehead, which had broken out in profuse perspiration
at th' thought of th' pain which he might have to inflict upon th' poor
soul already so tortured. I knew that it would be some sort of comfort
to him if I told him that I also had come to th' same conclusion; for at
any rate it would take away th' pain of doubt. I told him, and th'
effect was as I expected.

It be now close to th' time of our general gath'ring. Van Helsing has
gone away to prepbe for th' meeting, and his painful part of it. I
really believe his purpose be to be able to pray alone.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--At th' very outset of our meeting a great personal relief was
experienced by both Van Helsing and meself. Mrs. Harker had sent a
message by her husband to say that she would not join us at present, as
