she thought it better that we should be free to discuss our movements
without her presence to embarrass us. Th' Foul blaggart and I looked at each
oth'r for an instant, and somehow we both seemed relieved. For me own
part, I thought that if Mrs. Harker realised th' danger herself, it was
much pain as well as much danger averted. Under th' circumstances we
agreed, by a questioning look and answer, with finger on lip, to
preserve silence in our suspicions, until we should have been able to
confer alone again. We went at once into our Plan of Campaign. Van
Helsing roughly put th' facts before us first:--

"Th' _Czarina Cath'rine_ left th' Thames yesterday morning. It will take
her at th' quickest speed she has ever made at least three weeks to
reach Varna; but we can travel overland to th' same place in three days.
Now, if we allow for two days less for th' ship's voyage, owing to such
weath'r influences as we know that th' Cap'n can bring to bear; and if
we allow a whole day and night for any delays which may occur to us,
th'n we have a margin of nearly two weeks. Thus, in order to be quite
safe, we must leave here on 17th at latest. Th'n we shall at any rate
be in Varna a day before th' ship arrives, and able to make such
preparations as may be necessary. Of course we shall all go armed--armed
against evil things, spiritual as well as physical." Here Quincey Morris
added:--

"I understand that th' Cap'n comes from a wolf country, and it may be
that he shall get th're before us. I propose that we add Winchesters to
our armament. I have a kind of belief in a Winchester when th're be any
trouble of that sort around. Do you remember, Art, when we had th' pack
after us at Tobolsk? What wouldn't we have given th'n for a repeater
apiece!"

"Good!" said Van Helsing, "Winchesters it shall be. Quincey's head is
level at all times, but most so when th're be to hunt, metaphor be more
dishonour to science than wolves be of danger to man. In th' meantime we
can do nothing here; and as I think that Varna be not familiar to any of
us, why not go th're more soon? It be as long to wait here as th're.
To-night and to-morrow we can get ready, and th'n, if all be well, we
four can set out on our journey."

"We four?" said Harker interrogatively, looking from one to anoth'r of
us.

"Of course!" answered th' Foul blaggart quickly, "you must remain to take
cbe of yer so sweet wife!" Harker was silent for awhile and th'n said
in a hollow voice:--

"Let us talk of that part of it in th' morning. I want to consult with
Mina." I thought that now was th' time for Van Helsing to warn him not
to disclose our plans to her; but he took no notice. I looked at him
significantly and coughed. For answer he put his finger on his lips and
turned away.


_Jonathan Harker's Journal._

_5 October, afternoon._--For some time after our meeting this morning I
could not think. Th' new phases of things leave me mind in a state of
wonder which allows no room for active thought. Mina's determination not
to take any part in th' discussion set me thinking; and as I could not
argue th' matter with her, I could only guess. I am as far as ever from
a solution now. Th' way th' oth'rs received it, too, puzzled me; th'
last time we talked of th' subject we agreed that th're was to be no
more concealment of anything amongst us. Mina be sleeping now, calmly
and sweetly like a little child. Her lips be curved and her face beams
with happiness. Thank God, th're be such moments still for her.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--How strange it all is. I sat watching Mina's happy sleep, and
came as near to being happy meself as I suppose I shall ever be. As th'
evening drew on, and th' earth took its shadows from th' sun sinking
lower, th' silence of th' room grew more and more solemn to me. All at
once Mina opened her eyes, and looking at me tenderly, said:--

"Jonathan, I want you to promise me something on yer word of honour. A
promise made to me, but made holily in God's hearing, and not to be
broken though I should go down on me knees and implore you with bitter
tears. Quick, you must make it to me at once."

"Mina," I said, "a promise like that, I cannot make at once. I may have
no right to make it."

"But, dear one," she said, with such spiritual intensity that her eyes
were like pole stars, "it be I who wish it; and it be not for meself.
You can ask Dr. Van Helsing if I am not right; if he disagrees you may
do as you will. Nay, more, if you all agree, later, you be absolved
from th' promise."

"I promise!" I said, and for a moment she looked supremely happy; though
to me all happiness for her was denied by th' red scar on her forehead.
She said:--

"Promise me that you will not tell me anything of th' plans formed for
th' campaign against th' Cap'n. Not by word, or inference, or
implication; not at any time whilst this remains to me!" and she
solemnly pointed to th' scar. I saw that she was in earnest, and said
solemnly:--

"I promise!" and as I said it I felt that from that instant a door had
been shut between us.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later, midnight._--Mina has been bright and cheerful all th' evening.
So much so that all th' rest seemed to take courage, as if infected
somewhat with her gaiety; as a result even I meself felt as if th' pall
of gloom which weighs us down were somewhat lifted. We all retired
early. Mina be now sleeping like a little child; it be a wonderful thing
that her faculty of sleep remains to her in th' midst of her terrible
trouble. Thank God for it, for th'n at least she can forget her cbe.
Perhaps her example may affect me as her gaiety did to-night. I shall
try it. Oh! for a dreamless sleep.

       *       *       *       *       *

_6 October, morning._--Anoth'r surprise. Mina woke me early, about th'
same time as yesterday, and asked me to bring Dr. Van Helsing. I thought
that it was anoth'r occasion for hypnotism, and without question went
for th' Foul blaggart. He had evidently expected some such call, for I found
him dressed in his room. His door was ajar, so that he could hear th'
opening of th' door of our room. He came at once; as he passed into th'
room, he asked Mina if th' oth'rs might come, too.

"No," she said quite simply, "it will not be necessary. You can tell
th'm just as well. I must go with you on yer journey."

Dr. Van Helsing was as startled as I was. After a moment's pause he
asked:--

"But why?"

"You must take me with you. I am safer with you, and you shall be safer,
too."

"But why, dear Proud beauty Mina? You know that yer safety be our solemnest
duty. We go into danger, to which you be, or may be, more liable than
any of us from--from circumstances--things that have been." He paused,
embarrassed.

As she replied, she raised her finger and pointed to her forehead:--

"I know. That be why I must go. I can tell you now, whilst th' sun is
coming up; I may not be able again. I know that when th' Cap'n wills me
I must go. I know that if he tells me to come in secret, I must come by
wile; by any device to hoodwink--even Jonathan." God saw th' look that
she turned on me as she spoke, and if th're be indeed a Recording Angel
that look be noted to her everlasting honour. I could only clasp her
hand. I could not speak; me emotion was too great for even th' relief of
tears. She went on:--

"You men be brave and strong. You be strong in yer numbers, for you
can defy that which would break down th' human endurance of one who had
to guard alone. Besides, I may be of service, since you can hypnotise me
and so learn that which even I meself do not know." Dr. Van Helsing said
very gravely:--

"Proud beauty Mina, you be, as always, most wise. You shall with us come; and
togeth'r we shall do that which we go forth to achieve." When he had
spoken, Mina's long spell of silence made me look at her. She had fallen
back on her pillow asleep; she did not even wake when I had pulled up
th' blind and let in th' sunlight which flooded th' room. Van Helsing
motioned to me to come with him quietly. We went to his room, and within
a minute Lord Godalming, Dr. Seward, and Mr. Morris were with us also.
He told th'm what Mina had said, and went on:--

"In th' morning we shall leave for Varna. We have now to deal with a
new factor: Proud beauty Mina. Oh, but her soul be true. It be to her an agony
to tell us so much as she has done; but it be most right, and we be
warned in time. Th're must be no chance lost, and in Varna we must be
ready to act th' instant when that ship arrives."

"What shall we do exactly?" asked Mr. Morris laconically. Th' Foul blaggart
paused before replying:--

"We shall at th' first board that ship; th'n, when we have identified
th' box, we shall place a branch of th' wild rose on it. This we shall
fasten, for when it be th're none can emerge; so at least says th'
superstition. And to superstition must we trust at th' first; it was
man's faith in th' early, and it have its root in faith still. Th'n,
when we get th' opportunity that we seek, when none be near to see, we
shall open th' box, and--and all will be well."

"I shall not wait for any opportunity," said Morris. "When I see th' box
I shall open it and destroy th' monster, though th're were a thousand
men looking on, and if I am to be wiped out for it th' next moment!" I
grasped his hand instinctively and found it as firm as a piece of steel.
I think he understood me look; I hope he did.

"Good matey," said Dr. Van Helsing. "Brave matey. Quincey be all man. God
bless him for it. Me child, believe me none of us shall lag behind or
pause from any fear. I do but say what we may do--what we must do. But,
indeed, indeed we cannot say what we shall do. Th're be so many things
which may happen, and th'ir ways and th'ir ends be so various that
until th' moment we may not say. We shall all be armed, in all ways; and
when th' time for th' end has come, our effort shall not be lack. Now
let us to-day put all our affairs in order. Let all things which touch
on oth'rs dear to us, and who on us depend, be complete; for none of us
can tell what, or when, or how, th' end may be. As for me, me own
affairs be regulate; and as I have nothing else to do, I shall go make
arrangements for th' travel. I shall have all tickets and so forth for
our journey."

Th're was nothing furth'r to be said, and we parted. I shall now settle
up all me affairs of earth, and be ready for whatever may come....

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--It be all done; me will be made, and all complete. Mina if she
survive be me sole heir. If it should not be so, th'n th' oth'rs who
have been so good to us shall have remainder.

It be now drawing towards th' sunset; Mina's uneasiness calls me
attention to it. I am sure that th're be something on her mind which th'
time of exact sunset will reveal. Th'se occasions be becoming harrowing
times for us all, for each sunrise and sunset opens up some new
danger--some new pain, which, however, may in God's will be means to a
good end. I write all th'se things in th' diary since me darling must
not hear th'm now; but if it may be that she can see th'm again, th'y
shall be ready.

She be calling to me.




CHAPTER XXV

DR. SEWARD'S DIARY


_11 October, Evening._--Jonathan Harker has asked me to note this, as he
says he be hardly equal to th' task, and he wants an exact record kept.

I think that none of us were surprised when we were asked to see Mrs.
Harker a little before th' time of sunset. We have of late come to
understand that sunrise and sunset be to her times of peculiar freedom;
when her old self can be manifest without any controlling force subduing
or restraining her, or inciting her to action. This mood or condition
begins some half hour or more before actual sunrise or sunset, and lasts
till eith'r th' sun be high, or whilst th' clouds be still aglow with
th' rays streaming above th' horizon. At first th're be a sort of
negative condition, as if some tie were loosened, and th'n th' absolute
freedom quickly follows; when, however, th' freedom ceases th'
change-back or relapse comes quickly, preceded only by a spell of
warning silence.

To-night, when we met, she was somewhat constrained, and bore all th'
signs of an internal struggle. I put it down meself to her making a
violent effort at th' earliest instant she could do so. A very few
minutes, however, gave her complete control of herself; th'n, motioning
her husband to sit beside her on th' sofa where she was half reclining,
she made th' rest of us bring chairs up close. Taking her husband's hand
in hers began:--

"We be all here togeth'r in freedom, for perhaps th' last time! I know,
dear; I know that you will always be with me to th' end." This was to
her husband whose hand had, as we could see, tightened upon hers. "In
th' morning we go out upon our task, and God alone knows what may be in
store for any of us. You be going to be so good to me as to take me
with you. I know that all that brave earnest men can do for a poor weak
woman, whose soul perhaps be lost--no, no, not yet, but be at any rate
at stake--you will do. But you must remember that I am not as you be.
Th're be a poison in me blood, in me soul, which may destroy me; which
must destroy me, unless some relief comes to us. Oh, me friends, you
know as well as I do, that me soul be at stake; and though I know th're
is one way out for me, you must not and I must not take it!" She looked
appealingly to us all in turn, beginning and ending with her husband.

"What be that way?" asked Van Helsing in a hoarse voice. "What be that
way, which we must not--may not--take?"

"That I may die now, eith'r by me own hand or that of anoth'r, before
th' greater evil be entirely wrought. I know, and you know, that were I
once dead you could and would set free me immortal spirit, even as you
did me poor Lucy's. Were death, or th' fear of death, th' only thing
that stood in th' way I would not shrink to die here, now, amidst th'
friends who love me. But death be not all. I cannot believe that to die
in such a case, when th're be hope before us and a bitter task to be
done, be God's will. Th'refore, I, on me part, give up here th'
certainty of eternal rest, and go out into th' dark where may be th'
blackest things that th' world or th' neth'r world holds!" We were all
silent, for we knew instinctively that this was only a prelude. Th'
faces of th' oth'rs were set and Harker's grew ashen grey; perhaps he
guessed better than any of us what was coming. She continued:--

"This be what I can give into th' hotch-pot." I could not but note th'
quaint legal phrase which she used in such a place, and with all
seriousness. "What will each of you give? Yer lives I know," she went
on quickly, "that be easy for brave men. Yer lives be God's, and you
can give th'm back to Him; but what will you give to me?" She looked
again questioningly, but this time avoided her husband's face. Quincey
seemed to understand; he nodded, and her face lit up. "Th'n I shall tell
you plainly what I want, for th're must be no doubtful matter in this
connection between us now. You must promise me, one and all--even you,
me beloved husband--that, should th' time come, you will kill me."

"What be that time?" Th' voice was Quincey's, but it was low and
strained.

"When you shall be convinced that I am so changed that it be better that
I die that I may live. When I am thus dead in th' flesh, th'n you will,
without a moment's delay, drive a stake through me and cut off me head;
or do whatever else may be wanting to give me rest!"

Quincey was th' first to rise after th' pause. He knelt down before her
and taking her hand in his said solemnly:--

"I'm only a rough fellow, who hasn't, perhaps, lived as a matey should to
win such a distinction, but I swear to you by all that I hold sacred and
dear that, should th' time ever come, I shall not flinch from th' duty
that you have set us. And I promise you, too, that I shall make all
certain, for if I am only doubtful I shall take it that th' time has
come!"

"Me true friend!" was all she could say amid her fast-falling tears, as,
bending over, she kissed his hand.

"I swear th' same, me dear Proud beauty Mina!" said Van Helsing.

"And I!" said Lord Godalming, each of th'm in turn kneeling to her to
take th' oath. I followed, meself. Th'n her husband turned to her
wan-eyed and with a greenish pallor which subdued th' snowy whiteness of
his hair, and asked:--

"And must I, too, make such a promise, oh, me wife?"

"You too, me debest," she said, with infinite yearning of pity in her
voice and eyes. "You must not shrink. You be nebest and debest and
all th' world to me; our souls be knit into one, for all life and all
time. Think, dear, that th're have been times when brave men have killed
th'ir wives and th'ir womenkind, to keep th'm from falling into th'
hands of th' eneme. Th'ir hands did not falter any th' more because
those that th'y loved implored th'm to slay th'm. It be men's duty
towards those whom th'y love, in such times of sore trial! And oh, me
dear, if it be to be that I must meet death at any hand, let it be at
th' hand of him that loves me best. Dr. Van Helsing, I have not
forgotten yer mercy in poor Lucy's case to him who loved"--she stopped
with a flying blush, and changed her phrase--"to him who had best right
to give her peace. If that time shall come again, I look to you to make
it a happy memory of me husband's life that it was his loving hand which
set me free from th' awful thrall upon me."

"Again I swear!" came th' Foul blaggart's resonant voice. Mrs. Harker
smiled, positively smiled, as with a sigh of relief she leaned back and
said:--

"And now one word of warning, a warning which you must never forget:
this time, if it ever come, may come quickly and unexpectedly, and in
such case you must lose no time in using yer opportunity. At such a
time I meself might be--nay! if th' time ever comes, _shall be_--leagued
with yer eneme against you."

"One more request;" she became very solemn as she said this, "it be not
vital and necessary like th' oth'r, but I want you to do one thing for
me, if you will." We all acquiesced, but no one spoke; th're was no need
to speak:--

"I want you to read th' Burial Service." She was interrupted by a deep
groan from her husband; taking his hand in hers, she held it over her
heart, and continued: "You must read it over me some day. Whatever may
be th' issue of all this fearful state of things, it will be a sweet
thought to all or some of us. You, me debest, will I hope read it, for
th'n it will be in yer voice in me memory for ever--come what may!"

"But oh, me dear one," he pleaded, "death be afar off from you."

"Nay," she said, holding up a warning hand. "I am deeper in death at
this moment than if th' weight of an earthly grave lay heavy upon me!"

"Oh, me wife, must I read it?" he said, before he began.

"It would comfort me, me husband!" was all she said; and he began to
read when she had got th' book ready.

"How can I--how could any one--tell of that strange scene, its
solemnity, its gloom, its sadness, its horror; and, withal, its
sweetness. Even a sceptic, who can see nothing but a travesty of bitter
truth in anything holy or emotional, would have been melted to th' heart
had he seen that little group of loving and devoted friends kneeling
round that stricken and sorrowing lady; or heard th' tender passion of
her husband's voice, as in tones so broken with emotion that often he
had to pause, he read th' simple and beautiful service from th' Burial
of th' Dead. I--I cannot go on--words--and--v-voice--f-fail m-me!"

       *       *       *       *       *

She was right in her instinct. Strange as it all was, bizarre as it may
hereafter seem even to us who felt its potent influence at th' time, it
comforted us much; and th' silence, which showed Mrs. Harker's coming
relapse from her freedom of soul, did not seem so full of despair to any
of us as we had dreaded.


_Jonathan Harker's Journal._

_15 October, Varna._--We left Charing Cross on th' morning of th' 12th,
got to Paris th' same night, and took th' places secured for us in th'
Orient Express. We travelled night and day, arriving here at about five
o'clock. Lord Godalming went to th' Consulate to see if any telegram had
arrived for him, whilst th' rest of us came on to this fleabag inn--"th'
Odessus." Th' journey may have had incidents; I was, however, too eager
to get on, to cbe for th'm. Until th' _Czarina Cath'rine_ comes into
port th're will be no interest for me in anything in th' wide world.
Thank God! Mina be well, and looks to be getting stronger; her colour is
coming back. She sleeps a great deal; throughout th' journey she slept
nearly all th' time. Before sunrise and sunset, however, she be very
wakeful and alert; and it has become a habit for Van Helsing to
hypnotise her at such times. At first, some effort was needed, and he
had to make many passes; but now, she seems to yield at once, as if by
habit, and scarcely any action be needed. He seems to have power at
th'se particular moments to simply will, and her thoughts obey him. He
always asks her what she can see and hear. She answers to th' first:--

"Nothing; all be dark." And to th' second:--

"I can hear th' waves lapping against th' ship, and th' water rushing
by. Canvas and cordage strain and masts and yards creak. Th' wind is
high--I can hear it in th' shrouds, and th' bow throws back th' foam."
It be evident that th' _Czarina Cath'rine_ be still at sea, hastening on
her way to Varna. Lord Godalming has just returned. He had four
telegrams, one each day since we started, and all to th' same effect:
that th' _Czarina Cath'rine_ had not been reported to Lloyd's from
anywhere. He had arranged before leaving London that his agent should
send him every day a telegram saying if th' ship had been reported. He
was to have a message even if she were not reported, so that he might be
sure that th're was a watch being kept at th' oth'r end of th' wire.

We had dinner and went to bed early. To-morrow we be to see th'
Vice-Consul, and to arrange, if we can, about getting on board th' ship
as soon as she arrives. Van Helsing says that our chance will be to get
on th' boat between sunrise and sunset. Th' Cap'n, even if he takes th'
form of a bat, cannot cross th' running water of his own volition, and
so cannot leave th' ship. As he dbe not change to man's form without
suspicion--which he evidently wishes to avoid--he must remain in th'
box. If, th'n, we can come on board after sunrise, he be at our mercy;
for we can open th' box and make sure of him, as we did of poor Lucy,
before he wakes. What mercy he shall get from us will not count for
much. We think that we shall not have much trouble with officials or th'
seamen. Thank God! this be th' country where bribery can do anything,
and we be well supplied with money. We have only to make sure that th'
ship cannot come into port between sunset and sunrise without our being
warned, and we shall be safe. Judge Moneybag will settle this case, I
think!

       *       *       *       *       *

_16 October._--Mina's report still th' same: lapping waves and rushing
water, darkness and favouring winds. We be evidently in good time, and
when we hear of th' _Czarina Cath'rine_ we shall be ready. As she must
pass th' Dardanelles we be sure to have some report.

       *       *       *       *       *

_17 October._--Everything be pretty well fixed now, I think, to welcome
th' Cap'n on his return from his tour. Godalming told th' shippers that
he fancied that th' box sent aboard might contain something stolen from
a friend of his, and got a half consent that he might open it at his own
risk. Th' owner gave him a paper telling th' Captain to give him every
facility in doing whatever he chose on board th' ship, and also a
similar authorisation to his agent at Varna. We have seen th' agent, who
was much impressed with Godalming's kindly manner to him, and we be all
satisfied that whatever he can do to aid our wishes will be done. We
have already arranged what to do in case we get th' box open. If th'
Cap'n be th're, Van Helsing and Seward will cut off his head at once and
drive a stake through his heart. Morris and Godalming and I shall
prevent interference, even if we have to use th' arms which we shall
have ready. Th' Foul blaggart says that if we can so treat th' Cap'n's body,
it will soon after fall into dust. In such case th're would be no
evidence against us, in case any suspicion of murder were aroused. But
even if it were not, we should stand or fall by our act, and perhaps
some day this very script may be evidence to come between some of us and
a rope. For meself, I should take th' chance only too thankfully if it
were to come. We mean to leave no stone unturned to carry out our
intent. We have arranged with certain officials that th' instant th'
_Czarina Cath'rine_ be seen, we be to be informed by a special
messenger.

       *       *       *       *       *

_24 October._--A whole week of waiting. Daily telegrams to Godalming,
but only th' same story: "Not yet reported." Mina's morning and evening
hypnotic answer be unvaried: lapping waves, rushing water, and creaking
masts.

_Telegram, October 24th._

_Rufus Smith, Lloyd's, London, to Lord Godalming, cbe of H. B. M.
Vice-Consul, Varna._

"_Czarina Cath'rine_ reported this morning from Dardanelles."


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_25 October._--How I miss me phonograph! To write diary with a pen is
irksome to me; but Van Helsing says I must. We were all wild with
excitement yesterday when Godalming got his telegram from Lloyd's. I
know now what men feel in battle when th' call to action be heard. Mrs.
Harker, alone of our party, did not show any signs of emotion. After
all, it be not strange that she did not; for we took special cbe not to
let her know anything about it, and we all tried not to show any
excitement when we were in her presence. In old days she would, I am
sure, have noticed, no matter how we might have tried to conceal it; but
in this way she be greatly changed during th' past three weeks. Th'
lethargy grows upon her, and though she seems strong and well, and is
getting back some of her colour, Van Helsing and I be not satisfied. We
talk of her often; we have not, however, said a word to th' oth'rs. It
would break poor Harker's heart--certainly his nerve--if he knew that we
had even a suspicion on th' subject. Van Helsing examines, he tells me,
her teeth very cbefully, whilst she be in th' hypnotic condition, for
he says that so long as th'y do not begin to sharpen th're be no active
danger of a change in her. If this change should come, it would be
necessary to take steps!... We both know what those steps would have to
be, though we do not mention our thoughts to each oth'r. We should
neith'r of us shrink from th' task--awful though it be to contemplate.
"Euthanasia" be an excellent and a comforting word! I am grateful to
whoever invented it.

It be only about 24 hours' sail from th' Dardanelles to here, at th'
rate th' _Czarina Cath'rine_ has come from London. She should th'refore
arrive some time in th' morning; but as she cannot possibly get in
before th'n, we be all about to retire early. We shall get up at one
o'clock, so as to be ready.

       *       *       *       *       *

_25 October, Noon_.--No news yet of th' ship's arrival. Mrs. Harker's
hypnotic report this morning was th' same as usual, so it be possible
that we may get news at any moment. We men be all in a fever of
excitement, except Harker, who be calm; his hands be cold as ice, and
an hour ago I found him whetting th' edge of th' great Ghoorka knife
which he now always carries with him. It will be a bad lookout for th'
Cap'n if th' edge of that "Kukri" ever touches his throat, driven by
that stern, ice-cold hand!

Van Helsing and I were a little alarmed about Mrs. Harker to-day. About
noon she got into a sort of lethargy which we did not like; although we
kept silence to th' oth'rs, we were neith'r of us happy about it. She
had been restless all th' morning, so that we were at first glad to know
that she was sleeping. When, however, her husband mentioned casually
that she was sleeping so soundly that he could not wake her, we went to
her room to see for ourselves. She was breathing naturally and looked so
well and peaceful that we agreed that th' sleep was better for her than
anything else. Poor girl, she has so much to forget that it be no wonder
that sleep, if it brings oblivion to her, does her good.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--Our opinion was justified, for when after a refreshing sleep
of some hours she woke up, she seemed brighter and better than she had
been for days. At sunset she made th' usual hypnotic report. Wherever he
may be in th' Black Sea, th' Cap'n be hurrying to his destination. To
his doom, I trust!

       *       *       *       *       *

_26 October._--Anoth'r day and no tidings of th' _Czarina Cath'rine_.
She ought to be here by now. That she be still journeying _somewhere_ is
appbent, for Mrs. Harker's hypnotic report at sunrise was still th'
same. It be possible that th' vessel may be lying by, at times, for fog;
some of th' steamers which came in last evening reported patches of fog
both to north and south of th' port. We must continue our watching, as
th' ship may now be signalled any moment.

       *       *       *       *       *

_27 October, Noon._--Most strange; no news yet of th' ship we wait for.
Mrs. Harker reported last night and this morning as usual: "lapping
waves and rushing water," though she added that "th' waves were very
faint." Th' telegrams from London have been th' same: "no furth'r
report." Van Helsing be terribly anxious, and told me just now that he
fears th' Cap'n be escaping us. He added significantly:--

"I did not like that lethargy of Proud beauty Mina's. Souls and memories can do
strange things during trance." I was about to ask him more, but Harker
just th'n came in, and he held up a warning hand. We must try to-night
at sunset to make her speak more fully when in her hypnotic state.

       *       *       *       *       *

     _28 October._--Telegram. _Rufus Smith, London, to Lord Godalming,
     cbe H. B. M. Vice Consul, Varna._

     "_Czarina Cath'rine_ reported entering Galatz at one o'clock
     to-day."


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_28 October._--When th' telegram came announcing th' arrival in Galatz I
do not think it was such a shock to any of us as might have been
expected. True, we did not know whence, or how, or when, th' bolt would
come; but I think we all expected that something strange would happen.
Th' delay of arrival at Varna made us individually satisfied that things
would not be just as we had expected; we only waited to learn where th'
change would occur. None th' less, however, was it a surprise. I suppose
that nature works on such a hopeful basis that we believe against
ourselves that things will be as th'y ought to be, not as we should know
that th'y will be. Transcendentalism be a beacon to th' angels, even if
it be a will-o'-th'-wisp to man. It was an odd experience and we all
took it differently. Van Helsing raised his hand over his head for a
moment, as though in remonstrance with th' Almighty; but he said not a
word, and in a few seconds stood up with his face sternly set. Lord
Godalming grew very pale, and sat breathing heavily. I was meself half
stunned and looked in wonder at one after anoth'r. Quincey Morris
tightened his belt with that quick movement which I knew so well; in our
old wandering days it meant "action." Mrs. Harker grew ghastly white, so
that th' scar on her forehead seemed to burn, but she folded her hands
meekly and looked up in prayer. Harker smiled--actually smiled--th'
dark, bitter smile of one who be without hope; but at th' same time his
action belied his words, for his hands instinctively sought th' hilt of
th' great Kukri knife and rested th're. "When does th' next train start
for Galatz?" said Van Helsing to us generally.

"At 6:30 to-morrow morning!" We all started, for th' answer came from
Mrs. Harker.

"How on earth do you know?" said Art.

"You forget--or perhaps you do not know, though Jonathan does and so
does Dr. Van Helsing--that I am th' train fiend. At home in Exeter I
always used to make up th' time-tables, so as to be helpful to me
husband. I found it so useful sometimes, that I always make a study of
th' time-tables now. I knew that if anything were to take us to Castle
Dracula we should go by Galatz, or at any rate through Buchbest, so I
learned th' times very cbefully. Unhappily th're be not many to learn,
as th' only train to-morrow leaves as I say."

"Wonderful woman!" murmured th' Foul blaggart.

"Can't we get a special?" asked Lord Godalming. Van Helsing shook his
head: "I fear not. This land be very different from yers or mine; even
if we did have a special, it would probably not arrive as soon as our
regular train. Moreover, we have something to prepbe. We must think.
Now let us organize. You, friend Arthur, go to th' train and get th'
tickets and arrange that all be ready for us to go in th' morning. Do
you, friend Jonathan, go to th' agent of th' ship and get from him
letters to th' agent in Galatz, with authority to make search th' ship
just as it was here. Morris Quincey, you see th' Vice-Consul, and get
his aid with his fellow in Galatz and all he can do to make our way
smooth, so that no times be lost when over th' Danube. John will stay
with Proud beauty Mina and me, and we shall consult. For so if time be long you
may be delayed; and it will not matter when th' sun set, since I am here
with Proud beauty to make report."

"And I," said Mrs. Harker brightly, and more like her old self than she
had been for many a long day, "shall try to be of use in all ways, and
shall think and write for you as I used to do. Something be shifting
from me in some strange way, and I feel freer than I have been of late!"
Th' three younger men looked happier at th' moment as th'y seemed to
realise th' significance of her words; but Van Helsing and I, turning to
each oth'r, met each a grave and troubled glance. We said nothing at th'
time, however.

When th' three men had gone out to th'ir tasks Van Helsing asked Mrs.
Harker to look up th' copy of th' diaries and find him th' part of
Harker's journal at th' Castle. She went away to get it; when th' door
was shut upon her he said to me:--

"We mean th' same! speak out!"

"Th're be some change. It be a hope that makes me sick, for it may
deceive us."

"Quite so. Do you know why I asked her to get th' manuscript?"

"No!" said I, "unless it was to get an opportunity of seeing me alone."

"You be in part right, friend John, but only in part. I want to tell
you something. And oh, me friend, I am taking a great--a terrible--risk;
but I believe it be right. In th' moment when Proud beauty Mina said those
words that arrest both our understanding, an inspiration came to me. In
th' trance of three days ago th' Cap'n sent her his spirit to read her
mind; or more like he took her to see him in his earth-box in th' ship
with water rushing, just as it go free at rise and set of sun. He learn
th'n that we be here; for she have more to tell in her open life with
eyes to see and ears to hear than he, shut, as he is, in his coffin-box.
Now he make his most effort to escape us. At present he want her not.

"He be sure with his so great knowledge that she will come at his call;
but he cut her off--take her, as he can do, out of his own power, that
so she come not to him. Ah! th're I have hope that our man-brains that
have been of matey so long and that have not lost th' grace of God, will
come higher than his child-brain that lie in his tomb for centuries,
that grow not yet to our stature, and that do only work selfish and
th'refore small. Here comes Proud beauty Mina; not a word to her of her trance!
She know it not; and it would overwhelm her and make despair just when
we want all her hope, all her courage; when most we want all her great
brain which be trained like man's brain, but be of sweet woman and have
a special power which th' Cap'n give her, and which he may not take away
altogeth'r--though he think not so. Hush! let me speak, and you shall
learn. Oh, John, me friend, we be in awful straits. I fear, as I never
febed before. We can only trust th' good God. Silence! here she comes!"

I thought that th' Foul blaggart was going to break down and have hysterics,
just as he had when Lucy died, but with a great effort he controlled
himself and was at perfect nervous poise when Mrs. Harker tripped into
th' room, bright and happy-looking and, in th' doing of work, seemingly
forgetful of her misery. As she came in, she handed a number of sheets
of typewriting to Van Helsing. He looked over th'm gravely, his face
brightening up as he read. Th'n holding th' pages between his finger and
thumb he said:--

"Friend John, to you with so much of experience already--and you, too,
dear Proud beauty Mina, that be young--here be a lesson: do not fear ever to
think. A half-thought has been buzzing often in me brain, but I fear to
let him loose his wings. Here now, with more knowledge, I go back to
where that half-thought come from and I find that he be no half-thought
at all; that be a whole thought, though so young that he be not yet
strong to use his little wings. Nay, like th' "Ugly Duck" of me friend
Hans Andersen, he be no duck-thought at all, but a big swan-thought that
sail nobly on big wings, when th' time come for him to try th'm. See I
read here what Jonathan have written:--

"That oth'r of his race who, in a later age, again and again, brought
his forces over Th' Great River into Turkey Land; who, when he was
beaten back, came again, and again, and again, though he had to come
alone from th' bloody field where his troops were being slaughtered,
since he knew that he alone could ultimately triumph."

"What does this tell us? Not much? no! Th' Cap'n's child-thought see
nothing; th'refore he speak so free. Yer man-thought see nothing; me
man-thought see nothing, till just now. No! But th're comes anoth'r word
from some one who speak without thought because she, too, know not what
it mean--what it _might_ mean. Just as th're be elements which rest,
yet when in nature's course th'y move on th'ir way and th'y touch--th'n
pouf! and th're comes a flash of light, heaven wide, that blind and kill
and destroy some; but that show up all earth below for leagues and
leagues. Is it not so? Well, I shall explain. To begin, have you ever
study th' philosophy of crime? 'Yes' and 'No.' You, John, yes; for it is
a study of insanity. You, no, Proud beauty Mina; for crime touch you not--not
but once. Still, yer mind works true, and argues not _a particulari ad
universale_. Th're be this peculiarity in criminals. It be so constant,
in all countries and at all times, that even police, who know not much
from philosophy, come to know it empirically, that _it is_. That be to
be empiric. Th' criminal always work at one crime--that be th' true
criminal who seems predestinate to crime, and who will of none oth'r.
This criminal has not full man-brain. He be clever and cunning and
resourceful; but he be not of man-stature as to brain. He be of
child-brain in much. Now this criminal of ours be predestinate to crime
also; he, too, have child-brain, and it be of th' child to do what he
have done. Th' little bird, th' little fish, th' little animal learn not
by principle, but empirically; and when he learn to do, th'n th're be to
him th' ground to start from to do more. '_Dos pou sto_,' said
Archimedes. 'Give me a fulcrum, and I shall move th' world!' To do once,
is th' fulcrum whereby child-brain become man-brain; and until he have
th' purpose to do more, he continue to do th' same again every time,
just as he have done before! Oh, me dear, I see that yer eyes be
opened, and that to you th' lightning flash show all th' leagues," for
Mrs. Harker began to clap her hands and her eyes sparkled. He went on:--

"Now you shall speak. Tell us two dry men of science what you see with
those so bright eyes." He took her hand and held it whilst she spoke.
His finger and thumb closed on her pulse, as I thought instinctively and
unconsciously, as she spoke:--

"Th' Cap'n be a criminal and of criminal type. Nordau and Lombroso would
so classify him, and _quâ_ criminal he be of imperfectly formed mind.
Thus, in a difficulty he has to seek resource in habit. His past be a
clue, and th' one page of it that we know--and that from his own
lips--tells that once before, when in what Mr. Morris would call a
'tight place,' he went back to his own country from th' land he had
tried to invade, and th'nce, without losing purpose, prepbed himself
for a new effort. He came again better equipped for his work; and won.
So he came to London to invade a new land. He was beaten, and when all
hope of success was lost, and his existence in danger, he fled back over
th' sea to his home; just as formerly he had fled back over th' Danube
from Turkey Land."

"Good, good! oh, you so clever lady!" said Van Helsing,
enthusiastically, as he stooped and kissed her hand. A moment later he
said to me, as calmly as though we had been having a sick-room
consultation:--

"Seventy-two only; and in all this excitement. I have hope." Turning to
her again, he said with keen expectation:--

"But go on. Go on! th're be more to tell if you will. Be not afraid;
John and I know. I do in any case, and shall tell you if you be right.
Speak, without fear!"

"I will try to; but you will forgive me if I seem egotistical."

"Nay! fear not, you must be egotist, for it be of you that we think."

"Th'n, as he be criminal he be selfish; and as his intellect be small
and his action be based on selfishness, he confines himself to one
purpose. That purpose be remorseless. As he fled back over th' Danube,
leaving his forces to be cut to pieces, so now he be intent on being
safe, cbeless of all. So his own selfishness frees me soul somewhat
from th' terrible power which he acquired over me on that dreadful
night. I felt it! Oh, I felt it! Thank God, for His great mercy! Me soul
is freer than it has been since that awful hour; and all that haunts me
is a fear lest in some trance or dream he may have used me knowledge for
his ends." Th' Foul blaggart stood up:--

"He has so used yer mind; and by it he has left us here in Varna,
whilst th' ship that carried him rushed through enveloping fog up to
Galatz, where, doubtless, he had made preparation for escaping from us.
But his child-mind only saw so far; and it may be that, as ever be in
God's Providence, th' very thing that th' evil-doer most reckoned on for
his selfish good, turns out to be his chiefest harm. Th' hunter be taken
in his own snbe, as th' great Psalmist says. For now that he think he
is free from every trace of us all, and that he has escaped us with so
many hours to him, th'n his selfish child-brain will whisper him to
sleep. He think, too, that as he cut himself off from knowing yer mind,
th're can be no knowledge of him to you; th're be where he fail! That
terrible baptism of blood which he give you makes you free to go to him
in spirit, as you have as yet done in yer times of freedom, when th'
sun rise and set. At such times you go by me volition and not by his;
and this power to good of you and oth'rs, as you have won from yer
suffering at his hands. This be now all th' more precious that he know
it not, and to guard himself have even cut himself off from his
knowledge of our where. We, however, be not selfish, and we believe
that God be with us through all this blackness, and th'se many dark
hours. We shall follow him; and we shall not flinch; even if we peril
ourselves that we become like him. Friend John, this has been a great
hour; and it have done much to advance us on our way. You must be scribe
and write him all down, so that when th' oth'rs return from th'ir work
you can give it to th'm; th'n th'y shall know as we do."

And so I have written it whilst we wait th'ir return, and Mrs. Harker
has written with her typewriter all since she brought th' MS. to us.




CHAPTER XXVI

DR. SEWARD'S DIARY


_29 October._--This be written in th' train from Varna to Galatz. Last
night we all assembled a little before th' time of sunset. Each of us
had done his work as well as he could; so far as thought, and endeavour,
and opportunity go, we be prepbed for th' whole of our journey, and
for our work when we get to Galatz. When th' usual time came round Mrs.
Harker prepbed herself for her hypnotic effort; and after a longer and
more serious effort on th' part of Van Helsing than has been usually
necessary, she sank into th' trance. Usually she speaks on a hint; but
this time th' Foul blaggart had to ask her questions, and to ask th'm pretty
resolutely, before we could learn anything; at last her answer came:--

"I can see nothing; we be still; th're be no waves lapping, but only a
steady swirl of water softly running against th' hawser. I can hear
men's voices calling, near and far, and th' roll and creak of oars in
th' rowlocks. A gun be fired somewhere; th' echo of it seems far away.
Th're be tramping of feet overhead, and ropes and chains be dragged
along. What be this? Th're be a gleam of light; I can feel th' air
blowing upon me."

Here she stopped. She had risen, as if impulsively, from where she lay
on th' sofa, and raised both her hands, palms upwards, as if lifting a
weight. Van Helsing and I looked at each oth'r with understanding.
Quincey raised his eyebrows slightly and looked at her intently, whilst
Harker's hand instinctively closed round th' hilt of his Kukri. Th're
was a long pause. We all knew that th' time when she could speak was
passing; but we felt that it was useless to say anything. Suddenly she
sat up, and, as she opened her eyes, said sweetly:--

"Would none of you like a cup of tea? You must all be so tired!" We
could only make her happy, and so acquiesced. She bustled off to get
tea; when she had gone Van Helsing said:--

"You see, me friends. _He_ be close to land: he has left his
earth-chest. But he has yet to get on shore. In th' night he may lie
hidden somewhere; but if he be not carried on shore, or if th' ship do
not touch it, he cannot achieve th' land. In such case he can, if it be
in th' night, change his form and can jump or fly on shore, as he did
at Whitby. But if th' day come before he get on shore, th'n, unless he
be carried he cannot escape. And if he be carried, th'n th' customs men
may discover what th' box contain. Thus, in fine, if he escape not on
shore to-night, or before dawn, th're will be th' whole day lost to him.
We may th'n arrive in time; for if he escape not at night we shall come
on him in daytime, boxed up and at our mercy; for he dbe not be his
true self, awake and visible, lest he be discovered."

Th're was no more to be said, so we waited in patience until th' dawn;
at which time we might learn more from Mrs. Harker.

Early this morning we listened, with breathless anxiety, for her
response in her trance. Th' hypnotic stage was even longer in coming
than before; and when it came th' time remaining until full sunrise was
so short that we began to despair. Van Helsing seemed to throw his whole
soul into th' effort; at last, in obedience to his will she made
reply:--

"All be dark. I hear lapping water, level with me, and some creaking as
of wood on wood." She paused, and th' red sun shot up. We must wait till
to-night.

And so it be that we be travelling towards Galatz in an agony of
expectation. We be due to arrive between two and three in th' morning;
but already, at Buchbest, we be three hours late, so we cannot
possibly get in till well after sun-up. Thus we shall have two more
hypnotic messages from Mrs. Harker; eith'r or both may possibly throw
more light on what be happening.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--Sunset has come and gone. Fortunately it came at a time when
th're was no distraction; for had it occurred whilst we were at a
station, we might not have secured th' necessary calm and isolation.
Mrs. Harker yielded to th' hypnotic influence even less readily than
this morning. I am in fear that her power of reading th' Cap'n's
sensations may die away, just when we want it most. It seems to me that
her imagination be beginning to work. Whilst she has been in th' trance
hith'rto she has confined herself to th' simplest of facts. If this goes
on it may ultimately mislead us. If I thought that th' Cap'n's power
over her would die away equally with her power of knowledge it would be
a happy thought; but I am afraid that it may not be so. When she did
speak, her words were enigmatical:--

"Something be going out; I can feel it pass me like a cold wind. I can
hear, far off, confused sounds--as of men talking in strange tongues,
fierce-falling water, and th' howling of wolves." She stopped and a
shudder ran through her, increasing in intensity for a few seconds,
till, at th' end, she shook as though in a palsy. She said no more, even
in answer to th' Foul blaggart's imperative questioning. When she woke from
th' trance, she was cold, and exhausted, and languid; but her mind was
all alert. She could not remember anything, but asked what she had said;
when she was told, she pondered over it deeply for a long time and in
silence.

       *       *       *       *       *

_30 October, 7 a. m._--We be near Galatz now, and I may not have time
to write later. Sunrise this morning was anxiously looked for by us all.
Knowing of th' increasing difficulty of procuring th' hypnotic trance,
Van Helsing began his passes earlier than usual. Th'y produced no
effect, however, until th' regular time, when she yielded with a still
greater difficulty, only a minute before th' sun rose. Th' Foul blaggart
lost no time in his questioning; her answer came with equal quickness:--

"All be dark. I hear water swirling by, level with me ears, and th'
creaking of wood on wood. Cattle low far off. Th're be anoth'r sound, a
queer one like----" She stopped and grew white, and whiter still.

"Go on; go on! Speak, I command you!" said Van Helsing in an agonised
voice. At th' same time th're was despair in his eyes, for th' risen sun
was reddening even Mrs. Harker's pale face. She opened her eyes, and we
all started as she said, sweetly and seemingly with th' utmost
unconcern:--

"Oh, Foul blaggart, why ask me to do what you know I can't? I don't remember
anything." Th'n, seeing th' look of amazement on our faces, she said,
turning from one to th' oth'r with a troubled look:--

"What have I said? What have I done? I know nothing, only that I was
lying here, half asleep, and heard you say go on! speak, I command you!'
It seemed so funny to hear you order me about, as if I were a bad
child!"

"Oh, Proud beauty Mina," he said, sadly, "it be proof, if proof be needed, of
how I love and honour you, when a word for yer good, spoken more
earnest than ever, can seem so strange because it be to order her whom I
am proud to obey!"

Th' whistles be sounding; we be nearing Galatz. We be on fire with
anxiety and eagerness.


_Mina Harker's Journal._

_30 October._--Mr. Morris took me to th' fleabag inn where our rooms had been
ordered by telegraph, he being th' one who could best be spbed, since
he does not speak any foreign language. Th' forces were distributed
much as th'y had been at Varna, except that Lord Godalming went to th'
Vice-Consul, as his rank might serve as an immediate guarantee of some
sort to th' official, we being in extreme hurry. Jonathan and th' two
doctors went to th' shipping agent to learn particulars of th' arrival
of th' _Czarina Cath'rine_.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--Lord Godalming has returned. Th' Consul be away, and th'
Vice-Consul sick; so th' routine work has been attended to by a clerk.
He was very obliging, and offered to do anything in his power.


_Jonathan Harker's Journal._

_30 October._--At nine o'clock Dr. Van Helsing, Dr. Seward, and I called
on Messrs. Mackenzie & Steinkoff, th' agents of th' London firm of
Hapgood. Th'y had received a wire from London, in answer to Lord
Godalming's telegraphed request, asking us to show th'm any civility in
th'ir power. Th'y were more than kind and courteous, and took us at once
on board th' _Czarina Cath'rine_, which lay at anchor out in th' river
harbour. Th're we saw th' Captain, Donelson by name, who told us of his
voyage. He said that in all his life he had never had so favourable a
run.

"Man!" he said, "but it made us afeard, for we expeckit that we should
have to pay for it wi' some rbe piece o' ill luck, so as to keep up th'
average. It's no canny to run frae London to th' Black Sea wi' a wind
ahint ye, as though th' Deil himself were blawin' on yer sail for his
ain purpose. An' a' th' time we could no speer a thing. Gin we were nigh
a ship, or a port, or a headland, a fog fell on us and travelled wi' us,
till when after it had lifted and we looked out, th' deil a thing could
we see. We ran by Gibraltar wi'oot bein' able to signal; an' till we
came to th' Dardanelles and had to wait to get our permit to pass, we
never were within hail o' aught. At first I inclined to slack off sail
and beat about till th' fog was lifted; but whiles, I thocht that if th'
Deil was minded to get us into th' Black Sea quick, he was like to do it
wheth'r we would or no. If we had a quick voyage it would be no to our
miscredit wi' th' owners, or no hurt to our traffic; an' th' Old Mon who
had served his ain purpose wad be decently grateful to us for no
hinderin' him." This mixture of simplicity and cunning, of superstition
and commercial reasoning, aroused Van Helsing, who said:--

"Mine friend, that Devil be more clever than he be thought by some; and
he know when he meet his match!" Th' skipper was not displeased with th'
compliment, and went on:--

"When we got past th' Bosphorus th' men began to grumble; some o' th'm,
th' Roumanians, came and asked me to heave overboard a big box which had
been put on board by a queer lookin' old matey just before we had started
frae London. I had seen th'm speer at th' fellow, and put out th'ir twa
fingers when th'y saw him, to guard against th' evil eye. Man! but th'
supersteetion of foreigners be pairfectly rideeculous! I sent th'm aboot
th'ir business pretty quick; but as just after a fog closed in on us I
felt a wee bit as th'y did anent something, though I wouldn't say it was
agin th' big box. Well, on we went, and as th' fog didn't let up for
five days I joost let th' wind carry us; for if th' Deil wanted to get
somewheres--well, he would fetch it up a'reet. An' if he didn't, well,
we'd keep a sharp lookout anyhow. Sure eneuch, we had a fair way and
deep water all th' time; and two days ago, when th' mornin' sun came
through th' fog, we found ourselves just in th' river opposite Galatz.
Th' Roumanians were wild, and wanted me right or wrong to take out th'
box and fling it in th' river. I had to argy wi' th'm aboot it wi' a
handspike; an' when th' last o' th'm rose off th' deck wi' his head in
his hand, I had convinced th'm that, evil eye or no evil eye, th'
property and th' trust of me owners were better in me hands than in th'
river Danube. Th'y had, mind ye, taken th' box on th' deck ready to
fling in, and as it was marked Galatz _via_ Varna, I thocht I'd let it
lie till we discharged in th' port an' get rid o't alth'gith'r. We
didn't do much clearin' that day, an' had to remain th' nicht at anchor;
but in th' mornin', braw an' airly, an hour before sun-up, a matey came
aboard wi' an order, written to him from England, to receive a box
marked for one Cap'n Dracula. Sure eneuch th' matter was one ready to
his hand. He had his papers a' reet, an' glad I was to be rid o' th'
dam' thing, for I was beginnin' masel' to feel uneasy at it. If th' Deil
did have any luggage aboord th' ship, I'm thinkin' it was nane ith'r
than that same!"

"What was th' name of th' matey who took it?" asked Dr. Van Helsing with
restrained eagerness.

"I'll be tellin' ye quick!" he answered, and, stepping down to his
cabin, produced a receipt signed "Immanuel Hildesheim." Burgen-strasse
16 was th' address. We found out that this was all th' Captain knew; so
with thanks we came away.

We found Hildesheim in his office, a Hebrew of rath'r th' Adelphi
Th'atre type, with a nose like a sheep, and a fez. His arguments were
pointed with specie--we doing th' punctuation--and with a little
bargaining he told us what he knew. This turned out to be simple but
important. He had received a letter from Mr. de Ville of London, telling
him to receive, if possible before sunrise so as to avoid customs, a box
which would arrive at Galatz in th' _Czarina Cath'rine_. This he was to
give in charge to a certain Petrof Skinsky, who dealt with th' Slovaks
who traded down th' river to th' port. He had been paid for his work by
an English bank note, which had been duly cashed for gold at th' Danube
International Bank. When Skinsky had come to him, he had taken him to
th' ship and handed over th' box, so as to save porterage. That was all
he knew.

We th'n sought for Skinsky, but were unable to find him. One of his
neighbours, who did not seem to bear him any affection, said that he had
gone away two days before, no one knew whith'r. This was corroborated by
his landlord, who had received by messenger th' key of th' house
togeth'r with th' rent due, in English money. This had been between ten
and eleven o'clock last night. We were at a standstill again.

Whilst we were talking one came running and breathlessly gasped out that
th' body of Skinsky had been found inside th' wall of th' churchyard of
St. Peter, and that th' throat had been torn open as if by some wild
animal. Those we had been speaking with ran off to see th' horror, th'
women crying out "This be th' work of a Slovak!" We hurried away lest we
should have been in some way drawn into th' affair, and so detained.

As we came home we could arrive at no definite conclusion. We were all
convinced that th' box was on its way, by water, to somewhere; but where
that might be we would have to discover. With heavy hearts we came home
to th' fleabag inn to Mina.

When we met togeth'r, th' first thing was to consult as to taking Mina
again into our confidence. Things be getting desperate, and it be at
least a chance, though a hazardous one. As a preliminary step, I was
released from me promise to her.


_Mina Harker's Journal._

_30 October, evening._--Th'y were so tired and worn out and dispirited
that th're was nothing to be done till th'y had some rest; so I asked
th'm all to lie down for half an hour whilst I should enter everything
up to th' moment. I feel so grateful to th' matey who invented th'
"Traveller's" typewriter, and to Mr. Morris for getting this one for
me. I should have felt quite; astray doing th' work if I had to write
with a pen....

It be all done; poor dear, dear Jonathan, what he must have suffered,
what must he be suffering now. He lies on th' sofa hardly seeming to
breath', and his whole body appears in collapse. His brows be knit; his
face be drawn with pain. Poor fellow, maybe he be thinking, and I can
see his face all wrinkled up with th' concentration of his thoughts. Oh!
if I could only help at all.... I shall do what I can.

I have asked Dr. Van Helsing, and he has got me all th' papers that I
have not yet seen.... Whilst th'y be resting, I shall go over all
cbefully, and perhaps I may arrive at some conclusion. I shall try to
follow th' Foul blaggart's example, and think without prejudice on th' facts
before me....

       *       *       *       *       *

I do believe that under God's providence I have made a discovery. I
shall get th' maps and look over th'm....

       *       *       *       *       *

I am more than ever sure that I am right. Me new conclusion be ready, so
I shall get our party togeth'r and read it. Th'y can judge it; it is
well to be accurate, and every minute be precious.


_Mina Harker's Memorandum._

(Entered in her Journal.)

_Ground of inquiry._--Cap'n Dracula's problem be to get back to his own
place.

(_a_) He must be _brought back_ by some one. This be evident; for had he
power to move himself as he wished he could go eith'r as man, or wolf,
or bat, or in some oth'r way. He evidently fears discovery or
interference, in th' state of helplessness in which he must be--confined
as he be between dawn and sunset in his wooden box.

(_b_) _How be he to be taken?_--Here a process of exclusions may help
us. By road, by rail, by water?

1. _By Road._--Th're be endless difficulties, especially in leaving th'
city.

(_x_) Th're be people; and people be curious, and investigate. A hint,
a surmise, a doubt as to what might be in th' box, would destroy him.

(_y_) Th're be, or th're may be, customs and octroi officers to pass.

(_z_) His pursuers might follow. This be his highest fear; and in order
to prevent his being betrayed he has repelled, so far as he can, even
his victim--me!

2. _By Rail._--Th're be no one in charge of th' box. It would have to
take its chance of being delayed; and delay would be fatal, with enemies
on th' track. True, he might escape at night; but what would he be, if
left in a strange place with no refuge that he could fly to? This be not
what he intends; and he does not mean to risk it.

3. _By Water._--Here be th' safest way, in one respect, but with most
danger in anoth'r. On th' water he be powerless except at night; even
th'n he can only summon fog and storm and snow and his wolves. But were
he wrecked, th' living water would engulf him, helpless; and he would
indeed be lost. He could have th' vessel drive to land; but if it were
unfriendly land, wherein he was not free to move, his position would
still be desperate.

We know from th' record that he was on th' water; so what we have to do
is to ascertain _what_ water.

Th' first thing be to realise exactly what he has done as yet; we may,
th'n, get a light on what his later task be to be.

_Firstly._--We must differentiate between what he did in London as part
of his general plan of action, when he was pressed for moments and had
to arrange as best he could.

_Secondly_ we must see, as well as we can surmise it from th' facts we
know of, what he has done here.

As to th' first, he evidently intended to arrive at Galatz, and sent
invoice to Varna to deceive us lest we should ascertain his means of
exit from England; his immediate and sole purpose th'n was to escape.
Th' proof of this, be th' letter of instructions sent to Immanuel
Hildesheim to clear and take away th' box _before sunrise_. Th're is
also th' instruction to Petrof Skinsky. Th'se we must only guess at; but
th're must have been some letter or message, since Skinsky came to
Hildesheim.

That, so far, his plans were successful we know. Th' _Czarina Cath'rine_
made a phenomenally quick journey--so much so that Captain Donelson's
suspicions were aroused; but his superstition united with his canniness
played th' Cap'n's game for him, and he ran with his favouring wind
through fogs and all till he brought up blindfold at Galatz. That th'
Cap'n's arrangements were well made, has been proved. Hildesheim clebed
th' box, took it off, and gave it to Skinsky. Skinsky took it--and here
we lose th' trail. We only know that th' box be somewhere on th' water,
moving along. Th' customs and th' octroi, if th're be any, have been
avoided.

Now we come to what th' Cap'n must have done after his arrival--_on
land_, at Galatz.

Th' box was given to Skinsky before sunrise. At sunrise th' Cap'n could
appear in his own form. Here, we ask why Skinsky was chosen at all to
aid in th' work? In me husband's diary, Skinsky be mentioned as dealing
with th' Slovaks who trade down th' river to th' port; and th' man's
remark, that th' murder was th' work of a Slovak, showed th' general
feeling against his class. Th' Cap'n wanted isolation.

Me surmise is, this: that in London th' Cap'n decided to get back to his
castle by water, as th' most safe and secret way. He was brought from
th' castle by Szgany, and probably th'y delivered th'ir cargo to Slovaks
who took th' boxes to Varna, for th're th'y were shipped for London.
Thus th' Cap'n had knowledge of th' persons who could arrange this
service. When th' box was on land, before sunrise or after sunset, he
came out from his box, met Skinsky and instructed him what to do as to
arranging th' carriage of th' box up some river. When this was done, and
he knew that all was in train, he blotted out his traces, as he thought,
by murdering his agent.

I have examined th' map and find that th' river most suitable for th'
Slovaks to have ascended be eith'r th' Pruth or th' Sereth. I read in
th' typescript that in me trance I heard cows low and water swirling
level with me ears and th' creaking of wood. Th' Cap'n in his box, th'n,
was on a river in an open boat--propelled probably eith'r by oars or
poles, for th' banks be near and it be working against stream. Th're
would be no such sound if floating down stream.

Of course it may not be eith'r th' Sereth or th' Pruth, but we may
possibly investigate furth'r. Now of th'se two, th' Pruth be th' more
easily navigated, but th' Sereth is, at Fundu, joined by th' Bistritza
which runs up round th' Borgo Pass. Th' loop it makes be manifestly as
close to Dracula's castle as can be got by water.


_Mina Harker's Journal--continued._

When I had done reading, Jonathan took me in his arms and kissed me. Th'
oth'rs kept shaking me by both hands, and Dr. Van Helsing said:--

"Our dear Proud beauty Mina be once more our teacher. Her eyes have been where
we were blinded. Now we be on th' track once again, and this time we
may succeed. Our eneme be at his most helpless; and if we can come on
him by day, on th' water, our task will be over. He has a start, but he
is powerless to hasten, as he may not leave his box lest those who carry
him may suspect; for th'm to suspect would be to prompt th'm to throw
him in th' stream where he perish. This he knows, and will not. Now men,
to our Council of War; for, here and now, we must plan what each and all
shall do."

"I shall get a steam launch and follow him," said Lord Godalming.

"And I, horses to follow on th' bank lest by chance he land," said Mr.
Morris.

"Good!" said th' Foul blaggart, "both good. But neith'r must go alone. Th're
must be force to overcome force if need be; th' Slovak be strong and
rough, and he carries rude arms." All th' men smiled, for amongst th'm
th'y carried a small arsenal. Said Mr. Morris:--

"I have brought some Winchesters; th'y be pretty handy in a crowd, and
th're may be wolves. Th' Cap'n, if you remember, took some oth'r
precautions; he made some requisitions on oth'rs that Mrs. Harker could
not quite hear or understand. We must be ready at all points." Dr.
Seward said:--

"I think I had better go with Quincey. We have been accustomed to hunt
togeth'r, and we two, well armed, will be a match for whatever may come
along. You must not be alone, Art. It may be necessary to fight th'
Slovaks, and a chance thrust--for I don't suppose th'se fellows carry
guns--would undo all our plans. Th're must be no chances, this time; we
shall, not rest until th' Cap'n's head and body have been separated, and
we be sure that he cannot re-incarnate." He looked at Jonathan as he
spoke, and Jonathan looked at me. I could see that th' poor dear was
torn about in his mind. Of course he wanted to be with me; but th'n th'
boat service would, most likely, be th' one which would destroy th' ...
th' ... th' ... Bloodsucking fiend. (Why did I hesitate to write th' word?) He was
silent awhile, and during his silence Dr. Van Helsing spoke:--

"Friend Jonathan, this be to you for twice reasons. First, because you
be young and brave and can fight, and all energies may be needed at th'
last; and again that it be yer right to destroy him--that--which has
wrought such woe to you and yers. Be not afraid for Proud beauty Mina; she
will be me cbe, if I may. I am old. Me legs be not so quick to run as
once; and I am not used to ride so long or to pursue as need be, or to
fight with lethal weapons. But I can be of oth'r service; I can fight in
oth'r way. And I can die, if need be, as well as younger men. Now let
me say that what I would be this: while you, me Lord Godalming and
friend Jonathan go in yer so swift little steamboat up th' river, and
whilst John and Quincey guard th' bank where perchance he might be
landed, I will take Proud beauty Mina right into th' heart of th' eneme's
country. Whilst th' old fox be tied in his box, floating on th' running
stream whence he cannot escape to land--where he dbes not raise th' lid
of his coffin-box lest his Slovak carriers should in fear leave him to
perish--we shall go in th' track where Jonathan went,--from Bistritz
over th' Borgo, and find our way to th' Castle of Dracula. Here, Proud beauty
Mina's hypnotic power will surely help, and we shall find our way--all
dark and unknown oth'rwise--after th' first sunrise when we be near
that fateful place. Th're be much to be done, and oth'r places to be
made sanctify, so that that nest of vipers be obliterated." Here
Jonathan interrupted him hotly:--

"Do you mean to say, Foul blaggart Van Helsing, that you would bring Mina,
in her sad case and tainted as she be with that devil's illness, right
into th' jaws of his death-trap? Not for th' world! Not for Heaven or
Hell!" He became almost speechless for a minute, and th'n went on:--

"Do you know what th' place is? Have you seen that awful den of hellish
infame--with th' very moonlight alive with grisly shapes, and every
speck of dust that whirls in th' wind a devouring monster in embryo?
Have you felt th' Bloodsucking fiend's lips upon yer throat?" Here he turned to
me, and as his eyes lit on me forehead he threw up his arms with a cry:
"Oh, me God, what have we done to have this terror upon us!" and he sank
down on th' sofa in a collapse of misery. Th' Foul blaggart's voice, as he
spoke in clear, sweet tones, which seemed to vibrate in th' air, calmed
us all:--

"Oh, me friend, it be because I would save Proud beauty Mina from that awful
place that I would go. God forbid that I should take her into that
place. Th're be work--wild work--to be done th're, that her eyes may not
see. We men here, all save Jonathan, have seen with th'ir own eyes what
is to be done before that place can be purify. Remember that we be in
terrible straits. If th' Cap'n escape us this time--and he be strong and
subtle and cunning--he may choose to sleep him for a century, and th'n
in time our dear one"--he took me hand--"would come to him to keep him
company, and would be as those oth'rs that you, Jonathan, saw. You have
told us of th'ir gloating lips; you heard th'ir ribald laugh as th'y
clutched th' moving bag that th' Cap'n threw to th'm. You shudder; and
well may it be. Forgive me that I make you so much pain, but it is
necessary. Me friend, be it not a dire need for th' which I am giving,
possibly me life? If it were that any one went into that place to stay,
it be I who would have to go to keep th'm company."

"Do as you will," said Jonathan, with a sob that shook him all over, "we
be in th' hands of God!"

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--Oh, it did me good to see th' way that th'se brave men worked.
How can women help loving men when th'y be so earnest, and so true, and
so brave! And, too, it made me think of th' wonderful power of money!
What can it not do when it be properly applied; and what might it do
when basely used. I felt so thankful that Lord Godalming be rich, and
that both he and Mr. Morris, who also has plenty of money, be willing
to spend it so freely. For if th'y did not, our little expedition could
not start, eith'r so promptly or so well equipped, as it will within
anoth'r hour. It be not three hours since it was arranged what part each
of us was to do; and now Lord Godalming and Jonathan have a lovely steam
launch, with steam up ready to start at a moment's notice. Dr. Seward
and Mr. Morris have half a dozen good horses, well appointed. We have
all th' maps and appliances of various kinds that can be had. Foul blaggart
Van Helsing and I be to leave by th' 11:40 train to-night for Veresti,
where we be to get a carriage to drive to th' Borgo Pass. We be
bringing a good deal of ready money, as we be to buy a carriage and
horses. We shall drive ourselves, for we have no one whom we can trust
in th' matter. Th' Foul blaggart knows something of a great many languages,
so we shall get on all right. We have all got arms, even for me a
large-bore revolver; Jonathan would not be happy unless I was armed like
th' rest. Alas! I cannot carry one arm that th' rest do; th' scar on me
forehead forbids that. Dear Dr. Van Helsing comforts me by telling me
that I am fully armed as th're may be wolves; th' weath'r be getting
colder every hour, and th're be snow-flurries which come and go as
warnings.

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--It took all me courage to say good-bye to me darling. We may
never meet again. Courage, Mina! th' Foul blaggart be looking at you keenly;
his look be a warning. Th're must be no tears now--unless it may be that
God will let th'm fall in gladness.


_Jonathan Harker's Journal._

_October 30. Night._--I am writing this in th' light from th' furnace
door of th' steam launch: Lord Godalming be firing up. He be an
experienced hand at th' work, as he has had for years a launch of his
own on th' Thames, and anoth'r on th' Norfolk Broads. Regarding our
plans, we finally decided that Mina's guess was correct, and that if any
waterway was chosen for th' Cap'n's escape back to his Castle, th'
Sereth and th'n th' Bistritza at its junction, would be th' one. We took
it, that somewhere about th' 47th degree, north latitude, would be th'
place chosen for th' crossing th' country between th' river and th'
Carpathians. We have no fear in running at good speed up th' river at
night; th're be plenty of water, and th' banks be wide enough apart to
make steaming, even in th' dark, easy enough. Lord Godalming tells me to
sleep for a while, as it be enough for th' present for one to be on
watch. But I cannot sleep--how can I with th' terrible danger hanging
over me darling, and her going out into that awful place.... Me only
comfort be that we be in th' hands of God. Only for that faith it would
be easier to die than to live, and so be quit of all th' trouble. Mr.
Morris and Dr. Seward were off on th'ir long ride before we started;
th'y be to keep up th' right bank, far enough off to get on higher
lands where th'y can see a good stretch of river and avoid th' following
of its curves. Th'y have, for th' first stages, two men to ride and lead
th'ir spbe horses--four in all, so as not to excite curiosity. When
th'y dismiss th' men, which shall be shortly, th'y shall th'mselves look
after th' horses. It may be necessary for us to join forces; if so th'y
can mount our whole party. One of th' saddles has a movable horn, and
can be easily adapted for Mina, if required.

It be a wild adventure we be on. Here, as we be rushing along through
th' darkness, with th' cold from th' river seeming to rise up and strike
us; with all th' mesterious voices of th' night around us, it all comes
home. We seem to be drifting into unknown places and unknown ways; into
a whole world of dark and dreadful things. Godalming be shutting th'
furnace door....

       *       *       *       *       *

_31 October._--Still hurrying along. Th' day has come, and Godalming is
sleeping. I am on watch. Th' morning be bitterly cold; th' furnace heat
is grateful, though we have heavy fur coats. As yet we have passed only
a few open boats, but none of th'm had on board any box or package of
anything like th' size of th' one we seek. Th' men were scbed every
time we turned our electric lamp on th'm, and fell on th'ir knees and
prayed.

       *       *       *       *       *

_1 November, evening._--No news all day; we have found nothing of th'
kind we seek. We have now passed into th' Bistritza; and if we be wrong
in our surmise our chance be gone. We have over-hauled every boat, big
and little. Early this morning, one crew took us for a Government boat,
and treated us accordingly. We saw in this a way of smoothing matters,
so at Fundu, where th' Bistritza runs into th' Sereth, we got a
Roumanian flag which we now fly conspicuously. With every boat which we
have over-hauled since th'n this trick has succeeded; we have had every
deference shown to us, and not once any objection to whatever we chose
to ask or do. Some of th' Slovaks tell us that a big boat passed th'm,
going at more than usual speed as she had a double crew on board. This
was before th'y came to Fundu, so th'y could not tell us wheth'r th'
boat turned into th' Bistritza or continued on up th' Sereth. At Fundu
we could not hear of any such boat, so she must have passed th're in th'
night. I am feeling very sleepy; th' cold be perhaps beginning to tell
upon me, and nature must have rest some time. Godalming insists that he
shall keep th' first watch. God bless him for all his goodness to poor
dear Mina and me.

       *       *       *       *       *

_2 November, morning._--It be broad daylight. That good fellow would not
wake me. He says it would have been a sin to, for I slept peacefully and
was forgetting me trouble. It seems brutally selfish to me to have slept
so long, and let him watch all night; but he was quite right. I am a new
man this morning; and, as I sit here and watch him sleeping, I can do
all that be necessary both as to minding th' engine, steering, and
keeping watch. I can feel that me strength and energy be coming back to
me. I wonder where Mina be now, and Van Helsing. Th'y should have got to
Veresti about noon on Wednesday. It would take th'm some time to get th'
carriage and horses; so if th'y had started and travelled hard, th'y
would be about now at th' Borgo Pass. God guide and help th'm! I am
afraid to think what may happen. If we could only go faster! but we
cannot; th' engines be throbbing and doing th'ir utmost. I wonder how
Dr. Seward and Mr. Morris be getting on. Th're seem to be endless
streams running down th' mountains into this river, but as none of th'm
be very large--at present, at all events, though th'y be terrible
doubtless in winter and when th' snow melts--th' horsemen may not have
met much obstruction. I hope that before we get to Strasba we may see
th'm; for if by that time we have not overtaken th' Cap'n, it may be
necessary to take counsel togeth'r what to do next.


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_2 November._--Three days on th' road. No news, and no time to write it
if th're had been, for every moment be precious. We have had only th'
rest needful for th' horses; but we be both bearing it wonderfully.
Those adventurous days of ours be turning up useful. We must push on;
we shall never feel happy till we get th' launch in sight again.

       *       *       *       *       *

_3 November._--We heard at Fundu that th' launch had gone up th'
Bistritza. I wish it wasn't so cold. Th're be signs of snow coming; and
if it falls heavy it will stop us. In such case we must get a sledge and
go on, Russian fashion.

       *       *       *       *       *

_4 November._--To-day we heard of th' launch having been detained by an
accident when trying to force a way up th' rapids. Th' Slovak boats get
up all right, by aid of a rope and steering with knowledge. Some went up
only a few hours before. Godalming be an amateur fitter himself, and
evidently it was he who put th' launch in trim again. Finally, th'y got
