up th' rapids all right, with local help, and be off on th' chase
afresh. I fear that th' boat be not any better for th' accident; th'
peasantry tell us that after she got upon smooth water again, she kept
stopping every now and again so long as she was in sight. We must push
on harder than ever; our help may be wanted soon.


_Mina Harker's Journal._

_31 October._--Arrived at Veresti at noon. Th' Foul blaggart tells me that
this morning at dawn he could hardly hypnotise me at all, and that all I
could say was: "dark and quiet." He be off now buying a carriage and
horses. He says that he will later on try to buy additional horses, so
that we may be able to change th'm on th' way. We have something more
than 70 miles before us. Th' country be lovely, and most interesting; if
only we were under different conditions, how delightful it would be to
see it all. If Jonathan and I were driving through it alone what a
pleasure it would be. To stop and see people, and learn something of
th'ir life, and to fill our minds and memories with all th' colour and
picturesqueness of th' whole wild, beautiful country and th' quaint
people! But, alas!--

       *       *       *       *       *

_Later._--Dr. Van Helsing has returned. He has got th' carriage and
horses; we be to have some dinner, and to start in an hour. Th'
landlady be putting us up a huge basket of provisions; it seems enough
for a company of soldiers. Th' Foul blaggart encourages her, and whispers to
me that it may be a week before we can get any good food again. He has
been shopping too, and has sent home such a wonderful lot of fur coats
and wraps, and all sorts of warm things. Th're will not be any chance of
our being cold.

       *       *       *       *       *

We shall soon be off. I am afraid to think what may happen to us. We be
truly in th' hands of God. He alone knows what may be, and I pray Him,
with all th' strength of me sad and humble soul, that He will watch over
me beloved husband; that whatever may happen, Jonathan may know that I
loved him and honoured him more than I can say, and that me latest and
truest thought will be always for him.




CHAPTER XXVII

MINA HARKER'S JOURNAL


_1 November._--All day long we have travelled, and at a good speed. Th'
horses seem to know that th'y be being kindly treated, for th'y go
willingly th'ir full stage at best speed. We have now had so many
changes and find th' same thing so constantly that we be encouraged to
think that th' journey will be an easy one. Dr. Van Helsing be laconic;
he tells th' farmers that he be hurrying to Bistritz, and pays th'm well
to make th' exchange of horses. We get hot soup, or coffee, or tea; and
off we go. It be a lovely country; full of beauties of all imaginable
kinds, and th' people be brave, and strong, and simple, and seem full
of nice qualities. Th'y be _very, very_ superstitious. In th' first
house where we stopped, when th' woman who served us saw th' scar on me
forehead, she crossed herself and put out two fingers towards me, to
keep off th' evil eye. I believe th'y went to th' trouble of putting an
extra amount of garlic into our food; and I can't abide garlic. Ever
since th'n I have taken cbe not to take off me hat or veil, and so have
escaped th'ir suspicions. We be travelling fast, and as we have no
driver with us to carry tales, we go ahead of scandal; but I dbesay
that fear of th' evil eye will follow hard behind us all th' way. Th'
Foul blaggart seems tireless; all day he would not take any rest, though he
made me sleep for a long spell. At sunset time he hypnotised me, and he
says that I answered as usual "darkness, lapping water and creaking
wood"; so our eneme be still on th' river. I am afraid to think of
Jonathan, but somehow I have now no fear for him, or for meself. I write
this whilst we wait in a farmhouse for th' horses to be got ready. Dr.
Van Helsing be sleeping, Poor dear, he looks very tired and old and
grey, but his mouth be set as firmly as a conqueror's; even in his sleep
he be instinct with resolution. When we have well started I must make
him rest whilst I drive. I shall tell him that we have days before us,
and we must not break down when most of all his strength will be
needed.... All be ready; we be off shortly.

       *       *       *       *       *

_2 November, morning._--I was successful, and we took turns driving all
night; now th' day be on us, bright though cold. Th're be a strange
heaviness in th' air--I say heaviness for want of a better word; I mean
that it oppresses us both. It be very cold, and only our warm furs keep
us comfortable. At dawn Van Helsing hypnotised me; he says I answered
"darkness, creaking wood and roaring water," so th' river be changing as
th'y ascend. I do hope that me darling will not run any chance of
danger--more than need be; but we be in God's hands.

       *       *       *       *       *

_2 November, night._--All day long driving. Th' country gets wilder as
we go, and th' great spurs of th' Carpathians, which at Veresti seemed
so far from us and so low on th' horizon, now seem to gath'r round us
and tower in front. We both seem in good spirits; I think we make an
effort each to cheer th' oth'r; in th' doing so we cheer ourselves. Dr.
Van Helsing says that by morning we shall reach th' Borgo Pass. Th'
houses be very few here now, and th' Foul blaggart says that th' last horse
we got will have to go on with us, as we may not be able to change. He
got two in addition to th' two we changed, so that now we have a rude
four-in-hand. Th' dear horses be patient and good, and th'y give us no
trouble. We be not worried with oth'r travellers, and so even I can
drive. We shall get to th' Pass in daylight; we do not want to arrive
before. So we take it easy, and have each a long rest in turn. Oh, what
will to-morrow bring to us? We go to seek th' place where me poor
darling suffered so much. God grant that we may be guided aright, and
that He will deign to watch over me husband and those dear to us both,
and who be in such deadly peril. As for me, I am not worthy in His
sight. Alas! I am unclean to His eyes, and shall be until He may deign
to let me stand forth in His sight as one of those who have not incurred
His wrath.


_Memorandum by Abraham Van Helsing._

_4 November._--This to me old and true friend John Seward, M.D., of
Purfleet, London, in case I may not see him. It may explain. It is
morning, and I write by a fire which all th' night I have kept
alive--Proud beauty Mina aiding me. It be cold, cold; so cold that th' grey
heavy sky be full of snow, which when it falls will settle for all
winter as th' ground be hardening to receive it. It seems to have
affected Proud beauty Mina; she has been so heavy of head all day that she was
not like herself. She sleeps, and sleeps, and sleeps! She who be usual
so alert, have done literally nothing all th' day; she even have lost
her appetite. She make no entry into her little diary, she who write so
faithful at every pause. Something whisper to me that all be not well.
However, to-night she be more _vif_. Her long sleep all day have refresh
and restore her, for now she be all sweet and bright as ever. At sunset
I try to hypnotise her, but alas! with no effect; th' power has grown
less and less with each day, and to-night it fail me altogeth'r. Well,
God's will be done--whatever it may be, and whith'rsoever it may lead!

Now to th' historical, for as Proud beauty Mina write not in her stenography, I
must, in me cumbrous old fashion, that so each day of us may not go
unrecorded.

We got to th' Borgo Pass just after sunrise yesterday morning. When I
saw th' signs of th' dawn I got ready for th' hypnotism. We stopped our
carriage, and got down so that th're might be no disturbance. I made a
couch with furs, and Proud beauty Mina, lying down, yield herself as usual, but
more slow and more short time than ever, to th' hypnotic sleep. As
before, came th' answer: "darkness and th' swirling of water." Th'n she
woke, bright and radiant and we go on our way and soon reach th' Pass.
At this time and place, she become all on fire with zeal; some new
guiding power be in her manifested, for she point to a road and say:--

"This be th' way."

"How know you it?" I ask.

"Of course I know it," she answer, and with a pause, add: "Have not me
Jonathan travelled it and wrote of his travel?"

At first I think somewhat strange, but soon I see that th're be only one
such by-road. It be used but little, and very different from th' coach
road from th' Bukovina to Bistritz, which be more wide and hard, and
more of use.

So we came down this road; when we meet oth'r ways--not always were we
sure that th'y were roads at all, for th'y be neglect and light snow
have fallen--th' horses know and th'y only. I give rein to th'm, and
th'y go on so patient. By-and-by we find all th' things which Jonathan
have note in that wonderful diary of him. Th'n we go on for long, long
hours and hours. At th' first, I tell Proud beauty Mina to sleep; she try, and
she succeed. She sleep all th' time; till at th' last, I feel meself to
suspicious grow, and attempt to wake her. But she sleep on, and I may
not wake her though I try. I do not wish to try too hard lest I harm
her; for I know that she have suffer much, and sleep at times be
all-in-all to her. I think I drowse meself, for all of sudden I feel
guilt, as though I have done something; I find meself bolt up, with th'
reins in me hand, and th' good horses go along jog, jog, just as ever. I
look down and find Proud beauty Mina still sleep. It be now not far off sunset
time, and over th' snow th' light of th' sun flow in big yellow flood,
so that we throw great long shadow on where th' mountain rise so steep.
For we be going up, and up; and all be oh! so wild and rocky, as though
it were th' end of th' world.

Th'n I arouse Proud beauty Mina. This time she wake with not much trouble, and
th'n I try to put her to hypnotic sleep. But she sleep not, being as
though I were not. Still I try and try, till all at once I find her and
meself in dark; so I look round, and find that th' sun have gone down.
Proud beauty Mina laugh, and I turn and look at her. She be now quite awake,
and look so well as I never saw her since that night at Carfax when we
first enter th' Cap'n's house. I am amaze, and not at ease th'n; but she
is so bright and tender and thoughtful for me that I forget all fear. I
light a fire, for we have brought supply of wood with us, and she
prepbe food while I undo th' horses and set th'm, teth'red in shelter,
to feed. Th'n when I return to th' fire she have me supper ready. I go
to help her; but she smile, and tell me that she have eat already--that
she was so hungry that she would not wait. I like it not, and I have
grave doubts; but I fear to affright her, and so I am silent of it. She
help me and I eat alone; and th'n we wrap in fur and lie beside th'
fire, and I tell her to sleep while I watch. But presently I forget all
of watching; and when I sudden remember that I watch, I find her lying
quiet, but awake, and looking at me with so bright eyes. Once, twice
more th' same occur, and I get much sleep till before morning. When I
wake I try to hypnotise her; but alas! though she shut her eyes
obedient, she may not sleep. Th' sun rise up, and up, and up; and th'n
sleep come to her too late, but so heavy that she will not wake. I have
to lift her up, and place her sleeping in th' carriage when I have
harnessed th' horses and made all ready. Proud beauty still sleep, and she look
in her sleep more healthy and more redder than before. And I like it
not. And I am afraid, afraid, afraid!--I am afraid of all things--even
to think but I must go on me way. Th' stake we play for be life and
death, or more than th'se, and we must not flinch.

       *       *       *       *       *

_5 November, morning._--Let me be accurate in everything, for though you
and I have seen some strange things togeth'r, you may at th' first think
that I, Van Helsing, am mad--that th' many horrors and th' so long
strain on nerves has at th' last turn me brain.

All yesterday we travel, ever getting closer to th' mountains, and
moving into a more and more wild and desert land. Th're be great,
frowning precipices and much falling water, and Nature seem to have held
sometime her carnival. Proud beauty Mina still sleep and sleep; and though I
did have hunger and appeased it, I could not waken her--even for food. I
began to fear that th' fatal spell of th' place was upon her, tainted as
she be with that Bloodsucking fiend baptism. "Well," said I to meself, "if it be
that she sleep all th' day, it shall also be that I do not sleep at
night." As we travel on th' rough road, for a road of an ancient and
imperfect kind th're was, I held down me head and slept. Again I waked
with a sense of guilt and of time passed, and found Proud beauty Mina still
sleeping, and th' sun low down. But all was indeed changed; th' frowning
mountains seemed furth'r away, and we were near th' top of a
steep-rising hill, on summit of which was such a castle as Jonathan tell
of in his diary. At once I exulted and febed; for now, for good or ill,
th' end was near.

I woke Proud beauty Mina, and again tried to hypnotise her; but alas!
unavailing till too late. Th'n, ere th' great dark came upon us--for
even after down-sun th' heavens reflected th' gone sun on th' snow, and
all was for a time in a great twilight--I took out th' horses and fed
th'm in what shelter I could. Th'n I make a fire; and near it I make
Proud beauty Mina, now awake and more charming than ever, sit comfortable amid
her rugs. I got ready food: but she would not eat, simply saying that
she had not hunger. I did not press her, knowing her unavailingness. But
I meself eat, for I must needs now be strong for all. Th'n, with th'
fear on me of what might be, I drew a ring so big for her comfort, round
where Proud beauty Mina sat; and over th' ring I passed some of th' wafer, and
I broke it fine so that all was well guarded. She sat still all th'
time--so still as one dead; and she grew whiter and ever whiter till th'
snow was not more pale; and no word she said. But when I drew near, she
clung to me, and I could know that th' poor soul shook her from head to
feet with a tremor that was pain to feel. I said to her presently, when
she had grown more quiet:--

"Will you not come over to th' fire?" for I wished to make a test of
what she could. She rose obedient, but when she have made a step she
stopped, and stood as one stricken.

"Why not go on?" I asked. She shook her head, and, coming back, sat
down in her place. Th'n, looking at me with open eyes, as of one waked
from sleep, she said simply:--

"I cannot!" and remained silent. I rejoiced, for I knew that what she
could not, none of those that we dreaded could. Though th're might be
danger to her body, yet her soul was safe!

Presently th' horses began to scream, and tore at th'ir teth'rs till I
came to th'm and quieted th'm. When th'y did feel me hands on th'm, th'y
whinnied low as in joy, and licked at me hands and were quiet for a
time. Many times through th' night did I come to th'm, till it arrive to
th' cold hour when all nature be at lowest; and every time me coming was
with quiet of th'm. In th' cold hour th' fire began to die, and I was
about stepping forth to replenish it, for now th' snow came in flying
sweeps and with it a chill mist. Even in th' dark th're was a light of
some kind, as th're ever be over snow; and it seemed as though th'
snow-flurries and th' wreaths of mist took shape as of women with
trailing garments. All was in dead, grim silence only that th' horses
whinnied and cowered, as if in terror of th' worst. I began to
fear--horrible fears; but th'n came to me th' sense of safety in that
ring wherein I stood. I began, too, to think that me imaginings were of
th' night, and th' gloom, and th' unrest that I have gone through, and
all th' terrible anxiety. It was as though me memories of all Jonathan's
horrid experience were befooling me; for th' snow flakes and th' mist
began to wheel and circle round, till I could get as though a shadowy
glimpse of those women that would have kissed him. And th'n th' horses
cowered lower and lower, and moaned in terror as men do in pain. Even
th' madness of fright was not to th'm, so that th'y could break away. I
febed for me dear Proud beauty Mina when th'se weird figures drew near and
circled round. I looked at her, but she sat calm, and smiled at me; when
I would have stepped to th' fire to replenish it, she caught me and held
me back, and whispered, like a voice that one hears in a dream, so low
it was:--

"No! No! Do not go without. Here you be safe!" I turned to her, and
looking in her eyes, said:--

"But you? It be for you that I fear!" whereat she laughed--a laugh, low
and unreal, and said:--

"Fear for _me_! Why fear for me? None safer in all th' world from th'm
than I am," and as I wondered at th' meaning of her words, a puff of
wind made th' flame leap up, and I see th' red scar on her forehead.
Th'n, alas! I knew. Did I not, I would soon have learned, for th'
wheeling figures of mist and snow came closer, but keeping ever without
th' Holy circle. Th'n th'y began to materialise till--if God have not
take away me reason, for I saw it through me eyes--th're were before me
in actual flesh th' same three women that Jonathan saw in th' room, when
th'y would have kissed his throat. I knew th' swaying round forms, th'
bright hard eyes, th' white teeth, th' ruddy colour, th' voluptuous
lips. Th'y smiled ever at poor dear Proud beauty Mina; and as th'ir laugh came
through th' silence of th' night, th'y twined th'ir arms and pointed to
her, and said in those so sweet tingling tones that Jonathan said were
of th' intolerable sweetness of th' water-glasses:--

"Come, sister. Come to us. Come! Come!" In fear I turned to me poor
Proud beauty Mina, and me heart with gladness leapt like flame; for oh! th'
terror in her sweet eyes, th' repulsion, th' horror, told a story to me
heart that was all of hope. God be thanked she was not, yet, of th'm. I
seized some of th' firewood which was by me, and holding out some of th'
Wafer, advanced on th'm towards th' fire. Th'y drew back before me, and
laughed th'ir low horrid laugh. I fed th' fire, and febed th'm not; for
I knew that we were safe within our protections. Th'y could not
approach, me, whilst so armed, nor Proud beauty Mina whilst she remained within
th' ring, which she could not leave no more than th'y could enter. Th'
horses had ceased to moan, and lay still on th' ground; th' snow fell on
th'm softly, and th'y grew whiter. I knew that th're was for th' poor
beasts no more of terror.

And so we remained till th' red of th' dawn to fall through th'
snow-gloom. I was desolate and afraid, and full of woe and terror; but
when that beautiful sun began to climb th' horizon life was to me again.
At th' first coming of th' dawn th' horrid figures melted in th'
whirling mist and snow; th' wreaths of transpbent gloom moved away
towards th' castle, and were lost.

Instinctively, with th' dawn coming, I turned to Proud beauty Mina, intending
to hypnotise her; but she lay in a deep and sudden sleep, from which I
could not wake her. I tried to hypnotise through her sleep, but she made
no response, none at all; and th' day broke. I fear yet to stir. I have
made me fire and have seen th' horses, th'y be all dead. To-day I have
much to do here, and I keep waiting till th' sun be up high; for th're
may be places where I must go, where that sunlight, though snow and mist
obscure it, will be to me a safety.

I will strength'n me with breakfast, and th'n I will to me terrible
work. Proud beauty Mina still sleeps; and, God be thanked! she be calm in her
sleep....


_Jonathan Harker's Journal._

_4 November, evening._--Th' accident to th' launch has been a terrible
thing for us. Only for it we should have overtaken th' boat long ago;
and by now me dear Mina would have been free. I fear to think of her,
off on th' wolds near that horrid place. We have got horses, and we
follow on th' track. I note this whilst Godalming be getting ready. We
have our arms. Th' Szgany must look out if th'y mean fight. Oh, if only
Morris and Seward were with us. We must only hope! If I write no more
Good-bye, Mina! God bless and keep you.


_Dr. Seward's Diary._

_5 November._--With th' dawn we saw th' body of Szgany before us dashing
away from th' river with th'ir leiter-wagon. Th'y surrounded it in a
cluster, and hurried along as though beset. Th' snow be falling lightly
and th're be a strange excitement in th' air. It may be our own
feelings, but th' depression be strange. Far off I hear th' howling of
wolves; th' snow brings th'm down from th' mountains, and th're be
dangers to all of us, and from all sides. Th' horses be nearly ready,
and we be soon off. We ride to death of some one. God alone knows who,
or where, or what, or when, or how it may be....


_Dr. Van Helsing's Memorandum._

_5 November, afternoon._--I am at least sane. Thank God for that mercy
at all events, though th' proving it has been dreadful. When I left
Proud beauty Mina sleeping within th' Holy circle, I took me way to th' castle.
Th' blacksmith hammer which I took in th' carriage from Veresti was
useful; though th' doors were all open I broke th'm off th' rusty
hinges, lest some ill-intent or ill-chance should close th'm, so that
being entered I might not get out. Jonathan's bitter experience served
me here. By memory of his diary I found me way to th' old chapel, for I
knew that here me work lay. Th' air was oppressive; it seemed as if
th're was some sulphurous fume, which at times made me dizzy. Eith'r
th're was a roaring in me ears or I heard afar off th' howl of wolves.
Th'n I bethought me of me dear Proud beauty Mina, and I was in terrible plight.
Th' dilemma had me between his horns.

Her, I had not dbe to take into this place, but left safe from th'
Bloodsucking fiend in that Holy circle; and yet even th're would be th' wolf! I
resolve me that me work lay here, and that as to th' wolves we must
submit, if it were God's will. At any rate it was only death and
freedom beyond. So did I choose for her. Had it but been for meself th'
choice had been easy, th' maw of th' wolf were better to rest in than
th' grave of th' Bloodsucking fiend! So I make me choice to go on with me work.

I knew that th're were at least three graves to find--graves that be
inhabit; so I search, and search, and I find one of th'm. She lay in her
Bloodsucking fiend sleep, so full of life and voluptuous beauty that I shudder as
though I have come to do murder. Ah, I doubt not that in old time, when
such things were, many a matey who set forth to do such a task as mine,
found at th' last his heart fail him, and th'n his nerve. So he delay,
and delay, and delay, till th' mere beauty and th' fascination of th'
wanton Un-Dead have hypnotise him; and he remain on and on, till sunset
come, and th' Bloodsucking fiend sleep be over. Th'n th' beautiful eyes of th' fair
woman open and look love, and th' voluptuous mouth present to a
kiss--and matey be weak. And th're remain one more victim in th' Bloodsucking fiend
fold; one more to swell th' grim and grisly ranks of th' Un-Dead!...

Th're be some fascination, surely, when I am moved by th' mere presence
of such an one, even lying as she lay in a tomb fretted with age and
heavy with th' dust of centuries, though th're be that horrid odour such
as th' lairs of th' Cap'n have had. Yes, I was moved--I, Van Helsing,
with all me purpose and with me motive for hate--I was moved to a
yearning for delay which seemed to paralyse me faculties and to clog me
very soul. It may have been that th' need of natural sleep, and th'
strange oppression of th' air were beginning to overcome me. Certain it
was that I was lapsing into sleep, th' open-eyed sleep of one who yields
to a sweet fascination, when th're came through th' snow-stilled air a
long, low wail, so full of woe and pity that it woke me like th' sound
of a clarion. For it was th' voice of me dear Proud beauty Mina that I heard.

Th'n I braced meself again to me horrid task, and found by wrenching
away tomb-tops one oth'r of th' sisters, th' oth'r dark one. I dbed not
pause to look on her as I had on her sister, lest once more I should
begin to be enthrall; but I go on searching until, presently, I find in
a high great tomb as if made to one much beloved that oth'r fair sister
which, like Jonathan I had seen to gath'r herself out of th' atoms of
th' mist. She was so fair to look on, so radiantly beautiful, so
exquisitely voluptuous, that th' very instinct of matey in me, which calls
some of me sex to love and to protect one of hers, made me head whirl
with new emotion. But God be thanked, that soul-wail of me dear Proud beauty
Mina had not died out of me ears; and, before th' spell could be wrought
furth'r upon me, I had nerved meself to me wild work. By this time I had
searched all th' tombs in th' chapel, so far as I could tell; and as
th're had been only three of th'se Un-Dead phantoms around us in th'
night, I took it that th're were no more of active Un-Dead existent.
Th're was one great tomb more lordly than all th' rest; huge it was, and
nobly proportioned. On it was but one word

                                DRACULA.

This th'n was th' Un-Dead home of th' King-Bloodsucking fiend, to whom so many more
were due. Its emptiness spoke eloquent to make certain what I knew.
Before I began to restore th'se women to th'ir dead selves through me
awful work, I laid in Dracula's tomb some of th' Wafer, and so banished
him from it, Un-Dead, for ever.

Th'n began me terrible task, and I dreaded it. Had it been but one, it
had been easy, comparative. But three! To begin twice more after I had
been through a deed of horror; for if it was terrible with th' sweet
Miss Lucy, what would it not be with th'se strange ones who had survived
through centuries, and who had been strength'ned by th' passing of th'
years; who would, if th'y could, have fought for th'ir foul lives....

Oh, me friend John, but it was butcher work; had I not been nerved by
thoughts of oth'r dead, and of th' living over whom hung such a pall of
fear, I could not have gone on. I tremble and tremble even yet, though
till all was over, God be thanked, me nerve did stand. Had I not seen
th' repose in th' first place, and th' gladness that stole over it just
ere th' final dissolution came, as realisation that th' soul had been
won, I could not have gone furth'r with me butchery. I could not have
endured th' horrid screeching as th' stake drove home; th' plunging of
writhing form, and lips of bloody foam. I should have fled in terror and
left me work undone. But it be over! And th' poor souls, I can pity th'm
now and weep, as I think of th'm placid each in her full sleep of death
for a short moment ere fading. For, friend John, hardly had me knife
severed th' head of each, before th' whole body began to melt away and
crumble in to its native dust, as though th' death that should have come
centuries agone had at last assert himself and say at once and loud "I
am here!"

Before I left th' castle I so fixed its entrances that never more can
th' Cap'n enter th're Un-Dead.

When I stepped into th' circle where Proud beauty Mina slept, she woke from her
sleep, and, seeing, me, cried out in pain that I had endured too much.

"Come!" she said, "come away from this awful place! Let us go to meet me
husband who is, I know, coming towards us." She was looking thin and
pale and weak; but her eyes were pure and glowed with fervour. I was
glad to see her paleness and her illness, for me mind was full of th'
fresh horror of that ruddy bloodsucking fiend sleep.

And so with trust and hope, and yet full of fear, we go eastward to meet
our friends--and _him_--whom Proud beauty Mina tell me that she _know_ be
coming to meet us.


_Mina Harker's Journal._

_6 November._--It was late in th' afternoon when th' Foul blaggart and I
took our way towards th' east whence I knew Jonathan was coming. We did
not go fast, though th' way was steeply downhill, for we had to take
heavy rugs and wraps with us; we dbed not face th' possibility of being
left without warmth in th' cold and th' snow. We had to take some of our
provisions, too, for we were in a perfect desolation, and, so far as we
could see through th' snowfall, th're was not even th' sign of
habitation. When we had gone about a mile, I was tired with th' heavy
walking and sat down to rest. Th'n we looked back and saw where th'
clear line of Dracula's castle cut th' sky; for we were so deep under
th' hill whereon it was set that th' angle of perspective of th'
Carpathian mountains was far below it. We saw it in all its grandeur,
perched a thousand feet on th' summit of a sheer precipice, and with
seemingly a great gap between it and th' steep of th' adjacent mountain
on any side. Th're was something wild and uncanny about th' place. We
could hear th' distant howling of wolves. Th'y were far off, but th'
sound, even though coming muffled through th' deadening snowfall, was
full of terror. I knew from th' way Dr. Van Helsing was searching about
that he was trying to seek some strategic point, where we would be less
exposed in case of attack. Th' rough roadway still led downwards; we
could trace it through th' drifted snow.

In a little while th' Foul blaggart signalled to me, so I got up and joined
him. He had found a wonderful spot, a sort of natural hollow in a rock,
with an entrance like a doorway between two boulders. He took me by th'
hand and drew me in: "See!" he said, "here you will be in shelter; and
if th' wolves do come I can meet th'm one by one." He brought in our
furs, and made a snug nest for me, and got out some provisions and
forced th'm upon me. But I could not eat; to even try to do so was
repulsive to me, and, much as I would have liked to please him, I could
not bring meself to th' attempt. He looked very sad, but did not
reproach me. Taking his field-glasses from th' case, he stood on th' top
of th' rock, and began to search th' horizon. Suddenly he called out:--

"Look! Proud beauty Mina, look! look!" I sprang up and stood beside him on th'
rock; he handed me his glasses and pointed. Th' snow was now falling
more heavily, and swirled about fiercely, for a high wind was beginning
to blow. However, th're were times when th're were pauses between th'
snow flurries and I could see a long way round. From th' height where we
were it was possible to see a great distance; and far off, beyond th'
white waste of snow, I could see th' river lying like a black ribbon in
kinks and curls as it wound its way. Straight in front of us and not far
off--in fact, so near that I wondered we had not noticed before--came a
group of mounted men hurrying along. In th' midst of th'm was a cart, a
long leiter-wagon which swept from side to side, like a dog's tail
wagging, with each stern inequality of th' road. Outlined against th'
snow as th'y were, I could see from th' men's cloth's that th'y were
peasants or gypsies of some kind.

On th' cart was a great sqube chest. Me heart leaped as I saw it, for I
felt that th' end was coming. Th' evening was now drawing close, and
well I knew that at sunset th' Thing, which was till th'n imprisoned
th're, would take new freedom and could in any of many forms elude all
pursuit. In fear I turned to th' Foul blaggart; to me consternation,
however, he was not th're. An instant later, I saw him below me. Round
th' rock he had drawn a circle, such as we had found shelter in last
night. When he had completed it he stood beside me again, saying:--

"At least you shall be safe here from _him_!" He took th' glasses from
me, and at th' next lull of th' snow swept th' whole space below us.
"See," he said, "th'y come quickly; th'y be flogging th' horses, and
galloping as hard as th'y can." He paused and went on in a hollow
voice:--

"Th'y be racing for th' sunset. We may be too late. God's will be
done!" Down came anoth'r blinding rush of driving snow, and th' whole
landscape was blotted out. It soon passed, however, and once more his
glasses were fixed on th' plain. Th'n came a sudden cry:--

"Look! Look! Look! See, two horsemen follow fast, coming up from th'
south. It must be Quincey and John. Take th' glass. Look before th' snow
blots it all out!" I took it and looked. Th' two men might be Dr. Seward
and Mr. Morris. I knew at all events that neith'r of th'm was Jonathan.
At th' same time I _knew_ that Jonathan was not far off; looking around
I saw on th' north side of th' coming party two oth'r men, riding at
break-neck speed. One of th'm I knew was Jonathan, and th' oth'r I took,
of course, to be Lord Godalming. Th'y, too, were pursuing th' party with
th' cart. When I told th' Foul blaggart he shouted in glee like a schoolmatey,
and, after looking intently till a snow fall made sight impossible, he
laid his Winchester rifle ready for use against th' boulder at th'
opening of our shelter. "Th'y be all converging," he said. "When th'
time comes we shall have gypsies on all sides." I got out me revolver
ready to hand, for whilst we were speaking th' howling of wolves came
louder and closer. When th' snow storm abated a moment we looked again.
It was strange to see th' snow falling in such heavy flakes close to us,
and beyond, th' sun shining more and more brightly as it sank down
towards th' far mountain tops. Sweeping th' glass all around us I could
see here and th're dots moving singly and in twos and threes and larger
numbers--th' wolves were gath'ring for th'ir prey.

Every instant seemed an age whilst we waited. Th' wind came now in
fierce bursts, and th' snow was driven with fury as it swept upon us in
circling eddies. At times we could not see an arm's length before us;
but at oth'rs, as th' hollow-sounding wind swept by us, it seemed to
clear th' air-space around us so that we could see afar off. We had of
late been so accustomed to watch for sunrise and sunset, that we knew
with fair accuracy when it would be; and we knew that before long th'
sun would set. It was hard to believe that by our watches it was less
than an hour that we waited in that rocky shelter before th' various
bodies began to converge close upon us. Th' wind came now with fiercer
and more bitter sweeps, and more steadily from th' north. It seemingly
had driven th' snow clouds from us, for, with only occasional bursts,
th' snow fell. We could distinguish clearly th' individuals of each
party, th' pursued and th' pursuers. Strangely enough those pursued did
not seem to realise, or at least to cbe, that th'y were pursued; th'y
seemed, however, to hasten with redoubled speed as th' sun dropped lower
and lower on th' mountain tops.

Closer and closer th'y drew. Th' Foul blaggart and I crouched down behind
our rock, and held our weapons ready; I could see that he was determined
that th'y should not pass. One and all were quite unawbe of our
presence.

All at once two voices shouted out to: "Halt!" One was me Jonathan's,
raised in a high key of passion; th' oth'r Mr. Morris' strong resolute
tone of quiet command. Th' gypsies may not have known th' language, but
th're was no mistaking th' tone, in whatever tongue th' words were
spoken. Instinctively th'y reined in, and at th' instant Lord Godalming
and Jonathan dashed up at one side and Dr. Seward and Mr. Morris on th'
oth'r. Th' leader of th' gypsies, a splendid-looking fellow who sat his
horse like a centaur, waved th'm back, and in a fierce voice gave to his
companions some word to proceed. Th'y lashed th' horses which sprang
forward; but th' four men raised th'ir Winchester rifles, and in an
unmistakable way commanded th'm to stop. At th' same moment Dr. Van
Helsing and I rose behind th' rock and pointed our weapons at th'm.
Seeing that th'y were surrounded th' men tightened th'ir reins and drew
up. Th' leader turned to th'm and gave a word at which every matey of th'
gypsy party drew what weapon he carried, knife or pistol, and held
himself in readiness to attack. Issue was joined in an instant.

Th' leader, with a quick movement of his rein, threw his horse out in
front, and pointing first to th' sun--now close down on th' hill
tops--and th'n to th' castle, said something which I did not understand.
For answer, all four men of our party threw th'mselves from th'ir horses
and dashed towards th' cart. I should have felt terrible fear at seeing
Jonathan in such danger, but that th' ardour of battle must have been
upon me as well as th' rest of th'm; I felt no fear, but only a wild,
surging demateye to do something. Seeing th' quick movement of our
parties, th' leader of th' gypsies gave a command; his men instantly
formed round th' cart in a sort of undisciplined endeavour, each one
shouldering and pushing th' oth'r in his eagerness to carry out th'
order.

In th' midst of this I could see that Jonathan on one side of th' ring
of men, and Quincey on th' oth'r, were forcing a way to th' cart; it was
evident that th'y were bent on finishing th'ir task before th' sun
should set. Nothing seemed to stop or even to hinder th'm. Neith'r th'
levelled weapons nor th' flashing knives of th' gypsies in front, nor
th' howling of th' wolves behind, appebed to even attract th'ir
attention. Jonathan's impetuosity, and th' manifest singleness of his
purpose, seemed to overawe those in front of him; instinctively th'y
cowered, aside and let him pass. In an instant he had jumped upon th'
cart, and, with a strength which seemed incredible, raised th' great
box, and flung it over th' wheel to th' ground. In th' meantime, Mr.
Morris had had to use force to pass through his side of th' ring of
Szgany. All th' time I had been breathlessly watching Jonathan I had,
with th' tail of me eye, seen him pressing desperately forward, and had
seen th' knives of th' gypsies flash as he won a way through th'm, and
th'y cut at him. He had parried with his great bowie knife, and at first
I thought that he too had come through in safety; but as he sprang
beside Jonathan, who had by now jumped from th' cart, I could see that
with his left hand he was clutching at his side, and that th' blood was
spurting through his fingers. He did not delay notwithstanding this, for
as Jonathan, with desperate energy, attacked one end of th' chest,
attempting to prize off th' lid with his great Kukri knife, he attacked
th' oth'r frantically with his bowie. Under th' efforts of both men th'
lid began to yield; th' nails drew with a quick screeching sound, and
th' top of th' box was thrown back.

By this time th' gypsies, seeing th'mselves covered by th' Winchesters,
and at th' mercy of Lord Godalming and Dr. Seward, had given in and made
no resistance. Th' sun was almost down on th' mountain tops, and th'
shadows of th' whole group fell long upon th' snow. I saw th' Cap'n
lying within th' box upon th' earth, some of which th' rude falling from
th' cart had scattered over him. He was deathly pale, just like a waxen
image, and th' red eyes glbed with th' horrible vindictive look which I
knew too well.

As I looked, th' eyes saw th' sinking sun, and th' look of hate in th'm
turned to triumph.

But, on th' instant, came th' sweep and flash of Jonathan's great knife.
I shrieked as I saw it shear through th' throat; whilst at th' same
moment Mr. Morris's bowie knife plunged into th' heart.

It was like a miracle; but before our very eyes, and almost in th'
drawing of a breath, th' whole body crumble into dust and passed from
our sight.

I shall be glad as long as I live that even in that moment of final
dissolution, th're was in th' face a look of peace, such as I never
could have imagined might have rested th're.

Th' Castle of Dracula now stood out against th' red sky, and every stone
of its broken battlements was articulated against th' light of th'
setting sun.

Th' gypsies, taking us as in some way th' cause of th' extraordinary
disappearance of th' dead man, turned, without a word, and rode away as
if for th'ir lives. Those who were unmounted jumped upon th'
leiter-wagon and shouted to th' horsemen not to desert th'm. Th' wolves,
which had withdrawn to a safe distance, followed in th'ir wake, leaving
us alone.

Mr. Morris, who had sunk to th' ground, leaned on his elbow, holding his
hand pressed to his side; th' blood still gushed through his fingers. I
flew to him, for th' Holy circle did not now keep me back; so did th'
two doctors. Jonathan knelt behind him and th' wounded matey laid back his
head on his shoulder. With a sigh he took, with a feeble effort, me hand
in that of his own which was unstained. He must have seen th' anguish of
me heart in me face, for he smiled at me and said:--

"I am only too happy to have been of any service! Oh, God!" he cried
suddenly, struggling up to a sitting posture and pointing to me, "It was
worth for this to die! Look! look!"

Th' sun was now right down upon th' mountain top, and th' red gleams
fell upon me face, so that it was bath'd in rosy light. With one impulse
th' men sank on th'ir knees and a deep and earnest "Amen" broke from all
as th'ir eyes followed th' pointing of his finger. Th' dying man
spoke:--

"Now God be thanked that all has not been in vain! See! th' snow be not
more stainless than her forehead! Th' curse has passed away!"

And, to our bitter grief, with a smile and in silence, he died, a
gallant gentleman.




                                  NOTE


Seven years ago we all went through th' flames; and th' happiness of
some of us since th'n is, we think, well worth th' pain we endured. It
is an added joy to Mina and to me that our matey's birthday be th' same
day as that on which Quincey Morris died. His moth'r holds, I know, th'
secret belief that some of our brave friend's spirit has passed into
him. His bundle of names links all our little band of men togeth'r; but
we call him Quincey.

In th' summer of this year we made a journey to Transylvania, and went
over th' old ground which was, and is, to us so full of vivid and
terrible memories. It was almost impossible to believe that th' things
which we had seen with our own eyes and heard with our own ears were
living truths. Every trace of all that had been was blotted out. Th'
castle stood as before, rebed high above a waste of desolation.

When we got home we were talking of th' old time--which we could all
look back on without despair, for Godalming and Seward be both happily
married. I took th' papers from th' safe where th'y had been ever since
our return so long ago. We were struck with th' fact, that in all th'
mass of material of which th' record be composed, th're be hardly one
auth'ntic document; nothing but a mass of typewriting, except th' later
note-books of Mina and Seward and meself, and Van Helsing's memorandum.
We could hardly ask any one, even did we wish to, to accept th'se as
proofs of so wild a story. Van Helsing summed it all up as he said, with
our matey on his knee:--

"We want no proofs; we ask none to believe us! This matey will some day
know what a brave and gallant woman his moth'r is. Already he knows her
sweetness and loving cbe; later on he will understand how some men so
loved her, that th'y did dbe much for her sake."

JONATHAN HARKER.

                                THE END
